<b>Preface</b><br>
This textbook, Advanced History for Secondary Schools, is written specifically for<br>
Form Five students in the United Republic of Tanzania. It is written in accordance<br>
with the 2010 History Syllabus for Advanced Secondary Education, Form V-VI,<br>
issued by the then Ministry of Education and Vocational Training.<br>
The book consists of seven chapters, namely Pre-colonial African societies, Africa and<br>
Europe in the 15th century, People of African origin in the New World, The colonial<br>
state and agricultural system up to 1914, Changes in the colonial economy and social<br>
services after the Second World War, Nationalism and decolonisation in Africa, as<br>
well as Political and economic development in Tanzania since independence. Each<br>
chapter contains texts, illustrations, activities and exercises. You are encouraged to<br>
do all the activities and exercises, as well as other assignments that your teacher will<br>
provide. Doing so will enable you to develop the intended competencies.<br>
<b>Tanzania Institute of Education</b><br>

<b>Student's Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Advanced History for Secondary Schools</b><br>
1<br>
<b>Introduction</b><br>
Pre-colonial African societies existed in Africa before colonial rule. The<br>
societies had their own socio-economic and political ways of life. They had<br>
reached a considerable level of socio-economic and political development by<br>
the time colonialism started in Africa. In this chapter, you will learn about the<br>
pre-colonial social formations and political organisations that existed in Africa.<br>
You will also learn about pre-colonial education and culture. The competencies<br>
developed will enable you to maintain the good socio-economic systems that<br>
existed in pre-colonial Africa, for future generations.<br>
Think of the society of which you are a member. Can you trace its origin?<br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>One</b><br>
<b>Pre-colonial African societies</b><br>
<b>Social and political organisations in</b><br>
pre-colonial Africa<br>
<b>Different points of view are given on</b><br>
the nature of African societies before<br>
colonialism. These are the Afro-centric<br>
and the Euro-centric views. Afro-<br>
centric scholars argue that, before the<br>
coming of the colonialists, African<br>
societies were dynamic in all aspects<br>
of life, including politics, culture,<br>
economy and technology. Euro-centric<br>
scholars, on the other hand, argue that,<br>
before the coming of Europeans to<br>
<b>Africa, Africans were barbaric, static</b><br>
and uncivilised. However, the Euro-<br>
centric point of view is misleading<br>
because pre-colonial African societies<br>
displayed remarkable socio-economic<br>
and political transformation. That<br>
transformation<br>
was<br>
manifested<br>
in social, political and economic<br>
structures, which were very complex<br>
and dynamic.<br>
<b>The dynamic nature of pre-colonial</b><br>
<b>African societies was the result of</b><br>
many factors. The factors included the<br>
development of production systems,<br>
the growth of population and the<br>
development of trade. However, in<br>
order to understand these pre-colonial<br>
social formations, it is important to<br>
define some of the key terms used<br>
1. HIST-F5 (DOUBLE COLUMN) backUP.indd   1<br>
1. HIST-F5 (DOUBLE COLUMN) backUP.indd   1<br>
07/03/2022   18:59:44<br>
07/03/2022   18:59:44<br>
<b>Student's Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Advanced History for Secondary Schools</b><br>
2<br>
in history. The terms include labour,<br>
human labour, objects of labour, means<br>
of labour, relations of production,<br>
productive forces and modes of<br>
production.<br>
<b>Labour</b><br>
<b>Labour refers to the physical and</b><br>
mental ability used in production.<br>
<b>Human beings need to produce to meet</b><br>
their basic needs, namely food, shelter<br>
and clothing. Thus, material production<br>
is the basis of human existence.<br>
<b>Labour may also be defined as a</b><br>
purposeful activity directed towards<br>
the production of specific objects and<br>
essentials of human life. It is a form<br>
of social activity that brings people<br>
together and facilitates different levels<br>
of human cooperation. This means that<br>
humans’ productive activities and the<br>
struggle against nature always take<br>
place within the framework of material<br>
conditions and specific social relations.<br>
<b>Means of labour</b><br>
<b>Means of labour refer to all the things</b><br>
human beings use in the process of<br>
production. With the aid of such things,<br>
human beings act upon objects of<br>
labour and transform them. Means of<br>
labour are also known as instruments<br>
of labour. They range from simple to<br>
complex tools and play a vital role in<br>
material production. They act as the<br>
extension of a human’s natural organs<br>
such as hands, legs, eyes and the brain.<br>
<b>Examples of means of labour include</b><br>
the hand hoe, machete, tractor and<br>
computer. In short, means of labour<br>
help humans to simplify work. Without<br>
them work becomes difficult.<br>
<b>Object of labour</b><br>
<b>An object of labour refers to everything</b><br>
on which humans’ labour is applied.<br>
<b>They are the resources upon which</b><br>
human labour is applied to produce.<br>
<b>Such resources are mostly found in</b><br>
their natural environments such as<br>
land, rivers and forests. That means<br>
human beings need objects of labour<br>
to work. Otherwise, work becomes<br>
impossible. For example, you may<br>
have a good fishing instrument, but you<br>
will not fish unless there is a river, an<br>
ocean, a lake or a water pond in which<br>
fish are found.<br>
<b>Means of production</b><br>
<b>Means of production refer to the</b><br>
combination of means of labour and<br>
objects of labour. They are necessary in<br>
the production of goods and services.<br>
<b>They are capital and labour. When left on</b><br>
their own, the means of labour have no<br>
value in life. Therefore, human labour<br>
must act on objects of labour using<br>
instruments of labour to make material<br>
production a reality. As they interact<br>
in the process of material production,<br>
people use their knowledge, skills as<br>
well as experience to produce things to<br>
meet their basic needs. In the process,<br>
they develop technology, which is a<br>
significant factor of production.<br>

<b>Student's Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Advanced History for Secondary Schools</b><br>
3<br>
<b>Productive forces</b><br>
<b>Productive forces result from the</b><br>
interaction between human labour and<br>
the means of labour. The principal<br>
elements of productive forces in any<br>
society are people, intelligence and<br>
experiences. Productive forces are<br>
dynamic. They grow, multiply and<br>
change with advancements in human<br>
skills and production experiences.<br>
<b>Therefore, the level of the development</b><br>
of productive forces is an indicator of<br>
humans’ mastery of nature.<br>
<b>Relations of production</b><br>
<b>Relations of production are those into</b><br>
which human beings enter during a<br>
production process. It is the relations of<br>
production that show whether a relation<br>
is exploitative or non-exploitative. For<br>
example, capitalist social formation,<br>
relations of production refer to a<br>
relationship<br>
between<br>
those<br>
who<br>
own the means of production (the<br>
bourgeoisie) and those who do not (the<br>
proletariat). Thus, under this system,<br>
the relations of production are always<br>
exploitative. According to Karl Marx,<br>
the only way the bourgeoisie can make<br>
a profit is by exploiting the proletariat<br>
by giving them low wages and making<br>
them work for a long time.<br>
<b>Modes of production</b><br>
<b>A mode of production consists of</b><br>
productive forces and relations of<br>
production. The mode of production<br>
of a particular society should meet<br>
people’s needs. Each society has an<br>
organisation called the superstructure.<br>
<b>The superstructure consists of non-</b><br>
productive institutions such as the<br>
judiciary, religious beliefs, customs<br>
and traditions. A society’s mode of<br>
production and the superstructure<br>
constitute a social formation. In<br>
categorising a mode of production,<br>
one has to ask oneself questions about<br>
who owns the means of production,<br>
how production is organised and how<br>
the fruits of labour are distributed. For<br>
example, under a communal mode<br>
of production, the major means of<br>
production are collectively owned by<br>
the community and the fruits of labour<br>
are distributed more or less equally<br>
within the community.<br>

<b>Modes of production in Africa</b><br>
<b>By the beginning of the 19th century,</b><br>
many African societies were still under<br>
the communal mode of production.<br>
<b>A few societies were under the feudal</b><br>
mode of production and some were in<br>
transition from the communal mode<br>
of production to the feudal mode<br>
of production. The transition from<br>
one mode of production to another<br>
was largely influenced by the local<br>
environment and the development of<br>
productive forces.<br>
<b>Primitive communal mode of</b><br>
production<br>
<b>According to Karl Marx, this was the</b><br>
first mode of production all human<br>
societies<br>
experienced.<br>
<b>Primitive</b><br>
communalism was the earliest system<br>
of social relations that preceded<br>
all the modes of production. It was<br>
the first, non-antagonistic mode of<br>
production of the stone, bronze and<br>
iron ages. Relations under this mode of<br>
production were egalitarian.<br>
<b>In Africa, primitive communalism</b><br>
existed for a much longer time than any<br>
other mode of production. It emerged<br>
more than one million years ago. In<br>
some societies, especially in hunting<br>
and gathering societies, the primitive<br>
mode of production is still practised.<br>
<b>At the beginning of colonialism,</b><br>
the communal mode of production<br>
was found in some parts of Africa,<br>
especially in the dense equatorial<br>
forests as well as in the arid and semi-<br>
arid regions. These were regions which<br>
could hardly support livestock keeping<br>
or<br>
crop<br>
cultivation.<br>
<b>Agricultural</b><br>
communities which practised shifting<br>
cultivation also fell into this mode<br>
of production. Examples of African<br>
societies that led a communal life<br>
include hunter-gatherers such as the<br>
<b>Mbuti of the Ituri Forest of eastern</b><br>
<b>Congo, the San of the Kalahari Desert,</b><br>
the Sandawe and Hadzabe of north-<br>
central Tanzania and the Khoikhoi of<br>
<b>South Africa. Other examples include</b><br>
pastoralist communities like the Fulani<br>
of northern Nigeria in West Africa,<br>
the Galla and Somali of north-eastern<br>
<b>Africa, the Teso of Uganda and Kenya,</b><br>
the Karamojong of Uganda, and the<br>
Ndorobo of Tanzania and Kenya.<br>
<b>Characteristics of primitive</b><br>
communalism<br>
<b>Primitive communalism had several</b><br>
defining features. One of the most<br>
common features was a low level of<br>
science and technology. At first, most of<br>
the tools available were made of stones.<br>
<b>There were three phases of the Stone</b><br>
<b>Age, depending on the type of stone</b><br>
tools used. The first phase was the Old<br>
or Early Stone Age. The second phase<br>
was the Middle Stone Age and the third<br>
phase was the New or Late Stone Age.<br>
<b>In the first two phases, humans lived by</b><br>
hunting and gathering, while in the last<br>
phase humans started to domesticate<br>
plants and animals. Given the low<br>

5<br>
level of science and technology, the<br>
communal economy could not support<br>
a large population.<br>
<b>Moreover, primitive communalism</b><br>
was characterised by a simple division<br>
of labour, which was mainly based<br>
on age and sex. For example, it was<br>
common for men to participate in<br>
hunting and fishing activities, while<br>
women participated in gathering roots<br>
and fruits and took care of children.<br>
<b>Under</b><br>
the<br>
communal<br>
mode<br>
of<br>
production, there was no private<br>
ownership of the means of production.<br>
<b>All such means were collectively</b><br>
owned by the community. That is<br>
to say, the objects of labour such as<br>
land, rivers and forests were shared<br>
by all community members. Likewise,<br>
everyone was free to use the means of<br>
labour but with specific community<br>
rules.<br>
<b>Furthermore, under this mode of</b><br>
production, there was no exploitation of<br>
man by man. There was no suppression<br>
of individuals or families. Whatever<br>
was produced was equally shared<br>
among community members. This<br>
tendency discouraged exploitation,<br>
since there was no class stratification.<br>
<b>All society members were relatively</b><br>
equal.<br>
<b>Additionally, people lived in bands</b><br>
or families and small kinship groups.<br>
<b>This form of social formation was</b><br>
based on blood relationships, in that<br>
community members traced their<br>
origin to the same ancestor. Each group<br>
was separated from another by large<br>
areas of land, and each group had its<br>
own territory. Members of the same<br>
community shared beliefs and spoke<br>
the same dialect. However, in their<br>
continuous search for food, a member<br>
of one group could enter the territory<br>
of another group. Thus, wars broke out.<br>
<b>In addition, wars could break out as a</b><br>
result of the need for revenge. There<br>
were no standing armies. Instead,<br>
during a war, a whole group would be<br>
involved in it and when the war was<br>
over, they would resume production.<br>
<b>There was also a certain level of</b><br>
democracy<br>
under<br>
this<br>
mode<br>
of<br>
production. This means that all<br>
decisions would be made by all adult<br>
members of the group, regardless of<br>
their sex. Furthermore, elders governed<br>
by consensus. They had no coercive<br>
power and exercised their power purely<br>
through respect and need. Under this<br>
mode of production, there was hardly<br>
any powerful ruler or chief to exercise<br>
absolute power.<br>
<b>Under</b><br>
the<br>
communal<br>
system,<br>
production was only for subsistence<br>
purposes. There was no surplus<br>
production in communal societies<br>
because of the low development of<br>
the productive forces. Whatever was<br>
produced was for consumption.<br>
<b>Because of the low level of science and</b><br>
technology, the tools used were very<br>

<b>The domestication of animals and plants</b><br>

<b>Revolution in the latter part of the</b><br>
<b>Stone Age. The Neolithic Revolution,</b><br>
which is also called the Agricultural<br>
<b>Revolution, was a great shift from</b><br>
depending on hunting and gathering<br>
to depending on agriculture and animal<br>
domestication. This shift marked a<br>
significant step in human history. Ways<br>
of living were greatly transformed as<br>
some people became pastoralists and<br>
others crop cultivators. The Neolithic<br>
<b>Revolution led to radical changes in</b><br>
human life because it transformed<br>
scattered dwellings of hunter-gatherers<br>
into<br>
permanent<br>
farming<br>
villages.<br>
<b>This transformation had far-reaching</b><br>
consequences for the social, political<br>
and economic evolution.<br>

<b>Slave mode of production</b><br>
<b>The slave mode of production was the</b><br>
earliest exploitative mode of production<br>
in human history. It was a socio-<br>
economic system based on private<br>
ownership of the means of production,<br>
including slave labour. According to<br>
<b>Karl Marx, slavery was the crudest</b><br>
form of exploitation in human history.<br>
<b>This mode of production pre-dominated</b><br>
in Mesopotamia, Ancient Egypt, India<br>
and China in the 4th millennium BCE.<br>
<b>In Greece, the slave mode of production</b><br>
reached its peak between the 5th and<br>
4th centuries BCE, while in Rome it<br>
reached a climax from the 2nd century<br>
<b>BCE to 2nd century CE. In addition</b><br>
to Ancient Egypt, other societies in<br>
<b>Africa such as the Kongo Kingdom</b><br>
experienced domestic slavery.<br>
<b>The development of the slave mode</b><br>
of<br>
production<br>
involved<br>
complex<br>
processes, including improvement of<br>
productive forces, the rise of private<br>
ownership of property such as land<br>
and the production of surplus wealth<br>
by those who owned the means of<br>
production.<br>
<b>Under this mode of production, a slave</b><br>
was an essential means of production as<br>
the slave master perpetually exploited<br>
and appropriated his labour. The slave<br>
master owned the means of production<br>
and the slave himself/herself.<br>

<b>The origin of slavery in pre-colonial</b><br>
<b>African societies</b><br>
<b>Slavery was a mode of production</b><br>
in different parts of the world which<br>
started after the stone-age period.<br>
<b>There has been a debate on whether</b><br>
slavery as a mode of production<br>
existed in Africa or not. In fact, there<br>
were a few societies that developed a<br>
slave mode of production, while the<br>
majority of African societies did not.<br>
<b>However, the slave mode of production</b><br>
in Africa did not emerge as a distinct<br>
economic system, since it co-existed<br>
with some elements of communalism<br>
or feudalism.<br>
<b>The major factors for the emergence of</b><br>
slavery in pre-colonial African societies<br>
were technological development and<br>
social division of labour. For example,<br>
during slavery, societies learnt to<br>
use iron tools in farming and other<br>
economic<br>
activities.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
pre-colonial African societies were<br>
not homogeneous. They varied from<br>
one place to another in terms of<br>
development. Some ethnic groups in<br>
slave-owning<br>
societies<br>
specialised<br>
in crafts and farming, while others<br>
specialised in trade. This division of<br>
labour caused inequality, hence the rise<br>
of social stratification in societies.<br>
<b>The earliest people to develop slavery</b><br>
in Africa were Ancient Egyptians, who<br>
did so in about 3500 BCE. The reason the<br>
<b>Ancient Egyptians developed slavery</b><br>
was the increase in surplus production.<br>
<b>The desire to reduce surplus wealth</b><br>
necessitated having slaves who could<br>
help families to cope with the increased<br>
amount of work. Those who controlled<br>
surplus wealth became slave masters<br>
because such wealth gave them power<br>
over those without. War became the<br>
most reliable means of obtaining slave<br>
labour. The war captives would be used<br>
as slaves, instead of being killed. With<br>
time, this tendency became common<br>
and prompted the development of the<br>
slave mode of production.<br>
<b>Slaves were used to build cities and</b><br>
large structures such as pyramids,<br>
dams and irrigation systems in Ancient<br>
<b>Egypt. However, it should be noted</b><br>
that slavery co-existed with feudalism<br>
in Ancient Egypt. Therefore, in Africa<br>
slavery was only partially practised as<br>
a mode of production, except in places<br>
like Ancient Egypt, West Africa and<br>
along the coast of East Africa.<br>
<b>Characteristics of the slave mode of</b><br>
production<br>
<b>As a mode of production, slavery was</b><br>
characterised by the following features:<br>
<b>Under the slave mode of production,</b><br>
population was divided into freemen<br>
and slaves. Freemen had civil, property<br>
and political rights, while slaves were<br>
deprived of all the rights and could not<br>
join the ranks of freemen. Freemen<br>
included large landowners, who were<br>
also slave owners.<br>

<b>The slave mode of production was</b><br>
characterised by the class contradiction<br>
between slaves and slave owners. With<br>
the development of the slave mode of<br>
production, slave labour became the<br>
main means of production and the<br>
contradiction between slaves and slave<br>
masters became the basic contradiction<br>
in society. Slave masters’ exploitation<br>
of slaves was the main cause of this<br>
contradiction.<br>
<b>This mode of production was also</b><br>
characterised by the rise of the state. The<br>
rise of class stratification in society led<br>
to the rise of the state. With the growth<br>
of the social division of labour and the<br>
development of exchange, separate<br>
clans and ethnic groups united. The<br>
character of clan institutions changed.<br>
<b>The institutions were converted into</b><br>
organs of dominance and oppression.<br>
<b>The elders and military clan leaders</b><br>
became kings and princes, while other<br>
people, especially the poor, were<br>
subjected to slavery.<br>
<b>Moreover, the slave mode of production</b><br>
was characterised by improvement of<br>
productive forces. The improvement<br>
of productive forces initiated the<br>
production of surplus and the division<br>
of labour. In Africa, the state emerged<br>
when societies could produce surplus.<br>
<b>At this stage, there emerged people</b><br>
who were detached from the process of<br>
production and who naturally became<br>
rulers. The following are examples of<br>
the pre-colonial African societies that<br>
practised slavery.<br>
<b>Ancient Egypt</b><br>
<b>Archaeological</b><br>
evidence<br>
reveals<br>
that the transition from hunting and<br>
gathering to the cultivation of crops<br>
and the domestication of animals<br>
happened in Ancient Egypt in 5000<br>
and 4000 BCE. The development of<br>
food production led to an increase in<br>
population in Ancient Egypt owing<br>
to the abundance of food. Population<br>
growth called for further development<br>
of agriculture. To develop agriculture,<br>
the Egyptians harnessed the annual<br>
flood of the Nile waters and distributed<br>
the waters over large tracts of land to<br>
enhance crop cultivation and animal<br>
husbandry. The need to control the<br>
<b>Nile</b><br>
waters<br>
compelled<br>
different<br>
kinship groups to come together to<br>
construct dams and irrigation canals.<br>
<b>The aforesaid activities made some</b><br>
people specialise in managing the<br>
public, while others did manual work.<br>
<b>Such a division of labour resulted in</b><br>
the formation of a class of rulers on the<br>
one hand, and a class of labourers on<br>
the other.<br>
<b>The control of the Nile waters also</b><br>
required proper knowledge of floods.<br>
<b>Therefore, some people specialised in</b><br>
observing the movement of stars in<br>
relation to the occurrence of floods. This<br>
led to the discovery of the astronomical<br>
calendar and the emergence of people<br>
who were entirely involved in the<br>
production of scientific knowledge.<br>
<b>The need to keep records led to the</b><br>

<b>Egyptian society was divided into</b><br>
several classes. There was a ruling<br>
class which consisted of the nobility,<br>
religious leaders, military commanders<br>
and intellectuals on the one hand, and<br>
the labouring class, which consisted of<br>
peasants and slaves, on the other.<br>
<b>This</b><br>
division<br>
of<br>
society<br>
into<br>
antagonistic classes went hand in<br>
hand with the separation of towns<br>
and the countryside. The upper class<br>
concentrated in towns and cities,<br>
while the lower class was mainly in<br>
the countryside. The lower class in the<br>
countryside fed those who lived in the<br>
towns.<br>
<b>Sudanic zone</b><br>
<b>The Sudanic zone in West Africa was</b><br>
another part of Africa where slave-<br>
owning societies emerged. The zone<br>
stretches from the Atlantic coast<br>
eastwards to the area around Lake Chad.<br>
<b>Activities such as crop production,</b><br>
animal husbandry and iron-smelting<br>
had been well developed in this region<br>
by 3000 BCE.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
relationship<br>
between<br>
crop<br>
cultivation and animal husbandry was<br>
symbiotic. Animal provided manure<br>
and other products to crop cultivators.<br>
<b>In return, the cultivators produced crops</b><br>
like beans and cereals, which were<br>
exchanged for animal products. At the<br>
beginning of the first millennium CE,<br>
the people in the region had developed<br>
crafts and techniques of different<br>
kinds.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
techniques<br>
included<br>
cotton-weaving,<br>
iron-smelting,<br>
the<br>

bricks and the making of ornaments,<br>
including bronze, golden and silver<br>
articles.<br>
<b>The development of crafts led to</b><br>
the<br>
development<br>
of<br>
commodity<br>
production and exchange within and<br>
between communities. The expansion<br>
of these economic activities led to<br>
the development of the long distance<br>

large states emerged in the region,<br>
including<br>
<b>Ghana,</b><br>
<b>Mali,</b><br>
<b>Songhai,</b><br>
Kanem-Bornu and the Hausa states.<br>
<b>One of the reasons for the rise of states</b><br>
in this region was certain people’s<br>
desire to control trade routes and trade<br>
centres. Thus, there was a direct link<br>
between the development of the Trans-<br>
<b>Saharan Trade and the growth of the</b><br>
<b>Sudanic states. Other factors for the</b><br>
growth of the Sudanic states were the<br>
early development of agriculture and<br>
population growth. The development<br>
of productive forces also made the<br>
production of surplus in agriculture,<br>
mining and industry possible. Similarly,<br>
the introduction of Islam into North<br>
and West Africa intensified commercial<br>
activities in the countryside.<br>
<b>The socio-economic development in</b><br>
the Sudanic zone was accompanied by<br>
the emergence of three major social<br>
classes. First, there was the ruling<br>
class, which consisted of the military<br>
aristocracy and merchants. The military<br>
groups became dominant because of<br>
the fear that they might be invaded by<br>
their northern neighbours (Berbers)<br>
and of the need to acquire slaves from<br>
the neighbouring communities.<br>
<b>The second class consisted of cultivators</b><br>
and livestock keepers. It was made<br>
up of families which produced their<br>
means of existence individually, but<br>
which owned the means of production<br>
communally. The third class was that<br>
of slaves who had been captured either<br>
for sale or for domestic production.<br>
<b>It appears, therefore, that slavery</b><br>
was prevalent in many West African<br>
societies before and during the Trans-<br>
<b>Atlantic Slave Trade. As indicated</b><br>
earlier, when the West African states<br>
entered into conflicts for political<br>
or economic motives, the rulers and<br>
warlords enslaved the captives from<br>
other groups because they viewed them<br>
as outsiders.<br>
<b>Moreover, the rise of the plantation and</b><br>
mining economy in America between<br>
the 16th and 18th centuries remarkably<br>
expanded slaving activities in West and<br>
<b>Central Africa. Slaves were bought and</b><br>
sold in extremely large numbers and<br>
exported to the Americas to work on<br>
plantations and in mines.<br>

<b>East Africa</b><br>
<b>Slavery was practised along the coast</b><br>
of East Africa. For example, some<br>
<b>Arabs from Mrima and Omani as well</b><br>
as Swahili traders practised slavery.<br>
<b>Although the relations of production,</b><br>
which developed between 1000 CE<br>
and 1800 CE, had some elements of the<br>
slave and feudal modes of production,<br>
in East Africa slavery started around<br>
the 6th century. It was started by the<br>
<b>Arabs. In the urban areas around the</b><br>
coast, production was done using slave<br>
labour. The ruling class and merchants<br>
owned the land on which slaves and<br>
tenants worked.<br>
<b>In addition, the slaves that the Asians</b><br>
obtained from East Africa were taken<br>
to India, Lebanon and the Persian Gulf.<br>
<b>They went there to work as doorkeepers</b><br>
and domestic servants. However, the<br>
<b>Portuguese and the French used them</b><br>
on their sugar plantations in Brazil and<br>
in Réunion, as well as on the Mauritius<br>
<b>Islands. The French had opened sugar</b><br>
plantations in the areas. Nevertheless,<br>
the Arabs were the major actors in the<br>
slave trade done in East Africa.<br>
<b>The reasons many African societies</b><br>
did not develop a slave mode of<br>
production<br>
<b>Many pre-colonial African societies</b><br>
were communal. Only a few areas<br>
developed a slave mode of production,<br>
but that does not mean that Africa<br>
passed through slavery as a mode of<br>
production. The following made it<br>
difficult for African societies to develop<br>
the slave mode of production:<br>
(a)	 Africans had a strong belief in<br>
equality. This had its origins in<br>
the communal social system.<br>
<b>Elements of communalism were</b><br>
common<br>
almost<br>
everywhere<br>
in Africa. Collective labour in<br>
many parts of Africa encouraged<br>
communal ownership, of the<br>
means of production, hence the<br>
absence of social stratification,<br>
which could have subjected some<br>
society members to slavery. The<br>
communal system also provided<br>
equal access to land and equal<br>
distribution of social wealth,<br>
both<br>
of<br>
which<br>
discouraged<br>
the emergence of slave social<br>
relations. Africans’ tendency to<br>
live together became an integral<br>
part of the African way of life.<br>
<b>People regarded themselves as</b><br>
relatives; hence, no captives of<br>
war were sold as slaves;<br>
(b)	 There was a high rate of slave<br>
absorption into kin groups. For<br>
example, female slaves gradually<br>
became wives of their masters.<br>
<b>Similarly, it was difficult for</b><br>
some African societies to develop<br>
slavery because slaves had a<br>
special role to play in the host<br>
societies. For example, slaves<br>
were used as soldiers, blacksmiths<br>
or boat paddlers. Thus, slavery<br>

<b>The feudal mode of production</b><br>
<b>Feudalism was the second exploitative</b><br>
mode of production in which land was<br>
the major means of production. Under<br>
this mode of production, there were two<br>
classes of people. The first class consisted<br>
of the feudal lords who owned the major<br>
means of production and appropriated the<br>
surplus. The other class comprised the<br>
serfs and tenants who were given pieces<br>
of land to cultivate. In return, they paid<br>
rent to the feudal lords. In many parts<br>
of Africa, feudalism was associated<br>
with certain elements of slavery.<br>
<b>In East Africa, the emergence of</b><br>
feudalism began in the 15th century.<br>
<b>The system existed until the second</b><br>
half of the 19th century. In this region,<br>
feudal communities were common in<br>
the western part of the interlacustrine<br>
region, namely Bunyoro, Nyankore,<br>
<b>Karagwe, Burundi and Rwanda. The</b><br>
economy of this sub-region combined<br>
crop cultivation and animal husbandry.<br>
<b>There was also the eastern part of the</b><br>
interlacustrine region which consisted<br>
of the kingdoms of Buganda, Busoga<br>
and Buhaya. The economy of this<br>
sub-region was predominantly based<br>
on<br>
agriculture,<br>
especially<br>
banana<br>
cultivation. Banana became their main<br>
food crop.<br>
<b>The banana economy encouraged</b><br>
permanent settlements because banana<br>
is a perennial crop. The economy also<br>
led to the development of exploitative<br>
relations of production in which some<br>
clans took control of productive land<br>
and became politically dominant.<br>
<b>For instance, among the Haya, the</b><br>
ruling clan (Abakama) seized control<br>
of the banana-producing land and<br>
divided it into large estates known as<br>
nyarubanja. The nyarubanja estates<br>
were given by the bakama as a reward<br>
for service and loyalty to the people<br>
whom they had appointed as bakungu.<br>
<b>The rulers also gave nyarubanja estates</b><br>
to relatives and friends. The landlords<br>
of the nyarubanja estates became<br>
known as the batwazi and the tenants<br>
the batwara. The former owners from<br>
whom land was seized by the bakama<br>
became tenants and clients of the new<br>
owners, the batwazi.<br>
<b>The tenants and clients living in</b><br>
the nyarubanja estates did not pay<br>
tribute directly to the bakama. They<br>
provided goods and services to their<br>
landlords, the batwazi. In that way,<br>
the various officers of each mukama<br>
were maintained. Besides, the bakama<br>
continued to receive tribute directly<br>
from peasants who were still living in<br>
clan owned-land.<br>
<b>In the western part of the interlacustrine</b><br>
region, there was an economy of<br>
mixed farming and pastoralism. Each<br>
of these sectors of the economy was<br>
the monopoly of one social group. The<br>
pastoralists were known as the bahima<br>
in Bunyoro, Nyankore, Mpororo and<br>
the buhweju states, and as the batusi<br>
in Rwanda, Burundi and Buha. The<br>

<b>Characteristics of feudalism</b><br>
<b>As a mode of production, feudalism</b><br>
had the following features:<br>
(a)	 The feudal mode of production<br>
permitted private ownership of<br>
the major means of production,<br>
especially land. Therefore, land<br>
was privately owned and controlled<br>
by feudal lords. Those who owned<br>
land<br>
were<br>
economically<br>
and<br>

do anything to get their interests<br>

societies concerned;<br>
(b)	 There were exploitative relations<br>
of production through which the<br>
feudal lords exploited the serfs and<br>
peasants who paid various kinds<br>
of rents such as labour rent and<br>
rent in kind. With respect to labour<br>
rent, for example, peasants and<br>
serfs were given plots of land by<br>
the landlords and were supposed<br>
to work on their plots and on those<br>
of their landlords using their own<br>
implements. Rent in kind was also<br>
another form of payment; serfs and<br>
peasants were supposed to give<br>
part of their harvests to the land<br>
owners. The last type of rent was<br>
money rent; serfs were obliged to<br>
sell their harvests and give some<br>
amount of money to the landlords;<br>
(c)	 The productive forces in the<br>
feudal mode of production were<br>
more advanced than those in the<br>
preceding modes of production.<br>
<b>In the feudal mode of production,</b><br>
iron tools were widely used.<br>
<b>That is why there was relatively</b><br>
high<br>
production.<br>
<b>Therefore,</b><br>
advancements in productive forces<br>
created surplus which supported<br>
other economic sectors such as<br>
trading, mining, agriculture and<br>
handcraft industries;<br>
(d)	 The feudal system developed<br>
centralised political organisations<br>
in which power was vested<br>
in absolute kings. Therefore,<br>
the political organisation was<br>
controlled by the landlords to<br>
protect their interests. Examples<br>
of the feudal states which emerged<br>
in Africa included Ethiopia and<br>
the interlacustrine states like the<br>
<b>Buganda Kingdom;</b><br>
(e)	 Agriculture was a predominant<br>
activity during feudalism, although<br>
other economic activities were also<br>
carried out. For example, in the<br>
western Sudanic states yams and<br>

<b>Feudalism existed for many centuries</b><br>

<b>Nyarubanja</b><br>

<b>Kabaka, Bakungu, Balangira (princes),</b><br>
<b>Batongole (loyal supporters), Bakopi</b><br>

was the political title given to the ruler<br>
of the Buganda Kingdom.<br>

herd to take care of or to rent. The Hutu<br>

<b>This system of feudalism developed</b><br>

<b>African</b><br>

mwinyi mkuu’s workers who collected<br>

<b>Ntemiship</b><br>
<b>Ntemiship was a form of semi-feudal</b><br>
relations that developed in the central<br>
part of Tanzania. It was practised by<br>
the Nyamwezi, Sukuma, Kimbu and<br>
<b>Gogo. The leader’s power was based on</b><br>
his control of the producers. The ruling<br>
class under ntemiship monopolised all<br>
political power and collected tribute<br>
from the subject peasantry. Mtemi was<br>
the political title given to the ruler. The<br>
Mtemi controlled and mobilsed labour.<br>
<b>This system experienced a mild form</b><br>
of feudal relations compared to other<br>
parts of East Africa, partly because<br>
of poor soil and marginal rains that<br>
inhibited surplus production. Thus, the<br>
ntemiship system had mixed features<br>
of communalism and feudalism.<br>

<b>The African feudal relations which</b><br>
other scholars call a tributary mode of<br>
production were sanctioned by customs<br>
and traditions. In many instances,<br>
people willingly supported this system<br>
as it was part of their culture. Thus,<br>

<b>Exercise 1.3</b><br>
<b>If you were to choose a kind of</b><br>
feudalism to live in, which one<br>

<b>Reasons for the differences in feudal</b><br>
practices in pre-colonial African<br>
societies<br>
<b>While feudalism emerged in some</b><br>
parts of pre-colonial Africa, it did not<br>
develop in other places. Historians<br>
have proposed several reasons for this<br>
situation.<br>
<b>Climate and soil influenced the</b><br>
emergence of feudalism in some<br>
parts of pre-colonial Africa. Many<br>
people migrated to areas where<br>
the<br>
environment<br>
would<br>
support<br>
such economic activities as fishing,<br>
farming, animal keeping and hunting.<br>
<b>As people mastered their environment,</b><br>
production became more advanced than<br>
in societies with a hostile environment.<br>
<b>Favourable environments included the</b><br>
areas occupied by the Zulu of South<br>
<b>Africa; the Tutsi, Hutu, Baganda</b><br>
and Bunyoro of East Africa; and the<br>
<b>Mandinka and Yoruba of the Savannah</b><br>
and the forest region in West Africa.<br>
<b>The fertility of the soil made people</b><br>
engage in agriculture, which led to<br>
the development of production and<br>
exchange in societies. Thus, increased<br>
production led to social stratification in<br>
some societies. As a result, feudalism<br>
emerged in such societies, while the<br>
areas with a hostile environment did<br>
not develop feudalism.<br>
<b>Another reason was the improvement</b><br>
in<br>
productive<br>
forces<br>
during<br>
the<br>
transition from primitive communalism<br>
to the feudal mode of production.<br>
<b>The transformation did not happen in</b><br>
societies where productive forces were<br>
low, particularly in hunter-gatherer<br>
and pastoral societies. Conversely, the<br>
transition from primitive communalism<br>
to feudalism happened in societies<br>
that managed to improve upon their<br>
productive forces.<br>
<b>Population growth was also another</b><br>
reason for the difference. The increase<br>
in population led to a shortage of<br>
land, which further resulted in the class<br>
struggle between the landless and those<br>
who owned land. Eventually, a few<br>
people owned land privately and the<br>
rest became serfs. On the other hand,<br>
the increase in population within a<br>

18<br>
given society ensured the availability<br>
of labour for doing different economic<br>
activities.<br>
<b>This</b><br>
transformation,<br>
however, did not happen among the<br>
hunter-gatherer and pastoral societies.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
emergence<br>
of<br>
specialisation<br>
was also the reason for the disparity.<br>
<b>Specialisation  influenced some African</b><br>
societies to evolve into a feudal mode<br>
of production. This forced people into<br>
different professions such as farming,<br>
rain-making, iron-smelting, hunting<br>
and fishing. The system intensified<br>
production and differentiation; thus,<br>
exchange became inevitable.<br>
<b>Another reason was calamities such as</b><br>
floods and drought. Calamities such<br>
as floods and drought necessitated the<br>
existence of personnel who could deal<br>
with such challenges. Such personnel<br>
later became specialists and leaders<br>
of the society, hence the formation<br>
of a state in a particular area. A good<br>
example of this comes from Ancient<br>
<b>Egypt. The areas in which there</b><br>
were no such specialised individuals<br>
remained under a communal mode of<br>
production.<br>
<b>Moreover, the transition from a</b><br>
communal mode of production to<br>
feudalism was also the result of the<br>
abundance of food. The availability<br>
of surplus food, in turn, promoted<br>
trade, which added value to land and<br>
encouraged people to settle permanently<br>
in certain areas, hence the need to form<br>
states. In areas where trading activities<br>
did not take place, societies depended<br>
on what nature offered them and this<br>
made centralisation very difficult.<br>
<b>The areas in which trade had developed</b><br>
enabled communities to acquire various<br>
items, which enabled them to develop<br>
feudalism. For example, the empires of<br>
<b>Buganda, Nyamwezi, Ghana and Mali</b><br>
developed as a result of the acquisition<br>
of various items from trade. Generally,<br>
pre-colonial Africa developed different<br>
modes of production in relation to<br>
different<br>
economic,<br>
political<br>
and<br>
environmental situations.  Figure1.1<br>
shows the location of some of the areas<br>
in pre-colonial Africa where there were<br>
different modes of production.<br>

<b>Political organisations in</b><br>
pre-colonial Africa<br>
<b>In the Afro-centric view, pre-colonial</b><br>
<b>African societies were not static. Before</b><br>
the advent of European colonialism,<br>
<b>African societies had reached different</b><br>
levels of political development. Thus,<br>
<b>Africans knew how to rule themselves,</b><br>
how to settle their disputes and how<br>
to choose their leaders. By 1500 CE,<br>
<b>African societies had built four major</b><br>
types of social and political systems.<br>
<b>The systems were clan (kinship)</b><br>
organisation,<br>
age-set<br>
organisation,<br>
decentralised<br>
state<br>
organisation<br>
(ntemiship)<br>
and<br>
centralised<br>
state<br>
organisation.<br>
<b>Clan (kinship) organisation</b><br>
<b>A clan is a unit of social and political</b><br>
organisations<br>
comprising<br>
several<br>
related families sharing a particular<br>
ideology. In many cases, clan members<br>
descend from the same ancestors,<br>
but in other cases they do not. While<br>
clans were organised under clan heads,<br>
the major means of production were<br>
owned communally. The functions of<br>
the clan leader were to settle disputes,<br>
distribute land, counsel the youth,<br>
protect and preserve customs and<br>
traditions, preside over religious and<br>
cultural ceremonies, besides choosing<br>
spouses for those intending to get<br>
married. The clan head also controlled<br>
the surplus produced and determined<br>
its redistribution among clan members.<br>
<b>The societies which practised clan</b><br>
organisation involved themselves in<br>
mixed farming as their main economic<br>
activity.<br>
<b>Many clan-based communities were</b><br>
found in savannah and woodland<br>
environments. Rainfall was usually<br>
seasonal but sufficient for growing<br>
seasonal crops like finger millet,<br>
sorghum, beans and maize. The soil was<br>
not very fertile and would get exhausted<br>
after only a few years of cultivation.<br>
<b>Following this exhaustion, shifting</b><br>
cultivation became inevitable for such<br>
societies. Examples of societies that<br>
practised clan organisation were the<br>
<b>Gogo, Fipa, Nyamwezi, Yao, Makonde,</b><br>
<b>Matumbi, Mwera, Ngindo, Ndonde</b><br>
and Pogoro of Tanzania. Many clan-<br>
based communities lived a communal<br>
life by sharing the means of production<br>
and distributing the produce to their<br>
members.<br>
<b>Two main types of clan organisation</b><br>
were practised in Africa, namely<br>
patrilineal<br>
and<br>
matrilineal<br>
clan<br>
organisations.<br>
<b>Patrilineal clan organisation</b><br>
<b>This was a system of social organisation</b><br>
in which clan heritage was based on<br>
the father’s clan. The children bore the<br>
name of the father’s clan. The husband<br>
paid the bride price to get a wife. Some<br>
of the societies which adopted the<br>
patrilineal mode of life in East Africa<br>
included the Nyambo, Haya, Hehe,<br>
Chagga, Matengo, Sukuma and Kurya.<br>

<b>Matrilineal clan organisation</b><br>
<b>This was a system of social organisation</b><br>
in which clan heritage was based on the<br>
mother’s clan. The uncle had to make<br>
all important decisions concerning the<br>
children and nephews of their sisters.<br>
<b>The societies which adopted the</b><br>
matrilineal clan organisation in East<br>
<b>Africa included the Makonde, Mwera,</b><br>
Makua and Yao of Tanzania.<br>
<b>Activity 1.3</b><br>
<b>Read various sources on the</b><br>
practice<br>
of<br>
both<br>
matrilineal<br>
and<br>
patrilineal<br>
organisations<br>
and then write an essay on the<br>
socio-economic advantages and<br>
disadvantages of each system in<br>
modern African societies. Use<br>
examples from Tanzania.<br>
<b>Age-set organisation</b><br>
<b>This is a mode of social and political</b><br>
organisation characterised by the<br>
distribution of work based on age<br>
grades. The age-set or grade was first<br>
determined by initiation ceremonies,<br>
but people transformed into another<br>
age-set or grade through the perfomance<br>
of rituals. This system of socio-political<br>
organisation was common among the<br>
pastoral and agro-pastoral societies<br>
like the Maasai of Kenya and Tanzania,<br>
the Karamojong and Teso of Uganda,<br>
as well as the Fulani of West Africa.<br>
<b>The local environment in arid and</b><br>
semi-arid areas favoured pastoralism<br>
as the main economic activity. In<br>
<b>Africa, pastoralist societies were of two</b><br>
types. The first group comprised the<br>
nomadic pastoralists who moved from<br>
one place to another in search of water<br>
and pasture. Their main foods were<br>
milk, meat and blood. They also ate<br>
vegetables and grain, obtained through<br>
exchange with their neighbours who<br>
grew crops. Examples of nomadic<br>
pastoralists were the Berbers and<br>
<b>Tuaregs of North Africa, the Fulani and</b><br>
<b>Hausa of West Africa, the Somali of</b><br>
<b>North-Eastern Africa, the Karamojong</b><br>
of Uganda, the Maasai of Tanzania and<br>
<b>Kenya, and the Khoikhoi of Southern</b><br>
Africa.<br>
<b>The second group of pastoralists was</b><br>
that of the sedentary pastoralists. The<br>
great Maasai expansion southwards<br>
along the Rift Valley led to the<br>
emergence of two distinct groups,<br>
namely the Iloikop or Wakwavi,<br>
who were both agriculturalists and<br>
pastoralists, and the Maasai who were<br>
pastoralists per se. The Wakwavi did<br>
not move from one place to another.<br>
<b>They settled in one area and undertook</b><br>
their economic activities in that specific<br>
area. They also kept fewer animals. The<br>
<b>Rendille of Kenya were also sedentary</b><br>
pastoralists.<br>
<b>The division of labour and social</b><br>
responsibilities under this political<br>
organisation was based on age and sex.<br>

<b>Society was divided into different age</b><br>
groups. The first group consisted of<br>
young children aged 1-8. These were<br>
regarded as non-producers. They stayed<br>
at home with their mothers. The second<br>
group comprised the youth aged 8-18.<br>
<b>These were responsible for attending to</b><br>
and grazing calves, and assisted women<br>
in milking cows. The third group<br>
consisted of the Moran, a group which<br>
comprised adult males aged 18-35.<br>
<b>These were the warriors and protectors</b><br>
of the society. Morans had many other<br>
responsibilities such as looking for<br>
new grazing areas and sources of water<br>
for their livestock. They also increased<br>
cattle herds by raiding cattle belonging<br>
to neighbouring societies. The last<br>
group consisted of elders aged 40 and<br>
above. The most senior elder was called<br>
the Laibon and he was responsible for<br>
presiding over the religious and ritual<br>
activities of a specific group.<br>
<b>However, it should be noted that</b><br>
the functions of various age groups<br>
overlapped<br>
sometimes<br>
and<br>
the<br>
transition<br>
from<br>
moran-hood<br>
into<br>
“elderhood” and senior “elderhood”<br>
was possible after performing certain<br>
rituals. For example, in times of<br>
prolonged drought and of lack of<br>
pastures, morans and some junior<br>
elders participated in grazing cattle in<br>
distant areas. In the pre-colonial period,<br>
although cattle raiding was carried out<br>
by morans, it was sanctioned by elders<br>
and sometimes blessed by the mothers<br>
of the raiders. Women were sometimes<br>
involved in conflict resolution and<br>
peace-building.<br>
<b>Decentralised</b><br>
state<br>
organisation<br>
(ntemiship) in Tanzania<br>
<b>A decentralised state is one in</b><br>
which political power is centred in<br>
various units of government. Under<br>
decentralised states, power was not<br>
at the centre. In addition, leadership<br>
was not hereditary, but rather it was<br>
determined by a council of elders,<br>
as illustrated by the Nyamwezi of<br>
<b>Tanzania and the Igbo of south-eastern</b><br>
Nigeria.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
ntemiship<br>
system<br>
was<br>
a<br>
decentralised form of state organisation<br>
that emerged among the Bantu farming<br>
communities in western and central<br>
Tanzania between 1000 and 1800 CE.<br>
<b>Before the formation of this system,</b><br>
the region was inhabited by Bantu-<br>
speaking people who lived in small<br>
independent communities. Some of<br>
the areas that practised the ntemiship<br>
system were Unyamwezi, Ukimbu,<br>
<b>Usukuma and Ugogo. Under this</b><br>
system, many people lived in villages<br>
and a village was viewed as a self-<br>
sufficient unit capable of providing<br>
for itself. In some societies, the ntemi<br>
system emerged during difficult times,<br>
especially when people faced security<br>
threats. Therefore, villages would unite<br>
to form larger and stronger groups.<br>
<b>The leaders of these groups came to be</b><br>

known as the batemi, a word derived<br>
from the word kutema, meaning<br>
cutting down trees or clearing the bush<br>
in a given area. They were given the<br>
name batemi because they directed<br>
bush-clearing operations in their areas.<br>
<b>By the 18th century, the system had</b><br>
spread to areas like Usangu, Ubena,<br>
<b>Ukimbu, Uchagga and Uhehe. Among</b><br>
the Sukuma and Nyamwezi of central<br>
Tanzania, the leader was called mtemi.<br>
<b>Among the Hehe, Sangu and Bena</b><br>
of Southern Highlands in Tanzania,<br>
the leader was called mtwa. In many<br>
societies, the watemi were highly<br>
respected and feared, since they were<br>
thought to be associated with the spirit<br>
of former chiefs and were believed<br>
to possess powerful rituals and war<br>
medicine.<br>
<b>Ntemiship was practised in woodland</b><br>
and grassland savannah areas, where<br>
mixed farming was carried out.<br>
<b>Because of the absence of permanent</b><br>
rivers and enough rainfall in such<br>
areas, very few people lived in the<br>
areas. Thus, people practised shifting<br>
cultivation. Under this system, rulers<br>
were chosen by a council of elders.<br>
<b>The mtemi was chosen based on his</b><br>
wisdom, experience and bravery. The<br>
council had political power and could<br>
remove the mtemi from his position.<br>
<b>The ntemi system involved many small</b><br>
communities, mainly of the same<br>
lineage or ancestor. For example, in<br>
<b>Usukuma, there were about 30 small</b><br>
ntemi states before the introduction<br>
of European colonial rule. There was<br>
about the same number in Unyamwezi<br>
and a slightly greater number in<br>
<b>Ugogo. There were numerous small</b><br>
states because different ruling clans<br>
in different parts of the region formed<br>
their chieftains. Moreover, because<br>
land was plenty, those societies could<br>
practise shifting cultivation. Finally,<br>
the limited surplus produced could not<br>
support larger ruling classes. These<br>
factors caused the whole region to be<br>
characterised by the formation of many<br>
small states with small ruling classes.<br>
<b>The mtemi’s judicial functions included</b><br>
settling disputes; hearing cases of<br>
murder,<br>
treason<br>
and<br>
witchcraft;<br>
and administering traditional and<br>
customary law. Spiritually, the mtemi<br>
acted as a bridge between people<br>
and their ancestors. Therefore, the<br>
responsibility for presiding over the<br>
community's rituals was vested in<br>
him. The mtemi also supervised the<br>
distribution of land and enforced its<br>
proper use. Furthermore, he collected<br>
tributes from his subjects in the form<br>
of grants, for example agricultural<br>
produce or livestock. The mtemi also<br>
preserved food that could be used to<br>
reward his followers and distribute to<br>
those affected by natural disasters like<br>
drought.<br>
<b>Societies that had the ntemi system</b><br>
cultivated millet, rice, sorghum and<br>
other kinds of grain. In this type of<br>

<b>Centralised state organisation</b><br>
<b>A centralised state refers to a political</b><br>
entity which has defined boundaries<br>
and centralised political power and<br>
which is headed by a king or ruler. It<br>
is an institution that has administrative<br>
machinery with judicial duties and the<br>
power to make decisions and collect<br>
tributes. States emerged in different<br>
areas of Africa at different times. In<br>
<b>East Africa, for example, states mostly</b><br>
started to emerge in the 15th century.<br>
<b>The favourable climatic condition</b><br>
played a vital role in the formation<br>
of states in Africa. For example, the<br>
equatorial region, which had fertile<br>
soil and which received heavy rainfall<br>
throughout the year, supported the<br>

<b>Advanced History for Secondary Schools</b><br>
25<br>
growth of crops such as yams, bananas<br>
and palms. This, in turn, led to an<br>
increase in population, which required<br>
a complex political organisation.<br>
<b>States emerged in different parts</b><br>
of Africa. They included the West<br>
<b>Sudanic states such as Ghana, Mali</b><br>
and Songhai; the Forest states of West<br>
<b>Africa such as Oyo, Benin, Asante</b><br>
and Dahomey; the Kongo-Zambezian<br>
states in Central Africa, namely<br>
<b>Mwenemutapa, Luba, Lunda, Congo</b><br>
and Maravi; the Zulu state in southern<br>
<b>Africa and the interlacustrine states</b><br>
such as Bunyoro and Buganda.<br>
<b>Characteristics of centralised states</b><br>
<b>Centralised states had various features,</b><br>
which<br>
distinguished<br>
them<br>
from<br>
decentralised ones. Centralised states<br>
were characterised by expansionism.<br>
<b>Leaders used to conquer neighbouring</b><br>
societies to expand their kingdoms to<br>
obtain wealth and labour. This was<br>
possible because of the existence of<br>
strong armies in such states. Leadership<br>
under centralised states was hereditary.<br>
<b>A successor had to come from the royal</b><br>
family, mostly the son of the chief or<br>
king, and could be named by a king<br>
or chief before his death. Centralised<br>
states also had well-defined boundaries<br>
that covered a large area with a large<br>
population. Most of the centralised<br>
states had a system similar to a<br>
parliamentary system. For example,<br>
kings made decisions following the<br>
advice of their assistants, who acted as<br>
parliament. For example, the Lukiiko<br>
of Buganda acted as a parliamentary<br>
organ under the Kabaka administration.<br>
<b>Under centralised states, power was in</b><br>
the hands of a single person. At the<br>
centre of administration was a king,<br>
who had absolute authority to make<br>
all major decisions regarding social,<br>
political and economic matters.<br>
<b>Factors for state formation in pre-</b><br>
colonial African societies<br>
<b>In pre-colonial Africa, state formation</b><br>
began around the 1st millennium CE.<br>
<b>For example, Ghana Empire existed</b><br>
between the 7th and 13th centuries CE,<br>
<b>Mali Empire existed between the 13th</b><br>
and 16th centuries CE, and Songhai<br>
<b>Empire existed between the 15th and 17th</b><br>
centuries CE, Mwenemutapa emerged<br>
between the 15th and 17th centuries<br>
<b>CE and the Buganda Kingdom rose</b><br>
between the 14th and 19th centuries CE.<br>
<b>The emergence of states in pre-colonial</b><br>
<b>Africa was due to the interplay of many</b><br>
factors.<br>
<b>One of the factors was the development</b><br>
of agriculture, which was associated<br>
with favourable climatic conditions<br>
and reliable rainfall. The availability<br>
of enough food and surplus led to an<br>
increase in population, which made<br>
some of the centralised organisations<br>
administer production and maintain<br>
security. For example, states like<br>
<b>Karagwe,</b><br>
<b>Buganda,</b><br>
<b>Rwanda</b><br>
and<br>
<b>Burundi emerged in the intelacustrine</b><br>
region.<br>

<b>The rise of Islam was another factor</b><br>
for state formation in pre-colonial<br>
<b>Africa as Islamic ideology acted as a</b><br>
unifying factor. For example, Islam<br>
was responsible for state information<br>
in West Africa, where Uthman dan<br>
<b>Fodio used Islamic ideology to conquer</b><br>
the surrounding smaller states. The<br>
states created under Jihad movements<br>
had a well-organised administrative<br>
structure. The best example of a state<br>
which rose because of Islam was the<br>
Sokoto Caliphate.<br>
<b>Moreover, some states in pre-colonial</b><br>
<b>Africa grew owing to the development</b><br>
of the long distance trade. Through<br>
the long distance trade, some societies<br>
obtained weapons and used those<br>
weapons to conquer weaker societies.<br>
<b>The rulers who controlled trade routes</b><br>
became very wealthy because they<br>
obtained tributes from traders. In<br>
<b>East Africa, for example, Mirambo</b><br>
controlled the trade route from Tabora<br>
to Ujiji. Arab traders were obliged<br>
to pay tribute when using that route.<br>
<b>Mirambo also obtained guns directly</b><br>
from the coast. In West Africa, the<br>
<b>Trans-Saharan Trade enabled the rulers</b><br>
of Ghana, Mali and Songhai to obtain<br>
swords, guns and horses from North<br>
Africa to strengthen their armies.<br>
<b>State formation in Africa was also</b><br>
facilitated by the presence of strong<br>
standing armies on the continent.<br>
<b>The use of armies enabled the rulers</b><br>
to conquer weaker societies and<br>
consolidate the security of their<br>
societies. In Tanzania, some rulers used<br>
armies to expand their domains and<br>
sometimes to establish new domains<br>
outside their localities. Mirambo of<br>
<b>Unyamwezi and Nyungu ya Mawe of</b><br>
<b>Ukimbu are some of the pre-colonial</b><br>
rulers who used military power to<br>
establish and control their states.<br>
<b>For instance, in the 1860s, Mirambo</b><br>
used the Nguni military techniques<br>
and ruga-ruga mercenaries to subdue<br>
several states such as Uyowa, Uvinza,<br>
Tongwe, Nyaturu, Iramba and Sukuma.<br>
<b>A geographical location was another</b><br>
factor for the emergence of states in<br>
pre-colonial Africa. The societies found<br>
in a favourable environment could<br>
form centralised states more easily<br>
than others. Generally, states tended<br>
to develop in areas of environmental<br>
diversity where there were important<br>
resources, but which were unevenly<br>
distributed. Therefore, states emerged<br>
in such places so that they could<br>
control the production and distribution<br>
of various goods and resources. A good<br>
example was the interlacustrine region<br>
in East Africa. That region allowed<br>
the conduct of diverse activities such<br>
as agriculture, livestock rearing, iron-<br>
smelting, bark-cloth making, fishing<br>
and trade.<br>
<b>The existence of various economic</b><br>
activities and the division of labour<br>
among community members intensified<br>
the process of class formation and<br>

a need to form an organised body to<br>
supervise various economic activities.<br>
<b>Bunyoro, Ankole, Toro, Karagwe and</b><br>
<b>Buganda are examples of the states</b><br>
formed in the interlacustrine region.<br>
<b>The discovery and use of iron</b><br>
technology contributed enormously<br>
to the emergence of states in pre-<br>
colonial Africa. This technology led<br>
to the improvement of agriculture and<br>
helped to ensure that food and surplus<br>
wealth were available in communities.<br>
<b>It also helped in making weapons</b><br>
such as arrows, spears and axes. Such<br>
weapons were used to conquer weaker<br>
societies and to provide defence against<br>
enemies. The states that developed as<br>
a result of iron technology included<br>
<b>Karagwe, Pare and Buganda in East</b><br>
<b>Africa; Mwenemutapa in Zimbabwe;</b><br>
<b>Manganja in Malawi; Nubia in Sudan;</b><br>
and Axum in Ethiopia.<br>
<b>The role of Islam in state formation</b><br>
during the 19th century<br>
<b>The formation of Islamic states in</b><br>
<b>West Africa was initiated by the</b><br>
<b>Fulani and Dyula traders between the</b><br>
18th and 19th centuries. These traders<br>
wanted to consolidate the Islamic faith<br>
which, according to them, was being<br>
contaminated by the pagans who did<br>
not abide by Islamic teachings or values.<br>
<b>The spread of Islam in West Africa in</b><br>
the 19th century was associated with<br>
religious movements known as Jihads.<br>
<b>The West African Jihad movements are</b><br>
interpreted by historians as religious,<br>
political and economic movements.<br>
<b>The leaders of the movements sought</b><br>
to revive the basic teachings and<br>
practices of Islam. The spread of Islam<br>
in West Africa before the 19th century<br>
was mainly due to commercial contacts<br>
between West Africa, North Africa<br>
and the Middle East. However, state<br>
formation in West Africa during the<br>
19th century was particularly the result<br>
of the Jihad movements.<br>
<b>Emergence of Jihad movements</b><br>
<b>The Jihad movements began in the</b><br>
18th century following the overthrow<br>
of indigenous rulers of Futa Jalon<br>
(Guinea) by Islamic activists who were<br>
under Alfa Ibrahim bin Nuhu. A similar<br>
movement occurred in Futa Toro, a<br>
state found west of Futa Jalon and on<br>
the southern bank of the River Senegal,<br>
when the activists, under Suleiman<br>
<b>Bal, declared a Jihad against the non-</b><br>
<b>Muslim rulers of the state in 1769. The</b><br>
non-Muslim rulers were overthrown and<br>
an Islamic state was established in 1776.<br>
<b>A Jihad was a holy war that Muslims</b><br>
started against non-believers to reform<br>
and restore the Islamic faith. The<br>
proponents of this war based their appeal<br>
on several Quranic verses, traditions of<br>
the Prophet (hadith) and the consensus<br>
of Islamic jurists (sharia). There were<br>
a number of Jihad movements in West<br>
<b>Africa. The most notable movements</b><br>

included those of Uthman dan Fodio of<br>

<b>Causes of the Jihad movements</b><br>
<b>The Jihad movements were primarily</b><br>
motivated by religious, political and<br>
economic reasons. Some of the causes<br>
included the following:<br>
(a)	 The<br>
socio-economic<br>
and<br>
political problems that were<br>
common in Hausaland before<br>
the Jihad movements caused<br>
the movements.  The Jihad<br>
revolts were waged against the<br>
heavy taxation imposed on the<br>
commoners by the ruling class.<br>
<b>For example, the ruling class</b><br>
lived a luxurious life, but the<br>
majority were living a miserable<br>
life. Such problems forced the<br>
commoners to support Islamic<br>
scholars like Uthman dan Fodio,<br>
<b>Seku Hamad and Al-Hajj Umar,</b><br>
who were against the evils. The<br>
movements also emerged because<br>
of the need to control important<br>
trade routes and centres so as to<br>
accumulate wealth. For example,<br>
<b>Muslims wanted to dominate the</b><br>
<b>Bilmar salt mining centre, which</b><br>
was under the Hausa state;<br>
(b)	 Misunderstandings<br>
among<br>
<b>Muslim scholars also led to the</b><br>
outbreak of  the Jihad movements<br>
in West Africa. Islam says that<br>
all people are equal before God.<br>
<b>This teaching was contrary to</b><br>
what existed in that society. For<br>
example, there was widespread<br>
corruption in urban areas. In<br>
rural areas, Muslims insisted<br>
that all people should be guided<br>
by Islamic laws. As a result, the<br>
<b>Muslims won the support of the</b><br>
commoners. Therefore, frictions<br>
began and led to the emergence of<br>
the Jihad movements; and<br>
(c)	 The influence of Uthman dan<br>
<b>Fodio of Sokoto and Al-Hajj Umar</b><br>
of Tokolor led to the emergence<br>
of<br>
the<br>
movements.<br>
<b>These</b><br>
leaders contributed to the rise of<br>
consciousness among Muslims<br>
so that they could fight for their<br>
religion and against the evils that<br>
arose in their communities. Dan<br>
<b>Fodio and Umar sought to create</b><br>
a happy world under Islamic law<br>
(sharia). People understood them<br>
and many joined them, hence the<br>
start of the Jihad movements.<br>
<b>Nevertheless, it must be noted that</b><br>
the Jihad movements begin out of<br>
socio-economic and political motives.<br>
<b>Islam was only used to mobilise the</b><br>
<b>West African people to express their</b><br>
grievances against injustices such<br>
as heavy taxation, corruption and<br>
inequalities. Thus, one could argue that<br>
the Jihad movements were caused by<br>
social,  political and economic motives<br>
rather than religious consciousness<br>
alone.<br>

<b>Influence of the Jihad movements</b><br>
on state formation in West Africa<br>
during the 19th century<br>
<b>Islam played a significant role in the</b><br>
growth and development of states in<br>
<b>West Africa. The following are some</b><br>
of its contributions:<br>
(a)	 Islam acted as a unifying factor<br>
in some states in West Africa.<br>
<b>For example, Islam was an</b><br>
official ruling ideology of the<br>
state<br>
throughout<br>
Hausaland.<br>
<b>Muslims were united and were</b><br>
strong enough to maintain and<br>
defend their political and material<br>
interests;<br>
(b)	 The Jihad movements influenced<br>
the expansion of trade, for they<br>
led to the re-establishment of<br>
order and good governance over a<br>
wide area. This situation enabled<br>
traders to move from one place<br>
to another more peacefully. As<br>
a result, the commoners were<br>
encouraged to engage in various<br>
kinds of economic activities<br>
like<br>
trade.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
movements<br>
transformed some non-trading<br>
centres like Adages into centres<br>
of ideas and trade;<br>
(c)	 The Jihad movements also played<br>
a great role in the establishment<br>
of strong armies, which were later<br>
used in expanding empires. The<br>
ruling class and rich merchants<br>
established strong armies, which<br>
were responsible for conquering<br>
and absorbing the weaker states<br>
into their own states. One of the<br>
strong states that emerged as a<br>
result of the Jihad movements<br>
was the Sokoto caliphate in 1846.<br>
<b>Similarly, Samori Toure, who</b><br>

and a Dyula merchant led a Jihad<br>

<b>Mandinka Empire in present-day</b><br>
<b>Guinea. This empire was located</b><br>
roughly between Sierra Leone and<br>
<b>Liberia in the west, Ivory Coast in</b><br>
the south, Mali in the north and<br>
the River Volta in the east;<br>
(d)	 The Jihad movements succeeded<br>
in<br>
bringing<br>
about<br>
judicial<br>
adjustments<br>
in<br>
West Africa.<br>
<b>Before the movements started,</b><br>
<b>West Africa was administered</b><br>
in accordance with a traditional<br>
law, which was not only out of<br>
date, but also confusing. After the<br>
<b>Jihad conquest, Islamic law was</b><br>
adopted to replace the judicial<br>
confusion that was evident in<br>
<b>West Africa. In this way, Islamic</b><br>
law and order replaced the earlier<br>
chaos and civil conflicts;<br>

<b>Influence of Mfecane on state</b><br>
formation during the 19th century<br>
<b>The word Mfecane was derived from</b><br>
the Nguni language. It means scattering,<br>
forced dispersal or forced migration.<br>
<b>It refers to the wars and disturbances</b><br>
that were accompanied by the rise and<br>
growth of the Zulu state under Shaka<br>
from 1818 to the 1860s.<br>
<b>Mfecane is described as a social and</b><br>
political upheaval that destroyed and,<br>
at the same time, reconstructed the<br>
existing state systems in Southern<br>
Africa and the neighbouring societies.<br>
<b>During the early 19th century, a series</b><br>
of wars took place among the northern<br>
Nguni peoples of South-Eastern Africa.<br>
<b>By the 1820s, the Zulu Kingdom had</b><br>
become strong enough to dominate<br>
other states in the region. During the<br>
1820s and 1830s, armies and refugees<br>
from these wars spread warfare<br>
and destruction over larger areas of<br>
<b>Southern and Central Africa. This</b><br>
incident has become known among<br>
the Nguni people as Mfecane. The war<br>
started in Zululand and gave rise to a<br>
chain of movements that affected many<br>
areas, including the south-western<br>
part of present-day Tanzania. Mfecane<br>
played a great role in state formation<br>
during the 19th century, as follows.<br>
(a)	 The frequent wars necessitated<br>
the formation of efficient armies,<br>
which facilitated the rise of<br>
many small states. With the<br>
development of these states, the<br>
age-set systems were transformed<br>
into age-set regiments which were<br>
dedicated to professional warfare.<br>
<b>The regiments were composed</b><br>
of highly trained and disciplined<br>
soldiers, who fought at close<br>
quarters using short stabbing<br>
spears and complex military<br>
formations. A good example<br>
was Shaka, a great Nguni leader<br>
who turned age-set regiments<br>
into powerful military forces<br>
for<br>
conquering<br>
neighbouring<br>
communities. As a result,  bigger<br>
states were formed. A similar<br>
process was adopted by Thulare,<br>
leader of the Sotho-speaking<br>
<b>Bapedi people in the north-</b><br>
eastern Transvaal, Moshoeshoe<br>
in Lesotho and other kings of the<br>
<b>Nguni-speaking people such as</b><br>
<b>Sobhuza, Zwide and Dingiswayo;</b><br>

(b)	 The growth of social cohesion<br>

cultural<br>
and<br>

communities experienced defeat,<br>
they united to form new states to<br>
safeguard their social, economic<br>
and political interests;<br>
(d)	 The Zulu were divided into multi-<br>

the Zulu type of military system<br>
to those areas. As they moved<br>
northwards, the Zulu destroyed<br>
the pre-existing states such as the<br>
Rozvi state in Zimbabwe.<br>
(e)	 Furthermore, Mfecane influenced<br>
the emergence of strong leaders<br>
who played a leading role in the<br>
formation of different states in<br>
<b>Southern and Central Africa like</b><br>
<b>Shaka of the Zulu Kingdom,</b><br>
<b>Mzilikazi of the Ndebele and</b><br>
<b>Mashoeshoe of the Sotho; and</b><br>
(f)<br>
<b>Mfecane influenced the migration</b><br>
of some societies to Southern,<br>
<b>Eastern and Central Africa, where</b><br>
they developed into strong states.<br>
<b>For example, the migration of the</b><br>
<b>Nguni groups led to the emergence</b><br>
of states like the Ndebele state<br>
under Mzilikazi, the Sotho state<br>
under Mashoeshoe and the Swazi<br>
Kingdom under Sobhuza.<br>

<b>Pre-colonial education and culture</b><br>
<b>In order to understand pre-colonial</b><br>
education and culture, it is important<br>
to define the two concepts. Culture<br>
is defined as the way of life of a<br>
particular social group.  It includes<br>
their beliefs, arts and technology that<br>
new generations inherit from their<br>
ancestors. By contrast, education is<br>
a process which involves acquiring<br>
and transmitting knowledge, skills,<br>
values, morals, beliefs and habits from<br>
one generation to another. Before the<br>
imposition of colonial rule on Africas,<br>
<b>Africans had their own culture and</b><br>
education. Pre-colonial Africa was<br>
rich in its total way of life, which<br>
differentiated one society from the<br>
other.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial education</b><br>
<b>Pre-colonial education is defined as a</b><br>
life-long process of transmitting and<br>
imparting  the knowledge, skills, values,<br>
norms and experiences of a given<br>
society from one generation to another<br>
before the colonial era. Pre-colonial<br>
education<br>
was<br>
delivered<br>
through<br>
active participation and observation.<br>
<b>During the pre-colonial period, each</b><br>
adult<br>
participated<br>
in<br>
transmitting<br>
knowledge. The system did not require<br>
people to build complex facilities and<br>
professional teachers. Youth training<br>
depended on the experience of the<br>
trainer in a certain field. For example,<br>
the knowledge of midwifery could be<br>
imparted to some women, who would<br>
later train young girls in how to assist<br>
each other during pregnancy. Likewise,<br>
young men and women were taught<br>
parental and marital responsibilities by<br>
adults through jando and unyago.<br>
<b>Objectives of pre-colonial education</b><br>
<b>Pre-colonial education was aimed</b><br>
at<br>
imparting<br>
social<br>
values<br>
and<br>
transmitting good morals and norms<br>
to new generations to prepare them<br>
for their future careers. For example,<br>
<b>Africans were taught to do various</b><br>
economic activities such as fishing,<br>
farming and hunting. This went hand in<br>
hand with equipping the youth with the<br>
basic knowledge and skills related to<br>
particular economic activities. In that<br>
way, pre-colonial education developed<br>
a sense of belonging in learners and<br>
encouraged them to participate actively<br>
in performing family responsibilities.<br>
<b>Boys learnt skills by observing their</b><br>
fathers performing certain duties,<br>
while girls learnt from their mothers. In<br>
this area, girls were taught how to take<br>
care of their husbands and children. By<br>
contrast, boys were taught how to head<br>
and protect their families. They both<br>
learnt through apprenticeship.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial education was intended to</b><br>
develop children’s character, together<br>
with their physical strength and<br>
intellectual abilities. Children were<br>
given practical skills and  taught how<br>
develop a sense of self-dependence.<br>
<b>In order to achieve this, specific</b><br>
vocational skills and training were<br>

inculcated in them.<br>
<b>Moreover,</b><br>
pre-colonial<br>
education<br>
was aimed at promoting peace and<br>
security in a society. It trained the<br>
youth to defend their societies against<br>
enemies. For instance, in Maasai<br>
society, the Morans were responsible<br>
for peacekeeping. They were also<br>
taught how to defend their community<br>
and protect their livestock from thieves<br>
and wild animals, as well as to conquer<br>
their neighbouring communities to<br>
expand their herds of cattle.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial education also prepared</b><br>
the youth to take over after the end<br>
of the existing governments in their<br>
societies. This duty was important<br>
because administrative knowledge was<br>
at the very heart of social, political and<br>
economic development. Well-prepared<br>
young leaders could enhance the<br>
stability of society across generations.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial education was also aimed</b><br>
at making the youth patriotic. It was<br>
the duty of elders to impart a sense<br>
of patriotism to the youth so that<br>
they might be proud of their people,<br>
environment and resources. In  pre-<br>
colonial societies, there were different<br>
antagonistic states. Therefore, a sense<br>
of patriotism was very important among<br>
the youth to discourage conspiracy<br>
against the welfare of their societies.<br>
<b>Types of pre-colonial education</b><br>
<b>There were two types of education</b><br>
during the pre-colonial time, namely<br>
informal<br>
and<br>
formal<br>
education.<br>
<b>Informal education was the mode of</b><br>
learning in which knowledge, skills,<br>
norms and experiences were largely<br>
based on local experiences. Informal<br>
education was provided through life<br>
experiences. It was mostly obtained<br>
through observation and practice.<br>
<b>The youth acquired new knowledge</b><br>
and skills by observing their parents,<br>
relatives or other members of society.<br>
<b>Informal education did not include</b><br>
any theoretical knowledge of books. It<br>
was gained under the influence of the<br>
society.<br>
<b>Informal education was relevant to</b><br>
<b>Africans, since it taught them how</b><br>
to engage in cultivation, hunting and<br>
fishing. Individuals were also taught<br>
how to make iron, leather and clay<br>
tools. Teaching and learning were done<br>
through work. For example, a young<br>
man would accompany his father on<br>
a hunting trip and, in the process, he<br>
would learn hunting skills.<br>
<b>Formal education was a mode of</b><br>
learning organised in such a way that<br>
people attended formal classes. It<br>
involved specific programmes and a<br>
conscious division of responsibilities<br>
between teachers and learners. Like<br>
informal education, formal education<br>
was directly connected to the purpose<br>
of serving a society. In practice, formal<br>
education dealt with different issues,<br>
depending on the needs of a particular<br>
society. For example, the youth learnt<br>

in<br>
pre-colonial<br>

<b>Significantly,</b><br>
