and Africans. This is because it was<br>
mainly aimed at preparing Africans to<br>
be good and useful members of their<br>
societies.<br>
<b>Characteristics of pre-colonial</b><br>
education<br>
Pre-colonial education was practical.<br>
<b>For example, the youth were taken</b><br>
to the seas, oceans, rivers and lakes,<br>
where they were taught how to fish.<br>
<b>A similar thing was done among the</b><br>
agricultural communities, who taught<br>
young people how to farm. Moreover,<br>
those interested in black smithery or<br>
industries were practically taught how<br>
to make iron or other industrial goods.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial education was not class-</b><br>
biased. That means this kind of<br>
education was not discriminatory. It<br>
was provided to all society members,<br>
regardless of one’s wealth or status. The<br>
only difference was that sometimes boys<br>
were taught military techniques, while<br>
girls were not. This was influenced by<br>
the way informal education was given.<br>
<b>As a result, pre-colonial education</b><br>
did not create any classes in society.<br>
<b>Although pre-colonial education did</b><br>
not lead to the creation of any classes,<br>
it promoted specialisation as reflected<br>
in diviners or healers' activities.<br>
<b>This type of education was not</b><br>
financially motivated. During the pre-<br>
colonial era, every society member<br>
considered themselves as having great<br>
responsibilities to fulfil in the society.<br>
<b>Thus, instructors were not paid. To</b><br>
motivate instructors, the community<br>
gave them gifts in the form of domestic<br>
animals or crops, depending on what was<br>
available in the  community at the time.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial</b><br>
education<br>
emphasised<br>
good morals and good social conduct.<br>
The learners were taught good character.<br>
<b>Elders made sure that good values and</b><br>
behaviour such as “hardworking” traits<br>
were imparted successfully, while<br>
undesirable behaviour like theft and<br>
prostitution were strongly condemned.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial</b><br>
education<br>
lacked<br>
uniformity. It varied from one society<br>
to another, ,depending on the economic,<br>
social and environmental conditions<br>
in a given society. Each society had<br>
its own customs and traditions. For<br>
example, the customs and traditions of<br>

<b>Pre-colonial</b><br>
education<br>
was<br>

informal<br>

<b>In</b><br>
informal<br>
education, knowledge was imparted to<br>
the youth through verbal transmission,<br>
observation and apprenticeship. In<br>
formal education knowledge was<br>
imparted by a recognised trainer,<br>
with specified programmes in specific<br>
settings.<br>
<b>The roles of pre-colonial education</b><br>
in Africa<br>
<b>Pre-colonial</b><br>
education<br>
enabled<br>
children to learn and preserve the<br>
culture of their societies. For example,<br>
children were taught how to greet<br>
elders and how to dress. Thus, pre-<br>
colonial education moulded young<br>
people so that they could practise and<br>
perpetuate the customs and traditions<br>
of their societies.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial education helped the</b><br>
youth to develop a sense of harmony,<br>
unity<br>
and<br>
understanding<br>
among<br>
themselves and with other society<br>
members. The youth were also taught<br>
how to keep peace in society and how<br>
to respect each other. Thus, the system<br>
of education was not biased.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial education enabled the</b><br>
youth to develop knowledge and skills in<br>
various areas such as pottery, basketry,<br>
iron-smelting, crop cultivation and<br>
livestock keeping. Since the mode of<br>
education was practical, it equipped<br>
people with the tools necessary for the<br>
economic development of the entire<br>
society.<br>
<b>Moreover,</b><br>
pre-colonial<br>
education<br>
enabled individuals to become good<br>
leaders. For instance, the chief’s sons<br>
were given special skills which would<br>
enable them to inherit the throne after<br>
the death of their father.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial</b><br>
education<br>
enabled<br>
societies to produce traditional medical<br>
doctors and midwives. The traditional<br>
doctors handled medical cases such as<br>
malaria, stomach ache, snake bites, bone<br>
fractures and dislocations. Similarly,<br>
they obtained some knowledge of<br>
the traditional medicine that cured<br>
common childhood diseases. Elderly<br>
women shared the general knowledge<br>
and skills that enabled them to perform<br>
midwifery roles.<br>
<b>Furthermore, pre-colonial education</b><br>
provided special education on social<br>
and sex roles that benefited the whole<br>
society. For example, at puberty, boys<br>
and girls were not allowed to share<br>
sleeping rooms. Likewise, mothers<br>
taught their daughters how to sit or talk<br>
before elders. This, in turn, helped to<br>
promote good interpersonal behaviour<br>
that benefited the whole society.<br>

were initiation ceremonies through<br>

<b>Strengths of pre-colonial education</b><br>
<b>Pre-colonial education taught Africans</b><br>
various<br>
economic<br>
activities.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
youth were taught how to do various<br>
economic activities. For example, the<br>
youth were taught fishing, cultivation<br>
and trade. These economic activities<br>
acted as a catalyst for development.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial</b><br>
education<br>
was<br>
not<br>
static. It changed, depending on<br>
the circumstances and the mode of<br>
production in a particular society.<br>
<b>For example, when societies changed</b><br>
from communal to feudal modes of<br>
production, new patterns of education<br>
emerged as the development of<br>
technology necessitated specialisation<br>
and division of labour. This led to<br>
the development of a new system of<br>
education, through which the skills and<br>
techniques of iron-working, leather-<br>
making,<br>
cloth-manufacturing<br>
and<br>
pottery-moulding were imparted.<br>
<b>Furthermore, pre-colonial education</b><br>
was<br>
practical.<br>
<b>This</b><br>
practicability<br>
enhanced the knowledge and skills<br>
imparted to learners. For instance,<br>
children<br>
attended<br>
various<br>
social<br>
gatherings to learn about different<br>
aspects of their lives. Children had<br>
to<br>
demonstrate<br>
their<br>
knowledge<br>
before elders so that their level of<br>
understanding would be assessed.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial education instilled among</b><br>
the youth appreciation of cultural<br>
values, customs and traditions. Elders<br>
insisted on protecting their culture<br>
as it was their symbol. Therefore,<br>
pre-colonial education was a good<br>
custodian of the culture of a given<br>
society.<br>
<b>The education provided was collective</b><br>
and inclusive; it did not have any<br>
element of discrimination. Children<br>
were taught the same type of education<br>
in their particular age and sex groups.<br>
<b>Boys and girls attained education</b><br>
in accordance with the needs of<br>
their societies. They were equipped<br>
with knowledge for mastering their<br>
environment. They were also prepared<br>
to take care of their parents and elders.<br>

<b>Pre-colonial education instilled a sense</b><br>

<b>Sina of Iringa and Kibosho were too</b><br>
loyal and patriotic to betray their<br>
fellow Africans, although they were<br>
persuaded by the white intruders to<br>

<b>Is</b><br>
informal<br>
education<br>
still<br>
practised in modern Africa?<br>

<b>Weaknesses of pre-colonial education</b><br>
<b>Although pre-colonial education had</b><br>
several strengths, it also had a number<br>
of weaknesses, as shown below.<br>
<b>First,</b><br>
pre-colonial<br>
education<br>
did<br>
not involve the skills of writing or<br>
recording the knowledge imparted<br>
to learners. Very few areas provided<br>
writing skills, for example Timbuktu in<br>
<b>West Africa, Fez in Morocco and Egypt</b><br>
in North Africa. As a result, its success<br>
depended largely on one’s ability to<br>
remember what one had learnt. Thus,<br>
once people with such knowledge<br>
or skills died, their knowledge also<br>
disappeared.<br>
<b>Furthermore, the content of pre-colonial</b><br>
education included certain customs<br>
and traditions that were unnecessary.<br>
<b>In many cases, it undermined the rights</b><br>
of women. For example, a woman who<br>
lost her husband was not allowed to<br>
inherit the wealth left behind by the<br>
husband. Moreover, Female Genital<br>
<b>Mutilation (FGM), which is said to</b><br>
have detrimental effects on health, was<br>
encouraged in some ethnic groups.<br>
<b>In some societies, women were not</b><br>
allowed to eat certain types of food<br>
such as eggs, milk and some parts of<br>
animal meat such as the liver. Similarly,<br>
among the iron-working societies,<br>
women were not allowed to learn iron-<br>
working.<br>
<b>Additionally, pre-colonial education</b><br>
heavily relied on the economic activities<br>
of a particular society. For example, in<br>
agricultural societies, the youth could<br>
not learn about pastoral activities, and<br>
vice versa. This kind of specialisation,<br>
deprived young men and women of an<br>
opportunity to expand their knowledge.<br>
People could not fit in other societies.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial education lacked a specific</b><br>
syllabus. This affected consistency and<br>
a regulated flow of knowledge. There<br>
was no uniformity, with respect to<br>
the knowledge or skills learnt in the<br>
society. Each instructor could use his<br>
own approach and personal preferences<br>
to deliver the knowledge.<br>

<b>Exercise 1.6</b><br>
<b>To what extent was pre-colonial</b><br>
<b>African education relevant to</b><br>
learners environment?<br>
<b>Pre-colonial culture</b><br>
<b>Pre-colonial culture refers to Africans’</b><br>
way of life, which existed before the<br>
advent of European colonialism. It<br>
was a system of education, traditions,<br>
languages, customs, arts and craft. Pre-<br>
colonial education, therefore, was a<br>
means through which people learnt and<br>
acquired the culture of a given society.<br>
<b>Objectives of pre-colonial culture</b><br>
<b>Pre-colonial culture was aimed at</b><br>
imparting knowledge, skills and values<br>
to society members through the word of<br>
mouth, and also by directly involving<br>
learners in different activities like<br>
fishing, carpentry and painting; in most<br>
cases it was learning by doing. Parents<br>
fulfilled<br>
their<br>
responsibilities<br>
by<br>
directing their children to behave well.<br>
<b>The younger generation was taught to</b><br>
behave as Africans in the environment<br>
they lived in.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial culture was used to</b><br>
entertain people during difficult times<br>
such as funerals, wars and calamities<br>
through songs and traditional dances.<br>
<b>It also used to  entertain people during</b><br>
happy moments when people gathered<br>
and celebrated by dancing and singing.<br>
<b>The Matengo people, for instance,</b><br>
celebrated the harvest season by<br>
dancing mganda, a dance for men, and<br>
sioda, a dance for women.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial African culture was aimed</b><br>
at speeding up economic development.<br>
<b>It instilled a spirit of hard work in</b><br>
<b>Africans. In African societies, lazy men</b><br>
and women were not allowed to marry<br>
and some societies denounced lazy<br>
people by sending them far away so<br>
that they could not influence the youth.<br>
<b>Thus, since ancient times the African</b><br>
youth were taught by their parents<br>
not to be parasitic and to be self-<br>
reliant. Therefore, pre-colonial culture<br>
prepared the youth to be productive and<br>
self-reliant adults of the future.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial culture unified community</b><br>
members. Culture brought people<br>
together via various traditional fora<br>
for the youth, elders, leaders and<br>
the common people. For example,<br>
<b>Matengo elders and leaders had their</b><br>
forum known as pasengu, during which<br>
leaders from different clans met and<br>
discussed on the development and<br>
well-being of their people. Through<br>
pasengu, the leaders got information<br>
from different clans and families that<br>
helped them to make good decision.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial culture was also aimed at</b><br>
preparing good members of society.<br>
<b>It is through culture that good morals,</b><br>
values and beliefs were transmitted<br>
to the younger generation. Through<br>

children good behaviour. For example,<br>

had to kneel down while greeting<br>
their elders, as a sign of respect and<br>
obedience.<br>
<b>Pre-colonial culture showed African</b><br>
identities. Every society had its own<br>
set of customary practices. In order<br>
to distinguish one society from the<br>
other, the members of each society had<br>
to display some uniqueness in their<br>
cultural etiquette. The uniqueness of<br>
customs and practices helped to show<br>
how one community differed from<br>
another.<br>
<b>Moreover,</b><br>
pre-colonial<br>
culture<br>
promoted development by imparting<br>
practical knowledge and skills to<br>
people. It was the foundation of<br>
technological innovation, which was<br>
the basis for industrial, agricultural<br>
and commercial development. For<br>
example, the Makonde engaged in<br>
wood carving.<br>

<b>Introduction</b><br>
By the 15th century, Africa and Europe had achieved almost the same levels<br>
of development in terms of technology, politics and economy. The difference<br>
between the two continents could be seen in the development of maritime<br>
technology, of which Western Europe had made a remarkable achievement. In<br>
this chapter, you will learn about systems of exchange which existed in Africa<br>
and Europe, and about the levels of political, scientific and technological<br>
development which had been achieved by the two continents by the 15th century.<br>
You will also learn about the rise of the development gap between Africa and<br>
Europe from about the 15th century onwards. The competencies developed will<br>
enable you to suggest strategies that African societies could employ to develop<br>
economically and technologically.<br>
How is the trade relationship between Africa and Europe today?<br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Two</b><br>
<b>Africa and Europe in the 15th century</b><br>
<b>The concept of development</b><br>
<b>Development is a broad concept. At</b><br>
the level of an individual, development<br>
means an increase in skills, capacity,<br>
greater freedom, creativity and self-<br>
discipline. It also means increased<br>
responsibility and the material well-<br>
being of an individual. This suggests<br>
that development is a relative term, in<br>
the sense that it means different things<br>
to different people. At the community<br>
or societal level, the term development<br>
refers to the improvement of the quality<br>
of human lives in political, social and<br>
economic respects.<br>
<b>Many scholars define development in</b><br>
relation to underdevelopment, but it<br>
should be pointed out that the concept of<br>
underdevelopment does not imply the<br>
absence of development. That means<br>
people necessarily have developed<br>
in one way or another, regardless<br>
of their state of underdevelopment.<br>
<b>Therefore,</b><br>
underdevelopment<br>
or<br>

more countries are compared. This is<br>
because there is uneven development<br>
between nations. Some nations are poor<br>
and others rich. Like development,<br>
the concept of underdevelopment is a<br>
relative term.<br>
<b>Studies have shown that capitalism is</b><br>
the major cause of underdevelopment<br>
in Africa. It is argued that the<br>
development of capitalism in Europe<br>
led to uneven development in Europe<br>
and Africa. This is because, before<br>
the 15th century, African societies<br>
developed independently, but this<br>
process of development was disrupted<br>
when the Europeans arrived in Africa<br>
in the 15th century. From an Afro-<br>
centric point of view, African societies<br>
had already developed in areas of<br>
science and technology, trade, industry,<br>
agriculture, religion, education and<br>
politics when merchant capitalism<br>
began. Historians like Walter Rodney<br>
have argued that Africa was virtually<br>
at the same level of development as<br>
<b>Europe in the 15th century. On the</b><br>
contrary, Euro-centric historians refute<br>
this argument, claiming that there was<br>
no development at all in pre-colonial<br>
<b>African societies. To them, Africa</b><br>
was static, classless and stateless, and<br>
therefore development and civilisation<br>
in Africa are the result of the whites’<br>
activities such as colonialism.<br>
<b>Activity 2.1</b><br>
<b>Debate the meanings of development</b><br>
and underdevelopment. Then, use the<br>
points discussed to assess the validity<br>
of the Afro-centric interpretation<br>
of development in relation to pre-<br>
colonial African societies.<br>
<b>Systems of exchange in Africa</b><br>
<b>Trading activities existed in Africa</b><br>
before it came into contact with<br>
<b>Europe in the 15th century CE. Africans</b><br>
operated trade and exchange relations<br>
at local, regional, interregional and<br>
world levels. Local trade existed<br>
everywhere in Africa; traders hawked<br>
goods from house to house or village<br>
to village. Unequal distribution of<br>
resources contributed to the rise of<br>
trade and exchange relations not only<br>
in Africa, but also in other parts of<br>
the world. The main goods in these<br>
exchange relations were salt, iron ore,<br>
iron tools, copper, clay pots, livestock<br>
and livestock products, bananas and<br>
grain, especially sorghum and millet.<br>
<b>Other items of exchange were weapons</b><br>
such as poisoned iron-tipped arrows<br>
and spears used for hunting wild<br>
animals and for fishing. Generally,<br>
before the 15th century trade in Africa<br>
was mainly done to solve the problem<br>
of scarce goods, and not to create or<br>
acquire  wealth. This type of exchange<br>

about<br>
the<br>

<b>Trade contributed to the growth of</b><br>
powerful empires such as Ghana, Mali<br>
and Songhai, and the rise of an Islamic<br>
kingdom in Morocco, and centres of<br>
learning based in Cairo, Alexandria,<br>
Carthage, Tripoli and the Middle East.<br>
<b>It also contributed to the expansion of</b><br>
urban centres such as Tekrur, Wadan,<br>
<b>Agades, Ghadames and Kano. By</b><br>
the 10th century CE, a large number<br>
of North African traders who were<br>
visiting West Africa had contributed to<br>
the emergence of separate settlements<br>
for those practising African religions<br>
and those practising Islam in Ghana<br>
<b>Empire. Therefore, trade led to the</b><br>
spread of Islam in West Africa between<br>
the 11th and 15th centuries CE. Later on,<br>
the spread of Islam intensified trade<br>
between West Africa, the Mediterranean<br>
world and the Middle East. The spread<br>
of Islam also increased the use of the<br>
Arabic language in administration.<br>

<b>CE and for the next two centuries saw</b><br>

the international market places. The<br>

highways of the ancient world. New<br>
inventions,<br>
medicines,<br>
religious<br>

social<br>
customs<br>
were<br>

transported by people moving from<br>
one place to another to exchange them<br>
for other goods. Although silk was the<br>
main trading item, many other goods<br>
were transported along the Silk Road<br>
between Eastern Asia and Europe.<br>
<b>Over</b><br>
time,<br>
medicines,<br>
perfumes,<br>
plants like banana and rice, spices<br>
and livestock moved between the<br>
continents. Trading along the Silk Road<br>
became strong again between the 13th<br>
and 14th centuries, when the Mongols<br>
controlled Central Asia. During the<br>
age of explorations, the Silk Road lost<br>
its importance because new sea routes<br>
to Asia had been discovered. The<br>
<b>Silk Road was one of the first trading</b><br>
routes that connected the European<br>
world with the Asian world. It played<br>
a major role in the development of<br>
international trade. This was because<br>
this road facilitated the cultural<br>
and technological transmission that<br>
linked the merchants of Europe with<br>
<b>Persia, India and China for almost</b><br>
300 years. Europe also learnt much<br>
from the development of science and<br>
technology, astronomy, mathematics,<br>
calendar and medicines in China, India<br>
and the Middle East.<br>

<b>The impact of exchange and trade on</b><br>
economic systems in Europe<br>
<b>The development of the trade and</b><br>
exchange relations between Europe<br>
and Africa accelerated the development<br>
of the European economy. Through<br>
mercantalism, European merchants<br>
began to accumulate capital, which<br>
facilitated the establishment of new<br>
industries in Europe. Thus, trade played<br>
a great role in the future development<br>
of<br>
industries<br>
in<br>
Europe.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
accumulated capital was also used to<br>
improve European social and physical<br>
infrastructure like roads, railways,<br>
buildings, and water and power supply<br>
systems. Infrastructural development<br>
contributed to the growth of towns such<br>
as Amsterdam, Manchester, Liverpool,<br>
<b>London, Paris and Lisbon. Maritime</b><br>
technology was improved as more<br>
and larger ships for carrying bulky<br>
tropical goods were built. Moreover,<br>
the exchange relations enabled Europe<br>
to acquire raw materials, which were<br>
important for industrialisation. Africa<br>
lost most of its wealth by exchanging its<br>
valuable resources for cheap products<br>
from Europe.<br>
<b>The merchants gained huge profits from</b><br>
the unequal trade. The Portuguese,<br>
who had established themselves along<br>
the West African coast since the 1470s,<br>
exchanged clothes and other items for<br>
gold from the Akan gold fields.<br>
<b>Moreover,</b><br>
through<br>
primitive<br>
accumulation of capital mercantilism<br>
led to the establishment of financial<br>
institutions and insurance companies,<br>
which continued to finance commercial<br>
activities in Europe. For instance,<br>
<b>Barclays Bank had its origin in</b><br>
mercantalist activities which involved<br>
enslaving Africans and acquiring<br>
overseas colonies.<br>
<b>The profit obtained from  trade as</b><br>
well as the mastery of navigational<br>
technologies enabled Europe to expand<br>
to other parts of the world in the 15th<br>
and early 16th centuries. The Europeans<br>
were looking for precious metals and<br>
overseas colonies. The merchants also<br>
conducted wars of plunder in America<br>
and Africa<br>
thereby<br>
accumulating<br>
massive wealth in the form of gold,<br>
silver, copper, ivory and pepper.<br>

reasons.<br>

stagnation of African technologies and<br>
production.<br>
<b>Most of the slaves were captured from</b><br>
the Guinea coast to the south of the<br>
<b>Kongo Kingdom. For example, the</b><br>
<b>Kongo Kingdom became the major</b><br>
source of slaves for Portuguese traders<br>
and other European powers. The<br>
<b>Cantino world map of 1502 mentions</b><br>
the kingdom as the source of slaves<br>
for the island of São Tomé. The slaves<br>
were sold to Portuguese merchants.<br>
<b>Most of the slaves exported to the</b><br>
<b>Portuguese farms were war captives</b><br>
from<br>
the<br>
kingdom’s<br>
expansion<br>
campaigns. Every year, Portuguese<br>
slave traders would come with 12 to<br>
15 ships for carrying back between 5<br>
000 and 10 000 slaves.<br>

<b>The penetration of Christianity and</b><br>
<b>European culture such as dressing and</b><br>
eating styles into Africa undermined<br>
<b>African culture. The Europeans began</b><br>
to despise Africans’ aspects of life such<br>
as medicine, religion and hygiene.<br>
They regarded them as backward.<br>
<b>They wanted Africans to imitate their</b><br>
culture. The Portuguese explorer<br>
<b>Diogo Cão was the first European to</b><br>
visit the Kongo Kingdom. He visited<br>
the kingom in 1483. The kingdom rose<br>
in the 14th century CE. The Portuguese<br>
<b>Jesuit missionaries began to convert</b><br>
<b>Africans to Christianity. In 1491, some</b><br>
<b>Roman Catholic priests and soldiers</b><br>
went to the Kongo Kingdom. They<br>
baptised King Nzinga Nkuwu and his<br>
principal nobles, starting with the ruler<br>
of Soyo, in the coastal province. Other<br>
<b>Kongolese followed the example and</b><br>
were converted to Christianity as well.<br>
<b>At the same time, a literate Kongo</b><br>
citizen returning from Portugal opened<br>
the first school in the kingdom. King<br>
<b>Nzinga Nkuwu took the Christian</b><br>
name of João I in honour of Portugal’s<br>
<b>King at the time, João II. King João</b><br>
<b>I ruled until his death in 1506 and</b><br>
was succeeded by his son Afonso<br>
<b>Mvemba Nzinga. The penetration of</b><br>
<b>Catholicism into the Kongo Kingdom</b><br>
divided the royal family into Catholics<br>
and traditionalists. In 1509, instead<br>
of the usual election involving the<br>
nobles, a hereditary European-style<br>
succession led to the African King,<br>
Afonso I, succeeding his father, João I.<br>
<b>After taking over the throne, King</b><br>
<b>Afonso Mvemba Nzinga faced a</b><br>
serious challenge from his half-<br>
brother, Mpanzu a Kitima. The king<br>
defeated his brother in a battle waged<br>
at Mbanza Kongo. Inspired by this<br>
event, he subsequently designed a coat<br>
of arms for Kongo which was used by<br>
all the kings who came later in official<br>
documents and royal paraphernalia.<br>
<b>While King João I later returned to his</b><br>
traditional beliefs, Afonso I adopted<br>
<b>Christianity as the religion of his</b><br>
<b>Kingdom. King Afonso I introduced a</b><br>
version of the Roman Catholic Church<br>
into Kongo.<br>
<b>The Africans who were Christianised</b><br>
were seen as more civilised than<br>
those who retained their traditional<br>
beliefs. The two identities continued<br>
to struggle for the throne and later<br>
on this contributed to the collapse of<br>
the Kongo Kingdom. Thus, European<br>
culture and Christianity contributed to<br>
the collapse of the Kongo Kingdom.<br>

<b>Political systems in Africa and</b><br>
<b>Europe by the 15th century</b><br>
<b>A political system is defined as a set of</b><br>
formal legal institutions that constitute<br>
a government or state. It is usually<br>
compared to the legal, economic and<br>
social systems. By the 15th century,<br>
<b>Africa and Europe had developed</b><br>
various<br>
political<br>
organisations.<br>
<b>Although Africa and Europe had</b><br>
developed political organisations, the<br>
level of political organisation differed<br>
from place to place, depending on the<br>
level of the development of productive<br>
forces and the mode of production of a<br>
particular society.<br>
<b>The political systems in Africa by</b><br>
the 15th century<br>
<b>Pre-colonial African societies practised</b><br>
communal and feudal modes of<br>
production at different times, depending<br>
on the nature of their environment.<br>
<b>For example, societies that lived in</b><br>
areas endowed with natural resources<br>
developed state organisations under<br>
feudalism. Likewise, societies which<br>
lived in grassland areas and arid and<br>
semi-arid areas developed clan and<br>
age-set organisations, which existed<br>
side by side with communalism. The<br>
states formed were accompanied by<br>
the existence of military forces which<br>
defended the society and conquered<br>
neighbouring societies.<br>
<b>Moreover, African societies developed</b><br>
various laws which governed the<br>
payment of tribute and leadership<br>
roles. For example, the Mtemi was<br>
responsible for distributing land to his<br>
subjects, settling disputes and leading<br>
other in spiritual matters.<br>
<b>Nevertheless, some African societies</b><br>
developed<br>
a<br>
relatively<br>
formal<br>
parliamentary system. For example, the<br>
<b>Buganda Kingdom in East Africa had</b><br>
the Lukiiko, which was the parliament<br>
of the Buganda Kingdom. Members<br>
of the Lukiiko were elected directly<br>
from the countries under the Buganda<br>
<b>Kingdom. Some were appointed by</b><br>
the Kabaka. The Lukiiko advised the<br>
<b>Kabaka (King) on political, social</b><br>
and economic matters. It also made<br>
laws. In some societies, religion was<br>
the central part of state operation.<br>
<b>Despite performing their political</b><br>
responsibilities, leaders also performed<br>
religious functions. They were believed<br>
to be in direct contact with their<br>
ancestors (gods).<br>
<b>The political system in Europe by</b><br>
the 15th century<br>
<b>In the 15th century, almost all European</b><br>
societies had developed feudal systems<br>
which co-existed with state a formation<br>
process. For instance, the existence of<br>
states like Saxon, Vanetia and Frankish<br>
proves the validity of this statement,<br>
since these states were already under<br>
the authority of kings. Moreover, the<br>
states were more decentralised, since<br>
they had land manors or feudal lords<br>
who were sovereign.<br>

<b>However, after the 15th century,</b><br>
movements<br>
emerged<br>
in<br>
<b>Europe</b><br>
which sought to weaken the manors’<br>
sovereignty. Kings gained power using<br>
soldiers (knights) to break manorial<br>
walls. As a result, the kings took steps<br>
to own, organise and control the army.<br>
<b>This situati or example, the Roman</b><br>
<b>Catholic (clergy) took over many</b><br>
functions in the government, including<br>
law-making.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
<b>Roma</b><br>
<b>Catholic</b><br>
<b>Church was predominant in Medieval</b><br>

<b>Similarities in the political systems of</b><br>
<b>Africa and Europe by the 15th century</b><br>
<b>The political systems of Europe</b><br>
and Africa shared certain elements.<br>
<b>Both Europe and Africa developed</b><br>
centralised<br>
states.<br>
<b>Europe</b><br>
had<br>
monarchies, in which  power was<br>
centralised and the decisions the kings<br>
made were final. Likewise, in Africa,<br>
centralised states were governed by<br>
kings or queens who were the decision-<br>
makers. A good example was King<br>
<b>Joao I (Zinga Nkuwu) of the Kongo</b><br>
Kingdom.<br>
<b>Leadership was hereditary in both</b><br>
<b>Europe and Africa. The European</b><br>
royal families had a tendency to pass<br>
power to the son of the outgoing king.<br>
<b>Africans also passed power to the son</b><br>
of the outgoing king. For example, after<br>
the death of Zinga Nkuwu of Kongo,<br>
his son Zinga Mbemba (Don Affonso I)<br>
took over. Likewise, in the Buganda<br>
<b>Kingdom leaders followed the lineage</b><br>
of the Kabaka. In England, the monarch<br>
was hereditary and the royal family was<br>
the source of the next monarch. A good<br>
example was the house of Tudor, an<br>
<b>English royal dynasty of Welsh origin,</b><br>
which gave five sovereigns to England<br>
from 1485 to 1603.<br>
<b>Africa and Europe developed military</b><br>
forces, which were used to conquer<br>
neighbouring societies and to acquire<br>
wealth and labour. The army was also<br>
used to defend the territory against<br>
foreign invasions and to keep peace.<br>
<b>The political institutions in Europe and</b><br>
<b>Africa were directly involved in trading</b><br>
activities inside and outside their<br>
empires. For example, kings in empires<br>
like Ghana and Mali supported the long<br>
distance trade between West African<br>
societies and North African societies.<br>
<b>Likewise, European states, especially</b><br>
mercantilist states, supported and<br>
protected their merchants domestically<br>
and internationally.<br>
<b>Both European and African kings</b><br>
involved themselves directly in trade.<br>
<b>They guarded the trade routes that</b><br>
passed through their territories so<br>
that they could collected taxes from<br>
merchants. For example, Prince Henry,<br>
the Navigator of Portugal, sponsored<br>
the search for sea routes to India for<br>
trading purposes. Similarly, Tunka<br>
<b>Manin of Ghana, Sundiata and Mansa</b><br>
<b>Musa of Mali and Askia Mohamed</b><br>
of Songhai were involved directly<br>
in the Trans-Saharan Trade. Another<br>

<b>Religion was at the centre of the</b><br>

rights to rule. For the Catholic Church,<br>
the Pope was both a religious and<br>
political leader. Similarly, in Africa,<br>
political leaders were believed to have<br>
some spiritual power; hence, they were<br>
given the right to organise spiritual<br>
matters such as religious ceremonies<br>
and sacrifices.<br>
<b>Differences in the political systems</b><br>
of Africa and Europe by the 15th<br>
century<br>
<b>Despite the similarities regarding the</b><br>
political systems of Western Europe<br>
and Africa, there were also some slight<br>
differences between the two political<br>
systems.<br>
<b>The political systems that existed in</b><br>
<b>Europe around 1200 CE moved further</b><br>
ahead  of African ones. Europe started<br>
to establish absolute nation-states ruled<br>
by despotic kings. The kings ruled their<br>
nation-states, bending on the “Divine<br>
<b>Right of the Kings” principle, which</b><br>
stipulated that the kings were directly<br>
chosen by God. Hence, they were only<br>
accountable to God, who had put them<br>
on the throne. This principle caused<br>
Europe to have oppressive kings.<br>
<b>Examples of the nation-states that</b><br>
rose in Europe were Spain, France and<br>
Holland.<br>
<b>On</b><br>
the<br>
contrary,<br>
<b>Africa</b><br>
never<br>
developed nation-states like those of<br>
<b>Europe in the 15th century. The political</b><br>
system was based on both centralised<br>
and decentralised states. However,<br>
there were very few societies that<br>
established centralised states. This was<br>
due to the presence of strong leaders,<br>
strong armies and geographical factors<br>

knights, landlords and serfs. The<br>
political structure in Africa was very<br>
brief as it comprised kings and royal<br>
advisors.<br>

<b>Development of science and</b><br>
technology in Africa and<br>
<b>Europe up to the 15th century</b><br>
<b>The concepts of science and technology</b><br>
are interlinked. Science refers to a body<br>
of the knowledge obtained through<br>
observation and experimentation with<br>
the forces of nature (the physical<br>
world) and by which the theories<br>
explaining the behaviour of such<br>
forces are developed. In other words,<br>
science is the knowledge acquired<br>
from careful study of the structure<br>
and behaviour of the physical world<br>
through observation, measurement and<br>
experimentation aimed at arriving at<br>
some generalisations or development<br>
of scientific theories. Technology is<br>
defined as the application of scientific<br>
knowledge to solving social problems.<br>
<b>It appears, therefore, that science</b><br>
and technology are inseparable. This<br>
section explains the development of<br>
science and technology in both Africa<br>
and Europe in the period up to the<br>
15th century. The main argument is<br>
that, by the 15th century, African and<br>

shaduf (also called shadoof) methods of<br>
irrigation. Similarly, European societies<br>
applied green manure onto their farms,<br>
practised animal cross-breeding and<br>
used the enclosure system to increase<br>
production. Similarly, Africans used<br>
traditional methods of fertilising farms.<br>
<b>Specifically, they used constructed</b><br>
terraces to control soil erosion and<br>
practised shifting cultivation and mixed<br>
agriculture. Societies with the culture<br>
of banana consumption, for example,<br>
fertilised their banana groves using<br>
cow dung. In African societies  mixed<br>
agriculture began to evolve during the<br>
first Millennium CE.<br>
<b>By the 15th century, there was no much</b><br>
difference in the means of transport<br>
used in Africa and Europe. In Africa,<br>
rafts, canoes and dhows were the major<br>
means of water transport. The use of<br>
dhows and canoes was wide-spread<br>
among the societies found along the<br>
<b>Indian Ocean coast and among most</b><br>
of the societies found along the water<br>
bodies such as rivers and lakes. In<br>
<b>Africa, donkeys, camels and oxen were</b><br>
commonly used as means of transport.<br>
<b>Donkeys were used to carry goods</b><br>
across the Sahara before they were<br>
replaced by camels in the 3rd century<br>
<b>CE. Europeans also used donkeys</b><br>
and horses as means of transport. In<br>
<b>Europe, donkeys were used to pull</b><br>
carts and loads. The discovery of the<br>
wheel accelerated the development of<br>
transport in Europe as it marked the<br>
beginning of animal-driven carts and<br>
wagons.<br>
<b>Africa and Europe had also attained</b><br>
a considerable level of development<br>
in the field of medicine. In Africa<br>
traditional medicines were used to cure<br>
various  kinds of tropical diseases.<br>
<b>Traditional healing was the major</b><br>
preoccupation of African traditional<br>
healers or doctors who acquired the<br>
knowledge of healing diseases through<br>
inheritance or training. The healers<br>
existed in all African societies and<br>
were accorded respect and honour<br>
on account of their healing activities.<br>
<b>They knew different medicinal plants</b><br>
which were used to cure snake bites<br>
and numerous tropical diseases.<br>

<b>Rise of the development gap</b><br>
between Africa and Europe<br>
<b>Various</b><br>
historical,<br>
linguistic<br>
and<br>
archaeological pieces of evidence show<br>
that Africa was almost at the same<br>
level of development as Europe in the<br>
15th century. However, after the 15th<br>
century the development gap between<br>
Africa and Europe started to widen.<br>
<b>Europe began to develop much faster</b><br>
than Africa. There were several reasons<br>
for this situation.<br>
<b>Slavery is one of the major reasons</b><br>
for that. Through the slave trade, a<br>
substantial number of able-bodied<br>
men and women were taken from<br>
<b>Africa so that they could work on</b><br>
<b>European-owned plantations and in the</b><br>
mines in the Americas. This situation<br>
led to depopulation and, hence,<br>
stagnation of African economies and<br>
technologies. Africa was robbed of<br>
its healthy working people. Added to<br>
this problem was the insecurity caused<br>
by enslavement-related activities that<br>
took place in Africa at the time. This<br>
insecurity could not allow production<br>
and trading activities to continue<br>
smoothly. Thus, African economies<br>
were completely disrupted, thereby<br>
marking the beginning of Africa’s<br>
underdevelopment.<br>
<b>Unequal exchange was also another</b><br>
reason for the widening of the gap<br>
between the two continents. The<br>
trade relations between Africa and<br>
<b>Europe were unequal and unfair</b><br>
to Africans. For instance, Europe<br>
brought valueless items to Africa<br>
such as mirrors, gunpowder, beads,<br>
clothes and alcohol. These items were<br>
exchanged for valuable items from<br>
<b>Africa such as slaves, gold, silver and</b><br>
ivory. Similarly, there was also unequal<br>
exchange during the legitimate trade<br>
in the 19th century, after the abolition<br>
of the slave trade. Europeans brought<br>
manufactured goods with low value<br>
and exchanged them for African<br>
natural products like cocoa, rubber,<br>
lattex, sisal and groundnuts. Therefore,<br>
unequal exchange benefited Europe,<br>
but impoverished Africa.<br>
<b>Moreover, maritime technology was</b><br>
another important reason for Europe<br>
to be far ahead of Africa. Investment<br>
in science and technology in Europe<br>
continued in the subsequent centuries.<br>
<b>It contributed to the development of</b><br>
industries. With the development of<br>
maritime technology, the European<br>

<b>Impact of the development gap</b><br>

below.<br>

necessary for the development of<br>
<b>African people. Africa lost its labour</b><br>

was invested in European industries,<br>

the Industrial Revolution. Africa gave<br>
<b>Europe valuable goods like ivory, gold,</b><br>
spices and slave labour, while Europe<br>
imported into Africa manufactured<br>
goods of  low quality such as<br>
gunpowder, alcohol and utensils. With<br>
industrialisation came colonialism,<br>
which underdeveloped Africa further.<br>

<b>African population. Many energetic</b><br>
<b>Africans worked on the European</b><br>

<b>Islands) as slaves. Africa lost its</b><br>

<b>Introduction</b><br>
There are people of African origin in the New World. Such people are linked to<br>
the history of the development of capitalism in Europe. The European discovery<br>
of marine technology facilitated the coming of Europeans to Africa which, in<br>
turn, fuelled the migration of African people to the New World through slave<br>
the trade as an alternative source of labour. In this chapter, you will learn<br>
about the legacy of slavery and the slave trade, Black Solidarity and the Back-<br>
to-Africa Movement. You will also learn about Pan-Africanism and the Civil<br>
Rights Movement in America. The competencies developed will enable you to<br>
appreciate the initiatives taken by African Americans to eradicate the myths of<br>
white supremacy in America and the entire world. It is expected that you will<br>
also value and respect human dignity and identity in the world.<br>
<b>Have you ever thought of living outside your country?</b><br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Three</b><br>
<b>People of African origin in the New World</b><br>
<b>Background to the people of African</b><br>
origin in the New World<br>
<b>The term New World is used to refer to</b><br>
<b>North and South America and the West</b><br>
<b>Indies (the Caribbean Islands). Most</b><br>
people of African origin in the New<br>
<b>World are descendants of the former</b><br>
slaves who were forcefully taken<br>
from Africa by Europeans and sent<br>
to the Americas to work in mines and<br>
industries, and on plantations between<br>
the 15th and 19th centuries. Most of<br>
the slaves were taken from West and<br>
<b>Central Africa, especially Senegal,</b><br>
<b>Nigeria, Benin, Guinea-Bissau, Mali,</b><br>
<b>Angola, the Democratic Republic</b><br>
of Congo and Gabon. The slave<br>
trade is directly associated with the<br>
development of merchant capitalism<br>
or mercantilism. Interestingly, slavery<br>
had its contribution to the development<br>
of capitalism which, in turn, brought<br>
the slave trade to an end.<br>

<b>The concepts of slavery and</b><br>
slave trade<br>
<b>Slavery is a situation in which a person</b><br>
is owned and treated by another as an<br>
object of labour. It is an ancient form<br>
of exploitation and oppression. Slaves<br>
were deprived of their rights as they<br>
were bought and sold like commodities.<br>
<b>Sometimes, slave masters could kill</b><br>
their slaves without anyone questioning<br>
them. Slaves owned nothing, except<br>
their labour. In America, African slaves<br>
were intensively exploited as they<br>
worked for long hours on plantations<br>
and in mining centres.<br>
<b>The slave trade was the process of</b><br>
capturing, selling and buying human<br>
beings as a commodity. The Atlantic-<br>
<b>Slave Trade is an example of what the</b><br>
slave trade was. The main participants<br>
in the slave trade were Europeans, Arabs<br>
and some African rulers.<br>
<b>Historically, the Portuguese were the</b><br>
first to take slaves from West Africa,<br>
whereas Antao Goncalves was the<br>
first Portuguese sailor to buy Africans<br>
along the western coast of Africa in<br>
1441. The trade gained momentum<br>
in the early 16th century when many<br>
slaves were shipped to the New World,<br>
especially the West Indies. During the<br>
voyages slaves suffered humiliation<br>
and deprivation, and most of them<br>
died. As a result, there was a serious<br>
decline in Africa’s population in Africa.<br>
<b>For example, it was estimated that the</b><br>
population of the whole of Savannah<br>
and the Horn of Africa declined by about<br>
10 to 15 per cent, which was equivalent<br>
to over three million people. Further<br>
estimates show that the population of<br>
sub-Saharan Africa would have been<br>
nearly one hundred million instead of<br>
fifty million by 1850.<br>
<b>Think of the social system in</b><br>
your community that contains<br>
elements of slavery. Does it<br>
resemble the one practised by<br>
Europeans? Show how.<br>
<b>Origins of slavery and the slave trade</b><br>
<b>Slavery and the slave trade can be</b><br>
traced to as far back as the 14th century<br>
when Europe was disturbed by the<br>
bubonic plague, famine and epidemic<br>
diseases.<br>
<b>These</b><br>
disasters<br>
killed<br>
many people, thereby causing severe<br>
shortages of labour in the agricultural<br>
sector. Some European empires started<br>
looking for slaves in Eastern Europe<br>
and Russia. However, with the rise of<br>
<b>Sultan Mohamed II of the Ottoman</b><br>
<b>Empire (1451-1481) whose regime</b><br>
disrupted the slave trade in Europe,<br>
<b>European slave traders could no</b><br>
longer access sources of slaves within<br>
<b>Europe. Africa was, therefore, the only</b><br>
continent that could best serve that<br>
purpose. As a result, Western European<br>
people turned to Africa for slaves. The<br>
slave trade flourished owing to, among<br>
other factors, the development of<br>
marine technology, which transformed<br>
navigation technology.<br>

<b>At the beginning of the 15th century,</b><br>
<b>European slave-trading activities were</b><br>
generally confined to along the West<br>
<b>African coast. The Portuguese and</b><br>
<b>African local chiefs acted as the key</b><br>
actors. As early as the 15th century,<br>
the Portuguese had established sugar<br>
plantations in São Tomé and Principe<br>
which relied on the African slave labour<br>
obtained from along the coast of West<br>
<b>Africa. Slaves started to be transported</b><br>
from West Africa to America after<br>
the establishment of similar sugar<br>
plantations in the West Indies or the<br>
<b>Caribbean Islands, and silver mines in</b><br>
South America.<br>
<b>There were various reasons for the</b><br>
<b>Europeans’ preference for African</b><br>
slave labour. The following are some<br>
of them:<br>
(a)	 The Red Indians’ immunity to<br>
tropical diseases such as smallpox<br>
and malaria was weaker than that<br>
of Africans;<br>
(b)	 Europeans’ labour was proved to<br>
be unreliable owing to its limited<br>
supply and its high mobility. For<br>
example, European labourers such<br>
as indentured labourers worked<br>
on contract terms, which meant<br>
that they were free to leave their<br>
employers when the contracts<br>
expired;<br>
(c)	 Red Indians could escape from the<br>
slave masters because they were<br>
familiar with the environment,<br>
while Africans tended to remain<br>
in their locality because they<br>
did not know much about the<br>
environment;<br>
(d)	 African slaves were financially<br>
cheaper than European slaves and<br>
indentured labourers. European-<br>
indentured labourers were to be<br>
paid at the end of their contracts,<br>
usually in the form of a piece of<br>
land or a precious metal (gold<br>
and silver). Moreover, European<br>
slaves were criminals who were<br>
serving<br>
their<br>
sentences<br>
and,<br>
therefore, they were to be released<br>
at the end of their sentences, while<br>
<b>African slaves were tied to their</b><br>
masters indefinitely; and<br>
(e)	 Europeans<br>
preferred<br>
<b>African</b><br>
slaves because West Africa was<br>
nearer to America; hence, it was<br>
easy to transport slaves across the<br>
Atlantic Ocean to America.<br>
<b>The rise of the Trans-Atlantic Slave</b><br>
<b>Trade</b><br>
<b>The Trans-Atlantic Slave Trade started</b><br>
following the activities of European<br>
merchants and geographers who saw<br>
<b>Africa and America around the 15th</b><br>
century. The Italian sailor and explorer<br>
<b>Christopher Columbus, who was working</b><br>
for Queen Isabela of Spain, discovered<br>
the New World (America) in 1492. His<br>
discovery prompted the Europeans<br>
to open up various economic projects<br>
such as mines, plantations and trade in<br>

<b>America. These projects were a precursor</b><br>
to the development of mercantilism. The<br>
<b>Portuguese established themselves in Brazil,</b><br>
the Spanish settled in various parts of<br>
<b>South, Central and North America, while</b><br>
the English, the Dutch and the French<br>
settled in the Caribbean Islands and North<br>
<b>America. European activities in these</b><br>
areas demanded massive labour power.<br>
<b>The Trans-Atlantic Slave Trade, which</b><br>
is also called the Triangular Slave Trade<br>
or the great circuit, involved three<br>
continents, namely Africa, America<br>
and Europe.  The items of trade from<br>
<b>Africa included slaves, gold, ivory,</b><br>
timber, dye-hoods, gum, beeswax,<br>
animal skins and spices. America<br>
supplied raw materials like tobacco,<br>
cotton and sugarcane to European<br>
industries. Europe provided managerial<br>
organisation and manufactured goods.<br>
<b>The major participants in this trade</b><br>
were African local chiefs, the Dutch,<br>
the English, the Portuguese as well<br>
as the French. In West Africa, slaves<br>
came from the areas stretching from<br>
the Gold Coast (Ghana) to Cameroon.<br>
<b>Other slaves were obtained from as far</b><br>
interior as Congo and Angola. Slaves<br>
were transported from the interior to<br>
the coastal centres or ports. Among<br>
these famous ports are Axim, Elmina,<br>
<b>Bissau, Whydah (Benin), Lagos, Bonny</b><br>
and Old Calabar. Figure 3.1 shows the<br>
interconnection of the Triangular Trade<br>
between Europe, Africa and the New<br>
World.<br>

<b>The emergence of African</b><br>
<b>Americans in America</b><br>
<b>As explained earlier, the people of</b><br>
<b>African origin in the New World were</b><br>
descendants of ex-slaves.<br>
<b>The shipping of Africans to the New</b><br>
<b>World began early in the 16thcentury and</b><br>
continued until the last quarter of the<br>
19th century. It should be noted that, at<br>
the beginning, the European capitalists<br>
depended on indentured labourers,<br>
criminals, political refugees, religious<br>
non-conformists and wanderers. The<br>
<b>Europeans resorted to using Africans</b><br>
because of their advantage. They could<br>
not keep European slave labourers<br>
because it was difficult to control them.<br>
<b>For example, they could no longer</b><br>
hire their fellow Europeans when the<br>
contracts expired. Europeans were<br>
entitled to land as part of their working<br>
contract, but Africans were denied this<br>
right. For example, by 1645 there were<br>
11200 white farmers owning 5680<br>
<b>African slaves. Later in 1667, these</b><br>
small white farmers were replaced by<br>
only 745 big capitalist farmers owning<br>
8203 African slaves on the Barbados<br>
Island.<br>

(a)  In pairs, conduct a library research<br>
to distinguish between slavery<br>

<b>Europeans?</b><br>

of the class.<br>
<b>Africans’ life in the New World</b><br>
<b>Millions of Africans who were shipped</b><br>
to America starting from the early<br>
16th century faced various problems,<br>
especially after the Virginia Assembly<br>
of 1619. The assembly passed a<br>
resolution that black servants in the New<br>
<b>World should be slaves forever. That</b><br>
meant that they had no right to marry<br>
and own property, or they did not have<br>
any civil rights. The resolution made<br>
<b>Africans face serious economic, social</b><br>
and political problems, as explained in<br>
the following section.<br>
<b>Political problems</b><br>
<b>The people of African origin in the</b><br>
<b>New World faced various problems</b><br>
related to political exclusion. They<br>
were denied the right to vote or to be<br>
voted for. This denial was legalised<br>
by the resolutions of the assembly.<br>
<b>Africans did not have the right to vote</b><br>
for people who would represent their<br>
interests. African Americans were<br>
subjected to harsh conditions which<br>
limited their opportunity to obtain<br>
their political rights. In order for one<br>
to vote, one had to pass a literacy<br>
test, which most Africans could not<br>
pass, since they did not attend school<br>
and, therefore, they could not read<br>
and write. This trick was used to put<br>
<b>African Americans in a disadvantaged</b><br>
position and the situation did not<br>
change even after the attainment of<br>
independence. It appeared, therefore,<br>
that the anti-colonial campaigns and<br>

the solidarity which accompanied the<br>
wars of independence did not mean<br>
equality between whites and blacks.<br>
<b>Furthermore, African Americans were</b><br>
deprived of the right to join trade<br>
unions. It was feared that, through<br>
trade unions, African Americans would<br>
challenge the capitalist system. They<br>
would demand higher wages, better<br>
working conditions and retirement<br>
benefits.<br>
<b>However, on 1st January 1863, President</b><br>
<b>Abraham Lincoln initiated movements</b><br>
to abolish slavery. From there, the status<br>
and respect for African Americans were<br>
somehow elevated. The assassination<br>
of President Abraham Lincoln on<br>
14th April 1865 led to the increase in<br>
political discrimination against African<br>
<b>Americans. They were not allowed</b><br>
to assemble or hold any meetings.<br>
<b>This was done to prohibit them from</b><br>
organising liberation movements in the<br>
form of strikes and demonstrations.<br>
<b>Social problems</b><br>
<b>Having lost their language, customs,</b><br>
traditions and homeland as a result of<br>
the slave trade, in the diaspora Africans<br>
were subjected to acts of enslavement,<br>
persecution<br>
as<br>
well<br>
as<br>
racial<br>
discrimination. On the plantations and<br>
in the mines, for example, African<br>
slaves were treated badly and were<br>
oppressed compared to their European<br>
counterparts. Laws that were passed<br>
in various states like Virginia and<br>
<b>Maryland were aimed at oppressing</b><br>
Africans socially.<br>
<b>African Americans were also racially</b><br>
segregated in the provision of public<br>
services such as health, electricity, water<br>
and transport. For example, American<br>
bus services separated African seats<br>
from European ones. This was evident<br>
in the second half of the 20th century.<br>
<b>Whenever African Americans violated</b><br>
this racial arrangement, they were<br>
arrested. For example, Rosa Park, a<br>
42-year-old woman, was arrested after<br>
refusing to follow the bus driver’s order<br>
to leave a seat for the white person who<br>
had missed a seat in the first ten seats,<br>
which had been reserved for whites.<br>
<b>She was arrested on 1st December 1955</b><br>
in Montgomery, Alabama. Similar<br>
racial arrangements were evident in<br>
churches, where wooden benches for<br>
<b>African Americans were separated</b><br>
from those of White Americans. As a<br>
result, African Americans decided to<br>
start their own churches.<br>
<b>African Americans were segregated</b><br>
in schools. They were only allowed to<br>
join schools designed and reserved for<br>
them. These institutions were called<br>
“slaves’ schools”. For instance, in<br>
1957, nine African American students,<br>
who had been selected to join Little<br>
<b>Rock Central High School, were chased</b><br>
away by the head of the school, who<br>
said that they could cause violence and<br>
bloodshed if they were allowed to join<br>
the school.<br>

<b>Economic problems</b><br>
<b>African Americans were deprived of</b><br>
the right to own land. They had no right<br>
to access decent employment or any<br>
supervisory and administrative work.<br>
All these were reserved for whites.<br>
<b>African Americans were supposed to</b><br>
do manual work like cleaning. African<br>
<b>Americans were not allowed to own</b><br>
any property, but White  Americans<br>
had the right to own the major means<br>
of production like land, companies and<br>
financial institutions such as banks. As a<br>
consequence, African Americans were<br>
relegated to low-paid jobs. Therefore,<br>
they were generally paid low wages.<br>
<b>Exercise 3.1</b><br>
<b>Show why the people of African</b><br>
origin in the New World faced<br>
socio-economic and political<br>
problems in the period between<br>
the 17th century and 19th century.<br>
<b>Liberation struggles by African</b><br>
<b>Americans</b><br>
<b>African Americans resisted capitalist</b><br>
exploitation and this came to be known<br>
as Black American Nationalism. Their<br>
struggle started with the Virginia<br>
<b>Assembly of 1619 and was accelerated</b><br>
by the American war of independence<br>
between 1775 and 1783. They adopted<br>
different forms of struggle.<br>
<b>The forms of struggle they adopted</b><br>
ranged<br>
from<br>
small-to<br>
large-scale<br>
struggles. Small-scale struggles included<br>
escape; slaves escaped from their<br>
slave masters and hid in the forests,<br>
mountains and swampy areas. This<br>
form<br>
of<br>
struggle<br>
also<br>
involved<br>
demonstrations. African Americans<br>
destroyed equipment like hoes, rakes<br>
and machines; burnt plantations; killed<br>
slave masters; or committed suicide.<br>
<b>The large-scale struggles included mass</b><br>
participation in the campaigns to abolish<br>
slavery and the slave trade. African<br>
<b>American intellectuals, activists and</b><br>
international communities spearheaded<br>
the struggle. Although Great Britain<br>
spearheaded the abolition of the slave<br>
trade and consequently banned the trade<br>
in 1807, the shipment of African slaves<br>
to Brazil and Cuba continued until the<br>
1860s. Thus, the movements to abolish<br>
the slave trade became part of African<br>
<b>Americans’ struggles in the United States</b><br>
of America. The anti-slavery movements<br>
were well intensified during the American<br>
civil wars, which began in 1861 and<br>
ended in 1865. At the beginning, the<br>
anti-slavery coalitions used non-violent<br>
means to liberate African Americans from<br>
capitalist exploitation. The anti-slavery<br>
movements were supported by African<br>
<b>American leaders or activists, namely</b><br>
<b>Frederick Douglass, Booker Taliaferro</b><br>
<b>Washington, Marcus Aurelius Garvey,</b><br>
<b>Martin Luther King Junior, Malcom Little</b><br>
<b>Early (popularly known as Malcom X),</b><br>
<b>Edward Wilmot Blyden and William Du</b><br>
<b>Bois, whose contributions are explained</b><br>
below.<br>

(a)	 Frederick Douglass (1818-1895)<br>
<b>Douglass was an African American</b><br>
social reformer, abolitionist, orator,<br>
writer and statesman. He was born as<br>
a slave and worked in Maryland as a<br>
slave, before he escaped and became<br>
a national leader of the abolitionist<br>
movement in Massachusetts and New<br>
<b>York. Douglass concentrated his efforts</b><br>
on programmes meant for complete<br>
emancipation of African Americans.<br>
<b>He was an integrationist who wanted</b><br>
<b>African Americans to be integrated</b><br>
into American society politically and<br>
economically. After the proclamation<br>
of the abolition of slavery in the<br>
<b>USA, Douglass struggled for political</b><br>
equality between the people of African<br>
origin and others. His efforts bore some<br>
fruit as a few African Americans got<br>
voting rights. A few African Americans<br>
representatives were also elected to<br>
various state legislatures. For example,<br>
some African Americans were elected<br>
as congressmen and one was promoted<br>
to the position of senator. However,<br>
this political success was short-lived<br>
because in the 1880s and 1890s the<br>
whites in the south became reactionary.<br>
(b)    Booker Taliaferro Washington<br>
(1856-1915)<br>
<b>Washington was an African American</b><br>
educator, author and orator. He was<br>
born into slavery and rose to become<br>
a leading African intellectual of<br>
the 19th century. He was also the<br>
founder of the Tuskegee Normal and<br>
<b>Industrial Institute (now the Tuskegee</b><br>
<b>University) in 1881 and the National</b><br>
<b>Negro Business League, two years</b><br>
later. He also served as adviser to<br>
several presidents of the United States.<br>
<b>Between 1890 and 1915, Washington</b><br>
was the most dominant leader in<br>
the African American community.<br>
<b>Originally, he was a slave in the south,</b><br>
but later fought for his education to the<br>
point of becoming an activist, fighting<br>
for the rights of African Americans.<br>
<b>He advocated economic progress</b><br>
through the attainment of industrial<br>
vocational skills. He was convinced<br>
that the black people had first of all to<br>
acquire economic power as a prelude<br>
to acquiring political rights. He also<br>
believed that this could be achieved<br>
through the provision of industrial<br>
education.<br>
(c)<br>
<b>William Du Bois (1868-1963)</b><br>
<b>Du Bois was a prominent African</b><br>
<b>American sociologist, historian, author</b><br>
and editor. He was an important leader<br>
in the African Americans’ struggles in<br>
the USA during the first half of the 20th<br>
century. He wanted African Americans<br>
to attain political power, which was<br>
the first step towards the integration<br>
into American society. He formed an<br>
organisation known as the National<br>
<b>Association for the Advancement</b><br>
of the Coloured People. He wanted<br>
<b>African Americans to struggle for</b><br>
their civil rights and to be part and<br>

(d)  Marcus Mosiah Aurelius<br>
<b>Garvey (1887-1940)</b><br>
<b>He was a Jamaican from Black</b><br>
<b>Nationalist and leader of the Pan-</b><br>
<b>Africanism Movement, which sought</b><br>
to unify and connect the people of<br>
<b>African descent worldwide. Garvey</b><br>
attended school in Jamaica until he was<br>
15 when he left St. Ann Bay and went<br>
to Kingston, where he worked as an<br>
apprentice in a print shop. He did not<br>
advocate the integration of black people<br>
into American society. He wished to<br>
build a separate culture and power<br>
for the black people worldwide. His<br>
desire to establish a separate identity<br>
and culture for African Americans<br>
explains his adoption of the slogan<br>
“Back to Africa”. Garvey believed<br>
that Africa was black people’s original<br>
homeland and thus an ideal place for<br>
their emigration. His organisation,<br>
the Universal Negro Improvement<br>
<b>Association (UNIA), was formed in</b><br>
1916 and lasted until 1927. Like Du<br>
<b>Bois, Marcus fought so that black people</b><br>
could achieve political and economic<br>
prospects. He set up negro economic<br>
enterprises, including negro factories<br>
and a shipping line known as the Black<br>
<b>Star Line for conducting trade between</b><br>
<b>North America and West Africa. Marcus</b><br>
<b>Garvey was jailed in 1923 after he was</b><br>
found guilty of forgery. In 1925 he was<br>
released, after being pardoned by the<br>
president of the USA. He was deported<br>
to Jamaica in 1927.<br>

was primarily active in West Africa.<br>
<b>Having felt the impact of racial</b><br>
discrimination, he joined the waves<br>
of black immigrants who migrated to<br>
<b>Liberia in 1850. He dignified African</b><br>
personality and condemned white<br>

their ability to manage themselves.<br>
<b>He published on Pan-Africanism and</b><br>

<b>Riots, revolutions and demonstrations</b><br>
constituted another category of large-<br>
scale struggles by African Americans.<br>
<b>Riots against slavery happened in the</b><br>
<b>USA and in other parts of the New</b><br>
<b>World. In Brazil, for instance, slaves</b><br>
revolted against their slave masters and<br>
ended up establishing their own state<br>
called the Palmares, an African state.<br>
<b>Palmers lasted for one hundred years</b><br>
before the Portuguese slave masters<br>
destroyed it. Similarly, in Jamaica<br>
frequent slave revolts were reported<br>
during the 18th and 19th centuries.<br>
<b>The revolts taught the European slave</b><br>
masters that slavery was increasingly<br>
becoming unsafe and expensive to<br>
maintain. For instance, a group of slaves<br>
known as the Maroons revolted against<br>
their British masters. They established<br>
and maintained their own self-governing<br>
farming community before they were<br>
defeated by their masters. However, the<br>
biggest event in the form of a revolution<br>
happened in Haiti. French slave masters<br>
owned sugarcane plantations on the<br>
<b>Saint-Dominique</b><br>
<b>Island,</b><br>
currently<br>
known as Haiti.<br>
<b>In Haiti, Toussaint L’ouverture led</b><br>
his fellow slaves to revolt against the<br>
<b>French masters in 1791. Many French</b><br>
slave masters were killed by the slaves.<br>
<b>These frequent revolts worried Britain</b><br>
and France, which organised navies to<br>
suppress them in 1803. Surprisingly,<br>
their navies were defeated by the<br>
angry slaves. Consequently, the slaves<br>
established their independent republic<br>
of Haiti. This incident was referred to<br>
as the Haitian Revolution. As a result,<br>
government leaders and bankers in<br>
<b>France and Britain gradually stopped</b><br>
supporting slavery and the slave trade.<br>
<b>This was a major achievement for the</b><br>
slaves in the New World.<br>
<b>In</b><br>
addition,<br>
demonstrations<br>
were<br>
staged<br>
by<br>
<b>African</b><br>
Americans.<br>
<b>For example, in 1955, there were</b><br>
demonstrations in Montgomery, the<br>

<b>Why</b><br>

<b>African</b><br>
<b>Americans</b><br>
also<br>
used<br>
conferences as a form of struggle.<br>
<b>Conferences were held to create</b><br>

<b>Furthermore, African Americans used</b><br>

“We shall overcome” by Martin Luther<br>
and “It is better to die than to live as<br>
slaves” by Frederick Douglass. They<br>
fostered black solidarity.<br>
<b>Another form of struggle was the</b><br>

of<br>
<b>African</b><br>
<b>American</b><br>

independent<br>
black<br>
denomination<br>
called African Methodist Episcopal<br>
<b>Church (AME), which was established</b><br>
in Philadelphia, and the African<br>
Episcopal Methodist Zion (AMEZ).<br>
<b>These independent churches enabled</b><br>
the African Americans to get rid of<br>
racial oppression.<br>
<b>Moreover, African Americans used</b><br>
secret codes, languages and rituals in<br>
their struggle against slavery and other<br>
forms of oppression. For example,<br>
testimonies from oral traditions confirm<br>
that the earliest form of struggle was<br>
called the Underground Railroad Quilt<br>
<b>Code, which slaves used to navigate</b><br>
the Underground Railroad. In places<br>
of work, they also formulated some<br>

and phrases which enabled them to<br>
communicate in ways their white<br>
masters could not understand. Quilts<br>
had patterns labelled “wagon wheel,”<br>
“tumbling blocks,” and “bear’s paw”.<br>
<b>These carried secret messages meant</b><br>
for the slaves.<br>

rifle<br>

<b>The movement demanded immediate</b><br>
banning of the slave trade. In due<br>
course, the European capitalists in<br>
<b>Northern America supported the cause</b><br>
of the people of African origin. This<br>

<b>On</b><br>

<b>President Abraham Lincoln. The law</b><br>
demanded abolition of slavery and<br>

<b>The law declared slavery and the slave</b><br>

<b>Another attempt was the introduction</b><br>

law was passed by the American<br>
<b>Congress during the Civil War. The law</b><br>

whose economy solely depended on<br>
slave labour, and the capitalists in the<br>
north who ran industries and needed<br>
cheap labour. Eventually, the clash<br>
of interests led to the outbreak of the<br>
American Civil War of 1861-1865.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
abolitionists<br>
formed<br>
the<br>
<b>Emancipation League in 1861. It drew</b><br>
membership from both the industrialists<br>
in the north and the slaves in the south.<br>
<b>The league called for a mass uprising</b><br>
of slaves in the south. The league was<br>
aimed at bringing about emancipation<br>
throughout the USA by supporting the<br>
war on slavery until it put to an end the<br>
interstate slave trade and protected the<br>
fugitive slaves from kidnappers, who<br>
wanted to take them back to bondage.<br>
<b>During the war, the economy of the</b><br>
<b>USA declined drastically as agricultural</b><br>
and industrial production fell and trade<br>
was disrupted.<br>
<b>These are some of the problems that</b><br>
forced the government to carry out<br>
political reforms in favour of the people<br>
of African origin. Thus, as pointed out<br>
earlier, President Abraham Lincoln<br>
issued the Emancipation Decree in<br>
1862. The decree abolished slavery and<br>
the slave trade throughout the USA.<br>
<b>An anti-slavery coalition was formed</b><br>
by African Americans and White<br>
<b>Americans. Some African Americans</b><br>
started to move to the north and secure<br>
jobs in factories. Those who remained<br>
in the south formed the main base of<br>
the anti-secessionists. They boycotted<br>
work on the plantations and burnt the<br>
property and farms belonging to their<br>
masters. Thousands of them fled to the<br>
north. About 340,000 blacks from the<br>
south joined the Union Army.<br>

the<br>

of<br>

<b>The assassination of President Lincoln</b><br>
was plotted by White American planter<br>

<b>Andrew Johnson, a former slaveholder,</b><br>

in the south revenged bitterly on the<br>

many people of African origin. Others<br>

off and skulls fractured by the secret<br>
organisation known as the Ku-Klux<br>
<b>Klan, formed by young whites in the</b><br>
south.<br>
<b>The introduction of “Black Codes”</b><br>
and the formation of the Ku-Klux Klan<br>
during the presidency of Andrew<br>
<b>Johnson prevented African Americans</b><br>
from emancipating themselves from<br>
the institution of slavery, although the<br>
black people never gave up. They began<br>
to devise new methods of the struggle<br>
for freedom and rights. Movements<br>
were organised under the name of<br>
<b>Black Solidarity. The champions of</b><br>
<b>Black Solidarity were Marcus Mosiah</b><br>
Garvey and Martin Delaney Luther.<br>
<b>Black Solidarity and the Back-</b><br>
to-Africa Movement<br>
<b>Black Solidarity refers to the spirit of</b><br>
cooperation and togetherness among<br>
the oppressed and exploited people<br>
of African origin in America. These<br>
people were united by common forms<br>
of capitalist exploitation. A collective<br>
black identity was essential in the<br>
struggle against racial oppression.<br>
<b>Back-to-Africa Movement</b><br>
<b>The Back-to-Africa Movement was</b><br>
<b>African Americans’ collective movement</b><br>
for liberating themselves from American<br>
oppression, discrimination, humiliation<br>
and exploitation. It encouraged them<br>
to go back to their original homeland.<br>
<b>The emigration of African Americans</b><br>
to Africa was considered a solution to<br>
the problems they were facing in the<br>
diaspora.<br>

<b>The</b><br>
<b>Back-to-Africa</b><br>
<b>Movement</b><br>
mobilised<br>
thousands<br>
of<br>
<b>African</b><br>
<b>Americans who wished to go back to</b><br>
Africa, beginning in the late 1800s.<br>
<b>In the 1880s and 1890s, a substantial</b><br>
number of African Americans migrated<br>
to Liberia, although such migrations<br>
started as early as 1820. The migration<br>
was organised by the American<br>
<b>Colonisation Society (ACS), which</b><br>
was based in Washington DC. The<br>
<b>ACS founded the Republic of Liberia</b><br>
in 1847. Most African American ex-<br>
slaves migrated to Liberia. It was<br>
<b>Marcus Garvey who founded the The</b><br>
Back to Africa Movement in 1820.<br>
<b>He also formed the Universal Negro</b><br>
<b>Improvement</b><br>
<b>Association</b><br>
<b>(UNIA)</b><br>
and the African Communities Imperial<br>
<b>League (ACIL). Figure 3.3 shows a</b><br>
picture of Marcus Garvey.<br>
<b>Figure 3.3: Marcus Garvey</b><br>
<b>Garvey believed that the blacks in the</b><br>
<b>New World could find a true home in</b><br>
<b>Africa. To him, the duty of the people</b><br>
of African origin in the New World<br>
was to find ways to return to Africa. In<br>
<b>Africa, they could work together with</b><br>
their fellow Africans and build a free<br>
and mighty nation. The Back-to-Africa<br>
<b>Movement was generally aimed at</b><br>
establishing colonies for the ex-slaves<br>
from America.<br>
<b>The Back-to-Africa Movement gained</b><br>
momentum with the formation of the<br>
<b>American Society for Colonising the</b><br>
<b>Free People of Colour or American</b><br>
<b>Colonisation Society in 1816. The first</b><br>
attempt to take slaves to Africa was<br>
made in 1820 when some ex-slaves<br>
were taken to Sierra Leone. However,<br>
they died of tropical diseases.<br>
<b>Objectives of the Back-to-Africa</b><br>
<b>Movement</b><br>
<b>The movement intended to promote</b><br>
a sense of cooperation among black<br>
people all over the world. The Back-<br>
to-Africa Movement believed that all<br>
the black people in the New World and<br>
<b>Africa were one; as such, they should</b><br>
unite and fight for their rights. The idea<br>
was that the Africans in the diaspora<br>
should support their fellow Africans<br>
to fight for independence. In order to<br>
build a sense of unity among Africans,<br>
<b>Garvey established some connection</b><br>
with some political leaders in Africa<br>
such as Abd el-Karim of Morocco<br>
and established a parliament in the<br>
form of an international convention,<br>
where members from different parts<br>

emphasised the need to utilise Africans<br>
for the benefit of African people.<br>
<b>Moreover, the movement sought to</b><br>
bring together all black people from<br>
the Caribbean Islands, America and<br>
<b>Europe, who shared a common history,</b><br>
race and dignity. The African race<br>
should be respected by people all over<br>
the world. All black people faced the<br>
same problems caused by capitalist<br>
exploitation and, to resolve them, they<br>
had to work together.<br>
<b>Garvey emphasised much separate</b><br>

happen if Africans struggled for<br>
economic and political sovereignty.<br>
<b>The whole idea was, therefore, to</b><br>

bondage of slavery.<br>
<b>The Back-to-Africa Movement also</b><br>
sought to preserve the culture and<br>
beliefs of Africans, who had suffered<br>
oppression in America. African culture<br>
was considered worthy and, as such,<br>
deserved to be preserved. Therefore,<br>
the Back-to-Africa Movement was<br>
thought to be the best way of protecting<br>
African culture.<br>
<b>The</b><br>

worked<br>
hard<br>
to<br>

that African people had been subjected<br>
to. In an effort to liberate the people<br>
from racial oppression, the leaders<br>
frequently called on African Americans<br>
to be more unified for social change.<br>
<b>The philosophy of Black Solidarity led</b><br>
to the formation of such movements<br>
as the Back-to-Africa Movement, the<br>
<b>Pan-Africanist Movement as well as</b><br>
the Civil Rights Movement. These<br>
movements are explained in the<br>
subsequent section<br>
<b>The success of the Back-to-Africa</b><br>
<b>Movement</b><br>
<b>The movement succeeded in repatriating</b><br>
some of the African Americans who<br>
were willing to go back to Africa. They<br>
were transported by the Black Star<br>
<b>Shipping Company, which belonged to</b><br>
<b>Marcus Garvey. They settled in Liberia</b><br>
and Sierra Leone. They were given<br>
fertile land to cultivate and to establish<br>
new settlements on. By 1847, 3000 ex-<br>
slaves had been shipped to Liberia.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
<b>Back-to-Africa</b><br>
<b>Movement</b><br>
inculcated in Africans a sense of unity<br>
and love through slogans like “Africa<br>
for Africans”. This made Africans feel<br>
that they came from the same ancestor,<br>
so they had to help each other in their<br>
struggle for equality. For example,<br>
through an international convention<br>
that was initiated by Marcus Garvey,<br>
it was possible to establish a spirit of<br>
brotherhood that assisted in promoting<br>
independent African nations.<br>
<b>The movement improved the economic</b><br>
conditions of African Americans.<br>

<b>Problems which faced the</b><br>
<b>Back-to-Africa Movement</b><br>
<b>In 1923, Marcus Garvey was jailed</b><br>
for five years, but he was pardoned in<br>
1925. Later in 1927, he was deported to<br>
<b>Jamaica. Since Garvey was the initiator</b><br>
of the movement, his absence caused<br>
fear in his fellow African Americans,<br>
who found it very difficult to sustain the<br>
movement without him. Moreover, he<br>
was the main sponsor of the movement.<br>
<b>As a result, the people who were left</b><br>
behind could not afford the expenses of<br>
running the movement. The shipping<br>
company (Black Star) needed money<br>
to do its activities like supplying basic<br>
needs like food and other necessities.<br>
<b>Eventually, the movement became</b><br>
very weak.<br>
<b>Worse still, the colonial governments</b><br>
in Africa refused to accept the African<br>
<b>Americans from America, fearing</b><br>
that they might initiate anti-colonial<br>
movements on the continent. The new<br>
arrivals found themselves in a very<br>
precarious situation as they were now<br>
also unwanted in what they believed<br>
to be their original home. Only two<br>
countries in Africa (Liberia and Sierra<br>
<b>Leone) received immigrants from</b><br>
America.<br>
<b>There</b><br>
was<br>
also<br>
an<br>
ideological<br>
difference between Marcus Garvey and<br>
<b>William Du Bois on matters concerning</b><br>
the Back-to-Africa Movement. This<br>
caused confusion among other African<br>
<b>Americans,</b><br>
who<br>
eventually<br>
got<br>
divided. Garvey believed that, in order<br>
for African Americans to be free, they<br>
needed to go back to their motherland<br>
because America was a source of the<br>
problems they were facing. It was<br>
so because America was not their<br>
homeland. However, Du Bois believed<br>
that, if Africans wanted to fight for<br>
their rights, they had to fight while in<br>
<b>America. He once said, “If it happens</b><br>
that Africans should return to Africa,<br>
<b>I will be the last.” These two differing</b><br>
views left African Americans divided<br>
on how to go about fighting for their<br>
rights.<br>

to the development of American<br>

<b>Having seen the potential of African</b><br>
