<b>Exercise 3.3</b><br>
<b>Explain why the Back-to-Africa</b><br>
<b>Movement was important to</b><br>
African Americans.<br>
<b>Pan-Africanism</b><br>
<b>Pan-Africanism is the belief that</b><br>
<b>African people have much in common,</b><br>
something that deserves notice and<br>
even celebration. The belief in Pan-<br>
<b>Africanism is based on the uniqueness</b><br>
and spiritual unity of the people of<br>
<b>African origin. Pan-Africanism had its</b><br>
roots outside Africa as it was started<br>
by the people of African origin in the<br>
<b>New World. Thus, Pan-Africanism is a</b><br>
worldwide movement that encouraged<br>
and strengthened the solidarity of all<br>
black people. The movement brought<br>
<b>Africans together so that they could</b><br>
fight against the evils of imperialism.<br>
<b>Pan-Africanism</b><br>
was<br>
founded<br>
by<br>
<b>Edward Wilmot Blyden and later</b><br>
strengthened<br>
by<br>
<b>Henry</b><br>
<b>Sylvester</b><br>
<b>Williams (1869-1911). The latter was</b><br>
a lawyer from Trinidad. He convened<br>
the first Pan-African Conference in<br>
<b>London in 1900. The conference was</b><br>
attended by a few representatives from<br>
<b>Africa and a big number of delegates</b><br>
from America and the West Indies.<br>
<b>It was also attended by students like</b><br>
<b>Dr Du Bois, who later became a</b><br>
famous Pan-Africanist. In 1910, he<br>
formed the National Association for<br>
the Advancement of Coloured People<br>
(NAACP), through which he advocated<br>
Pan-Africanism.<br>
<b>Objectives of Pan-Africanism</b><br>
<b>The objectives of Pan-Africanism</b><br>
were to spread democracy all over<br>
the world and to lead struggles on<br>

how Africans could regain freedom<br>
and dignity worldwide. As a result<br>
of the triangular trade and slavery,<br>
<b>Africans were scattered in many</b><br>
parts of the world. Moreover, they<br>
faced several economic, political and<br>
social problems. To overcome the<br>
problems, Africans wanted to form an<br>
organisation that would be a hub for<br>
coordinating and uniting Africans from<br>
different places.<br>
<b>Pan-Africanism started as a movement</b><br>
geared towards achieving African<br>
dignity and freedom in the New World,<br>
but later turned out to be a worldwide<br>
movement that protested against all<br>
forms of imperialist domination and<br>
oppression. The major objective of<br>
<b>Pan-Africanism was, therefore, to</b><br>
liberate Africans from imperialism.<br>
<b>This would be achieved by establishing</b><br>
global black solidarity.<br>
<b>In</b><br>
<b>America,</b><br>
the<br>
<b>Pan-Africanist</b><br>
<b>Movement</b><br>
was<br>
geared<br>
towards<br>
attaining Africans Americans’ rights<br>
and equality. The American Pan-<br>
<b>Africanists were fighting for the total</b><br>
liberation of African people within<br>
and outside America. In Africa, Pan-<br>
<b>Africanism focused on the liberation</b><br>
of African countries. This goal was<br>
made clear during the Manchester Pan-<br>
<b>African Conference of 1945, which</b><br>
called for African solidarity to end<br>
imperial rule. No wonder that Accra<br>
was chosen as the headquarters of<br>
<b>Pan-Africanism after Ghana became</b><br>
independent in 1957. This meant<br>
that, by this time, the Pan-Africanist<br>
activities had virtually shifted to<br>
Africa.<br>
<b>Challenges that faced Pan-Africanism</b><br>
<b>Pan-Africanism encountered the strong</b><br>
hand of Anti-African movements. The<br>
whites in the USA did not want to see<br>
<b>African Americans liberate themselves;</b><br>
thus, they used various forms of<br>
sabotage against their movements. For<br>
instance, the Ku Klux Klan was used to<br>
suppress the movements through the<br>
assassination of the leaders of African<br>
Americans.<br>
There was also a shortage of funds.<br>
<b>Most of the members of Pan-Africanism</b><br>
were too poor to financially support the<br>
movement to carry out activities such<br>
as organising meetings. For example,<br>
the conferences held in Britain, France<br>
and the USA were attended by a few<br>
people because the majority could<br>
not meet transport costs. Lack of<br>
reliable sources of income made the<br>
organisation ineffective.<br>

the following:<br>
<b>The London Conference of 1900</b><br>
<b>This</b><br>
was<br>
the<br>
first<br>
<b>Pan-African</b><br>
conference held in London from 23rd<br>
to 25th July 1900. The conference<br>
was organised by the Trinidadian<br>
lawyer Henry Sylvester Williams. It<br>
was attended by various people of<br>
African origin including Dr Du Bois.<br>
<b>The London conference had various</b><br>
aims, including bringing people of<br>
<b>African origin together. It also acted</b><br>
as a forum for protesting against<br>
colonial  exploitation in Africa. The<br>
conference coined the concept of “Pan-<br>
<b>Africanism”. The conference was</b><br>
attended by Du Bois, who popularised<br>
<b>Pan-Africanism afterwards. It appealed</b><br>
to the British government to protect<br>
the people of African origin from<br>
imperialist exploitation and called<br>
for solidarity among Africans in the<br>
diaspora.<br>
<b>The Paris Congress of 1919</b><br>
<b>This congress was organised by Du Bois</b><br>
and Ida Gibbs Hunt. It was held in Paris<br>
in 1919 just after the First World War,<br>
when African Americans demanded<br>
the Versailles Peace Conference that<br>
was held in France to address Africans’<br>
problems as well. It was attended by 57<br>
delegates. Thus, the congress appealed<br>
to the Versailles Peace Conference<br>
to adopt a policy of mandate for<br>
the former colonies of Germany,<br>
which were Togo, South West Africa<br>
(Namibia) and German East Africa<br>
(Tanganyika, Rwanda and Burundi).<br>
<b>This petition was eventually adopted by</b><br>
those who attended the Versailles Peace<br>
<b>Conference. The resolutions passed</b><br>
included enacting a code of laws for<br>
providing international protection to<br>
the natives of Africa, abolishing slavery<br>
and the slave trade, enforcing universal<br>
education for African children and<br>
upholding the rights of Africans to own<br>
land.<br>
<b>The Brussels Congress of 1921</b><br>
<b>This</b><br>
congress<br>
brought<br>
together<br>
113 delegates. Africa alone had 41<br>
delegates, most of whom came from<br>
the French colonies. At the  congress,<br>
<b>Blaise Diagne of Senegal opposed</b><br>
the resolutions passed by his fellow<br>
<b>Africans. This is because Diagne</b><br>
continued to advocate an African role<br>
in France. In short, he was a beneficiary<br>
of French colonialism in Senegal.<br>
<b>However, the congress, especially</b><br>
<b>Du Bois, harshly condemned him for</b><br>
his lack of commitment to Africans’<br>
interests. Generally, the  congress<br>
condemned racial segregation and<br>
demanded equal rights for all people<br>
of African origin. It also condemned<br>
colonialism and called for self-<br>
determination for  all African nations.<br>

<b>The Lisbon Congress of 1923</b><br>
<b>The congress was held in Lisbon,</b><br>
<b>Portugal, for two purposes. The first</b><br>
was to express its dissatisfaction<br>
with Garveyism. Garvey advocated<br>
the Universal Negro Improvement<br>
<b>Association (UNIA) and the African</b><br>
<b>Communities</b><br>
<b>Imperial</b><br>
<b>League</b><br>

in which he declared himself a<br>

of his fellow Pan-Africanists objected<br>
to his declaration. The second was to<br>
protest against the suppression of Pan-<br>
<b>Africanism. At the end of the congress,</b><br>
various resolutions were reached.<br>
<b>Africans should have a voice in their</b><br>
governments, should own land and<br>
enjoy its resource, should have access<br>
to free education and should have a fair<br>
trial under an established process of<br>
law. Generally, the congress demanded<br>
the black people in the world to be<br>
treated as human beings.<br>
<b>The New York Pan-African Congress</b><br>
of 1927<br>
<b>The congress was held in New York,</b><br>
<b>USA. It discussed the threats to</b><br>
world peace caused by the rise of<br>
fascism in Italy, Germany and Japan.<br>
<b>Specifically, it was rumoured that Italy</b><br>
was planning to invade and colonise<br>
Ethiopia, formerly known as Abyssinia.<br>
<b>Following this, the congress formed</b><br>
an association called the International<br>
<b>African Friends of Abyssinia. This</b><br>
association had two main purposes,<br>
namely to arouse sympathy and support<br>
for the victims of the fascist aggression<br>
in Ethiopia, and to help the people of<br>
<b>Ethiopia to preserve their territorial</b><br>
integrity and political independence.<br>
<b>The Manchester Congress of 1945</b><br>
<b>The ongress was a turning point in</b><br>
the history of Africa, since it was<br>
attended by some African students<br>
studying abroad in the 1940s. These<br>
included Jomo Kenyatta, JB Danquah,<br>
<b>Kwame Nkrumah, DWB Dubois,</b><br>
<b>George Padmore, Magnus Williams,</b><br>
<b>Peter Abrahams, Otto Mackonnel and</b><br>
<b>Nnamdi Azikiwe. The conference</b><br>
led to the formation of the Pan-<br>
<b>African Federation of West African</b><br>
<b>States, which vowed to struggle for</b><br>
independence<br>
using<br>
non-violent<br>
means. The West African delegates<br>
also founded newspapers to spread<br>
the decisions of the congress. One of<br>
the newspapers was the International<br>
African Opinion.<br>
<b>The congress attacked some of the</b><br>
colonial constitutional reforms in<br>
their countries, saying that they were<br>
irrelevant to the needs of the African<br>
people. It also attacked the indirect<br>
rule system, calling it an instrument<br>
of<br>
oppression<br>
and<br>
exploitation.<br>
<b>It</b><br>
condemned<br>
the<br>
segregationist<br>
laws and practices in South Africa<br>

<b>On the basis of what you have</b><br>

<b>Relationship between the Back-to-</b><br>
<b>Africa Movement, Black Solidarity</b><br>
and Pan-Africanism<br>
<b>The Back-to-Africa Movement, Black</b><br>
<b>Solidarity and Pan-Africanism were</b><br>
closely related movements. All sought<br>
to eliminate all forms of racism and<br>
oppression.<br>
<b>Each movement had its own way of</b><br>
achieving this goal. For instance, the<br>
<b>Back-to-Africa Movement focused on</b><br>
encouraging Africans in the diaspora<br>
to go back to Africa, where they could<br>
live freely. Black Solidarity awakened<br>
<b>Africans so that thet could fight against</b><br>
racism using riots and demonstrations.<br>
<b>Pan-Africanism</b><br>
used<br>
conferences<br>
to<br>
propagate<br>
<b>African</b><br>
solidarity<br>
for<br>
liberation<br>
purposes. All<br>
the<br>
movements, therefore, were intended<br>
to emancipate Africans and eradicate<br>
all the imperialists’ evil practices.<br>
<b>Generally, the movements focused on</b><br>
total liberation of Africans within and<br>
outside America. They were fighting<br>
against a common enemy, namely<br>
capitalistic imperialism. The lesson<br>
we learn from these movements is<br>
that, whenever there is exploitation or<br>
oppression, the exploited or oppressed<br>
should use every means possible to get<br>
rid of such exploitation or oppression<br>
or any other kind of injustice.<br>
<b>The Civil Rights Movement</b><br>
<b>The Civil Rights Movement refers</b><br>
to the struggle organised by African<br>
<b>Americans to protest against legalised</b><br>
racial<br>
discrimination,<br>
segregation<br>

<b>Objectives of the Civil Rights</b><br>
<b>Movement</b><br>
<b>The ultimate goal of the Civil Rights</b><br>
<b>Movement was to achieve equality</b><br>
between African Americans and other<br>
<b>Americans. African Americans fought</b><br>
for equal social, political and economic<br>
rights such as the right to intermarriage,<br>
the right to better social services, the<br>
right to integrate with people of other<br>
races, the right to vote and be voted for,<br>
freedom of association and movement,<br>
the right to own property and the right<br>
to better jobs.<br>
<b>The Civil Rights Movement generally</b><br>
aimed to force the government to grant<br>
equal employment opportunities to<br>
blacks and whites.<br>

<b>Causes of the Civil Rights Movement</b><br>
<b>There were many long-term causes of</b><br>
the Civil Rights Movement; they date<br>
back to the 19th century. However, the<br>
immediate cause of the movement was<br>
the mistreatment of two high-level<br>
students in Montgomery, Alabama.<br>
<b>On 1st December 1955, two African</b><br>
<b>American women, Claudette Colvin</b><br>
and Rosa Park, refused to give their<br>
seat to a white passenger on a public<br>
bus<br>
in<br>
<b>Montgomery,</b><br>
Alabama.<br>
<b>Thereafter,</b><br>
they<br>
were<br>
arrested,<br>
something that culminated in riots and<br>
demonstrations. This action led to the<br>
bankruptcy of some bus companies<br>
because most of the passengers were<br>
African Americans.<br>
<b>Another cause was the coming to power of</b><br>
<b>President John F. Kennedy.  When he was</b><br>
a presidential candidate for the Democratic<br>
<b>Party for the 1960 presidential elections,</b><br>
<b>Kennedy promised he would exercise moral</b><br>
leadership in restoring civil rights to African<br>
<b>American citizens. As a result, African</b><br>
Americans overwhelmingly voted for him.<br>
<b>As president, he promoted employment for</b><br>
<b>African Americans in federal-supported</b><br>
programmes and established an equal<br>
employment opportunity committee that<br>
was expected to look into this issue. He<br>
promoted African American affairs, but<br>
he was assassinated in November 1963.<br>
<b>Forms of struggle adopted by the</b><br>
<b>Civil Rights Movement</b><br>
<b>During the struggle for civil rights in</b><br>
<b>America, African American civil rights</b><br>
activists used different methods and<br>
approaches that differed according to<br>
circumstances and time. The activists<br>
demanded political, social, economic<br>
and<br>
cultural<br>
rights<br>
for<br>
<b>African</b><br>
Americans as citizens of the USA.<br>
<b>Non-violence was one of the methods</b><br>
adopted by the Civil Rights Movement<br>
activists. This method was advocated<br>
by Martin Luther King Jr. It comprised<br>
peaceful demonstrations and boycotts.<br>
<b>The movement carried out transport</b><br>
boycott campaigns in Montgomery,<br>
<b>Alabama, between 1955 and 1956, to</b><br>
protest segregationist laws in public<br>
transport and the arrest of Rosa Park<br>
for violating such laws. In 1960,<br>
they started a “sit-ins” campaign<br>
at Greensboro in North Carolina to<br>
enforce racial integration in hotels and<br>
restaurants. The most important turning<br>
point of the non-violence movement<br>
was the “match of Washington” in<br>
<b>August 1963. The rally was attended</b><br>
by over 200,000 people and addressed<br>
by US President John F. Kennedy and<br>
<b>Martin Luther King Jr, who delivered</b><br>
an inspiring and memorable speech<br>
called “I have a dream”. The movement<br>
attracted support from some white<br>
Americans.<br>
<b>The use of violence and black militants</b><br>
was another form of struggle that<br>
<b>African Americans adopted. During</b><br>
the civil rights struggle, some African<br>
<b>Americans believed that it was almost</b><br>
impossible for the white people in<br>

96<br>
<b>America to change their racial attitudes</b><br>
towards black people. They considered<br>
the movement that used non-violence as<br>
meaningless in a society where African<br>
<b>Americans were facing extrajudicial</b><br>
killings, torture and intimidations.<br>
<b>They pointed out that White Americans</b><br>
had institutionalised the segregationist<br>
system that denied African Americans<br>
civil rights. Examples of famous<br>
violent or militant groups included<br>
the Black Muslims led by Malcolm<br>
<b>X, who called for African unity and</b><br>
total separation between whites and<br>
blacks, arguing that the white people<br>
would never change their attitude.<br>
There was also the Black Panthers.<br>
<b>This was another militant civil rights</b><br>
group formed by the followers of<br>
<b>Malcolm X after he was assassinated</b><br>
in 1965. This group was established<br>
by P. Huey Newton, Bobby Seale and<br>
<b>Eldridge Cleaver, who believed that a</b><br>
revolution was necessary for African<br>
Americans to secure their civil rights.<br>
<b>Another group was Black Power, led</b><br>
by Stokely Carmichael, H Rap Brown<br>
and others, who had defected from<br>
the non-violence movement after the<br>
assassination of James Meredith, the<br>
first black student to be admitted to the<br>
<b>University of Mississippi. Thus, they</b><br>
organised themselves for self-defence<br>
and campaigned for black pride.<br>
<b>Moreover, African Americans struggled</b><br>
for civil rights by writing books and<br>
publishing journals, newspapers and<br>
pamphlets to raise consciousness and<br>
awareness among blacks and whites.<br>
<b>The most famous writers were James</b><br>
<b>Weldon Johnson, Langstone Hughes,</b><br>
<b>Nela Larsen, Claude Mackey, and Jean</b><br>
<b>Tommer from Harlem in New York</b><br>
<b>City. Others were Richard Wright and</b><br>
<b>Gwendolyn Brooks, who wrote about</b><br>
the problems of racial segregation and<br>
the need for Black Nationalism. Their<br>
writings and publications helped to<br>
raise consciousness and awareness<br>
among African Americans about the<br>
question of racial equality. Likewise,<br>
some of the white people who read<br>
the publications changed their opinion<br>
about racial segregation.<br>
<b>Other civil rights activists used</b><br>
constitutional and judicial means. This<br>
group believed that there was nothing<br>
wrong with the US constitution and<br>
that the 13th, 14th and 15th constitution<br>
amendments had fully guaranteed<br>
the black people civil rights. Hence,<br>
they decided to challenge the laws<br>
of some of the states in court. For<br>
instance, the National Association<br>
for the Advancement of the Coloured<br>
<b>People (NAACP) led by WE Du Bois</b><br>
filed several cases in the Supreme<br>
<b>Court to oppose the violation of the</b><br>
<b>US constitution. For instance, the</b><br>
1954 supreme court ruling in the case<br>
“Mr Brown against Topeka Board<br>
of Education became the basis for<br>
desegregation in schools and public<br>
services thereafter. In addition, the<br>
civil rights case, Baker against Carry,<br>
helped to introduce a system of one<br>
man one vote.<br>

<b>African Americans extended their</b><br>

<b>Olympic Games in Mexico City and</b><br>
earned worldwide recognition. African<br>
<b>Americans’ participation in culture,</b><br>
games and sport became a major<br>
source of their earnings, since the<br>
circumstances did not allow them to get<br>
better employment. Their competence<br>
in these fields also won them respect in<br>
<b>America, which facilitated the struggle</b><br>
against racial segregation.<br>
<b>Achievements of the Civil Rights</b><br>
<b>Movement</b><br>
<b>The Civil Rights Movement enabled</b><br>
<b>African Americans to attain some</b><br>
political rights. This was reflected in<br>
the Civil Rights Act of 1965 (Voting<br>
<b>Rights Act of 1965), which allowed</b><br>
and protected the right to vote for<br>
<b>African Americans. This law was put</b><br>
in place during the reign of President<br>
<b>Andrew Johnson. The law allowed</b><br>
<b>African Americans to exercise their</b><br>
political rights. In 1975, there were 18<br>
<b>African Americans in the American</b><br>
<b>Congress. Thomas Bradley was elected</b><br>
as the first African American Mayor<br>
of Los Angeles. Harold Washington<br>
became the Mayor of Chicago in 1983<br>
and Condoleezza Rice became the first<br>
woman to be Chief National Security<br>
and Adviser to the President of the<br>
<b>United States of America, in 1989, and</b><br>
later served as Secretary of State during<br>
the reign of  President George W. Bush.<br>
<b>Likewise, in 2008 Barack Obama was</b><br>
elected president of the United States,<br>
the first African American to hold that<br>
office. In 2020, Kamala Devi Harris<br>
became the first African American<br>
vice president and the highest-ranking<br>
female official in the US.<br>
<b>In addition, the right to education for</b><br>
<b>African Americans came after the</b><br>
enactment of the Education Act of 1954.<br>
<b>The law declared the separation of</b><br>
schools on a racial basis illegal. The law<br>
gave African Americans an opportunity<br>
to get an education in the schools that<br>
had been reserved for whites. Increased<br>
educational opportunities for African<br>
<b>Americans led to massive advances in</b><br>
their educational attainment. However,<br>
the enrolment exercise was still<br>
characterised by racism. For example,<br>
from 1970 to the 1980s, college<br>
enrolments among African Americans<br>
rose from about 600 000 to 1 300 000.<br>
<b>Moreover, the Civil Rights Movement</b><br>
brought freedom of movement. African<br>
<b>Americans could now move without</b><br>
any restrictions in any town in the<br>

<b>USA. Hence, this right promoted the</b><br>
mobility and interaction of African<br>
Americans inside and outside the USA.<br>
<b>The Civil Rights Act of 1968,</b><br>
commonly known as the “Fair Housing<br>
<b>Act,” provided equal housing to all</b><br>
racial groups. In essence, this law<br>
banned housing discrimination in both<br>
sales or rentals.<br>
<b>The Civil Rights Movement ended</b><br>
racial segregation in public buses in<br>
1956. Following a one-year African<br>
<b>Americans’ boycott of Alabama bus</b><br>
services, which caused the bankruptcy<br>
of bus owners, the High Court banned<br>
racial segregation in all the buses<br>
operating in Alabama.<br>
<b>Again, in 1978, the Civil Rights</b><br>
<b>Movement led to the introduction of</b><br>
a government-supervised programme<br>
for promoting the minority groups in<br>
<b>America, most particularly African</b><br>
<b>Americans. The programme, which was</b><br>
otherwise called Affirmative Action,<br>
was intended to reduce the economic<br>
gap<br>
between<br>
<b>African</b><br>
<b>Americans</b><br>
and White Americans by extending<br>
more employment and educational<br>
opportunities to the former. The<br>
implementation of Affirmative Action<br>
enabled some African Americans to be<br>
admitted to American medical schools<br>
and universities, opportunities which<br>
had been reserved for White Americans.<br>
<b>It should, however, be noted that this</b><br>
arrangement was met with strong<br>
resistance from White Americans.<br>
<b>Challenges that faced the Civil</b><br>
<b>Rights Movement</b><br>
<b>Despite the achievements of the Civil</b><br>
<b>Rights Movement, the conditions of</b><br>
<b>African Americans did not improve</b><br>
drastically for most of them. The<br>
movement led to the assassination<br>
of some Civil Rights Activists like<br>
<b>Martin Luther King Jr, Fred Hampton,</b><br>
<b>Malcom X and Mark Clark. It also</b><br>
led to the assassination of President<br>
<b>John F Kennedy, who had pioneered</b><br>
constitutional reforms to promote civil<br>
rights for African Americans. Following<br>
the black riots which accompanied<br>
these movements between 1963 and<br>
1965, hundreds of thousands of people<br>
of African origin were either arrested,<br>
tortured or killed.<br>
<b>Moreover, the majority of African</b><br>
<b>Americans remained uneducated. It</b><br>
was estimated that about 99 per cent<br>
of the people of African origin could<br>
neither read nor write. This made<br>
them vulnerable to exploitation on<br>
plantations, in mines and in industries.<br>
<b>It was very hard for the leaders to</b><br>
mobilise them because they lacked<br>
civic education.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
government<br>
officials<br>
did not support the struggle and those<br>
who did were strongly opposed or<br>
assassinated. For instance, on 26th<br>
<b>September 1962 an African American</b><br>
student James Meredith was prevented<br>
from joining Mississippi University.<br>

<b>Introduction</b><br>
The colonisation of Africa passed through three major phases. The first phase<br>
was characterised by the arrival of colonial agents in Africa. Their arrival was<br>
more vivid from the early 19th century to the second half of the 19th century. The<br>
second phase was during the scramble for and partition of Africa in the last<br>
quarter of the 19th century. The last phase was the establishment of colonial<br>
rule, which was cemented by the establishment of the colonial state. In this<br>
chapter, you will learn about the meaning, objectives, nature, characteristics<br>
and functions of the colonial state in Africa. You will also learn about different<br>
colonial agricultural systems, their characteristics as well as the reasons for<br>
their adoption. The competencies developed will enable you to transform the<br>
colonial oriented type of production into a sustainable type of agriculture.<br>
Why didn’t the colonial state strengthen the agricultural sector up to 1914?<br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Four</b><br>
<b>The colonial state and</b><br>
agricultural systems up to 1914<br>
<b>The colonial state</b><br>
<b>The colonial state refers to the colonial</b><br>
government introduced in Africa by the<br>
<b>European colonial powers to safeguard</b><br>
their interests in the colonies. The<br>
colonial state was a geographical<br>
extension of the metropolitan state. The<br>
main aim of the metropolitan state in the<br>
metropole was to promote and protect<br>
bourgeois interests. The colonial state<br>
served the same function when the<br>
imperial capitalist powers established<br>
colonies in Africa. Thus, the colonial<br>
state did not develop as a result of<br>
<b>Africans’ initiatives and wishes. It</b><br>
was rather imposed on Africans by<br>
the capitalist powers who wanted to<br>
safeguard their interests. Essentially,<br>
among other things, the colonial state<br>
wanted to establish a proper colonial<br>
economy, which would directly respond<br>
to the economic needs of metropolitan<br>
capitalists. Thus, immediately after<br>
the Berlin Conference of 1884-1885,<br>
the European colonial powers set up<br>

colonial states in Africa as one of the manifestations of the Doctrine of Effective<br>
Occupation. The establishment of the colonial state was also associated with the<br>
setting up of state apparatus such as the army, police, court and prisons. Figure 4.1<br>
shows German colonial askaris in German East Africa.<br>
<b>Figure 4.1: German colonial askaris</b><br>
<b>The state apparatus was used to</b><br>
supervise and facilitate the undertaking<br>
of the colonial economy on behalf of<br>
the metropolitan governments. Thus,<br>
the creation of the colonial state and<br>
its coercive apparatus was the major<br>
strategy<br>
for<br>
facilitating<br>
efficient<br>
exploitation of the colonies to meet the<br>
needs of the metropolitan government.<br>
<b>As already pointed out, one of the</b><br>
important roles of the colonial state<br>
was to establish a colonial economy<br>
in the colonies. The establishment,<br>
of the colonial economy, however,<br>
involved<br>
restructuring<br>
pre-colonial<br>
<b>African economies so that Africans</b><br>
could produce raw materials and<br>
also be a market for European<br>
manufactured goods. Thus, the pre-<br>
colonial economy, which was largely<br>
subsistent but self-sufficient in the<br>
long run, was negatively transformed<br>
through the introduction of new forms<br>
of an economic system such as money.<br>
<b>This transformation and undermining</b><br>
of the pre-colonial economy was aimed<br>
at integrating African colonies into the<br>
world capitalist industrial economic<br>
system.<br>
<b>Objectives of the colonial state</b><br>
<b>The colonialists established colonial</b><br>
states in Africa. The establishment of<br>
the colonial state was an important step<br>
towards realising the capitalists’ goal.<br>
<b>The colonial state wanted to achieve</b><br>
several objectives.<br>

<b>First, the colonial state aimed to suppress</b><br>
<b>African resistance against colonial</b><br>
rule. The suppression of African<br>
resistance was aimed at ensuring that<br>
there was a peaceful and conducive<br>
environment for the establishment<br>
and running of the colonial economy.<br>
<b>A conducive environment would ease</b><br>
the exploitation of African resources<br>
and facilitate smooth running of the<br>
colonial economy. That is why African<br>
resistance such as the Maji maji war in<br>
<b>German East Africa and the Nama and</b><br>
<b>Herero war in South West Africa had to</b><br>
be suppressed by the German colonial<br>
state.<br>
<b>The suppression of resistance was vivid</b><br>
from 1885 to 1910. During this period,<br>
many Africans died on the battlefields,<br>
while others were either hanged or died<br>
from famine. In addition, the colonial<br>
state confiscated a lot of livestock for<br>
food. This weakened the economic<br>
stability of African societies, to whom<br>
livestock keeping was the major source<br>
of livelihood. The same was witnessed<br>
among farming societies, whose crops<br>
were burnt to weaken them.<br>
<b>The colonial state was also supposed to</b><br>
provide a link between the metropolitan<br>
capitalists and the colonies by making<br>
sure that all the economic needs of<br>
the capitalist industries were met at<br>
the cheapest cost possible. The needs<br>
included cheap raw materials such as<br>
cotton, tea, sisal, coffee and timber.<br>
<b>The colonies also  had to be markets</b><br>
as well as areas in which to  invest.<br>
<b>The colonial state was also expected to</b><br>
exploit minerals like gold and copper.<br>
<b>These valuable minerals were found</b><br>
in areas like Belgian-Congo, Southern<br>
<b>Rhodesia and Northern Rhodesia. Gold</b><br>
was used as an international currency,<br>
while copper was used as a raw material<br>
in electrical industries in Europe. The<br>
coal from Wankie in Southern Rhodesia<br>
was used as a source of power in South<br>
African industries.<br>
<b>The colonial state was also expected</b><br>
to ensure a constant supply of cheap<br>
labour in mines, on plantations and in<br>
other colonial projects. The colonial<br>
state was supposed to make sure that<br>
there was abundant cheap labour<br>
force in the colonies. Labourers were<br>
obtained through various techniques<br>
such as taxation, land alienation and<br>
labour laws such as the Masters’<br>
and Native Servants’ Amendment<br>
<b>Ordinance of 1921, which popularised</b><br>
the<br>
<b>Kipande</b><br>
system<br>
in<br>
Kenya.<br>
Likewise, taxes had to be paid in cash.<br>
<b>Africans were forced either to produce</b><br>
cash crops or to sell their labour power<br>
in the colonial projects to earn money<br>
for paying taxes. However, when there<br>
was a serious shortage of labour in<br>
some colonies, a tax could be paid in<br>
the form of labour.<br>
<b>The colonial state was supposed to</b><br>
construct transport infrastructure to<br>
open up the interior of the African<br>
continent so as to exploit it. For<br>

roads,<br>

facilitated the transport of European<br>
manufactured goods from the coast<br>
to the interior and raw materials from<br>
the production areas to the coast for<br>
shipping to the metropole. In addition,<br>
the construction of infrastructure was<br>
for transporting migrant labourers<br>
from the labour reserve areas to the<br>
production areas. For example, in<br>
<b>German East Africa, the central railway</b><br>
was used to transport labourers from<br>
<b>Kigoma and Tabora, labour reserves, to</b><br>
the plantations in Morogoro and Tanga.<br>
<b>Another prominent railway line was the</b><br>
<b>Uganda railway line from Mombasa</b><br>
to Kampala. The transport networks<br>
would also transport the colonial<br>
administrative staff and soldiers to and<br>
from different parts of the colonies.<br>
<b>Thus, the transport facilities were meant</b><br>
to make the implementation of colonial<br>
projects efficient and profitable.<br>
<b>The colonial state was expected</b><br>
to provide social services such as<br>
education and health. Such services<br>
were important to the colonial state<br>
and the economy. As for education, the<br>
colonial administration expected to get<br>
subordinates who would do low-ranking<br>
jobs such as being clerks, foremen,<br>
watchmen and ward attendants. Health<br>
facilities such as dispensaries, health<br>
centres and hospitals were equally<br>
important as they facilitated smooth<br>
running of the colonial economy. Apart<br>
from serving the colonial officials and<br>
the rest of the European population<br>
in the  colonies, the colonial health<br>
facilities were important in maintaining<br>
the health of African peasants and<br>
migrant labourers.<br>
<b>The colonial state was also expected to</b><br>
maintain law and order in the colonies.<br>
<b>The colonial laws were used to ensure</b><br>
that the interests of the metropolitan<br>
capitalists were protected. For instance,<br>
laws related to land alienation and<br>
taxation were meant to ensure effective<br>
exploitation of African resources.<br>
<b>Therefore, such laws were enforced in</b><br>
the colonies by whatever means.<br>
<b>Nature and character of the colonial</b><br>
state<br>
<b>The achievement of the aforementioned</b><br>
objectives influenced the nature and<br>
character of the colonial state. The<br>
colonial state behaved in such a way<br>
that it could easily fulfil its functions.<br>
<b>Thus, the colonial state had the</b><br>
following characteristics:<br>
<b>The colonial state was violent. It</b><br>
used violence in performing most of<br>
its responsibilities and pursuing its<br>
objectives. In this respect, the colonial<br>
state established the colonial coercive<br>
apparatus such as the police, army, court<br>
and prisons to suppress those who were<br>
against the colonial interests. Coercion<br>
was also used to destroy African<br>
handicraft industries. Throughout the<br>
colonies, such industries were declared<br>

illegal. In Belgian-Congo, for example,<br>
<b>Africans who continued working in</b><br>
traditional industries after the colonial<br>

their hands were chopped off. This<br>
was done under the leadership of King<br>
<b>Leopold II of Belgium. The gross</b><br>
impact of all this was that African<br>
economies lost their technological<br>
base. This was done so that Africans<br>
would be forced to import industrial<br>
products from Belgium. Thus, Africa<br>
was turned into a market for European<br>
manufactured goods and the African<br>
people were forcefully integrated into<br>
the money economy.<br>
<b>The violent nature of the colonial state</b><br>
was also evident at the time of colonial<br>
conquests. During the conquests,<br>
violence<br>
was<br>
common<br>
almost<br>
everywhere as colonial troops opened<br>
fire against Africans, who resisted the<br>
colonial intrusion. In German East<br>
<b>Africa, for example, the Germans</b><br>
fought with the Hehe under Mkwawa,<br>
the Yao under Machemba and the<br>
<b>Nyamwezi under Isike. In West Africa,</b><br>
the French used force to subdue Samori<br>
<b>Touré of Mandinka, while in Southern</b><br>
<b>Rhodesia the British South African</b><br>
<b>Company used force to conquer the</b><br>
<b>Shona and Ndebele. However, Africans</b><br>
were not passively conquered. They<br>
actively resisted the conquests. Armed<br>
resistance, for instance, occurred at the<br>
beginning of the colonial conquests,<br>
that is, when the European colonialists<br>
entered Africa without  Africans’<br>
consent. This made Africans resist<br>
colonial intrusion even more. African<br>
resistance was also inevitable as the<br>
colonialists evicted Africans from their<br>
fertile land so as to establish settler<br>
farms, plantations and mines, and to<br>
construct infrastructure like roads,<br>
ports and railways. Africans would<br>
subsequently be settled in infertile land<br>
so that they could continue providing<br>
cheap labour. This happened in Kenya,<br>
<b>Algeria, Southern Rhodesia and South</b><br>
Africa.<br>
<b>The colonial state used violence to</b><br>
compell Africans to produce cash<br>
crops. Force was used when Africans<br>
did not respond positively to cash<br>
crop production. African chiefs and<br>
headmen were ordered to force their<br>
fellow Africans to produce cash crops.<br>
<b>In some instances, Africans were forced</b><br>
to work on plantations without being<br>
paid. Forced labour was one of the<br>
reasons for Africans’ resistance against<br>
colonialism. For example, in German<br>
<b>East Africa, the Germans forced the</b><br>
cultivation of cotton in the Rufiji River<br>
<b>Valley. The forced cultivation of cotton</b><br>
was one of the reasons for the outbreak<br>
of the Maji maji war of 1905-1907.<br>
<b>Moreover, violence was used in</b><br>
collecting<br>
taxes.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
colonialists<br>
imposed heavy taxation, which became<br>
a big burden to Africans. Examples of<br>
such taxes were the head tax and the<br>
hut tax. The heavy taxation imposed on<br>

<b>Because Africans were reluctant to</b><br>

<b>Similarly, violence was also used by</b><br>

interests of the British in the colony.<br>
<b>It was the duty of the colonial state</b><br>
to create a conducive environment<br>
for smooth exploitation of resources.<br>
<b>Such physical infrastructure was also</b><br>
intended to serve administrative and<br>
religious purposes. They were mainly<br>
constructed with funds obtained from<br>
taxation and from the metropole.<br>
<b>The colonial state was exploitative</b><br>
and oppressive. It was characterised<br>
by intensive exploitation of Africans<br>
through various ways such as land<br>
alienation, forced labour and heavy<br>
taxation. For instance, the Kikuyu in<br>
<b>Kenya were forcefully evicted from</b><br>
their land, which turned them into<br>
landless labourers. That was also the<br>
case with the labourers, who worked<br>
in profitable colonial projects but<br>
who were paid very low wages. Some<br>
<b>Africans were also forced to work</b><br>
in the colonial projects. At the same<br>
time African labourers were forced to<br>
work for long hours. All the operations<br>
associated with colonial exploitation<br>
and oppression were supervised and<br>
controlled by the colonial state.<br>
<b>Furthermore, the colonial state was</b><br>
based on racial prejudice. It was<br>
heavily preoccupied with the racial<br>
supremacy syndrome. It believed<br>
in the superiority  of the white race<br>
over the black race. Therefore, its<br>
operations<br>
were<br>
associated<br>
with<br>
preference for Europeans to Africans<br>
because Europeans were believed to<br>
be mentally and culturally superior to<br>
<b>Africans. In the administrative sector,</b><br>
for example, high-ranking positions<br>
were<br>
held<br>
by<br>
<b>Europeans,</b><br>
while<br>
Africans held low-ranking positions.<br>
<b>The provision of social services was</b><br>
done along racial lines. Europeans<br>
received the best quality services such<br>
as water, housing, electricity, education<br>
and health, while Africans received<br>
very low quality  services. This was<br>
common in Apartheid South Africa,<br>
where the Afrikaner regime provided<br>
separate services for whites and blacks.<br>
<b>The former received better health and</b><br>
education services than the latter.<br>
<b>Generally, the colonial state was</b><br>
guided by the capitalist ideology,<br>
which showed that African culture<br>
was barbaric and primitive. Thus,<br>
according<br>
to<br>
<b>Europeans,</b><br>
<b>African</b><br>

culture was supposed to be destroyed<br>
and replaced by Western culture. The<br>
<b>French colonial state, for instance,</b><br>
implemented the assimilation policy<br>
for the purpose of destroying African<br>
values and glorifying French values.<br>
<b>Europeans had the duty to civilise</b><br>

were used to justify the Europeans’<br>

<b>Moreover, the colonial state created a</b><br>
regional imbalance; the most productive<br>
areas were provided with social<br>

but other areas were not. For instance,<br>
in Tanganyika, such production areas as<br>

services, while labour reserves such<br>
as Kigoma, Tabora, Singida, Dodoma,<br>
<b>Lindi and Mtwara were not. Therefore,</b><br>
the colonial state was discriminatory<br>
and this caused a regional imbalance<br>
in terms of social and economic<br>
development.<br>

<b>Functions of the colonial state</b><br>
<b>As already indicated, the colonial state</b><br>
was established to meet the interests of<br>
the metropolitan capitalists in Europe.<br>
<b>In order to realise the  interests, the</b><br>
colonial state had to perform the<br>
following functions:<br>
<b>The colonial state made sure that there</b><br>
was a constant supply of labour to all<br>
sectors of the colonial economy such<br>
as agriculture, mining and industry, as<br>
well as to the construction of transport<br>
infrastructure and public buildings. To<br>
make sure that there was a constant<br>
supply of labour to European settler<br>
farms, the colonial state alienated<br>
<b>Kenya Highlands to make the Kikuyu</b><br>
landless and force them to sell their<br>
labour-power for survival. For example,<br>
the British East Africa (Kenya) colonial<br>
state enacted the Master and Native<br>
<b>Servant Ordinance in 1906 which</b><br>
required Kikuyu young men to provide<br>
labour to European settlers’ farms for<br>
90 days per year. In 1918 the colonial<br>
government enacted another Master<br>
and Native Servant Ordinance which<br>
required the Kikuyu young men to<br>
work on European settlers’ farms for<br>
180 days a year. Moreover, in 1921 the<br>
colonial government enacted another<br>

in Kenya. The Kabarega resistance of<br>

in Uganda. Likewise, in British West<br>
<b>Africa, the Ashanti resistance was</b><br>
crushed by the British forces in 1890.<br>
<b>The Ndebele uprising of 1894, led by</b><br>

resistance of 1896-1897 were crushed<br>
by the colonial state in Southern<br>
<b>Rhodesia. All European powers faced</b><br>
resistance across the continent. The<br>
suppression of African resistance was<br>
aimed at preparing a good atmosphere<br>
for colonial administration and for the<br>
establishment of colonial economies.<br>

charged<br>
with<br>

for<br>

the running of the colonial government<br>
such as paying government officials and<br>
they forced Africans to either provide<br>
labour on the farms and plantations,<br>
and in the mines or engage in cash crop<br>

<b>Africa, for example, the Germans used</b><br>

<b>The colonial state also supervised the</b><br>

<b>The transport networks such as roads</b><br>
and railways were necessary for<br>
transporting raw materials from the<br>
interior to the coast (harbours) for<br>

and Tanga.<br>

opened in 1905. The railway linked the<br>
port of Lobito in Angola and Tenke town<br>
in the Lualaba province of Belgian-<br>
<b>Congo. Tenke produced copper and</b><br>
cobalt. Moreover, the railway line from<br>
<b>Lagos to Kano transported groundnuts</b><br>
and cotton from northern Nigeria.<br>
<b>The railway line from Port Harcourt</b><br>
to Maiduguri and Jos Plateau also<br>
transported palm oil and tin, whereas<br>
the railway line from Douala to Mount<br>
<b>Cameroon transported timber, cotton,</b><br>
coffee and sisal. The Uganda railway<br>
line reached Kampala in 1931. It was<br>
used to transport cotton and coffee<br>
from Uganda to the port of Mombasa<br>
in Kenya before being exported to<br>
Britain.<br>
<b>Generally, the colonial state ensured</b><br>
that all the needs of the metropolitan<br>
capitalists were cheaply met in the<br>
colonies. Thus, it violently suppressed<br>
the Africans who resisted colonial rule.<br>
<b>However, as argued before, all Africans</b><br>
were defeated and the colonial state<br>
established a colonial economy that<br>
directly responded to the needs of the<br>
metropolitan capitalists.<br>

<b>The establishment of the colonial</b><br>
economy in Africa took a number of<br>
years. It involved transforming African<br>
subsistence economies to make them<br>
inferior<br>
using<br>
de-industrialisation<br>
practices and laws such as coercion,<br>
land alienation, imposition of a money<br>
economy and taxation. Since the<br>
colonies were meant to produce raw<br>
materials, the main sectors emphasised<br>
were agriculture and mining. Thus,<br>
a detailed description of colonial<br>
agriculture in Africa is given in the<br>
following section.<br>
<b>Activity 4.2</b><br>
<b>The colonial state made considerable</b><br>
efforts<br>
to<br>
expand<br>
agricultural<br>
production within the colonies. Yet,<br>
the agricultural sector remained<br>
technologically backward. Conduct<br>
search in a library and explain the<br>
reasons for this?<br>
<b>Colonial agricultural systems</b><br>
<b>By 1914, the imperialist nations had</b><br>
almost finished dividing the Africa<br>
continent among themselves. The<br>
next major step was establishing and<br>
consolidating the colonial economy.<br>
<b>One of the major economic sectors</b><br>
established<br>
in<br>
the<br>
colonies<br>
was<br>
agriculture. Agriculture formed the<br>
backbone of the colonial economy.<br>
<b>This</b><br>
was<br>
because<br>
it<br>
provided<br>
agricultural raw materials such as<br>
sisal, cotton, coffee, tea, pyrethrum and<br>
tobacco, which were highly needed by<br>
metropolitan industries. Agriculture<br>
also involved producing  food crops<br>
that included cereals such as maize and<br>
rice, as well as fruits such as vineyards,<br>
pineapples and oranges for feeding<br>
the European population in Africa<br>
and African labourers. Cocoa and<br>
groundnuts were important food crops<br>
to the European food market. The<br>
<b>Europeans invested in agriculture as it</b><br>
facilitated the maximisation of profit by<br>
buying African crops at low prices and<br>
paying low wages to the labourers who<br>
worked on settler or plantation farms.<br>
<b>Thus, there were three types of colonial</b><br>
agriculture<br>
developed<br>
in<br>
<b>African</b><br>
colonies, namely peasant, settler and<br>
plantation agriculture. The details of<br>
each type are presented below.<br>
<b>Peasant agriculture</b><br>
<b>Under this type of agriculture, the colonial</b><br>
state allowed Africans to cultivate both<br>
cash crops and food crops. This type of<br>
colonial agriculture involved small-scale<br>
production of cash crops by individuals to<br>
earn money and to get food for domestic<br>
consumption. Peasant agriculture was<br>
mostly practised in Uganda,  the Gold<br>
<b>Coast, Nyasaland, Northern Rhodesia,</b><br>
<b>Ivory Coast, Senegal, the Gambia and</b><br>
northern Nigeria.<br>
<b>In the process of setting up the colonial</b><br>
economy, the colonial state alienated<br>
<b>African land. Part of the land was given</b><br>
to African chiefs as in the case of the<br>
<b>Mailo system in Buganda through the</b><br>

<b>Buganda Agreement of 1900, while the</b><br>
rest of the land was declared crown land.<br>
<b>In 1895, the colonial state in German</b><br>
<b>East Africa issued a decree, which</b><br>
declared all unoccupied land crown<br>
land. Such land could be given under<br>
the consent of the Governor to land<br>
applicants, especially German settlers.<br>
<b>The land occupied by indigenous</b><br>
<b>Africans could also be appropriated</b><br>
by the Governor for public use, for<br>
example building a school or mining;<br>
<b>Africans could be evicted from their</b><br>
land after being given a small amount<br>
of money as compensation.<br>
<b>At first, it was difficult for the colonial</b><br>
state to compell Africans produce<br>
cash crops. However, the missionaries<br>
collaborated with the colonial state<br>
in persuading Africans to produce<br>
cash crops. The missionaries taught<br>
their converts that Christianity and<br>
the production of cash crops were<br>
inseparable. They encouraged them to<br>
grow coffee, tea and cocoa with three<br>
goals.  The first was to subsidise their<br>
income. The second was to discourage<br>
the slave trade and encourage Africans<br>
to participate in the money economy<br>
and the third was to enable African<br>
converts to get money for making<br>
the church self-relient and for buying<br>
manufactured goods from Europe.<br>
<b>Consequently, by the beginning of the</b><br>
20th century, Christian converts had<br>
begun to grow cash crops. Therefore,<br>
it was not by accident that the people<br>
who lived in areas where missionaries<br>
had built their mission stations started<br>
to cultivate cash crops. This tactic,<br>
however, did not yield the quantities<br>
of cash crops needed. As a result, the<br>
colonial state used force to destroy<br>
<b>African economies. African chiefs</b><br>
and headmen were ordered to force<br>
<b>Africans to produce cash crops. Seeds</b><br>
and seedlings were distributed and<br>
their planting was supervised. Peasants<br>
were expected to produce both cash<br>
crops and food crops. They were also<br>
required to produce both raw materials<br>
for metropolitan industries and food for<br>
their families. For example, the Ashanti<br>
in the Gold Coast began to grow cocoa<br>
as a cash crop and continued to grow<br>
yams as their food crop. Similarly,<br>
in Uganda, the Baganda and Basoga<br>
began to grow cotton as a cash crop,<br>
while the Banyoro and Batoro began to<br>
grow coffee around Mount Ruwenzori.<br>
<b>All these continued to grow banana as</b><br>
a food crop. However, the prices of<br>
cash crops were low.<br>
<b>Moreover, industrialisation and the</b><br>
use of advanced technology were<br>
discouraged in the colonies. The<br>
colonies were told to produce raw<br>
materials for export to the metropole.<br>
<b>Thus, peasants continued to use</b><br>
the hand hoe. Labour organisation<br>
remained pre-capitalist so that the<br>
family continued to be the unit of<br>
production. This limited the amount<br>
of land that could be cultivated. Thus,<br>
the more the time spent on producing<br>

<b>The colonial state also intervened</b><br>
in determining producer prices. At<br>
the beginning of the colonial period,<br>
<b>Asians and Arabs in East Africa and</b><br>
<b>Syrians and Lebanese in West Africa</b><br>
were middlemen for African producers<br>
and metropolitan bourgeoisie, taking<br>
the lion’s share of the money the latter<br>
paid. This discouraged Africans from<br>
increasing production on the one hand,<br>
and encouraged their agitation against<br>
exploitation on the other. Therefore,<br>
the colonial state set producer prices so<br>
as to solve the two problems without<br>
reducing the profit margins of the<br>
metropolitan bourgeoisie and their<br>
trading companies.<br>
<b>Exercise 4.1</b><br>
(a)	 Why was the colonial state<br>
considered violent?<br>
(b)	 In what ways was the<br>
colonial state influential<br>
in the development of<br>
agriculture<br>
in<br>
colonial<br>
<b>Africa?</b><br>
<b>Features of peasant agriculture</b><br>
<b>From an economic point of view,</b><br>
peasant agriculture was economically<br>
profitable to the colonial state and<br>
<b>European trading companies. This</b><br>
was because the cost of agricultural<br>
production and management was<br>
shouldered by peasants.<br>
<b>Peasant agriculture was characterised</b><br>
by the cultivation of cash crops for<br>
sale and food crops for domestic<br>
consumption. However, the colonial<br>
state placed greater emphasis on the<br>
cultivation of cash crops, which were<br>
needed as raw materials by capitalist<br>
industries in the metropole. Some<br>
of the cash crops produced by the<br>
peasants were cotton, cocoa, coffee and<br>
groundnuts. The emphasis on cash crop<br>
production had a negative implication<br>
for food production, in that, it caused<br>
frequent food shortages, which in<br>
some instances led to famine among<br>
peasants.<br>
<b>Peasant agriculture was characterised</b><br>
by the use of relatively poor tools of<br>
production. Walter Rodney argues that<br>
peasants entered into colonialism with<br>
the hand hoe and got out of it with<br>
the same hoe. With colonial peasant<br>
agriculture, there was no major change<br>
in peasants’ tools of production.<br>
<b>Peasants  used the same tools they had</b><br>
used during the pre-colonial period.<br>
<b>Thus, pre-colonial tools like pangas</b><br>
(machetes) and hand hoes were still<br>
in use. Despite using poor tools,<br>

113<br>
sometimes peasants produced enough<br>
food crops for consumption and cash<br>
crops for sale. This was achieved<br>
because they used  agricultural inputs<br>
such as improved seeds and fertilizers,<br>
and followed the advice  given by<br>
agricultural experts. Thus, peasant<br>
production increased. For example,<br>
there was high production of cotton in<br>
Uganda.<br>
<b>Under this form of colonial agriculture,</b><br>
the family remained the basic unit of<br>
production. In most cases, peasant<br>
agriculture<br>
reduced<br>
the<br>
cost<br>
of<br>
production because it rarely used hired<br>
labourers. In this kind of agriculture,<br>
all family members participated in<br>
production under the leadership of<br>
the father, who was the head of the<br>
family. The use of family members in<br>
production produced super profit for<br>
the Europeans. However, in some areas,<br>
rich peasants paid migrant labourers so<br>
that they could work on their farms. For<br>
example, Sene-Gambian groundnut<br>
farming attracted seasonal labourers<br>
from the hinterland of Senegal  and<br>
the River Niger, while Ugandan coffee<br>
growers drew labourers from Ruanda<br>
and northern Uganda.  Cocoa growers<br>
in the Gold Coast and Ivory Coast drew<br>
migrant labourers from Upper Volta.<br>
<b>Moreover,</b><br>
traders<br>
and<br>
trading<br>
companies<br>
exploited<br>
peasants.<br>
<b>Trading companies throughout Africa</b><br>
safeguarded their interests by refusing<br>
to compete among themselves, while<br>
middlemen like Asians in East Africa<br>
and the Lebanese in West Africa set<br>
prices and conditions to limit African<br>
peasants’ options in terms of prices<br>
of their produce. Thus, inequality<br>
was a common feature in the trading<br>
process between the middlemen and<br>
the peasants.<br>
<b>In many instances, peasant agriculture</b><br>
was practised in densely populated<br>
areas. The Gold Coast and northern<br>
<b>Nigeria are good examples of such</b><br>
areas. It is argued that such areas could<br>
easily attract strong resistance if settler<br>
or plantation agriculture were adopted<br>
because the two forms of agriculture<br>
were associated with massive land<br>
alienation. Thus, alienation could<br>
anger the peasants and they would<br>
thus react against the state. In order<br>
to avoid this unnecessary clash, most<br>
of the densely populated areas were<br>
allowed to continue practising peasant<br>
agriculture.<br>
<b>In principle, colonial peasant agriculture</b><br>
involved small-scale farming. Peasants<br>
cultivated small portions of land<br>
because they had an amount of capital<br>
which could not support extensive<br>
cultivation. Therefore, they could not<br>
buy modern agricultural implements<br>
such as tractors and they had no power<br>
to hire a big number of labourers.<br>
<b>Thus, with their meagre capital, they</b><br>
cultivated only small portions of land<br>
to produce both cash and food crops.<br>
<b>In other words, peasant agriculture was</b><br>

114<br>
financed with a low level of capital<br>
investment and simple technology.<br>
<b>Factors that favoured the adoption</b><br>
of peasant agriculture<br>
<b>A number of factors determined</b><br>
whether a specific colony had to<br>
adopt peasant agriculture. The factors<br>
included the presence of traditional<br>
leadership, population density, pre-<br>
colonial experience in cash crop<br>
production and diseases.<br>
<b>The areas which had put up strong</b><br>
resistance against colonial occupation<br>
and had strong leadership such as the<br>
<b>Buganda Kingdom and the Bunyoro</b><br>
<b>Kingdom were allowed to practise</b><br>
peasant agriculture because it was not<br>
easy to alienate their land. Therefore,<br>
peasant agriculture was predominant in<br>
areas with strong centralised political<br>
systems. In such areas, African societies<br>
could not guarantee the security and<br>
protection of the European colonialists<br>
in the plantation and settler estates.<br>
<b>Thus, the colonial state found that</b><br>
peasant agriculture was the only option.<br>
<b>Apart from Buganda in Uganda, the</b><br>
other areas where peasant agriculture<br>
was highly encouraged were northern<br>
Nigeria and Ashanti in the Gold Coast.<br>
<b>Highly populated areas were also</b><br>
suitable for peasant agriculture. This<br>
is because land alienation was difficult<br>
in such areas. Therefore, the best way<br>
to sustain the colonial economy was to<br>
adopt peasant agriculture. For example,<br>
the French introduced peasant farming<br>
into Senegal, Guinea and Ivory<br>
<b>Coast because it was impossible to</b><br>
alienate land as the areas were densely<br>
populated. In the Gezira plains of<br>
<b>Sudan, the British colonial government</b><br>
supported a vast irrigation scheme<br>
to promote African peasant cotton<br>
production. Cotton was highly needed<br>
by British textile industries.<br>
<b>Poor climatic conditions and the</b><br>
prevalence of diseases were other<br>
factors that favoured the adoption of<br>
peasant agriculture in colonial Africa.<br>
<b>Areas with such features threatened</b><br>
the health of the colonialists. For<br>
example, peasant agriculture was<br>
introduced around the lakeside areas<br>
of Buganda, Bunyoro and Bukoba<br>
because of the presence of sleeping<br>
sickness, smallpox, malaria, influenza,<br>
meningitis and jiggers, which were a<br>
big health challenge to white settlers.<br>
<b>Similarly, Europeans could not settle in</b><br>
areas with hot climate. In this regard,<br>
peasant agriculture was  adopted as<br>
it could be handled by Africans, who<br>
were used to living in such areas.<br>
<b>The pre-colonial African experience</b><br>
in cash crop production played an<br>
important role in the introduction<br>
of peasant agriculture. Before the<br>
imposition of colonial rule, some<br>
societies in Africa produced cash crops.<br>
<b>For example, in West Africa, palm</b><br>
oil was produced by peasants before<br>
colonialism. In Uganda, the indigenous<br>
people used to grow local species<br>

willingness of these societies to grow<br>
important cash crops on their farms<br>
was influenced by their experience in<br>
crop cultivation. Thus, such societies<br>
were allowed to continue with peasant<br>
cash crop production. The colonial<br>
state introduced into Uganda new<br>
species of cotton, which were more<br>
productive than the local species.<br>
<b>Similarly, in some areas missionaries</b><br>
introduced cash crops long before the<br>
establishment of colonialism; hence,<br>
<b>Africans were used to producing cash</b><br>
crops. Therefore, the colonial state<br>
decided not to change the system of<br>
production. A good example was coffee<br>
production in Kilimanjaro and Kagera.<br>
<b>The preference and arrangements of</b><br>
colonial masters was another factor<br>
that influenced the establishment of<br>
peasant agriculture. In some cases,<br>
the colonial state decided that a given<br>
colony should  practise a certain type<br>
of agriculture. For example, the British,<br>
who had many colonies in Africa,<br>
decided on the form of agriculture that<br>
should be practised in their colonies.<br>
<b>For example, Nyasaland had to produce</b><br>
tea.<br>
<b>Governors’ preference also influenced</b><br>
the development of peasant agriculture.<br>
<b>For</b><br>
instance,<br>
in<br>
<b>Uganda,</b><br>
while<br>
<b>Chief Justice William Morris Carter</b><br>
preferred settler agriculture, Governor<br>
<b>Sir Henry Hesketh Bell encouraged</b><br>
the development of peasant agriculture<br>
in the early 1900s. As a result, cotton<br>
was grown for commercial purposes<br>
in Buganda and Busoga. By the mid-<br>
1920s, cotton exports enabled the<br>
<b>Uganda government to fund its budget</b><br>
without receiving grants or aid from<br>
<b>Britain. This happened after Governor</b><br>
<b>Bell passed the Cotton Ordinance,</b><br>
which resulted in high quality American<br>
cotton seeds replacing the wild seeds in<br>
<b>Uganda. He also banned hand ginning</b><br>
and instead opened new ginneries<br>
to process cotton. The government<br>
also encouraged peasants in Bunyoro<br>
and Toro in south-western Uganda to<br>
grow cotton in the early 1900s. Coffee<br>
thrived very well in these areas. As a<br>
result, by the 1930s, coffee had become<br>
Uganda’s second largest export.<br>
<b>Settler agriculture</b><br>
<b>This type of agriculture was controlled</b><br>
by Europeans, who had come to live<br>
in the colonies. They established large<br>
farms in various African countries<br>
like Kenya, South Africa, Algeria,<br>
<b>Mozambique, Angola and Southern</b><br>
<b>Rhodesia. The settlers had close links</b><br>
with their mother countries and had<br>
strong influence on the colonial state.<br>
<b>Thus, the colonial state’s policy had to</b><br>
consider their interests, namely capital,<br>
land and labour. Initially, many settlers<br>
had neither the expertise nor the<br>
capital with which to start large-scale<br>
agricultural production. Thus, they<br>
acquired capital through government<br>
assistance and bank loans at very low<br>

interest rates. As for the acquisition<br>
of land, the state carried out extensive<br>
land expropriation. The land was given<br>
to the settlers and planters freely or at<br>
a very cheap price. The colonial state<br>
enacted laws that compelled Africans<br>
to provide labour to the settlers.<br>
<b>In most cases, the settlers did not use</b><br>
all the land. In the first place, they<br>
lacked enough capital to develop all the<br>
land they had acquired. In the second<br>
place, extensive land was acquired for<br>
speculative purposes. This meant that<br>
a settler could sell part of the land at a<br>
higher price at a future date.<br>
<b>Characteristics of settler agriculture</b><br>
<b>Settler agriculture was one of the</b><br>
important agricultural systems under<br>
the colonial agricultural sector. It was<br>
large-scale farming under the European<br>
investors. In other words, it was owned<br>
by Europeans who permanently settled<br>
in Africa. This type of agriculture had<br>
the following characteristics:<br>
<b>Settler agriculture went hand in hand</b><br>
with massive land alienation. To<br>
alienate land, the colonial state enacted<br>
land ordinances that legalised private<br>
ownership of land and legitimised<br>
the alienation of African land for the<br>
settlers. In 1903, for example, the<br>
colonial government in Kenya gave<br>
large pieces of land to the settlers in<br>
the Naivasha Valley in the Central Rift<br>
<b>Valley, thereby evicting 5000 African</b><br>
land owners. Thus, Africans were<br>
pushed into reserves where they could<br>
not produce enough for themselves.<br>
<b>To supplement their produce, they</b><br>
had to sell their labour power to the<br>
settlers. In South Africa, the 1913 Land<br>
<b>Act prohibited Africans from owning</b><br>
the land which had been reserved for<br>
<b>European and Afrikaner settlers. It</b><br>
also barred squatters from farming<br>
<b>European-owned land. This was done</b><br>
at a time when the African-European<br>
ratio was 4 to 1. In 1906, about 1.6<br>
million hectares (3.52 million acres)<br>
of land were transferred to European<br>
ownership in Algeria.<br>
<b>Moreover, settler agriculture was based</b><br>
on forced labour, since it was a labour-<br>
intensive type of agriculture. Thus, the<br>
colonial state devised various laws and<br>
strategies to make sure that there was<br>
an abundant and constant supply of<br>
labour in the settler estates. In Kenya,<br>
for example, the Masters and Native<br>
<b>Servants Ordinance of 1906 stipulated</b><br>
that a squatter had to work for the<br>
settlers for 90 days a year at a nominal<br>
wage. Thus, the colonial state made<br>
sure that Africans provided labour<br>
power to the settlers. Apart from law<br>
enforcement, the colonial government<br>
imposed land alienation and various<br>
taxes to force Africans to provide their<br>
labour power to the settlers.<br>
<b>Settler agriculture was characterised</b><br>
by the colonial state protecting settlers’<br>
interests. This was shown during the<br>
conflicts between peasants and settlers.<br>
<b>In 1903, for example, the government</b><br>

the metropolitan bourgeoisie the latter<br>
used a populist ideology, that is, they<br>
pretended to uphold Africans’ interests<br>
to exert power over the settlers.<br>
<b>Essentially, both the metropolitan</b><br>
bourgeoisie and the settlers had agreed<br>
to exploit Africans, but they differed<br>
on the method of exploitation and the<br>
distribution of the loot.<br>
<b>Settler agriculture was characterised</b><br>
by improved infrastructure like roads<br>
and railways. This infrastructure was<br>
very important to the transport of<br>
raw materials from the interior to the<br>
coast. To a great extent, the areas in<br>
which settler agriculture was practised<br>
were favoured by the colonial state<br>
and, therefore, they were provided<br>
with better economic and social<br>
infrastructure, as well as security. This<br>
was done to make settlers to invest in<br>
agriculture. For instance, in Kenya,<br>
railways were built from Mombasa<br>
in 1895 to Kisumu in 1899, and from<br>
<b>Nairobi via Thika to Nanyuki in</b><br>

around Kenyan Highlands. Moreover,<br>
the railway line from Bulawayo to<br>
<b>Salisbury (Harare) served the European</b><br>
settler farms on which maize, coffee<br>
and tobacco were being grown and<br>
the dairy farms in Matebeleland in<br>
<b>Southern Rhodesia. Portuguese settlers</b><br>
also occupied the Bie Plateau (1520m<br>
to 1830m) with cool climate and high<br>
rains to grow coffee, maize, rice and<br>
sugarcane. The Benguela railway line<br>
served the settlers by transporting their<br>
crops to the port of Lobito for export to<br>
Portugal and Western Europe.<br>
<b>Furthermore, settler agriculture was</b><br>
based on crop production, preferably<br>
cash crops. In most cases, settler<br>
agriculture<br>
concentrated<br>
on<br>
the<br>
production of crops that were raw<br>
materials for metropolitan industries.<br>
<b>Crops such as coffee and tea were</b><br>
favoured in Kenya. Tobacco and coffee<br>
were grown in Southern Rhodesia<br>
and Angola. Settlers also engaged in<br>
commercial dairy farming so as to<br>
export milk, meat and hides. Settlers<br>
in Southern Rhodesia grew maize<br>
and established dairy farms to feed<br>
the food market at the Rand mining<br>
and commercial area in South Africa.<br>
<b>Similarly, settlers in Northern Rhodesia</b><br>
produced maize and milk to feed the<br>
food market in the copper belt.<br>
<b>Settler</b><br>
agriculture<br>
was<br>
also<br>
characterised by large-scale farming.<br>
<b>Settlers cultivated very large tracts</b><br>
of land for commercial purposes.<br>
<b>Therefore, settler agriculture was a</b><br>
capital-intensive type of farming. Heavy<br>
capital was needed to buy modern<br>
agricultural implements such as tractors<br>
and other machines, pay labourers and<br>
buy other necessary inputs.<br>

<b>Factors that favoured settler</b><br>
agriculture<br>
<b>Settler agriculture was practised in</b><br>
areas with a kind of climate similar to<br>
that of Europe. Hot climate was not<br>
suitable for Europeans; hence, they<br>
opted to settle in highland areas such<br>
as Kenya Highlands, Bie Plateau and<br>
Matebele Plateau.<br>
<b>Areas with plenty of labourers also</b><br>
favoured settler agriculture. This was<br>
because settler agriculture was a large-<br>
scale undertaking, which required a<br>
large number of labourers. Additionally,<br>
the preferences of the colonial masters<br>
and<br>
governors<br>
influenced<br>
settler<br>
agriculture. Governors like Sir Charles<br>
<b>Eliot of Kenya was interested in settler</b><br>
agriculture; hence, Sir Eliot promoted<br>
its establishment in Kenya.<br>
<b>Moreover, settlers were motivated</b><br>
to settle in areas where the colonial<br>
state had established good transport<br>
and communication networks. The<br>
availability of roads and railways<br>
motivated settlers to settle in such areas.<br>
<b>It was easy for them to  transport cash</b><br>
crops from the production areas to the<br>
cities or ports. A good example is the<br>
<b>Uganda railway, which made settlers</b><br>
establish farms in Kenyan Highlands<br>
and some parts of the Rift Valley.<br>
<b>Infrastructure also made Portuguese</b><br>
settlers settle on Bie Plateau in Angola<br>
and British settlers on Matebele Plateau<br>
in Southern Rhodesia.<br>
<b>Furthermore, this type of agriculture</b><br>
was dominant in areas with less or<br>
without strong resistance from Africans.<br>
<b>Since settler agriculture involved</b><br>
cultivating huge tracts of land, strong<br>
resistance would occur if such areas<br>
had strong centralised societies with<br>
strong armies. This was because settler<br>
agriculture needed huge pieces of land.<br>
<b>Europeans opted to establish settler</b><br>
agriculture in areas with less resistance<br>
such as Kenya, Angola, South Africa,<br>
the north-eastern part of Northern<br>
<b>Rhodesia, Cholo and the areas around</b><br>
Mount Mlanje in Nyasaland.<br>
<b>How the colonial state supported</b><br>
settlers<br>
<b>The colonial state supported settlers in</b><br>
various ways. For example, it exempted<br>
them from paying taxes. This was<br>
done to allow them produce more cash<br>
crops so as to get raw materials for<br>
industries in Europe. For example, in<br>
<b>Kenya, tax exemption enabled settlers</b><br>
to accumulate massive wealth.<br>
<b>The colonial state also forced Africans</b><br>
to provide cheap labour to settlers in<br>
different ways. This happened in South<br>
<b>Africa, Kenya and Southern Rhodesia</b><br>
(Zimbabwe). The alienation of African<br>
land in the early 20th century was<br>
intended to make Africans landless,<br>
thus forcing them to work on settler<br>
farms as labourers.<br>
<b>Moreover, the colonial state constructed</b><br>
transport infrastructure in the colonies.<br>

<b>For example, in Kenya, the Uganda</b><br>
railway line was constructed by the<br>
colonial state. In South Africa, railway<br>
lines were constructed to link certain<br>
parts of South Africa with Southern<br>
Rhodesia and Northern Rhodesia.<br>
<b>These infrastructural facilities were</b><br>
used by settlers to transport labourers<br>
and crops.<br>
<b>The colonial state created an enabling</b><br>
environment<br>
for<br>
settlers<br>
to<br>
do<br>
agricultural activities smoothly. For<br>
example, European settlers were given<br>
the right to own land for 33, 66 and<br>
99 years land and to grow certain cash<br>
crops. For example, in Kenya, Africans<br>
were forbidden to grow Arabica coffee,<br>
the country’s most profitable cash<br>
crop. Thus, Europeans produced much<br>
coffee  and, by 1913, coffee exports<br>

to fund its budget without receiving<br>
subsidies from Britain. In Nyasaland,<br>
<b>Africans were excluded from growing</b><br>
the most lucrative varieties of tobacco.<br>
<b>They were prohibited from growing</b><br>
flue-cured Virginia tobacco, a key<br>
constituent in blended cigarettes from<br>
1908. They were also banned from<br>
growing<br>
burley<br>
tobacco,<br>
another<br>
lucrative and profitable variety. These<br>
were reserved for European settlers.<br>
<b>Africans were denied the right to own</b><br>
land and grow important cash crops like<br>
coffee, tea and pyrethrum. Moreover,<br>
settlers received marketing assistance<br>
and cash subsidies from the colonial<br>
government.<br>
<b>In addition, settlers were given security</b><br>
by the colonial government. The<br>
colonial apparatus like the police and<br>
army maintained peace and security to<br>
protect the settlers and their property.<br>
<b>Plantation agriculture</b><br>
<b>Plantation agriculture was another</b><br>
type of agriculture established in<br>
various colonies in Africa. This type<br>
of agriculture involved opening large<br>
farms that needed large tracts of land.<br>
<b>Many owners of plantations lived in</b><br>
<b>Europe and employed farm managers</b><br>
to<br>
supervise<br>
production.<br>
<b>Each</b><br>
company specialised in the production<br>
of a specific cash crop. Plantation<br>
agriculture was practised in German<br>
<b>East Africa, Belgian-Congo, Congo-</b><br>
<b>Brazzaville, Gabon, Cameroon and</b><br>
<b>Liberia. For example, whereas German</b><br>
<b>East Africa, produced sisal, Mauritius</b><br>
specialised in sugarcane production<br>
and Liberia produced rubber. By<br>
contrast, Ivory Coast and Madagascar<br>
specialised in coffee production and<br>
<b>Sudan in cotton production. Some</b><br>
plantations were opened in Uganda.<br>
<b>Asians opened sugarcane plantations</b><br>
in Lugazi and Kakira near Jinja. Other<br>
<b>Asian planters grew rubber. Figure 4.2</b><br>
shows a sisal plantation in German<br>
East Africa.<br>

<b>Characteristics of plantation</b><br>
agriculture<br>
<b>Plantation agriculture was characterised</b><br>
by<br>
large-scale<br>
farming.<br>
<b>Planters’</b><br>
interest was in producing a large<br>
quantity of raw materials for European<br>
industries. The nature of such crops<br>
as sisal, rubber, sugarcane and cloves<br>
demanded large pieces of land.<br>
<b>Furthermore, plantation agriculture</b><br>
needed huge capital. Money was<br>
needed to pay labourers, clear the<br>
land, buy fertilizers and buy advanced<br>
agricultural equipment like tractors and<br>
sisal decortication machines. Money<br>
was also used to build infrastructure<br>
such as irrigation systems, fire control<br>
systems, paths and others. Thus,<br>
this type of agriculture was capital<br>
intensive.<br>
<b>Plantation agriculture was monoculture</b><br>
in nature. This kind of farming was<br>
commonly based on the cultivation of<br>
one crop in large estates. It focused on<br>
the quantity and quality of one crop<br>
needed in metropolitan countries. For<br>
instance, in German East Africa areas<br>
like Tanga and Morogoro specialised<br>
in sisal production whereas Mauritius<br>
produced<br>
sugarcane.<br>
<b>Similarly,</b><br>
<b>Zanzibar</b><br>
concentrated<br>
on<br>
clove<br>
production. This specialisation was<br>
aimed at increasing the production of<br>
a given crop in the colony to meet the<br>
industrial needs of the metropole.<br>
<b>Plantation agriculture preferred using</b><br>
migrant labour. This form of agriculture<br>
was associated with the development<br>
of migrant labourers. Being labour-<br>
intensive, plantation agriculture needed<br>

<b>The</b><br>

of<br>

<b>Some of the factors are:</b><br>
<b>A good climate, fertile soil and reliable</b><br>
rainfall made colonial companies<br>
establish plantations in certain parts of<br>
<b>Africa. For instance, Belgian-Congo</b><br>
was one of the areas which had good<br>
climatic conditions that attracted the<br>
<b>Belgians to establish rubber plantations</b><br>
there. In German East Africa, the<br>
climate of Tanga and Morogoro<br>
favoured sisal production. In Mauritius,<br>
the climate was suitable for sugarcane<br>
cultivation. However, in German East<br>
<b>Africa the attempt to establish rubber</b><br>
plantations failed in 1913 as the rubber<br>
produced was of low quality compared<br>
to the rubber produced in Malaysia,<br>
Belgian-Congo and Brazil.<br>
<b>Plantation agriculture also depended on</b><br>
effective management and supervision<br>
by European managers. The famous<br>
plantation companies that operated in<br>
<b>Africa were Unilever’s palm oil estate</b><br>
in Belgian-Congo and Mozambique<br>
<b>Company Sugar Plantation in northern</b><br>
Mozambique.<br>
<b>These</b><br>
companies<br>
employed<br>
<b>European</b><br>
managers<br>
to<br>
run their plantations. Production on<br>
the plantations was fairly inefficient<br>
because there was little investment in<br>
research, technology and transport.<br>
<b>Therefore, the plantations heavily used</b><br>
violence to force Africans to provide<br>
labour.<br>
<b>Another factor for the thriving of</b><br>
plantation agriculture was the existence<br>
of a less centralised African political<br>
organisation. Plantation agriculture was<br>
practised in areas with less centralised<br>
political systems. This is because in<br>
such areas there were relatively less<br>
organised societies that could not<br>
strongly resist land alienation. In areas<br>
that had no strong resistance, including<br>
some parts of German East Africa,<br>
plantation agriculture flourished.<br>

<b>Plantation agriculture needed a big</b><br>
