<b>Reasons for adopting different</b><br>
colonial agricultural systems<br>
<b>Agriculture was the backbone of the</b><br>
metropolitan economies. It produced<br>
agricultural raw materials to meet<br>
the needs of European industries.<br>
<b>The capitalists adopted three types</b><br>
of agricultural production in the<br>
colonies: peasant, settler and plantation<br>
agriculture. Each type was established,<br>
depending on the material conditions<br>
obtaining in a particular colony.<br>
<b>However, there is a debate regarding</b><br>
the introduction of different types of<br>
agriculture in the colonies. The debate<br>
involves European scholars and their<br>
critics. According to the former, many<br>
of whom are apologists for colonialism,<br>
some of the colonies in Africa<br>
developed peasant agriculture because<br>
they had high temperatures, which<br>
discouraged Europeans to develop<br>
either settler or plantation agriculture.<br>
<b>They also argue that tropical diseases</b><br>
such as malaria and bilharzia prevented<br>
settler plantation and agriculture. They<br>
also mention other factors such as<br>
strong African political systems and<br>
the status of a colony, that is, whether<br>
it was a settler colony or a protectorate.<br>
<b>The same historians advance the issue</b><br>
of a huge African population, which<br>
always caused land pressure. They<br>
cite Uganda, which had strong feudal<br>
relations which the British colonialists<br>
could not break. Finally, the British<br>
trusteeship policy adopted in some<br>
<b>West African colonies is said to have</b><br>
prevented the establishment of settler<br>
and plantation agriculture in such<br>
colonies.<br>
<b>The critics, however, critique the above</b><br>
<b>Eurocentric views. They say that the</b><br>
<b>Eurocentric views ignore the role of</b><br>
the colonial state in determining the<br>
type of colonial agriculture that should<br>
be developed in a colony.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
<b>Eurocentric</b><br>
argument<br>
that<br>
malaria and other tropical diseases<br>
were obstacles to the development of<br>
settler or plantation agriculture does<br>
not hold water either. Logically, the<br>

peasant<br>
agriculture<br>
would thrive just as it did in Uganda<br>
because all these countries had similar<br>
climatic conditions, but the colonialists<br>
developed plantation agriculture in the<br>
colonies because it was favoured by<br>
the colonial state.<br>
<b>Therefore,</b><br>
hot<br>
temperatures<br>
and<br>
tropical diseases did not prevent the<br>
introduction of settler or plantation<br>
agriculture in Africa. Thus, there is a<br>
need to find the factors from economic,<br>
political<br>
and<br>
<b>African</b><br>
initiative<br>
perspectives. The factors are explained<br>
in the following section.<br>
<b>The colonial state policy determined the</b><br>
type of agricultural system introduced<br>
in a given colony. For instance, in<br>
<b>German East Africa, the colonial state</b><br>
favoured all three types of agriculture.<br>
<b>However, the British, who took over</b><br>
the colony after the First World War<br>
and renamed it Tanganyika, did not<br>
want to make Tanganyika a settler<br>
colony. The government feared that<br>
making Tanganyika a settler colony<br>
would force Africans to provide labour<br>
to settlers and would involve alienating<br>
more land from Africans, which would<br>
cause another Majimaji war.<br>
<b>Likewise, between 1915 and 1920, the</b><br>
colonial state set up two commissions<br>
in Uganda to decide whether Uganda<br>
settler agriculture should introduce or<br>
peasant agriculture should continue.<br>
<b>The few European settlers in Uganda</b><br>
wanted the colonial state to introduce<br>
new policies that would enable them<br>
to start settler agriculture. Two reports<br>

<b>African initiatives also determined</b><br>
the type of agriculture adopted by the<br>
colonialists. In some colonies, the<br>
<b>Africans who wanted to improve their</b><br>
living standards planted cash crops like<br>
coffee, cocoa and cotton. For instance,<br>
in the early 1900s, a few European<br>
settlers in Kilimanjaro planted over<br>
one<br>

<b>Some</b><br>
<b>Chagga peasants who planted coffee</b><br>

compete with a few European settlers<br>

asked the German colonial state to<br>
ban the growing of arabica coffee by<br>
the Chagga. The German colonial<br>
state, however, did not take any<br>
action against the Chagga peasants.<br>

a shortage of migrant labourers. The<br>
settlers were finally eliminated by the<br>
<b>Chagga peasants. As a result, peasant</b><br>
agriculture flourished in Kilimanjaro.<br>
<b>By 1913, over three-quarters of the</b><br>
coffee produced in the colony was<br>
produced by African peasants.<br>
<b>A similar experience happened to the</b><br>
<b>West African peasants who, by the</b><br>
1890s, had already entered the cash<br>
crop export economy. They were<br>
producing export crops like palm oil,<br>
cocoa and groundnuts. Hence, the<br>
colonial state felt that, if it encouraged<br>
settler or plantation agriculture, it<br>
would interfere with the traditional<br>
land rights, thus causing resistance.<br>
<b>The state also feared that Africans</b><br>
would not be ready to work for the<br>
settlers, while they had their own plots<br>
of land to work on. The few Africans,<br>
who would have volunteered to work,<br>
would have demanded high wages,<br>
something that the European settlers<br>
and planters were totally against.<br>
<b>The high production costs influenced</b><br>
the type of agriculture established in a<br>
given colony. In many African colonies,<br>
settler<br>
and<br>
plantation<br>
agriculture<br>
were not developed because of high<br>
production costs and the long time the<br>
crops took to mature. For instance,<br>
<b>Europeans feared to open cocoa</b><br>
plantations in Ivory Coast and the Gold<br>
<b>Coast because the plant take 15 years</b><br>
to mature. Thus, the Europeans did<br>
want to grow that crop. After all, they<br>
were not familiar with the crop.<br>

<b>The</b><br>
presence<br>
of<br>
transport<br>
and<br>
communication networks in a colony<br>
was a factor for introducing a given<br>
type of agriculture. Settler agriculture<br>
and even peasant agriculture were<br>
introduced in areas that had reliable<br>
transport and communication systems<br>
like railways and telegraphic services.<br>
<b>For instance, the construction of the</b><br>
<b>Uganda railway line from Mombasa to</b><br>
<b>Kampala favoured the establishment of</b><br>
settler agriculture in Kenya and peasant<br>
agriculture in Uganda. The cash crops<br>
that the settlers produced were easily<br>
transported to the coast, ready for<br>
shipment to Europe and other parts of<br>
the world. Similarly, the cotton, coffee<br>
and tea that African peasants produced<br>
in Uganda were transported to Europe<br>
via the port of Mombasa. The railway<br>
line eased transport, reduced costs<br>
and opened up the interior in Kenyan<br>
Highlands.<br>
<b>In addition, with the expansion of</b><br>
peasant farms and plantations in<br>
<b>Uganda,</b><br>
<b>Governor</b><br>
<b>Hesketh</b><br>
<b>Bell</b><br>
encouraged the importation of bicycles<br>
and lorries to ease the transport of<br>
goods from remote areas. He also<br>
expanded the construction of railway<br>
lines from Kampala to Port Bell, Jinja<br>
and Namasagali to open up the remote<br>
areas by linking them with the Uganda<br>
railway line.<br>
<b>Plantation</b><br>
or<br>
settler<br>
agriculture<br>
flourished because of the availability<br>
of migrant labourers. For instance,<br>
sisal plantations became successful<br>
in German East Africa because of the<br>
availability of cheap migrant labourers<br>
from Rukwa, Kigoma, Ruvuma, Lindi<br>
and Mtwara.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
availability<br>
of<br>
capital<br>
and<br>
labourers also determined the nature<br>
of the agricultural system introduced<br>
in a colony. Between the 1890s and<br>
early 1900s, French firms established<br>
some plantations in Ivory Coast<br>
and Dahomey, but when the French<br>
colonial authority refused to give them<br>
concession, the firms collapsed and<br>
plantation agriculture ended. Moreover,<br>
the availability of labour was key to the<br>
specific agricultural system introduced.<br>
<b>For example, settler agriculture was</b><br>
established in Kenya partly because of<br>
the presence of African labourers, who<br>
were forced to work for the settlers<br>
under the Kipande system.<br>

<b>It stimulated the construction of</b><br>
transport infrastructure such as ports,<br>
roads and railways in different parts<br>
of Africa. For instate, in German<br>
<b>East Africa roads and railways were</b><br>
constructed to connect the production<br>
areas with the harbours and ports. The<br>
infrastructural facilities facilitated the<br>
transport of European manufactured<br>
goods from the coast to the interior<br>
markets in the colonies. They also,<br>
facilitated<br>
the<br>
transport<br>
of<br>
raw<br>
materials from the interior (production<br>
areas) to the coast for shipment to the<br>
metropolitan countries.<br>
<b>Agriculture was a good source of</b><br>
tropical raw materials for metropolitan<br>
industries. The raw materials produced<br>
in colonial Africa were rubber in<br>
<b>Belgian-Congo, cotton in Sudan and</b><br>
<b>Egypt, coffee and sisal in German East</b><br>
<b>Africa, cloves in Zanzibar, sugarcane in</b><br>
Mauritius and cocoa in the Gold Coast.<br>
<b>All these raw materials were needed</b><br>
in European industries. Thus, colonial<br>
agriculture helped meet one of the<br>
needs of the metropolitan capitalists.<br>
<b>It also stimulated further industrial</b><br>
development in the metropole.<br>
<b>Agriculture was also a source of</b><br>
employment for both Europeans and<br>
<b>Africans. For example, many people</b><br>
in Europe lost their means of survival<br>
when machines took their place after<br>
the Industrial Revolution. The surplus<br>
population came to do farming activities<br>
in Africa, for example in Southern<br>
<b>Rhodesia, South Africa, Kenya and</b><br>
<b>German East Africa. On top of that,</b><br>
colonial agriculture absorbed many<br>
<b>Africans as peasants and agricultural</b><br>
labourers.<br>
<b>Agriculture stimulated improvement of</b><br>
the quality of health services in Africa,<br>
including the setting up of dispensaries<br>
and hospitals, especially in the highly<br>
productive areas. For example, in<br>
<b>German East Africa, the colonial state</b><br>
improved the quality of health services<br>
in Moshi, Arusha and Morogoro to<br>
treat labourers and colonial officials so<br>
as to increase their efficiency.<br>
<b>Europeans acquired markets for their</b><br>
goods in Africa. For instance, in<br>
<b>Uganda, Kenya, German East Africa</b><br>
and<br>
<b>Southern</b><br>
<b>Rhodesia, Africans</b><br>
produced cash crops and sold them to<br>
<b>Europeans. Manufactured goods were</b><br>
later imported into Africa.<br>
<b>Colonial agriculture influenced the</b><br>
colonial exploitation of Africa through<br>
colonial taxes and human labour.<br>
<b>For instance, to force Africans to</b><br>
cultivate cash crops, the colonial state<br>
imposed taxes on them. Since paying<br>
taxes was compulsory, Africans had<br>
to work on the colonial plantations.<br>
<b>Likewise, colonial agriculture led to</b><br>
the exploitation of African peasants by<br>
buying their crops at low prices.<br>
<b>Generally, the development of colonial</b><br>
agriculture in Africa was not intended<br>
to benefit Africans. Thus, colonial<br>

<b>Introduction</b><br>
After the Second World War, such European colonial powers as Britain, France,<br>
and Italy experienced an economic crisis as a result of the war. The crisis forced<br>
the European powers to formulate policies that would assist them in recovering<br>
from the economic woes. In this chapter, you will learn about the changes in<br>
colonial agriculture, industrial policies and trade, as well as labour policies in<br>
colonial Africa made after the Second World War. You will also learn about the<br>
changes in colonial transport and communication infrastructure, as well as the<br>
changes in social service policies. The competencies developed will enable you<br>
to participate in the process of bring about socio-economic changes at family,<br>
societal and national levels.<br>
How exploitative were the colonial agricultural projects established after<br>
the Second World War?<br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Five</b><br>
<b>Changes in the colonial economy and social</b><br>
services after the Second World War<br>
<b>The concept of colonial economy</b><br>
<b>The concept of colonial economy</b><br>
refers to the systems of production,<br>
distribution and consumption which<br>
were introduced into Africa by the<br>
<b>European colonialists to meet the</b><br>
industrial needs in the metropole. The<br>
industrial needs stemmed from the<br>
<b>Industrial Revolution, which took place</b><br>
in Western Europe in the 19th century.<br>
<b>The European industrial economy</b><br>
made Africa a supply of agricultural<br>
raw materials and minerals, as well as<br>
a market for European manufactured<br>
goods. European colonial governments<br>
also imposed colonial economies<br>
in Africa. Thus, by the 1900s the<br>
colonial economy had set its foot in<br>
the colonies. Sectors like agriculture,<br>
mining, trade, infrastructure and labour<br>
were in motion. However, the outbreak<br>
of the Second World War caused the<br>
collapse of the European economy. For<br>
example, during the war the industrial,<br>
agricultural and commercial sectors, as<br>
well as the trade and transport sectors<br>
were severely affected by heavy<br>
bombings and the depletion of the<br>
treasure used to finance the war.<br>

1. HIST-F5 (DOUBLE COLUMN) backUP.indd   129<br>

<b>Student's Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Advanced History for Secondary Schools</b><br>
130<br>
<b>Features of the colonial economy</b><br>
<b>The colonial economy was an extension</b><br>
of the European capitalist economy in<br>
the colonies. It responded to the needs<br>
of the imperial colonial powers in<br>
<b>Western Europe. The major aim of the</b><br>
<b>European imperialists was to acquire</b><br>
raw materials for their industries and<br>
markets for their industrial goods.<br>
<b>Thus, the colonies were for meeting the</b><br>
economic needs of Western European<br>
imperialism.<br>
<b>In some parts of Africa, the colonial</b><br>
economy was monocultural, in that it<br>
heavily depended on the production of<br>
a single cash crop. For example, the<br>
<b>Gold Coast produced cocoa, Mauritius</b><br>
produced<br>
sugarcane<br>
and<br>
<b>Liberia</b><br>
specialised<br>
in<br>
rubber<br>
production.<br>
<b>Similarly, Zanzibar produced cloves,</b><br>
<b>Northern</b><br>
<b>Rhodesia</b><br>
and<br>
<b>Belgian-</b><br>
<b>Congo produced copper, while Algeria</b><br>
produced grapes and olives.<br>
<b>The colonial economy was based on</b><br>
coercion. Africans were forced to<br>
produce for export rather than for their<br>
consumption. The emphasis placed<br>
on the production of cash crops left<br>
<b>Africans with little time to produce</b><br>
sufficient food crops; hence, frequent<br>
food shortages occurred in the colonies.<br>
<b>Similarly, many Africans were not</b><br>
ready to work on colonial plantations<br>
and farms, and in mines because they<br>
were paid low wages and worked<br>
for long hours under harsh and poor<br>
working conditions. Thus, force was<br>
used to make them work. The workers<br>
who did not observe working laws and<br>
regulations were punished severely by<br>
their masters or supervisors.<br>
<b>Moreover,</b><br>
the<br>
colonial<br>
economy<br>
was export-import oriented. It was<br>
characterised<br>
by<br>
the<br>
production<br>
of exportable raw materials for<br>
metropolitan industries while finished<br>
manufactured goods were imported into<br>
the colonies. Therefore, the colonial<br>
economy served the interests of the<br>
<b>European capitalists. The capitalists</b><br>
acquired raw materials such as cotton,<br>
sisal, animal skins and hides, tobacco<br>
and minerals at low prices from the<br>
colonies, while the colonies bought<br>
industrially manufactured goods such<br>
as clothes, mirrors, drinks and shoes at<br>
high prices. This was a type of economy<br>
in which Africans consumed what they<br>
did not produce and produced what<br>
they did not consume.<br>
<b>The colonial economy was also</b><br>
characterised by semi-processing and<br>
import  substitution industries. The<br>
semi-processing industries found in<br>
<b>Africa after the Second World War</b><br>
were meant to reduce the bulkiness<br>
of raw materials to reduce the cost of<br>
transporting them to Europe. Import-<br>
substitution industries were set up<br>
so that they could produce consumer<br>
goods<br>
like<br>
cigarettes,<br>
beverages,<br>
butter and soap to meet the needs of<br>
the colonial officials and commercial<br>
elites.<br>
<b>Significant</b><br>
development<br>

of the industrial sector, especially<br>
the<br>

was<br>
discouraged to make Africa a market<br>
for<br>
<b>European</b><br>
industrial<br>
products<br>
and a source of raw materials for<br>
<b>European industries. This created</b><br>
economic dependence, in that African<br>
colonies exported raw materials to earn<br>
foreign exchange that was used to buy<br>
manufactured goods such as clothes,<br>
machines, automobiles, oil, spare parts<br>
and medicines from Europe.<br>
<b>The colonial economy was exploitative</b><br>
in nature. Africans were exploited<br>
in various ways. The hut and poll<br>
taxes introduced contributed to the<br>
development of the colonial economy<br>
by forcing young men to work as<br>
migrant labourers to earn money for<br>
paying taxes. In Kenya, for example,<br>
the Nandi were evicted from their<br>
arable land and became landless.<br>
<b>Landless Africans and those who lived</b><br>
as squatters in settler-dominated areas<br>
were compelled to provide cheap labour<br>
on colonial farms and plantations, and<br>
in mines. For example, the Kikuyu in<br>
<b>Kenya were forced to work on settler</b><br>
farms in the Kenyan Highlands. The<br>
<b>Kikuyu, Kamba and Luo also worked as</b><br>
migrant labourers on sisal and coconut<br>
plantations in coastal Kenya. Others<br>
sold cash crops and livestock at low<br>
prices so as to pay taxes.<br>
<b>Another</b><br>
feature<br>
of<br>
the<br>
colonial<br>
economy was the use of one currency<br>
in the colonies and the mother country.<br>
<b>Moreover, any transaction between one</b><br>
colony and another involved using the<br>
currency of the colonising power. For<br>
example, in British East Africa, Kenya,<br>
<b>Uganda, Tanganyika and Zanzibar used</b><br>
the same currency called the shilling.<br>
<b>In French West Africa, all French</b><br>
colonies used the French franc in all<br>
commercial and financial transactions.<br>
<b>Furthermore, whatever was earned</b><br>
in the colony was deposited in the<br>
metropolitan banks, which contributed<br>
to the development of the metropolitan<br>
economy. For example, during the<br>
cocoa boom of the 1950s, African<br>
cocoa farmers from the Gold Coast<br>
complained that their money had been<br>
deposited in banks in London, instead<br>
of being deposited in the banks in Accra.<br>
<b>The major European banks opened</b><br>
branches in the colonies to facilitate<br>
trade between the colonies and the<br>
metropolitan countries. For example,<br>
the Barclays Bank Dominion, Colonies<br>
and<br>
<b>Overseas</b><br>
<b>Departments</b><br>
<b>(DC)</b><br>
enabled British traders to buy cocoa,<br>
coffee, tea, sisal, pyrethrum, palm<br>
oil, cotton and minerals from British<br>
<b>Africa. The Paris Bank also provided</b><br>
loans to French companies. By 1930,<br>
the French Compagnie Française  de<br>
l’Afrique Occidentale (CFAO) and Soci<br>

(in English known as Company of<br>
the Senegal and of the West Coast of<br>
<b>Africa) were involved in the palm oil</b><br>

<b>West African colonies of Nigeria, the</b><br>

<b>The UAC was the biggest of the three</b><br>

was the largest company in colonial<br>

<b>However, the value of the metropolitan</b><br>
currency was higher than the value of the<br>
colonial currency. This led to unequal<br>
exchange between the colonies and the<br>
metropolitan countries. Therefore, the<br>
colonial economy was characterised by<br>
a money economy. All the transactions<br>
and exchanges involved using money.<br>
<b>Besides, the colonial economy was</b><br>
characterised by the construction of<br>
roads and railways to facilitate colonial<br>
exploitation of African resources. They<br>
facilitated the transport of resources<br>
from the interior to the coast for<br>
shipment to Europe. It is not surprising<br>
that colonial railways and roads ran<br>
almost perpendicular to the coast.<br>
<b>In Nigeria, the British colonialists</b><br>
constructed a port in Lagos from which<br>
railway lines radiated to northern<br>
<b>Nigeria so as to transport groundnuts</b><br>
and cotton. The railway line also<br>
transported palm oil from central and<br>
eastern Nigeria to Port Harcourt. In<br>
<b>Tanganyika, the central railway line</b><br>
was extended from Tabora to Mwanza<br>
in 1928, from Manyoni to Kinyangiri<br>
in Singida in 1932 and from Kaliua to<br>
<b>Mpanda in 1949. The central railway</b><br>
line was used to transport cotton from<br>
the Lake Zone to the port of Dar es<br>
<b>Salaam before being exported to</b><br>
<b>Britain. In West Africa, the construction</b><br>
of railways took place between 1880<br>
and 1914. In 1914, a railway line was<br>
constructed to connect the palm oil<br>
belt of Sierra Leone to the coast. Other<br>
railways were built to connect mining<br>
centres to the coast, for example the<br>
83-km line connecting Marampa and<br>
<b>Pepel in Sierra Leone. Apart from that,</b><br>
the colonial transport infrastructure<br>
was used to transport manufactured<br>
goods from the coast to the interior.<br>
<b>The colonial transport infrastructure</b><br>
also facilitated the transpor of labourers<br>
from labour reserve areas to production<br>
areas.<br>
<b>Methods used to establish the</b><br>
colonial economy in Africa<br>
<b>The introduction of the colonial</b><br>
economy in Africa met with resistance<br>
from Africans, who were reluctant<br>
to conform to the demands and<br>
strategies of colonial production. Since<br>
the colonies were to produce raw<br>
materials for metropolitan industries,<br>
the colonial governments employed<br>
various techniques to achieve their<br>
aims. The colonialists transformed pre-<br>

133<br>
colonial African economies through<br>
preservation, creation and destruction<br>
methods.<br>
<b>The colonialists preserved traditional</b><br>
tools of production like the panga and<br>
the hand hoe. These tools remained<br>
the main instruments of production<br>
that Africans used. As the basic unit<br>
of production, the family was also<br>
retained. They also preserved peasant<br>
agriculture as one of the major sources<br>
of raw materials. Peasant production<br>
was preserved in colonies such as<br>
Uganda, Tanganyika and Nigeria.<br>
<b>In such areas, production was left</b><br>
in the hands of Africans who were<br>
supervised by local rulers on behalf<br>
of the colonial state. This was done to<br>
reduce production costs. Furthermore,<br>
in the British colonies, indirect rule<br>
was used; local rulers and traditional<br>
political institutions retained their<br>
positions<br>
but<br>
implemented<br>
the<br>
colonisers’ instructions. This helped to<br>
reduce resistance, administration costs<br>
and the costs of running the economy.<br>
<b>The creation method consisted of</b><br>
different mechanisms such as the<br>
introduction of  new methods of<br>
running the economy such as the use<br>
of money, taxes and the cash crops<br>
needed by European industries. The<br>
creation method was used to compel<br>
Africans to produce for the colonialists.<br>
<b>The introduction of a money economy</b><br>
forced Africans to work for their<br>
colonial masters. For example, taxes<br>
were imposed on Africans and had to<br>
be paid in cash. There were two main<br>
reasons for introducing taxes. First,<br>
taxes enabled the colonies to meet<br>
administration costs. Secondly, taxes<br>
compelled Africans to enter into the<br>
money economy by producing raw<br>
materials and supplying cheap labour<br>
to colonial enterprises. Thus, barter<br>
trade had declined in many parts of<br>
Africa by the 1920s.<br>
<b>The European colonialists also used</b><br>
the destruction method to destroy<br>
certain<br>
elements<br>
of<br>
pre-colonial<br>
economies which contradicted the<br>
colonial interests. For example, they<br>
discouraged<br>
local<br>
industries<br>
like<br>
handicraft industries. For example,<br>
in Belgian-Congo, the indigenous<br>
<b>Congolese who engaged in handcraft</b><br>
activities had their hands chopped<br>
off by the Belgians. In addition, local<br>
trading networks like long distance<br>
trade were discouraged so that the<br>
money economy could grow. The<br>
money economy enabled European<br>
traders and their companies to do<br>
business  without any competition.<br>

<b>Colonial economic reforms after the</b><br>
<b>Second World War</b><br>
<b>The Second World War ruined the</b><br>
<b>European economy. Europe witnessed</b><br>
heavy destruction in various sectors<br>
such as industries, agriculture and<br>
transport. The destruction caused the<br>
decline of the European economy.<br>
<b>Likewise, the imperialist powers sunk</b><br>
much money into the war, which<br>
left them economically exhausted.<br>
<b>Thus, after the Second World War,</b><br>
the colonialists came up with various<br>
economic plans and strategies to revive<br>
their economies. The strategies included<br>
reforming agriculture, industry, labour<br>
policies, transport and communication<br>
and commerce, as well as adopting<br>
long-term development plans.<br>
<b>Changes in colonial agriculture</b><br>
<b>Agriculture continued to be the</b><br>
backbone of the colonial economy in<br>
Africa throughout the colonial period.<br>
<b>This was because colonial agriculture</b><br>
was important to the survival of the<br>
metropolitan industries. Owing to the<br>
economic impact of the Second World<br>
<b>War, the colonialists made several</b><br>
changes in agricultural production to<br>
increase agricultural output for export.<br>
<b>For example, Britain and France</b><br>
introduced several policies aimed at<br>
improving agricultural productivity.<br>
<b>One strategy for increasing agricultural</b><br>
production was the establishment of<br>
progressive farmers. After the war, the<br>
<b>Europeans found that African farming</b><br>
methods and skills could not produce<br>
enough cash crops. Thus, there was a<br>
need to establish progressive farmers.<br>
<b>Such farmers received training from</b><br>
colonial agricultural officers in how<br>
they should use modern farming<br>
methods.  The colonial agricultural<br>
officers introduced modern farming<br>
methods to African farmers, hoping that<br>
agricultural production and productivity<br>
would increase. The methods included<br>
the use of tractors and fertilizers, the<br>
control of soil erosion and the control<br>
of pests that destroyed  crops. For<br>
example, in all British colonies in<br>
<b>East and Central Africa, the nurturing</b><br>
of progressive farmers became an<br>
official policy during the 1940s and<br>
1950s. In Kenya there were the Land<br>
<b>Consolidation Programme of 1953 and</b><br>
the Swynnerton Plan of 1954. These<br>
were intended to develop independent<br>
farmers. In Northern Rhodesia there<br>
was the African Farming Improvement<br>
<b>Scheme, mainly in the southern and</b><br>
eastern provinces. There were similar<br>
projects in Uganda and Tanganyika.<br>
<b>The establishment of agricultural</b><br>
schemes was another strategy aimed at<br>
deepening the exploitation of Africans.<br>
<b>The colonial governments introduced</b><br>
agricultural schemes to recover their<br>
economies, which had been destroyed<br>
by the Second World War. The schemes<br>
encouraged African farmers to adopt<br>
modern farming techniques to increase<br>

<b>Agricultural</b><br>
schemes<br>
concentrated on, among other things,<br>
crop production, soil conservation and<br>
animal husbandry.<br>
<b>Another strategy was the establishment</b><br>
and expansion of marketing boards.<br>
<b>The colonialists established marketing</b><br>
boards to control and supervise the<br>
production and marketing of cash crops<br>
in Africa. For example, a cocoa board<br>
was established in the Gold Coast in<br>
1947. The West Africa Produce Control<br>
<b>Board was also established to purchase</b><br>
cocoa from all West African countries.<br>
<b>In other areas in Africa, the marketing</b><br>
boards and cooperatives established<br>
in the 1920s and 1930s experienced<br>
massive expansion during this time.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
the<br>
colonialists<br>
introduced settlement and population<br>
schemes. The schemes were aimed at<br>
getting arable land for agriculture. For<br>
instance, after the Second World War,<br>
in Southern Rhodesia people were<br>
moved from densely populated areas to<br>
less populated areas so that agricultural<br>
activities could be expanded, especially<br>
maize farming and dairy farming.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
colonialists<br>
also<br>
introduced<br>
changes in labour recruitment to<br>
increase production. The expansion of<br>
agricultural activities went hand in hand<br>
with the growing need for labourers,<br>
hence the need for a labour-recruitment<br>
system. For example, in 1947 the<br>
indigenous people aged between 18<br>
and 55 in the Portuguese colonies were<br>
forced to work for six months on the<br>
<b>Portuguese plantations. In Tanganyika,</b><br>
migrant labour was intensified and the<br>
<b>Sisal Labour Bureau (SILABU) was</b><br>
introduced to recruit labourers for sisal<br>
plantations. As already pointed out,<br>
migrant labourers came from Kigoma,<br>
Tabora, Singida, Songea and Mbeya.<br>
<b>Objectives of introducing progressive</b><br>
farmers<br>
<b>Many African farmers did not have</b><br>
enough capital for buying farming<br>
tools and equipments such as tractors,<br>
fertilizers,<br>
improved<br>
seeds<br>
and<br>
irrigation equipment. These were used<br>
at various stages of farming from soil<br>
preparation, ploughing to harvesting.<br>
<b>Consequently, in 1945, the Europeans</b><br>
identified a few places in Africa in<br>
which to open demonstration farms<br>
for progressive farmers. Progressive<br>
farmers, also called master farmers,<br>
would be taught modern agricultural<br>
practices like the use of pesticides,<br>
insecticides,<br>
fertilizers,<br>
extension<br>
services and tractors. Moreover, they<br>
were given technical assistance. Other<br>
peasants or farmers were expected to<br>
learn the modern farming practices<br>
from the progressive farmers.<br>
<b>One of the objectives of promoting</b><br>
progressive farmers was to control the<br>
problem of soil erosion in the colonies.<br>
<b>Soil erosion reduced productivity</b><br>
in many areas in Africa during the<br>
expansion of crop production. For<br>
example,<br>
peasants<br>
expanded<br>
the<br>

<b>Kahama in Sukumaland in Tanganyika</b><br>
in the 1930s and 1940s, which led to<br>

and the Central Province in Dodoma<br>
and Singida in Tanganyika. Progressive<br>
farmers planted trees and constructed<br>

from using fertilizers, they used manure<br>
on their farms. Mulching was also used<br>
to keep the soil moist and damp, hence<br>
improving crop production.<br>
<b>Another</b><br>
objective<br>
was<br>
to<br>
train<br>
progressive<br>

who<br>
would<br>

farmers who, according to the Europe<br>
and were still primitive. The colonial<br>
government established agricultural<br>
colleges to train African agricultural<br>
field and extension officers who would<br>
disseminate the knowledge gained to<br>
progressive farmers. The latter were<br>
expected to transmit the knowledge<br>
gained to their fellow Africans who<br>
did not have it. The main objective<br>
was to improve agricultural production<br>
in the colonies. In Tanganyika, for<br>

and other kinds of grain, and Tumbi in<br>
<b>Tabora, which specialised in tobacco</b><br>
research. These colleges produced<br>
hybrid or improved seeds and trained<br>
more agricultural extension officers to<br>
assist progressive farmers.<br>
<b>In Tanganyika, progressive farmers</b><br>
were found at Oldeani in Mbulu, where<br>
they cultivated wheat, Rungwe, where<br>
they grew coffee and tea, Isimani, where<br>
they grew maize, and Sukumaland,<br>
where they grew cotton. They were also<br>
found in Nachingwea and Kongwa,<br>
where they grew groundnuts and<br>
Urambo, where tobacco was produced.<br>
<b>The notable agricultural innovation</b><br>
during this period was mechanisation,<br>
which came with tractors. Agricultural<br>
mechanisation<br>
increased<br>
as<br>
the<br>
growing number of African progressive<br>
farmers who owned tractors increased.<br>
<b>In addition, many African peasants</b><br>
engaged in ox-ploughing in the Lake<br>
<b>Province, west Kilimanjaro, the Mount</b><br>
Meru area and the Southern Province.<br>
<b>Despite their achievements such as the</b><br>
rise in production, mechanisation and<br>
soil conservation, progressive farmers<br>
experienced certain problems. For<br>
example, African peasants accused<br>
progressive farmers of exploiting them<br>
as progressive farmers owned large<br>
pieces of land and needed to exploit<br>
labour from poor peasants. This system<br>
also led to the emergence of classes<br>
among the peasants. Thus, progressive<br>
farmers formed a class of rich peasants.<br>

<b>Agricultural schemes</b><br>
<b>The period after the Second World</b><br>
<b>War witnessed an increase in the</b><br>
colonial government's involvement in<br>
agriculture. The colonialists introduced<br>
large-scale agricultural projects in<br>
their colonies to increase agricultural<br>
production.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
agricultural<br>
schemes were aimed at increasing<br>
and controlling the production and<br>
marketing of cash crops, particularly<br>
cotton and groundnuts. Thousands<br>
of acres of land were used to grow<br>
cotton in Mwanza and Shinyanga, as<br>
well as groundnuts in Nachingwea and<br>
<b>Kongwa. The following were some of</b><br>
the agricultural schemes introduced in<br>
<b>Tanganyika:</b><br>
<b>Kongwa, Urambo and Nachingwea</b><br>
agricultural schemes<br>
<b>After the Second World War, there was</b><br>
a severe shortage of edible fats and oil<br>
in Britain. Samuel Frank, the Director-<br>
<b>General of United Africa Company,</b><br>
a subsidiary company of Uniliver<br>
<b>Company which manufactured soap</b><br>
and margarine like Blue Band, asked<br>
the British Labour Government to<br>
start groundnut schemes in Kenya,<br>
the Rhodesias and Tanganyika. The<br>
project was expected to cost around<br>
£24 million, which would be provided<br>
by the British government in London.<br>
<b>In Tanganyika, the John Wakefield</b><br>
<b>Commission was formed in 1946 to</b><br>
study the establishment of schemes. It<br>
was recommended that such schemes<br>
should be established in Kongwa<br>
and Urambo in central Tanzania and<br>
Nachingwea in southern Tanzania.<br>
<b>Corporations were formed to manage</b><br>
the schemes. First came Overseas Food<br>
<b>Corporation in 1948 and Tanganyika</b><br>
<b>Agricultural</b><br>
<b>Corporation,</b><br>
which<br>
replaced the former in the 1950s.<br>
<b>Implementation involved land clearing</b><br>
using heavy types of machinery such<br>
as bulldozers, graders and planters. Up<br>
to the end of the 1950s, the schemes<br>
had cost £35 million. That amount<br>
exceeded the government expenditure<br>
for 1946 and 1950.<br>
<b>Effects of agricultural schemes in</b><br>
<b>Africa</b><br>
<b>Agricultural schemes increased the</b><br>
demand for forced labour. Africans<br>
had to spend much time implementing<br>
the projects. The schemes contributed<br>
to food shortages as Africans in some<br>
places were forced out of their land<br>
to give way for the implementation of<br>
the projects. Much time was invested<br>
in the production of cash crops at the<br>
expense of food crops. The schemes<br>
left Africans on barren land or made<br>
them landless.<br>
<b>Moreover, the schemes increased the</b><br>
number of migrant labourers. Most<br>
young men from Nachingwea, Urambo<br>
and Mpwapwa migrated to the places<br>
where<br>
agricultural<br>
schemes<br>
were<br>
being implemented to search for wage<br>
labour. As a result, the labour reserve<br>
areas were deprived of their labour and<br>

<b>The agricultural schemes also led to</b><br>
the occurrence of resistance in areas<br>
like Kongwa, Urambo, Usambara and<br>
<b>Ukiliguru. In such areas, Africans</b><br>
expressed their discontent through<br>
boycotts, demonstrations and strikes.<br>
<b>They were unwilling to surrender their</b><br>
land to the Europeans and to be forced<br>
to work in the agricultural schemes.<br>
<b>In general, the agricultural schemes</b><br>
were expected to boost agricultural<br>
production, thereby increasing raw<br>
materials for European industries<br>
and food production in the colonies.<br>
<b>The modernisation of agricultural</b><br>
production was, therefore, inevitable.<br>
<b>The modernisation process favoured</b><br>
a small class of African peasants<br>
(progressive farmers), but the majority<br>
were<br>
victims<br>
of<br>
forced<br>
labour,<br>
destocking, land appropriation and<br>
forced cultivation of cash crops.<br>
<b>Africans were not only forced to work</b><br>
in the schemes, but also lost their<br>
fertile land. The majority of peasants in<br>
different parts of Tanganyika protested<br>
against the colonial projects, which<br>
meant that the colonial system was<br>
increasingly becoming weak towards<br>
the 1960s. The protests were part of the<br>
larger nationalist struggles in Africa.<br>
<b>Impact of the agricultural schemes</b><br>
<b>The undertaking of the schemes yielded</b><br>
some good results. They included<br>
the construction of infrastructure,<br>
the building of dams, environmental<br>
conservation and improvement of<br>
the quality of health services. The<br>
agricultural schemes<br>
in<br>
<b>Kongwa,</b><br>
<b>Urambo and Nachingwea led to the</b><br>
improvement of the railway lines and<br>
roads to facilitate the transport of<br>
labourers to the farms and raw materials<br>
to the coast ready for shipment abroad.<br>
<b>The port of Mtwara, for example,</b><br>
was built to simplify the transport<br>
of raw materials to Britain. Health<br>
facilities were also built by the British<br>
colonialists after the Second World War<br>
because the colonialists needed healthy<br>
labourers. In the 1950s, the colonial<br>
government built more hospitals and<br>
dispensaries to maintain the health of<br>
those working in the schemes.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
<b>Europeans</b><br>
introduced<br>
environmental conservation measures<br>
that involved protecting wildlife and<br>
forests, and controlling soil erosion.<br>
<b>The conservation efforts were not</b><br>
intended to improve the quality of<br>
<b>Africans’ lives. Rather, they were</b><br>
introduced in the interest of the<br>
colonial masters. Reforestation took<br>
place in different parts of Tanganyika,<br>
especially in areas that had been<br>
seriously affected by soil erosion. The<br>
areas included Morogoro, where the<br>
<b>Uluguru Soil Conservation Scheme</b><br>
was introduced. Reafforestation also<br>
took place in Dodoma. In Sukumaland,<br>
the British introduced the Sukuma<br>
<b>Land Development Plan in 1947. It</b><br>

<b>Failure of the agricultural schemes</b><br>
in Africa<br>
<b>Despite the achievements discussed</b><br>
above, the implementation of the<br>
agricultural schemes was not an easy<br>
task on the part of the colonial officials.<br>
<b>Hence, by the early 1950s, almost</b><br>
all the schemes had died for several<br>
reasons.<br>
<b>African peasants generally opposed</b><br>
the schemes. The enforcement of<br>
compulsory agricultural practices and<br>
orders caused resistance. The colonial<br>
officers who supervised the schemes<br>
used force and threats during the<br>
implementation of the schemes. They<br>
frequently fined and even imprisoned<br>
<b>Africans who did not cooperate with</b><br>
them. Generally, African peasants were<br>
against the schemes.<br>
<b>For example, the Luguru refused</b><br>
to support the Uluguru Land Usage<br>
<b>Scheme for three reasons. First,</b><br>
they thought that the scheme was<br>
not adding any value to production.<br>
<b>For them, rice was doing very well</b><br>
in areas without terraces. Secondly,<br>
the implementation of the schemes<br>
was tedious and demanding in terms<br>
of labour and time. Thirdly, the<br>
traditional chiefs who supervised the<br>
work oppressed their fellow Africans.<br>
<b>In 1955, when the British continued</b><br>
to attempt using forced labour in the<br>
<b>Uluguru scheme, the Luguru reacted</b><br>
by rioting. Similar riots occurred in<br>
the Usambara Highlands in 1957. The<br>
<b>Sambaa resisted compulsory ridging</b><br>
and terracing. As a result, the schemes<br>
were abandoned by the British colonial<br>
authority.<br>
<b>In</b><br>
<b>Sukumaland,</b><br>
the<br>
colonial<br>
government introduced compulsory<br>
legislation and cattle destocking. The<br>
government imposed a compulsory<br>
cattle tax, cattle dipping fees and<br>
compulsory cultivation of cotton. The<br>
<b>Sukuma refused to comply with any</b><br>
one of these regulations. The colonial<br>
government reacted by arresting some<br>
peasants. The peasants organised riots<br>
in 1953 and 1954, which forced the<br>
colonial government to abandon the<br>
development schemes.<br>
<b>Above all, Sukuma peasants regarded</b><br>
the new agricultural changes as a threat<br>
to their economic security and culture.<br>
<b>As far as destocking was concerned, for</b><br>
example, the Sukuma were required by<br>
the law to slaughter a certain number<br>
of cattle annually to reduce the number<br>
of cattle. This law also demanded<br>
that livestock keepers give hides to<br>
the local government officers to show<br>
that they had slaughtered the cattle.<br>
<b>Destocking was, however, highly</b><br>
opposed by the Sukuma as it interfered<br>
with their culture. Economically, a<br>
person was considered wealthy if he<br>
owned a large herd of cattle. Socially,<br>
the Sukuma had for many years used<br>

of 1949, only 20 000 hectares of land<br>
had been cultivated and this work<br>
had consumed £35,870,000, which<br>
exceeded the original budget.<br>
<b>Moreover, poor planning by the</b><br>
colonial bureaucracy also contributed<br>
to the collapse of the agricultural<br>
schemes. For example, the areas around<br>
<b>Korogwe had salty soils, which did not</b><br>
support the growing of cotton or rice.<br>
<b>Similarly, the soil of Kongwa was clay,</b><br>
thus not appropriate for groundnut<br>
production.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
there<br>
was<br>
no<br>
enough research done before the<br>
implementation of the schemes. The<br>
<b>Colonial Agricultural Officers neither</b><br>
conducted thorough research on the<br>
type of soil suitable for the types of<br>
crops they wanted to cultivate nor did<br>
they study the weather of the areas<br>
where the schemes were introduced.<br>
<b>They</b><br>
also<br>
ignored<br>
indigenous<br>
knowledge of crop cultivation. For<br>
example, soon after the British had<br>
finished establishing the Kongwa<br>
<b>Groundnuts Scheme, they realised that</b><br>
the area received very little rainfall.<br>
<b>This situation hampered the progress of</b><br>
the schemes. In other places, however,<br>
the<br>
<b>Europeans</b><br>
constructed<br>
dams<br>

<b>Activity 5.1</b><br>
<b>Organise a class discussion on</b><br>
the reasons for the failure of the<br>
agricultural schemes established in<br>
colonial Tanganyika.<br>
<b>Cooperatives and marketing boards</b><br>
<b>The colonial government set up</b><br>
marketing boards to regulate the<br>
buying and selling of cash crops<br>
in the colonies. Likewise, African<br>
peasants formed cooperative societies<br>
to encourage cash crop production.<br>
<b>The colonial governments established</b><br>
most of the cooperatives and marketing<br>
boards in the 1930s and 1940s to<br>
supervise and regulate crop production<br>
and<br>
marketing<br>
in<br>
Africa.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
institutions were established to solve<br>
two major economic problems which<br>
were facing African peasants. The<br>
first problem related to the price of the<br>
cash crops produced by peasants. The<br>
second problem related to the question<br>
of markets for the crops produced.<br>
<b>To address the problems, the colonial</b><br>
government set up marketing boards<br>
and cooperatives. They, for example,<br>
fixed and harmonised the prices of<br>
the cash crops produced by African<br>
peasants.<br>
<b>In order to facilitate the workings of</b><br>
cooperative societies in Tanganyika<br>
for instance, the colonial government<br>
passed the first cooperative law in<br>
1932. After that, many cooperatives<br>
were established, especially after the<br>
<b>Second World War. Examples of the</b><br>
cooperatives and marketing boards<br>
introduced were the Bukoba Coffee<br>
<b>Control Board under Clemens Kiiza</b><br>
(established in 1941), the Cocoa<br>
<b>Marketing Board established in Ghana</b><br>
in 1948 and the Coffee Marketing Board<br>
established in Uganda in 1956. Other<br>
examples of cooperative unions were<br>
the Kilimanjaro Native Cooperative<br>
<b>Union (KNCU) founded in 1930 by</b><br>
<b>Charles Dundas, the British District</b><br>
<b>Commissioner of Moshi, which was</b><br>
registered in 1933, the Ngoni-Matengo<br>
<b>Cooperative registered in 1936 and the</b><br>
<b>Lake Province Growers Association,</b><br>
which later changed its name to the<br>
<b>Victoria Federation of Cooperative</b><br>
<b>Unions. It  was founded by Paul</b><br>
<b>Bomani in 1950. These cooperative</b><br>
unions bought coffee and cotton from<br>
<b>African farmers who were registered</b><br>
members and sold both to other traders.<br>
<b>The main aim of the cooperative was to</b><br>
undermine the position of middlemen<br>
like Indians and Arabs, who were<br>
paying Africans very little money.<br>
<b>The cooperatives also controlled price</b><br>
fluctuation.<br>
<b>Despite the importance of the marketing</b><br>
boards and cooperatives, they were accused<br>

when the boards and cooperatives set<br>

from the Bukoba Coffee Board, which<br>
in 1947 signed a seven-year contract at<br>
£65 a tonne to sell coffee to the British<br>
<b>Ministry of Food and Supply. The ministry,</b><br>

to private British agencies and in 1950,<br>
when the price of coffee rose to £132 a<br>

taken by the agencies rather than the<br>
peasants.<br>
<b>Changes in the colonial industrial</b><br>
policies<br>
<b>The industrial sector was not given</b><br>
sufficient consideration as other sectors<br>
such as agriculture. However, some<br>
industries were established in colonial<br>
<b>Africa. The European colonial state</b><br>
encouraged the establishment of two<br>
types of industries in the colonies.<br>
<b>The first form was that of extractive</b><br>
industries.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
industries<br>
mainly<br>
extracted or processed minerals like<br>
gold, diamond, limestone and caustic<br>
soda. The second form was that of<br>
agricultural<br>
processing<br>
factories<br>
such as sisal decortications, cotton<br>
ginneries, coffee hulling machines<br>
and tobacco curing industries. These<br>
industries were built to reduce the<br>
cost of transporting the products from<br>
the interior to the ports and then onto<br>
<b>Europe. All agricultural processing</b><br>
industries were aimed at reducing the<br>
bulkiness of agricultural raw materials<br>
before being exported to Europe.<br>
<b>The industries depended on African</b><br>
labourers, who were paid low wages,<br>
although they worked for many hours.<br>
<b>This was done to maximise profits and</b><br>
minimise production costs. A small<br>
number of Europeans were employed<br>
in industries as administrators and<br>
supervisors.<br>
<b>During</b><br>
the<br>
<b>Second</b><br>
<b>World War, the European nations</b><br>
got affected. The war prevented<br>
<b>European commercial ships from</b><br>
transporting consumer goods such as<br>
soap, cosmetics, sugar, shoes, textiles,<br>
blankets, cheese, butter, margerine,<br>
whisky, wine, cigarettes, tinned meat<br>
and mattresses to the colonies. The<br>
goods were largely consumed by the<br>
<b>European officials and settlers who</b><br>
lived in the colonies. The scarcity of<br>
consumer goods forced the colonial<br>
state to establish import-substitution<br>
industries<br>
(ISIs).<br>
<b>The</b><br>
industries<br>
replaced those of Europe which<br>
produced similar consumer goods.<br>
<b>Another change that happened after</b><br>
the war related to the international<br>
institutions, companies and firms that<br>
financed import-substitution industries.<br>
<b>For example, the International Bank</b><br>
for Reconstruction and Development<br>
(now the World Bank), which was<br>
formed in 1946, began to sponsor major<br>
economic projects in some African<br>
colonies with the purpose of expanding<br>
industrial production. For example, the<br>
bank sponsored the construction of the<br>

<b>Kariba Dam to produce hydroelectric</b><br>
power for many import-substitution<br>
industries in Salisbury (Harare) in<br>
<b>Southern Rhodesia. It also funded</b><br>
copper mining in Northern Rhodesia.<br>
<b>In Uganda, in collaboration with the</b><br>
colonial state, Frobisher’s Company<br>
constructed a hydro-electric power<br>
plant at Owen Falls, near Jinja, in 1954.<br>
<b>With a steady supply of electricity,</b><br>
a textile factory was established in<br>
<b>Jinja. Meanwhile, electricity was</b><br>
also supplied to the copper industries<br>
in Kasese and Kilembe in western<br>
<b>Uganda. Surplus electricity was sold</b><br>
to Nairobi, Kenya, where most of the<br>
import-substitution industries had been<br>
opened. However, import-substitution<br>
industries were unevenly distributed<br>
within and between the colonies. In<br>
<b>East Africa, most of such industries</b><br>
were established in Kenya. Tanganyika<br>
had<br>
very<br>
few<br>
import-substitution<br>
industries. The few industries built in<br>
<b>Tanganyika included Tanganyika Meat</b><br>
<b>Packers, Tanganyika Cigarette and</b><br>
<b>Bata Shoe Factory. Most of these were</b><br>
located in Dar es Salaam.<br>
<b>Characteristics of import-substitution</b><br>
industries<br>
<b>Many import-substitution industries</b><br>
(ISIs) were located in towns. For<br>
instance, in Kenya the ISIs were<br>
located in Mombasa and Nairobi. In<br>
<b>Tanganyika, the ISIs were located in</b><br>
<b>Dar es Salaam. The ISIs were located in</b><br>
the towns where many consumers lived.<br>
<b>The ISIs dealt mainly with the production</b><br>
of simple consumer goods like juice,<br>
biscuits, sweets and cigarettes. There<br>
were no textile industries. Manufacturing<br>
industries hardly existed in Africa. Only<br>
light industries were established. Examples<br>
of the light industries established in the<br>
1940s included soap manufacturing<br>
industries in Nigeria, Kenya, Southern<br>
Rhodesia, the Gold Coast and Ivory Coast.<br>
<b>In the 1950s, brick-making and copper</b><br>
smelting industries were established in<br>
<b>Belgian-Congo and oven-making industries</b><br>
in Southern Rhodesia.<br>
The ISIs depended on African labourers.<br>
<b>In Tanganyika, for example, labourers</b><br>
were mainly obtained from various labour<br>
reserve regions such as Rukwa, Kigoma<br>
and Mtwara. In Nigeria and Ivory Coast,<br>
the migrant labourers working in both<br>
processing and extractive industries, as<br>
well as on plantations came from the<br>
interior colonies of Niger, Upper Volta<br>
(Burkina Faso) and French Sudan. African<br>
labourers did unskilled and semi-skilled<br>
jobs, while Europeans did skilled jobs<br>
like supervision and administration.<br>
<b>Moreover, simple technology was</b><br>
used in the industries. For example, a<br>
soap-making industry needed a simple<br>
machine and raw materials. Similarly,<br>
no formal training was given to the<br>
workers so that they could operate<br>
the machines. African workers only<br>
received training when they were at the<br>
operational centres.<br>

<b>Location of colonial industries</b><br>
<b>There were various factors that</b><br>
influenced the location of the industries<br>
established in colonial Africa. The<br>
factors are explained in the following<br>
section.<br>
<b>The choice of the location of industries</b><br>
in colonial Africa depended on the<br>
availability of raw materials. For<br>
example,<br>
industries<br>
were<br>
located<br>
in Dar es Salaam, Morogoro and<br>
<b>Tanga in Tanganyika because of the</b><br>
availability of sisal. Moreover, the<br>
availability of labourers was another<br>
factor that influenced the location<br>
of industries. For instance, Kenya<br>
had many industries because of its<br>
good system of recruiting labourers<br>
through the Kipande system. Likewise<br>
in Tanganyika, SILABU ensured a<br>
constant recruitment and supply of<br>
labourers to the sisal estates. Labourers<br>
were very useful in the running of the<br>
colonial economy, particularly in the<br>
agricultural and industrial sectors.<br>
<b>Furthermore, colonial industries were</b><br>
also located in areas which had markets<br>
for European manufactured goods. For<br>
instance, productive areas like areas<br>
with mines and plantations acted as<br>
markets for European manufactured<br>
goods. In such areas the labourers were<br>
potential consumers of manufactured<br>
goods.<br>
<b>Furthermore, colonial industries were</b><br>
located into areas which had good<br>
transport systems. For example, in<br>
<b>Tanganyika industries were located</b><br>
in Dar es Salaam, Morogoro and<br>
<b>Tanga, while in Kenya industries were</b><br>
concentrated in Nairobi, Mombasa and<br>
<b>Kisumu. In Nigeria, industries were</b><br>
concentrated in Lagos, in the Gold<br>
<b>Coast industries were built in Accra</b><br>
and in Southern Rhodesia industries<br>
were located in Salisbury.<br>
<b>Although the colonial state introduced</b><br>
major changes in the industrial sector,<br>
the sector was not treated with the<br>
same importance as the agricultural<br>
and mining sectors. This was done<br>
to limit competition with European<br>
industries. Africa had to continue being<br>
a producer of raw materials and a buyer<br>
of European manufactured goods.<br>
<b>Activity 5.2</b><br>
<b>Prepare the points that you will</b><br>
use either to support or oppose the<br>
proposition that the changes made to<br>
the colonial industrial policy benefited<br>
Africans after the Second World War.<br>
<b>Changes in the colonial trade</b><br>
<b>The colonial trade was one of</b><br>
the<br>
important<br>
economic<br>
sectors<br>
which created wealth for European<br>
imperialists. The imperialists imposed<br>
their own system of trade on Africans,<br>
while at the same time distorting<br>
<b>Africa’s commercial interests. It is</b><br>
clear that the pattern of colonial trade<br>
in African colonies was geared towards<br>

<b>European manufactured goods they</b><br>

<b>The</b><br>
colonial<br>
commercial<br>
sector<br>
was dominated by such European<br>
companies as Smith Makenzie in<br>
<b>East Africa, United Africa Company</b><br>
in British West Africa and SCOA<br>
and CFAO in French Africa. These<br>
companies dominated over 60 per<br>
cent of the import-export trade in the<br>
colonies. In British East Africa and<br>
<b>British Central Africa, Indians acted as</b><br>
middlemen by supplying the European<br>
goods they obtained from European<br>
commercial companies to the rural<br>
areas, where Arabs and Africans had<br>
established retail shops. In British<br>
<b>West Africa, the Lebanese and Syrian</b><br>
merchants served as middlemen.<br>
<b>The colonial state formulated policies</b><br>
which enabled European merchants to<br>
dominate the trade. European merchants<br>
owned capital and, therefore, they were<br>
the main beneficiaries of the exchange<br>
system. Apart from European traders,<br>
<b>Indians, Lebanese and Syrians were</b><br>
given licences to buy cash crops directly<br>
from African peasants and sell them<br>
to European commercial companies.<br>
<b>This situation denied Africans the</b><br>
opportunity to participate either in the<br>
wholesale or the retail trade.<br>
<b>Before 1945, the colonial trade policy</b><br>
explicitly stated that Africans should<br>
not be given trade licences. Africans<br>
had to remain suppliers of labour in<br>
various colonial economic sectors<br>
and producers of raw materials. After<br>
1945, however, the colonial state<br>
introduced some changes into the<br>
commercial sector. One of the major<br>
changes made was allowing Africans<br>
to have access to and to apply for trade<br>
licences. However, they lacked capital,<br>
experience and entrepreneurial skills<br>
for undertaking trade and commerce.<br>
<b>As noted earlier, the import-export trade</b><br>
remained under European companies.<br>
<b>By the 1950s, some African peasants in</b><br>
<b>East and West Africa had begun to invest</b><br>
in retail trade using the capital they had<br>
accumulated after selling cash crops.<br>
<b>Successful African businessmen raised</b><br>
their share of the import-export trade,<br>
which previously had been dominated<br>
by European trading companies. For<br>
example, in Nigeria, the African share<br>
of the export-import trade (EIT) rose<br>
from 5 per cent in 1949 to 20 per cent<br>
in the late 1950s and early 1960s.<br>
<b>The changes made to the colonial trade</b><br>
policy were also accompanied by high<br>
rates of exploiting African peasants<br>
by European merchants and Asian<br>
businessmen. The marketing boards<br>
became a major tool for exploiting<br>
<b>Africans. The boards set low prices</b><br>
for the cash crops produced by African<br>
peasants. In colonial Tanganyika,<br>
for example, in 1951, Africans who<br>
produced cotton were not paid the<br>

after 1945. This was due to the fact that<br>

invested money in their businesses to<br>
maximise profit.<br>
<b>Changes in colonial labour policies</b><br>
<b>European economic activities depended</b><br>
on African labour. Labourers were<br>
needed on plantations, in mines and in<br>
the construction of roads and railways.<br>
<b>In essence, the structure of colonial</b><br>
labour that had been established before<br>
1945 remained almost the same. This<br>
was because the colonialists did not<br>
change the colonial economic sectors<br>
after the Second World War. Thus,<br>
colonial plantations, farms and mines<br>
continued to depend on migrant labour.<br>
<b>However, minor changes were made</b><br>
to the labour policies owing to the<br>
growing demand for labourers during<br>
and after the Second World War. Hence,<br>
the colonial governments came up with<br>
some new policies to appropriate the<br>
changing colonial economic system.<br>
<b>Migrant labour</b><br>
<b>As pointed out earlier, migrant labour</b><br>
involved people moving from their<br>
homes and going to production areas<br>
to work in colonial economic projects<br>
such as mines and plantations. Migrant<br>
labourers were divided into two<br>
categories. First, labourers moved from<br>
one place to another within a given<br>
colony. This was referred to as intra-<br>
territorial migrant labour. For example,<br>
labourers moved from Kigoma to<br>
<b>Morogoro. In the second category,</b><br>
labourers moved from one colony to<br>
another. For example, they moved from<br>
<b>Belgian-Congo to Tanganyika or from</b><br>
<b>Niger to Nigeria. This was referred to</b><br>
as inter-territorial migrant labour.<br>
<b>Migrant labourers were usually paid</b><br>
very low wages. Most of them were<br>
either<br>
unskilled<br>
or<br>
semi-skilled.<br>
<b>The low wages paid forced them to</b><br>
continue working in colonial economic<br>
projects for a long time. Besides,<br>
they lived in compounds  belonging<br>
to the companies they were working<br>
for. For example, migrant labourers<br>
lived in houses belonging to the sisal<br>
companies in Morogoro, Kilosa and<br>
<b>Tanga, and this made them work for a</b><br>
long period. Thus, migrant labour was<br>
cheap. Furthermore, migrant labour<br>
was safe. The capitalist preferred using<br>
unskilled migrant labourers because<br>
they could  not demand their rights. So,<br>
they could easily be exploited.<br>
<b>Furthermore, migrant labourers worked</b><br>
under very poor conditions, including<br>
poor safety facilities or protective gear.<br>
<b>For example, miners worked without</b><br>
personal protective gear. They suffered<br>
from whooping cough and tuberculosis<br>
(TB) because they inhaled large<br>
amounts of dust and worked in an<br>

147<br>
unsafe environment. In addition, they<br>
lacked basic social welfare. Generally,<br>
migrant labourers had no right to social<br>
services like education. They also<br>
lacked reliable health services.<br>
<b>Methods used to obtain labourers</b><br>
<b>There were a number of methods which</b><br>
the colonialists used to get labourers.<br>
<b>One such method was recruiting</b><br>
labourers<br>
from<br>
distant<br>
places,<br>
especially from non-productive areas.<br>
<b>The colonial governments thought that</b><br>
by getting labourers from far away it<br>
would be difficult for the labourers to<br>
escape and return home. Moreover, the<br>
environment and surroundings would<br>
be too alien for them to organise any<br>
kind of revolt.<br>
<b>Another way of obtaining labourers</b><br>
was the use of force. Forced labour<br>
appeared in different forms. It was not<br>
very much different from slave labour.<br>
<b>In both cases, force was used and a</b><br>
labourer was not paid. The difference<br>
was that under forced labour the<br>
labourer was not owned. In this case, a<br>
number of villages were allocated to a<br>
settler. Each day, the village headman<br>
had to provide a specific number of<br>
labourers to the settler. There were<br>
many instances of forced labour in<br>
<b>Africa. In Belgian-Congo, for example,</b><br>
villagers were forced to collect wild<br>
rubber. In 1947, the Portuguese forced<br>
adult males aged between 18 and 55 to<br>
work on their plantations.<br>
<b>The eviction of Africans from their</b><br>
land and taking them to reserves or<br>
barren land was also a mechanism for<br>
obtaining labourers. Africans were<br>
evicted from fertile land  and taken<br>
to reserves or overcrowded areas as<br>
squatters. The capitalists grabbed fertile<br>
land for opening farms and plantations,<br>
as well as mines to extract precious<br>
metals like gold, diamond, copper and<br>
silver. Africans had no option but to sell<br>
their labour power to the colonialists<br>
for their survival. By the end of the<br>
colonial period, for example, the<br>
amount of land expropriated in Kenya<br>
was 7.68 million acres of the most<br>
fertile land. In Northern Rhodesia,<br>
about 10.4 million acres of land had<br>
been expropriated.<br>
<b>Another method was establishment of</b><br>
organisations for recruiting labourers.<br>
<b>For instance, in Tanganyika, there was</b><br>
<b>SILABU. SILABU recruited many</b><br>
people from various districts for sisal<br>
plantations. Thus, by the 1940s the<br>
<b>Makonde and Yao labourers formed the</b><br>
backbone of the Tanga sisal industry.<br>
<b>There were  also many labourers from</b><br>
<b>Rwanda and Burundi on the sisal</b><br>
plantations located along the coast of<br>
<b>Tanganyika. In the 1940s and 1950s, the</b><br>
<b>South African Witwatersrand Native</b><br>
<b>Labour Agency (WNLA) had branches</b><br>
in Nyasaland and Tanganyika and<br>
recruited a large number of labourers<br>
from Nyasaland and the Southern<br>
<b>Highlands of Tanganyika for the gold</b><br>
mines in South Africa.<br>

<b>The colonial state also introduced</b><br>
various taxes which were supposed<br>
to be paid in cash. For example, in<br>
<b>Tanganyika there were head and hut</b><br>
taxes. Likewise, in Kenya and Uganda<br>
there were similar taxes. This situation<br>
forced Africans to work on the colonial<br>
plantations and in the mines to get<br>
money for paying taxes.<br>
<b>The colonialists also created labour</b><br>
reserve<br>
zones.<br>
<b>For</b><br>
instance,<br>
in<br>
<b>Tanganyika labour reserve zones were</b><br>
established in areas such as Kigoma,<br>
<b>Tabora, Rukwa and Dodoma. These</b><br>
areas were considered less productive;<br>
hence, they had to supply labour to the<br>
colonial economic activities.<br>
<b>Reasons for using migrant labourers</b><br>
<b>The colonialists preferred using migrant</b><br>
labourers because migrant labourers<br>
could not escape from the production<br>
centres. This is because they worked in<br>
a new environment, which was very far<br>
from their homes. They were also new<br>
to the areas. That is why it was very<br>
difficult for them to hide or escape.<br>
<b>Migrant labourers were also preferred</b><br>
because they could work for a long<br>
time and were always available.  They<br>
lived in the camps found near where<br>
they worked.<br>
<b>Migrant labourers were preferred to</b><br>
other labourers because they could not<br>
resist easily and, therefore, they were<br>
politically safe. Migrant labourers<br>
came<br>
from<br>
different<br>
areas<br>
with<br>
diverse ethnic origins and historical<br>
backgrounds. It was difficult for<br>
them to organise themselves because<br>
they were not familiar to each other.<br>
<b>Migrant labourers easily accepted the</b><br>
demands of the colonialists because of<br>
their economic and social conditions.<br>
<b>The colonialists also preferred using</b><br>
migrant<br>
labourers<br>
because<br>
they<br>
were also a market for European<br>
manufactured goods. For instance,<br>
the South African Boers used migrant<br>
labourers from Southern Rhodesia,<br>
<b>Mozambique and Namibia who were</b><br>
a source of market for their finished<br>
goods. Generally, migrant labourers<br>
served as a lucrative market for<br>
<b>European goods such as clothes, shoes,</b><br>
drinks and  bicycles.<br>
<b>Migrant labour was also preferred</b><br>
because it was cheap. This is because<br>
labourers were paid low wages and<br>
sometimes the wages were paid by<br>
installment. In this respect, it was<br>
profitable to the employers.<br>

labour in colonial Africa?<br>
(b)	 Does the same prevail in<br>
the post-colonial African<br>

<b>Expansion of labour and labour</b><br>
migration after 1945<br>
<b>After the Second World War, there was a</b><br>
need to increase the number of migrant<br>
labourers on the colonial plantations<br>
and in the mines. Before the Second<br>
<b>World War, migrant labourers in</b><br>
<b>Tanganyika mainly came from Kigoma</b><br>
and Tabora, but after the Second World<br>
<b>War they came from Rukwa, Ruvuma,</b><br>
Mbeya, Singida and Dodoma.<br>
<b>The post-war period also witnessed the</b><br>
expansion of technical infrastructure,<br>
including roads and railways that<br>
facilitated the transport of migrant<br>
labourers in Africa. In Tanganyika, for<br>
example, infrastructure was expanded<br>
following the establishment of various<br>
schemes in Nachingwea, Kongwa and<br>
<b>Urambo. Thus, massive labour was</b><br>
needed in the construction of roads<br>
and railways in order to facilitate the<br>
transport of the crops produced in the<br>
schemes.<br>
<b>The establishment of agricultural</b><br>
processing<br>
and<br>
import-substitution<br>
industries needed labour. Examples of<br>
processing industries in Tanganyika<br>
were Tanganyika Packers Limited in<br>
<b>Dar es Salaam and cotton ginneries in</b><br>
<b>Shinyanga and Geita. The establishment</b><br>
of these industries increased the<br>
demand for more labourers, who were<br>
needed to revamp the economy, which<br>
had been destroyed by the Second<br>
World War.<br>
<b>The establishment of agricultural</b><br>
schemes led to a high demand for<br>
migrant labourers. In Tanganyika,<br>
the colonial government established<br>
many projects, including those of<br>
Nachingwea, Kongwa and Uluguru.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
agricultural<br>
schemes<br>
needed<br>
many labourers. To ensure that there<br>
was a constant supply of labour, the<br>
colonial government introduced labour<br>
contracts that ranged from 6 to 12<br>
months. In order to reduce expenses,<br>
initially, migrant labourers were not<br>
allowed to go to the mines or plantations<br>
with their wives and children. The<br>
colonial employers provided food to<br>
all migrant labourers. However, this<br>
system changed after1945 because<br>
it was found to be very expensive.<br>
<b>Henceforth, migrant labourers were</b><br>
allowed to go with their families to the<br>
mines and plantations. The colonial<br>
authorities also stopped providing food<br>
to the labourers. The labourers could<br>
now use their monthly wages to buy<br>
all their families’ needs. They were no<br>
longer dependent on their  employers.<br>
<b>Impact of the changes in colonial</b><br>
labour policies<br>
<b>The changes made to the colonial</b><br>
labour policies had various kinds of<br>
impact. Some of the impacts are:<br>
<b>The changes in the colonial labour</b><br>
policies in Africa led to the expansion<br>
and increase of movement of people<br>
from their home areas to other places<br>
in search of jobs. In the case of<br>

<b>The changes also increased the number</b><br>
of<br>
educational<br>
opportunities.<br>
<b>For</b><br>
example, in the production areas like<br>
<b>Kilimanjaro, schools were built to train</b><br>
people in how to use better farming<br>
methods to increase productivity. The<br>
educational centres such as schools<br>
started to make social and economic<br>
differences among Africans.<br>
<b>The changes also led to the growth of</b><br>
urban centres, which were the result of<br>
the movement of people from rural areas<br>
to urban centres. Some people moved<br>
from rural areas to towns, believing that<br>
life was better in the towns. Eventually,<br>
they became a source of labour for the<br>
colonial industries and other projects.<br>
<b>Towns such as Morogoro and Tanga</b><br>
grew as a result of the movement of<br>
people who were seeking employment.<br>
<b>Moreover, the changes led to the</b><br>
expansion of agricultural production.<br>
<b>The new labour policies made labour</b><br>
abundant and available. For instance, in<br>
<b>Tanganyika, soon after the colonial labour</b><br>
policies changed, new schemes were<br>
initiated such as the groundnut schemes<br>
because labour was available. The same<br>
scenario was common in colonial Africa<br>
after the Second World War.<br>
<b>Eventually, the changes increased the</b><br>
exploitation, harshness and brutality<br>
that Africans suffered. All these led to<br>
the rise of working class movements<br>
which fought for workers’ rights. This<br>
marked the beginning of trade unions<br>
such as the Union of Dock Workers in<br>
<b>Tanganyika and the Rhodesia Railway</b><br>
Employees’ Association.<br>
<b>Workers’ movements</b><br>
<b>Workers’</b><br>
movements<br>
were<br>
organisations that Africans formed to<br>
fight for their rights. The movements<br>
were formed from various workers’<br>
welfare associations and trade unions,<br>
which<br>
represented<br>
teachers<br>
and<br>
industrial, dock and railway workers.<br>
<b>Examples</b><br>
of<br>
workers’<br>
welfare<br>
associations<br>
in<br>
<b>Tanganyika</b><br>
were<br>
the Union of the Commercial Road<br>
<b>Transport Workers, the Union of Dock</b><br>
Workers and Railways Workers’ Union.<br>
<b>Apart from these welfare associations,</b><br>
there was one prominent trade union<br>
called Tanganyika Federation of Labour<br>
(TFL), which was under the leadership<br>
of Rashid Mfaume Kawawa.<br>
<b>The African working-class welfare</b><br>
associations and trade unions, which<br>
emerged after 1945, had certain<br>
characteristics. Most leaders were<br>
responsible for organising the workers<br>
over a period of time rather than<br>
emerging as spokesmen in particular<br>
protests. Furthermore, the working-<br>
class movements were largely confined<br>
to urban areas as they were born out of<br>
the colonial economy. These solidarity<br>
of the workers in particular enterprises<br>

<b>The rise and consolidation of</b><br>
workers’ movements after 1945<br>
<b>There were a number of reasons for</b><br>
the emergence of workers’ unions after<br>
the Second World War. One was the<br>
need to protect and safeguard workers’<br>
interests and rights. For instance, they<br>
demanded better wages and better<br>
working conditions. Most miners from<br>
different parts, especially those in the<br>
<b>Gold Coast, worked in bad conditions,</b><br>
which caused deaths and injuries.<br>
<b>The emergence of an elite class was</b><br>
another factor for the rise of workers’<br>
movements. This was a group of<br>
educated workers who influenced the<br>
rise of workers movements because<br>
they had been exposed to various<br>
issues pertaining to the working-<br>
class movements. Some had even<br>
read various works on working-class<br>
movements in Europe. For instance,<br>
<b>Joshua Nkomo of Southern Rhodesia</b><br>
organised a movement called the<br>
<b>Rhodesia Railways African Employees’</b><br>
<b>Association to demand better welfare</b><br>
for workers.<br>
<b>Moreover,</b><br>
the<br>
expansion<br>
of<br>
infrastructure was responsible for<br>
the rise of workers movements. As<br>
already pointed out, the colonialists<br>
expanded and improved infrastructure<br>
to facilitate the exploitation of African<br>
resources. Eventually, the improvement<br>
in infrastructure became advantageous<br>
to Africans because it was easier for<br>
them to communicate and interact with<br>
each other as they were in the process<br>
of forming their movements.<br>
<b>The presence of diverse industries in</b><br>
the colonies also contributed to the<br>
rise of workers’ movements. After the<br>
war, the colonialists opened different<br>
types of industries in the colonies<br>
such as import-export substitution and<br>
processing industries for the purpose<br>
of reviving their economies as well as<br>
the service sector. This increased the<br>
number of permanent labourers, who<br>
stayed together for a long time; hence,<br>
they mobilised each other to demand<br>
their rights.<br>
<b>Finally, the colonial government's</b><br>
policy<br>
of<br>
allowing<br>
unions<br>
and<br>
organisations also contributed to the<br>
development of workers’ movements<br>
in Africa. After the Second World War,<br>
the colonial governments allowed the<br>
formation of trade unions and other<br>
workers’ movements and organisations.<br>
<b>The formation of various workers’</b><br>
organisations such as trade unions<br>
was aimed at fighting for workers’<br>
rights, good  wages and good working<br>
conditions. The importance of workers’<br>
organisations is that during the struggle<br>
for independence they allied with<br>
political parties in the struggle for<br>
independence.<br>

<b>Activity 5.3</b><br>
<b>Conduct a library research on the</b><br>
colonial labour question and prepare<br>
a presentation on the various methods<br>
that the colonial state used to recruit<br>
labourers.<br>
<b>Assess the effect of such methods in</b><br>
colonial Africa. Use the findings to<br>
prepare notes for revision.<br>
<b>Changes in the colonial transport</b><br>
and communication systems<br>
<b>After the Second World War, the</b><br>
colonial state improved transport<br>
and communication networks in the<br>
colonies. The colonialists constructed,<br>
improved and expanded railways,<br>
roads and harbours for transporting<br>
goods and providing services. To<br>
improve the communication systems,<br>
the state established postal services at<br>
the headquarters of each district. In East<br>
<b>Africa, the three Governors of Kenya,</b><br>
<b>Tanganyika and Uganda established</b><br>
the East African High Commission<br>
(EAHC) in 1948 to provide common<br>
services. A telephone service between<br>
the colonies and the metropole was<br>
also introduced.<br>
<b>Some of the railways which were</b><br>
expanded after the Second World War<br>
were the  railway line from Lagos to<br>
<b>Kano in Nigeria for transporting palm</b><br>
oil and the railway line from Accra<br>
to Kumasi for transporting cocoa. In<br>
<b>Central Africa, there was a railway line</b><br>
for transporting minerals from Southern<br>
<b>Rhodesia to the port of Beira in</b><br>
Portuguese East Africa (Mozambique).<br>
<b>In the French West African territories,</b><br>
feeder lines brought export goods to<br>
the port of Dakar in Senegal and the<br>
port of Conakry in Guinea.<br>
<b>Objectives of the expansion of the</b><br>
colonial transport systems<br>
<b>Expansion of the colonial transport</b><br>
systems was to link agricultural areas<br>
in the interior to the coast. The colonial<br>
powers built railways and roads in<br>
various areas to transport raw materials<br>
from remote areas to the coast.<br>
<b>Expansion of the transport systems</b><br>
was<br>
also<br>
aimed<br>
at<br>
transporting<br>
colonial officers within the colonies.<br>
<b>For instance, the systems were used</b><br>
to transport the administrators who<br>
were supervising various colonial<br>
government<br>
tasks<br>
and<br>
projects,<br>
plantations, mines and industries.<br>
<b>The systems were used to transport</b><br>
migrant labourers from their homes<br>
to the production areas. For instance,<br>
in Tanganyika the central railway<br>
was extended from Tabora to Mpanda<br>
to transport migrant labourers from<br>
<b>Rukwa and the neighbouring regions to</b><br>
the production areas such as Morogoro<br>
and Dar es Salaam.<br>
