<b>The systems were also used to</b><br>
transport soldiers to areas where there<br>
was active resistance. The expansion<br>
of the transport systems facilitated<br>
the suppression of Africans who were<br>
resisting colonialism.<br>
<b>Characteristics of the colonial</b><br>
transport systems<br>
<b>In colonial Africa roads and railways</b><br>
were developed in such a way that they<br>
ran from the ports to the major mining<br>
and cash-crop producing areas in the<br>
interior. The roads and railways did<br>
not integrate the colonies, but they ran<br>
mostly to economic productive areas.<br>
<b>For example, in the Gold Coast ran</b><br>
a few miles from the coast railways<br>
to the interior in the cocoa producing<br>
areas in Kumasi.<br>
<b>Railways and roads rarely connected</b><br>
two territories. Except where two<br>
territories belonged to one colonial<br>
master, railways and roads had no inter-<br>
territorial integration. For example,<br>
<b>Uganda and Kenya shared the Uganda</b><br>
railway line because they belonged to<br>
the British colonial master. However,<br>
there was no connection between<br>
colonies, for example between the Gold<br>
<b>Coast and Nigeria, and Tanganyika,</b><br>
Rwanda and Burundi.<br>
<b>Another</b><br>
characteristic<br>
of<br>
the<br>
communication systems in Africa was<br>
the dominance of vehicles for carrying<br>
goods over vehicles for carrying<br>
passengers. The colonialists preferred<br>
wagons to passengers’ vehicles because<br>
they wanted to transport raw materials<br>
to the coast (ports) for shipment to<br>
<b>Europe and to transport manufactured</b><br>
goods from Europe to the interior<br>
African markets.<br>
<b>During the colonial time, there were</b><br>
networks of feeder roads and railways<br>
which were connected to the main<br>
railways or trunk roads. The feeder<br>
railways and roads carried raw<br>
materials to the main railways or<br>
trunk roads. One example of a feeder<br>
railway line in Tanganyika was the<br>
<b>Kaliua-Mpanda railway. The railway</b><br>
transported labourers from Mpanda to<br>
the main railway line, that is, the Dar<br>
es Salaam-Kigoma railway line.<br>
<b>Many roads and railways covered a</b><br>
short distance. They extended to the<br>
areas that produced raw materials.<br>
<b>For example, in Tanganyika there was</b><br>
a short railway line which ran from<br>
the coast (Mtwara) to the interior<br>
(Nachingwea),<br>
which<br>
produced<br>
groundnuts. Figure 5.1 shows the main<br>
railway lines in colonial Tanganyika.<br>

<b>The</b><br>

system<br>
facilitated<br>
the transport of migrant labourers.<br>
<b>The construction of the transport</b><br>
infrastructure was not for opening<br>
up the colonies to develop of African<br>
societies. Some railway lines were built<br>
to transport migrant labourers only.<br>
<b>For example, the central railway line</b><br>
in Tanganyika that transported migrant<br>

<b>The sector was a source of employment</b><br>
to a few Africans. Some Africans were<br>

and dock workers. Others maintained<br>
roads.<br>
<b>Railways played a major role in further</b><br>
opening up the interior of Africa by<br>
incorporating it into the world capitalist<br>
economy. This benefited the Europeans<br>
in various ways as they were able to put<br>
the colonies under full their control.<br>
<b>The transport system made it easy</b><br>
to transport troops and colonial<br>
administrators to the interior. Troops<br>
were dispatched into the interior<br>
using railways and roads. Colonial<br>
information<br>
was<br>
also<br>
transmitted<br>
from the coast to the interior through<br>
the same communication networks.<br>
<b>Furthermore, during the Maumau war</b><br>
in Kenya, African soldiers failed to<br>
defeat the British forces because of the<br>
improved railways and roads, which<br>
facilitated rapid transport of British<br>
troops to the areas where people were<br>
resisting colonial rule.<br>
<b>Many towns developed along the</b><br>
railway lines in the interior of Africa.<br>
<b>In Tanganyika, the towns that emerged</b><br>
along the railway lines included<br>
<b>Morogoro, Kilosa, Mpwapwa, Itigi,</b><br>
<b>Manyoni, Tabora, Urambo, Mpanda</b><br>
and Kigoma. In Kenya, the towns that<br>
emerged along the lines were Nairobi,<br>
<b>Kisumu,</b><br>
<b>Voi,</b><br>
<b>Naivasha,</b><br>
<b>Nakuru,</b><br>
<b>Nanyuki and Thika. In Uganda, the</b><br>
towns that developed as a result of<br>
the Uganda railway line were Jinja,<br>
<b>Kampala, Kasese and Gulu. Thus,</b><br>
railway lines and roads influenced<br>
settlement patterns and towns.<br>
<b>Improvements in the transport systems</b><br>
stimulated internal communication.<br>
<b>During this period, postal services such</b><br>
as transporting letters and telegraph<br>
services became so important that<br>
offices were opened in every district.<br>
<b>The transport networks facilitated</b><br>
interaction between people, both within<br>
and outside the colonies. For example,<br>
when the British Governors of Kenya,<br>
<b>Tanganyika and Uganda formed the</b><br>
<b>East Africa High Commission in 1948,</b><br>
roads and railways facilitated smooth<br>
movement of goods and people within<br>
East Africa.<br>

direction of the railway lines and roads<br>

<b>Exercise 5.3</b><br>
<b>Discuss the legacies of the</b><br>
colonial agricultural schemes<br>
established after the Second<br>
<b>World War in any African</b><br>
country of your choice.<br>
<b>Introduction of Long-Term</b><br>
<b>Development Plans</b><br>
<b>The colonial governments initiated long-</b><br>
term development plans. For example,<br>
the British colonial state introduced<br>
the 1945 Colonial Development and<br>
<b>Welfare Act. The law showed Britain’s</b><br>
commitment to the development of<br>
the colonies in her own interest. Thus,<br>
after the war, Britain attempted to<br>
expand production through agricultural<br>
stations,<br>
extension<br>
programmes,<br>
the promotion of technology and<br>
conservations measures. To facilitate<br>
the implementation of this law, the<br>
<b>British colonial state adopted a Ten-</b><br>
Year Development Plan (1947-1957).<br>
<b>The objectives of the plan were to</b><br>
improve upon colonial bureaucracy,<br>
transport as well as education. Another<br>
objective of the plan was to make sure<br>
that the colonies were self-sufficient<br>
financially. The plan was also aimed<br>
at improving wildlife conservation,<br>
fisheries and livestock keeping. In<br>
<b>Tanganyika, for example, the schemes</b><br>
which benefited from British funds<br>
were the Uluguru Mountain Land<br>
<b>Usage and Rehabilitation Schemes,</b><br>
the Sukumaland Livestock and Land<br>
<b>Usage Development Scheme, the</b><br>
<b>Nachingwea Groundnuts Scheme and</b><br>
the Kongwa Groundnuts Scheme. The<br>
schemes were under the supervision<br>
of local authorities. It is worth noting<br>
that the plan, among other things, paid<br>
attention to the expansion of social<br>
services.<br>
<b>Changes in social service policies</b><br>
<b>After the Second World War, the</b><br>
colonialists made various changes to<br>
the provision of social services in the<br>
colonies. The colonial social services<br>
which were expanded included health,<br>
education, water supply, housing and<br>
electricity.<br>
<b>Distribution pattern of colonial</b><br>
social services<br>
<b>The imposition of colonial rule on</b><br>
<b>Africans was done simultaneously</b><br>
with the imposition of the colonial<br>
economy and the establishment of<br>
social services like education, health,<br>
water, housing and electricity. The<br>
provision of colonial social services<br>
in the colonies was determined by the<br>
following factors:<br>
<b>The presence of European settlers in</b><br>
a colony was one of the factors that<br>
determined the provision of social<br>
services in the colony. Colonies like<br>

<b>South West Africa, Kenya and Southern</b><br>
<b>Rhodesia had a large number of</b><br>
<b>Europeans, who were growing export</b><br>
crops such as coffee, tea, pyrethrum and<br>
tobacco. Others had invested in dairy<br>
farming. The presence of European<br>
settlers in such areas forced the colonial<br>
state to develop or establish social<br>
services. Such areas had an adequate<br>
supply of water and adequate provision<br>
of health services and education, and<br>
they were served by well-built roads.<br>
<b>Urbanisation was another factor that</b><br>
determined the provision of social<br>
services. With the onset of colonial<br>
rule, Africa began to witness the rise<br>
and growth of modern towns and cities.<br>
<b>Examples of such towns and cities</b><br>
were Dar es Salaam, Nairobi, Kampala,<br>
<b>Blantyre, Salisbury and Lusaka. The</b><br>
colonialists built some administrative<br>
centres in the expanding towns to meet<br>
the growing need for social services<br>
by the then growing urban population.<br>
<b>Towns and cities also functioned as</b><br>
hubs of all types of transport services<br>
like railways, harbours, roads and<br>
airports.<br>
<b>The presence of European missionaries</b><br>
in rural areas in the colonies was<br>
another factor that determined the<br>
provision of social services. For<br>
example, in Tanganyika, missionaries<br>
opened mission centres in Mwanza,<br>
<b>Tabora, Songea, Bukoba, Moshi and</b><br>
<b>Mbeya. As a result of their activities,</b><br>
the areas began to enjoy social services<br>
like education, health, and electricity.<br>
<b>Another factor that determined the</b><br>
provision of social services was the<br>
presence in a colony of precious<br>
minerals like gold, diamond, copper,<br>
iron ore, coal, cobalt, manganese,<br>
uranium tin and bauxite. Examples of<br>
the mining centres established in Africa<br>
included the copperbelt of Northen<br>
<b>Rhodesia, the Mwadui diamond mine</b><br>
in Tanganyika and the Ashanti gold<br>
fields in the Gold Coast. These areas<br>
enjoyed adequate provision of water,<br>
health, transport and housing services.<br>
<b>In some parts of Africa, the colonialists</b><br>
encouraged the development of African<br>
peasant agriculture. In East Africa,<br>
for example, peasant agriculture was<br>
established in Buganda, Kilimanjaro<br>
and Bukoba. The practice of peasant<br>
agriculture in such areas motivated the<br>
colonialists to build roads and railways<br>
which connected the areas with the<br>
coastal areas where the Europeans had<br>
built modern harbours. For example,<br>
the Uganda railway line, which ran<br>
from Mombasa in Kenya to Kampala<br>
in Uganda, was built with British<br>
capital to transport the coffee, tea and<br>
cotton produced by the white settlers in<br>
Kenya and Uganda.<br>
<b>Finally,</b><br>
the<br>
social<br>
services<br>
and<br>
infrastructure were provided in urban<br>
centres, where the Europeans had<br>
established<br>
their<br>
settlements<br>
and<br>
import-substitution industries. Such<br>
infrastructure was a link between the<br>

metropolitan power and the colonial<br>

<b>Changes in colonial education</b><br>
<b>Colonial</b><br>
education<br>
was<br>
offered<br>
to African pupils through formal<br>
instruction in the classroom. Teachers<br>
were trained and employed so that they<br>
could supervise teaching and learning.<br>
<b>The media of instruction were mainly</b><br>
metropolitan languages, particularly<br>
<b>English,</b><br>
<b>French</b><br>
and<br>
Portuguese.<br>
<b>Native languages were only tolerated</b><br>
among beginners. Missionaries and<br>
the colonial government established<br>
and ran many schools. Each agency<br>
ran various types of schools. For<br>
example, the missionaries ran bush<br>
schools, primary (lower and upper)<br>
schools, vocational training centres and<br>
seminaries. The colonial government<br>
ran central government schools as well<br>
as native authority schools.<br>
<b>Objectives of expanding colonial</b><br>
education at different levels<br>
<b>Colonial education was aimed at</b><br>
creating a class of literate Africans<br>
who could be employed as soldiers,<br>
warrant chiefs, teachers, clerks, book<br>
keepers, store keepers, farmers, animal<br>
husbandry experts, catechists and<br>
carpenters. All these were expected to<br>
be subordinates of European officials<br>
in different sectors and were usually<br>
paid low wages.<br>
<b>The colonial government expanded</b><br>
education so as to use it as an ideological<br>
tool to make Africans accept colonial<br>
rule and European culture. This<br>
accelerated the culturalisation process;<br>
educated people were seen as people<br>
who had adopted European culture<br>
and who despised their own (African)<br>
culture.<br>
<b>The missionaries converted many</b><br>
<b>Africans to Christianity. However, the</b><br>
evangelisation process faced a number<br>
of challenges because the majority of<br>
<b>Africans did not know how to read and</b><br>
interpret the Bible. The missionaries<br>
pressurised the colonial government to<br>
provide education to as many Africans<br>
as possible so that they could understand<br>
the Bible. The converts were prepared<br>
to be local religious leaders such as<br>
catechists or evangelists.<br>
<b>The expansion of colonial education,</b><br>
especially after the Second World War,<br>
was aimed at preparing Africans to be<br>
puppet leaders and to pave the way for<br>
neo-colonialism to start. The colonial<br>
government found that the situation<br>
after the Second World War in Africa<br>
was difficult owing to the rise of political<br>
awareness and the determination to<br>
struggle for independence. Therefore,<br>
the colonial government expanded<br>
colonial education to prepare a class<br>
of petty bourgeoisie who could serve<br>
their interests and act as collaborators<br>
or sub-imperialists.<br>
<b>After the Second World War, colonial</b><br>

159<br>
education was used to  justify racism<br>
and racial segregation in the provision<br>
of education itself and health services,<br>
and in residential areas. Education was<br>
also used to make the African child<br>
develop a liking for order, cleanliness,<br>
diligence and dutifulness, including<br>
the virtues of obedience and fear<br>
of colonial laws and orders. Thus,<br>
colonial education was to expose<br>
<b>Africans to Western culture. It also</b><br>
helped to reduce illiteracy and give<br>
skills to Africans who later on carried<br>
out activities.<br>
<b>Characteristics of colonial education</b><br>
after the Second World War<br>
<b>The</b><br>
characteristics<br>
of<br>
colonial<br>
education after the Second World<br>
<b>War may be analysed using British</b><br>
colonies as an example. One of the<br>
characteristics of colonial education<br>
was<br>
regional<br>
imbalances.<br>
<b>After</b><br>
the Second World War, the native<br>
authorities were required to provide<br>
funds for running primary schools.<br>
<b>This favoured the cash-crop producing</b><br>
areas because their native authorities<br>
had much money. The residents of such<br>
areas could also pay school fees.<br>
<b>Colonial education was basically</b><br>
gender biased. The bias stemmed<br>
from the colonialists’ assumption<br>
that African women were responsible<br>
for taking care of the family. Thus,<br>
they were to be given an education<br>
that would enable them to know how<br>
to read and write. The figures for<br>
<b>Tanganyika in 1956 clearly reveal</b><br>
the extent of this bias. In the case of<br>
primary schools (Standards I-IV), the<br>
total number of pupils was 336 000, of<br>
whom 105 000 were Standards V-VIII<br>
girls. The total number of pupils was<br>
28 000; out of these, girls were 4900.<br>
<b>In the case of secondary schools, the</b><br>
total number was 4409, out of which<br>
only 204 were girls. Thus, the number<br>
of girls or female students decreased<br>
with the rising level of education.<br>
<b>The content of the education provided</b><br>
to Africans also reflected the needs<br>
of capitalism. Those who received<br>
it would go back to the village and<br>
increase agricultural production. The<br>
central government schools were<br>
geared towards realising the provision<br>
of other social services and the running<br>
of the few industries that existed in the<br>
colonies. Thus, they taught arithmetic,<br>
literacy,<br>
masonry,<br>
carpentry<br>
and<br>
printing. Emphasis was put on white<br>
collar jobs, which discouraged manual<br>
labour. White collar jobs implied one<br>
had higher education and was civilised,<br>
while manual labour implied that one<br>
was illiterate.<br>
<b>Moreover, the content of colonial</b><br>
education<br>
was<br>
mainly<br>
<b>Western</b><br>
oriented and, therefore, irrelevant to<br>
the African situation. One would, for<br>
example, study about mountains in<br>
<b>Europe without ever studying about</b><br>
the mountains found on the African<br>
continent.<br>

The education provided was theoretical.<br>
<b>Likewise,</b><br>

encouraged to memorise new items as<br>

<b>At all levels, discipline and respect for</b><br>

<b>It was not meant to cultivate self-</b><br>

<b>Africans, but rather to produce people</b><br>

was not intended to develop Africans<br>
in terms of science and technology.<br>
<b>Africans were supposed to produce</b><br>
raw materials for export and to buy<br>
European manufactured goods.<br>

education after the Second World<br>
<b>War</b><br>
<b>The major changes made to colonial</b><br>
education after the Second World War<br>
related to the colonial government<br>
encouraging missionaries to open more<br>
schools. Missionaries were provided<br>
with state grants for that purpose. The<br>
situation after 1945 made it clear that<br>
it was burdensome and very expensive<br>
for the colonial government to establish<br>
and fund schools all over the colony.<br>
<b>Therefore, the colonial government</b><br>
asked missionaries to open more schools<br>
so as to accommodate the large number<br>
of African children. In Tanganyika, for<br>

<b>Moshi School. The Mary Knoll Fathers</b><br>

<b>Secondary</b><br>

built St. Francis College (present-day<br>
<b>Pugu High School) at Pugu, in Dar es</b><br>
Salaam, just to mention a few.<br>
<b>The colonial government increased the</b><br>
number of schools and colleges within<br>
the colonies. They included Fourah-<br>
<b>Bay School and Bo-School (Sierra-</b><br>

and most of them were built in the rural<br>
areas to educate rural children. Subjects<br>
which were taught in such schools<br>
were related to the needs of European<br>
capitalism.<br>
<b>They</b><br>
were<br>
reading,<br>

enrolment stood at seven per cent. By<br>
1950, primary school enrolment in<br>
the French West African colonies was<br>
six per cent, in Kenya 26 per cent and<br>
Nigeria 16 per cent.<br>
<b>The impact of colonial education on</b><br>
<b>African societies</b><br>
<b>Colonial education had the following</b><br>
impact on Africa:<br>
<b>Colonial education created two classes</b><br>
in African societies, namely the<br>
educated and the non-educated. These<br>
two classes caused misunderstandings<br>
in society because the educated<br>
regarded themselves as superior and<br>
the non-educated as inferior. The<br>
colonialists did this to divide Africans<br>
so as to rule them more easily. For<br>
example, in Tanganyika, only a few<br>
people, especially the sons of chiefs,<br>
received colonial education.<br>

<b>Colonial education encouraged and</b><br>
maintained racism in Africa. For<br>
instance, whites got the best education,<br>
<b>Asians received better education and</b><br>
<b>Africans received poor education. This</b><br>
was done to create a class of rulers<br>
and that of the ruled. African pupils<br>
were given knowledge pertaining<br>
to agricultural activities, while the<br>
education given to Asian pupils related<br>
to business. The education given to<br>
white pupils was intended to prepare<br>
them to be administrators in the<br>
colonial offices and to supervise work<br>
in colonial projects or undertakings.<br>
<b>The disparity in the provision of</b><br>
colonial education propagated racism.<br>
<b>Colonial education produced African</b><br>
intellectuals who could question and<br>
analyse various issues, especially<br>
political issues. These were people<br>
like Milton Obote (Uganda), Kwame<br>
<b>Nkrumah</b><br>
(Gold<br>
<b>Coast),</b><br>
<b>Nnamdi</b><br>
<b>Azikiwe</b><br>
(Nigeria)<br>
and<br>
<b>Julius</b><br>
<b>Kambarage Nyerere (Tanganyika). The</b><br>
<b>Africans who got colonial education</b><br>
knew how they had been exploited<br>
by their colonial masters. They saw<br>
the importance to regaining their lost<br>
freedom; hence, they spearheaded<br>
African nationalism.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
colonial<br>
education<br>
promoted Western culture in Africa,<br>
which made Africans abandon their<br>
ways of life and instead adopt new<br>
lifestyles, including acquiring Western<br>
names and languages. For instance, in<br>
<b>Kenya and many other British colonies,</b><br>
<b>Africans learnt English, while in the</b><br>
French colonies they learnt French.<br>
<b>The Western culture that Africans</b><br>
acquired resulted divided them into<br>
superior and inferior Africans. Those<br>
who were westernised were considered<br>
to be civilised and the non-westernised<br>
were considered to be uncivilised.<br>
<b>Colonial health services after 1945</b><br>
<b>As a result of the Second World War,</b><br>
the colonial masters changed and<br>
expanded health services in Africa. This<br>
was done to increase productivity on<br>
plantations, in mines and in industries.<br>
<b>The characteristics of colonial health</b><br>
services are presented below.<br>
<b>The services were unevenly distributed,</b><br>
in that a large number of hospitals<br>
and dispensaries were built in urban,<br>
plantation and mining centres, where<br>
there were many workers or settlers<br>
who were producing cash crops.<br>
<b>Areas that did not have any economic</b><br>
potential were marginalised.<br>
<b>Health services were more curative</b><br>
than preventive. The colonial health<br>
services were not aimed at preventing<br>
diseases. The services were provided<br>
to treat Africans so that they could<br>
provide cheap labour to the whites.<br>
<b>Besides, Africans were a market for</b><br>
European medicines.<br>
<b>The services were provided on a racial</b><br>
basis. The health services given to<br>
<b>Africans were not of the same quality</b><br>

as those provided to Europeans.<br>
<b>Europeans received better services</b><br>
and Africans received poor services.<br>
<b>For example, in Dar es Salaam, Ocean</b><br>
<b>Road Hospital offered medical services</b><br>
to whites, while Sewa Haji Hospital<br>
was for Africans and other non-whites.<br>
<b>Moreover, most of the doctors were</b><br>
<b>Europeans. This could be explained</b><br>
by the fact that they were the ones<br>
who received appropriate education.<br>
<b>Africans were prepared to serve as</b><br>
nurses, wound dressers and ward<br>
attendants. Later on, especially during<br>
the post-colonial period, this caused<br>
problems in most African health<br>
centres as they had an acute shortage<br>
of medical health personnel.<br>
<b>African</b><br>

medicine<br>
was<br>
despised, but for a reason. Allowing<br>
<b>African local medicine to enter the</b><br>
market would cause competition with<br>
foreign medicine. The missionaries<br>
who owned many health institutions<br>
argued that African medical practices<br>
were incompatible with Christian<br>
teachings. Since the time of penetrating<br>
and establishing colonialism in Africa,<br>
most Africans had been depending on<br>
Western medicine.<br>
<b>In Tanganyika, most of the colonial</b><br>
health facilities for African labourers<br>
were located in Tanga and Morogoro,<br>
where there were sisal plantations.<br>
<b>Similarly, Kenyan Highlands in Kenya</b><br>
were provided with health services as<br>
they had camps for migrant labourers.<br>
<b>All this reminds us that the minimum</b><br>
maintenance of workers’ health by<br>
the colonialists was an economic<br>
investment for them. That is why the<br>
<b>Africans living outside the web of the</b><br>
colonial economic sites were ignored<br>
altogether.<br>
<b>The reasons for expanding colonial</b><br>
health services after 1945 are, therefore,<br>
identified. To modernise social services,<br>
the colonial government expanded the<br>
<b>African health sector. The increase in</b><br>
population and the resultant expansion<br>
of towns after the war resulted in an<br>
increase in diseases. Colonial hospitals<br>
were established and dealt with such<br>
diseases as malaria, tuberculosis (TB),<br>
typhoid and smallpox. Others were<br>
hookworms and sexually transmitted<br>
diseases (STDs) such as gonorrhoea<br>
and syphilis. Health research centres<br>
were established so that they could<br>
carry out research on tropical diseases.<br>
<b>The fact that tropical diseases were</b><br>
posing a serious threat to the European<br>
community in Africa meant that<br>
measures had to be taken to improve<br>
the quality of health services for<br>
<b>Africans and Europeans alike. It must</b><br>
be emphasised, however, that Africans’<br>
health services were relatively poor.<br>
<b>The health centres for Europeans were</b><br>
built in most of the settler colonies<br>
such as Kenya and Southern Rhodesia.<br>
<b>Hospitals for Africans and Europeans</b><br>
were established in Tanganyika as well.<br>

<b>Exercise 5.4</b><br>
<b>Is there any consideration in the</b><br>
provision of social services in<br>
modern Africa? Explain.<br>
<b>Changes in housing and water supply</b><br>
<b>The British government planned to</b><br>
allocate more funds to improve the<br>
quality of social services with a view<br>
to increasing production so as to<br>
rehabilitate the war-torn economies.<br>
<b>It was after 1945 that the colonial</b><br>
regime planned to spend money on<br>
the provision of housing and water<br>
services to employees. However, the<br>
<b>British provided relatively little money</b><br>
for African housing as compared to<br>
other services or projects such as<br>
road construction. This was done to<br>
maximise profit. Following the 1940<br>
<b>British Act of Colonial Development</b><br>
and Welfare (CDW), the colonial regime<br>
was determined to increase funding for<br>
projects such as housing in its colonies<br>
so as to increase production. The 1945<br>
<b>Colonial Development and Welfare</b><br>
was a significant extension of the 1940<br>
<b>Act. The law increased the amount of</b><br>
money that could be spent between<br>
1946 and 1956 on development projects<br>
in the British colonies.<br>
<b>The early housing and water services</b><br>
were for meeting the needs of the<br>
labourers on plantations and settler<br>
farms. There were houses on the sisal<br>
plantations in Tanga and Kilimanjaro;<br>
the houses were supplied with water.<br>
<b>The house supervisors and factory</b><br>
workers were built near the areas in<br>
which they worked. Europeans put<br>
well-drilled water wells around the<br>
camps for their workers. Such services<br>
were important to the settlers who<br>
supervised the agricultural projects.<br>
<b>The British colonial government was</b><br>
also determined to provide funds for<br>
constructing water and sanitation<br>
infrastructure with a view to increasing<br>
production. For example, the 1948<br>
<b>Memorandum of African Housing</b><br>
contained detailed information on<br>
the indigenous population and the<br>
housing stock, including squatters’<br>
house, housing needs and proposed<br>
housing development projects. The<br>
schemes launched under CDW resulted<br>
in increased construction of houses,<br>
although housing shortages in urban<br>
centres like Dar es Salaam were not<br>
adequately addressed. It was estimated<br>
that over two-thirds of the city’s houses<br>
were overcrowded and that 3000<br>
houses were required to alleviate the<br>
shortage, but the colonial government<br>
planned to construct 250 houses in<br>
five years. The houses available to the<br>
<b>African population in urban areas were</b><br>
significantly few. Thus, most Africans<br>
in urban centres lived in congested<br>
slums, which resulted in the spread<br>
of infectious diseases like pneumonia,<br>
typhoid and diarrhoea. In the slums,<br>

<b>The few houses constructed in the</b><br>
<b>African-dominated areas lacked the</b><br>

amenities<br>
like<br>

water. Various letters and notes written<br>
between 1949 and 1954 showed the<br>

hundreds of homes shared a single<br>
public water point. Colonial documents<br>
reveal shortcomings in the provision in<br>
drainage, sewerage and water services.<br>
<b>The services were also provided on a</b><br>
racial basis. For example, in Dar es<br>
<b>Salaam, pit latrines were designated</b><br>
for Africans, while flush toilets were<br>
designated for Europeans.<br>
<b>Reasons for expanding water and</b><br>
housing services after 1945<br>
<b>In the early years of colonial rule, there</b><br>
were no housing or water services for<br>
<b>African and Asian employees. It was</b><br>
only after 1945 that the colonial state<br>
increased budgets for providing social<br>
services like housing and water to<br>
<b>African employees. Housing and water</b><br>
services were offered to Africans in the<br>
plantation and settler economies. For<br>
example, the Amboni sisal plantation<br>
in Tanga had its own schools, mosques,<br>
churches,<br>
hospitals<br>
and<br>
welfare<br>
centres. Apart from the sisal cutters,<br>
a few Africans such as clerks, wound<br>
dressers, school teachers, store keepers<br>
and messengers were also employed.<br>
<b>The most privileged manual workers</b><br>
were the headmen, overseers and<br>
factory workers. These lived in camps,<br>
since they were required to be at work<br>
all the time. That is why the colonial<br>
government built houses for such<br>
workers. They also dug wells from<br>
which the workers could draw water.<br>
<b>Moreover, the colonial housing and</b><br>
water services were provided in order<br>
to minimise workers’ resistance. The<br>
fear of resistance forced the capitalists<br>
to provide water and housing services<br>
in economically important places<br>
like plantation areas, urban centres,<br>
industrial areas and missionary centres.<br>
<b>However, it has to be noted that the</b><br>
colonialists did not provide such<br>
services for the benefit of Africans.<br>

<b>Introduction</b><br>
During the nationalist struggle in Africa, there were events that happened<br>
outside Africa which supported the struggle. The events were referred to as<br>
external influences on the rise of nationalism in Africa. In this chapter, you will<br>
learn about the impact of the peace settlement after the First World War and<br>
the Pan-African Movement. You will also learn about the impact of the Second<br>
World War and the subsequent changes it brought about, the influence of the<br>
Bandung Conference as well as the independence of India and Burma on the<br>
struggle for independence in Africa. The competencies developed will enable<br>
you to appreciate the efforts that African leaders made towards independence<br>
and, therefore, develop a sense of pride in national security and development.<br>
What happens when people have no peace, freedom and dignity in their<br>
society?<br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Six</b><br>
<b>Nationalism and decolonisation in Africa</b><br>
<b>Background to nationalism</b><br>
<b>Pre-colonial Africa had different social</b><br>
and political organisations, which<br>
ranged from kinship to centralised state<br>
organisations in the form of kingdoms<br>
and empires. With the advent of<br>
colonialism, different ethnic groups<br>
with different historical backgrounds<br>
were forcefully united to form colonial<br>
territories. With the introduction of<br>
colonial economies into the colonies,<br>
<b>Africans were exploited, humiliated</b><br>
and oppressed. Colonial exploitation,<br>
humiliation and oppression forced<br>
<b>African people to come together to</b><br>
fight against colonial rule. This sense of<br>
togetherness against colonial injustices<br>
and the desire to get rid of colonialism<br>
came to be known as nationalism. In<br>
<b>Africa and Asia nationalist movements</b><br>
started in the 20th century.<br>

<b>Advanced History for Secondary Schools</b><br>
169<br>
<b>Nationalism was Africans’ desire to</b><br>
liberate themselves from colonial<br>
domination. African nationalism gained<br>
momentum after the Second World War<br>
because at the time political awareness<br>
increased and, for most Africans, the war<br>
had given them the reason to campaign<br>
for self-determination. African scholars<br>
like Adu Boahen, Ali Mazrui, Mahamood<br>
<b>Mamdani and Kwame Nkurumah have</b><br>
given various definitions of nationalism,<br>
as shown below:<br>
(a)	 Nationalism is the desire for<br>
self-determination, the desire for<br>
political, economic and cultural<br>
independence by Africans within<br>
<b>Africa and in the diaspora;</b><br>
(b)	 Nationalism is a strong sentiment<br>
against foreign domination and the<br>
belief that European colonialism<br>
in Africa was a great hindrance to<br>
<b>Africans’ economic growth and</b><br>
development;<br>
(c)	 Nationalism is the natural love<br>
and pride which Africans have for<br>
their countries and continent; and<br>
(d)	 Nationalism is the ideological<br>
channel<br>
of<br>
the<br>
anti-colonial<br>
struggle<br>
that<br>
represents<br>
the<br>
demand for national independence<br>
of the colonised people.<br>
<b>Thus, African nationalism grew out of</b><br>
the realisation or consciousness that<br>
the Europeans had deprived Africans<br>
of their political, economic and cultural<br>
freedom. This made Africans demand<br>
their lost freedom. Nationalism was<br>
generally Africans’ desire to come<br>
together in order to end foreign rule so<br>
that they could  rule themselves.<br>
<b>Internal forces for the rise of African</b><br>
nationalism<br>
<b>Most of the forces that contributed to</b><br>
the rise of nationalism emanated from<br>
the colonial system itself. The colonial<br>
subjects resisted economic exploitation.<br>
<b>For example, peasants complained</b><br>
about low prices of their produce. They<br>
were seriously affected during the Great<br>
<b>Economic Depression when the prices</b><br>
of crops such as cotton, coffee and tea<br>
fell drastically. Therefore, they formed<br>
cooperative unions to promote their<br>
demands, mainly better prices for their<br>
produce. In Tanganyika, for example,<br>
there were the Bukoba-Bahaya Union<br>
(1924),<br>
the<br>
<b>Kilimanjaro</b><br>
<b>Native</b><br>
<b>Planters’ Association (1925) as well</b><br>
as the Ngoni-Matengo Cooperative<br>
Union which was registered in 1936.<br>
<b>Examples from Nigeria included the</b><br>
<b>Cocoa Producers’ Co-operative of</b><br>
<b>Agage Planters’ Union and the Egba</b><br>
<b>Farmers’ Union registered in 1926</b><br>
as well as the Gbedun Co-operative<br>
<b>Produce Marketing Society, which</b><br>
was registered in 1937. The peasant<br>
cooperatives helped to make Africans<br>
aware of the evils of the colonialists;<br>
hence, Africans started to fight for their<br>
rights. In colonial Kenya, the Kikuyu<br>
wanted to be allowed to grow coffee,<br>
which was reserved for white settlers.<br>

<b>They also demanded ownership of land;</b><br>
land was in the hands of the settlers.<br>
<b>Likewise,</b><br>
in<br>
<b>Southern</b><br>
<b>Rhodesia,</b><br>
Africans wanted their land back.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
workers<br>
had<br>
many<br>
discontents, which culminated in their<br>
forming workers’ welfare associations.<br>
<b>For example, in 1922 workers in</b><br>
<b>Tanganyika formed the Tanganyika</b><br>
<b>Territory Civil Servant Association</b><br>
under Martin Kayamba which laid<br>
the foundation for mass nationalism<br>
after the Second World War. Likewise,<br>
workers in Nigeria established the<br>
Nigeria Civil Service Union in 1912.<br>
<b>These associations protested against</b><br>
low wages and long working hours.<br>
<b>The working class was composed of</b><br>
civil servants such as teachers, clerks,<br>
nurses and drivers. They struggled to<br>
eliminate economic injustices through<br>
demonstrations,<br>
strikes,<br>
petitions<br>
and boycotts. For example, the dock<br>
workers in Dar es Salaam and Mombasa<br>
organised strikes in 1947, demanding<br>
better salaries. In West Africa, youth<br>
clubs organised similar strikes during<br>
the 1920s and 1930s. Similarly, African<br>
trade unions like those of railway<br>
workers in Sierra Leone organised<br>
workers’ strikes during the 1930s and<br>
1940s. Generally, the African working<br>
class complained about low wages,<br>
poor working conditions and racism<br>
at the workplace and in the provision<br>
of poor social services like health and<br>
housing.<br>
<b>Africans also sought to eliminate</b><br>
segregation in the provision of social<br>
services. For example, there were<br>
schools for European, Indian, Arab<br>
and African pupils. The schools for<br>
<b>European pupils were better equipped</b><br>
and had their own curriculum. The<br>
curriculum for African pupils focused<br>
on agriculture and some rudimentary<br>
technical<br>
skills.<br>
<b>Similarly,</b><br>
health<br>
services were provided along racial<br>
lines.<br>
<b>There</b><br>
were<br>
hospitals<br>
for<br>
Europeans, Asians and Africans.<br>
<b>Poor health services were provided</b><br>
to Africans. For example, those who<br>
worked in the hospitals in which<br>
<b>Africans were treated as wound</b><br>
dressers, nurses and clinical officers<br>
received<br>
basic<br>
and<br>
rudimentary<br>
training. There were no medical<br>
specialists in most of such hospitals.<br>
<b>In Tanganyika, for instance, a special</b><br>
hospital, namely Ocean Road Hospital,<br>
was for the Europeans who were living<br>
in Tanganyika during German colonial<br>
rule. Africans were not treated in that<br>
hospital. Similarly, in Nigeria the British<br>
colonial government constructed the<br>
hospitals in Lagos and Calabar in the<br>
1970s. Initially, the hospitals served<br>
only Europeans. Later on, the services<br>
were extended to the Africans who had<br>
been employed by Europeans.<br>
<b>Moreover, Africans complained about</b><br>
political exclusion and marginalisation<br>
in<br>
the<br>
colonial<br>
administration,<br>
especially in decision-making. There<br>

<b>It could, therefore, be argued that the</b><br>

oppressive. Hence, it dug its own grave.<br>
<b>Africans could not put up with colonial</b><br>
exploitation indefinitely. Workers and<br>
peasants organised themselves and<br>
protested against colonial exploitation<br>
and oppression.<br>
<b>Activity 6.1</b><br>
<b>Imagine</b><br>
you<br>
were<br>
invited<br>
to<br>

“Internal<br>

<b>External factors for the rise of</b><br>
nationalism<br>
<b>The colonial contradictions within</b><br>
<b>African colonies and the protests</b><br>
which took place in different parts of<br>
the world contributed to the rise of<br>
<b>African nationalism. The external and</b><br>
internal forces led to the growing of<br>
the wish to struggle for independence<br>
in Africa.<br>
<b>Whereas</b><br>
the<br>
internal<br>
factors for nationalism related to<br>
<b>Africans’ protests against colonial</b><br>
exploitation, the external factors were<br>
the factors fuelled by the interests of<br>
the superpowers, the USA and USSR,<br>
which accelerated the whole process of<br>
nationalism. The following discussion<br>
focuses on the external factors for the<br>
rise of nationalism in Africa.<br>
<b>The Versailles Peace Treaty of 1919</b><br>
<b>The Versailles Peace Treaty was signed</b><br>
in Paris, France, in June 1919, that<br>
is, after the First World War. It was<br>

<b>Terms of the Versailles Peace Treaty</b><br>
<b>The treaty blamed Germany for starting</b><br>

2.<br>

<b>Britain, France and Belgium;</b><br>
3.<br>

<b>German territory of East Prussia</b><br>

4.<br>

western<br>

5.<br>
<b>The German army was prevented</b><br>

soldiers;<br>
6.<br>
<b>The German navy was limited</b><br>

7.<br>

<b>Germany had to surrender most</b><br>
of its merchant ships and one-<br>

<b>Exercise 6.1</b><br>
(a)   Discuss the significance of the<br>
Versailles Peace Treaty.<br>
(b)  To what extent were the<br>
<b>Fourteen Points of US President</b><br>
<b>Woodrow Wilson important</b><br>
to world peace after the First<br>
<b>World War?</b><br>
<b>The principle of self- determination</b><br>
<b>Self-determination refers to the state</b><br>
of being self-conscious and self-<br>
ruled with respect to a certain group<br>
of people. Self-determination was<br>
one of Wilson’s fourteen points. The<br>
principle of self-determination was<br>
put into practice when the Versailles<br>
<b>Peace Treaty granted independence to</b><br>
the nation-states of Estonia, Latvia and<br>
<b>Lithuania. The US president said that</b><br>
nations should be freed from foreign<br>
rule and should be given democratic<br>
governments of their own choice.<br>
<b>Apart from the USA, Britain and</b><br>
<b>France also adopted the principle of</b><br>
self-determination for their colonies.<br>
<b>The United Nations encouraged the</b><br>
colonial powers to apply the principle<br>
of self-rule in their colonies during the<br>
struggle for independence in Africa.<br>
<b>Most African nationalist elites such</b><br>
as Julius Kambarage Nyerere and<br>
<b>Kwame Nkrumah used the principle</b><br>
of self-determination to demand self-<br>
government.<br>
<b>Impact of the Versailles Peace Treaty</b><br>
of 1919 and the decolonisation of<br>
<b>Africa</b><br>
<b>The Versailles Peace Treaty adopted</b><br>
the principle of self-determination at<br>
the Paris Conference of 1919. The<br>
members signed the document which<br>
established the League of Nations in<br>
1920. They hoped that the League of<br>
<b>Nations would promote collective</b><br>
security in the world. The League of<br>
<b>Nations agreed that all German colonies</b><br>
in Africa should become mandate<br>
territories. For example, Article 119<br>
of the Versailles Treaty provided for<br>
<b>Germany to cede Tanganyika, which</b><br>
was part of German East Africa<br>
(Deutsch Ostafrika), to Britain as a<br>
mandate territory. Ruanda and Urundi,<br>
which were also part of German<br>
East Africa, were given to Belgium.<br>
<b>Theoretically, it was agreed that the</b><br>
powers should rule the colonies while<br>
at the same time preparing them for<br>
independence. Thus, Britain, France<br>
and Belgium governed the mandate<br>
territories on behalf of the League of<br>
Nations.<br>
<b>The Versailles Peace Treaty gave an</b><br>
opportunity to African nationalists to<br>
use the principle of self-determination<br>
to demand self-government. As a<br>
result, when they started campaigns for<br>
political independence the nationalists<br>
frequently referred to the principle.<br>

<b>For</b><br>

determination. This was also the case<br>
for the nationalist leaders of British<br>
and French West Africa.<br>
<b>The principle of self-determination</b><br>
increased<br>
<b>Africans’</b><br>
political<br>
awareness and desire to struggle<br>
against colonialism. It encouraged the<br>

<b>With the principle of self-determination,</b><br>
even after the Second World War,<br>
it was easy for the United Nations<br>
to pressurise the colonial powers to<br>
grant independence to their colonies.<br>
<b>The UN Decolonisation Committee</b><br>
frequently visited the colonies to assess<br>
the implementation of the principle<br>
of self-determination. For example,<br>
the committee visited Tanganyika in<br>
1954 and wrote a report to UNO. The<br>
committee also suggested to UNO that<br>
the South African Apartheid regime<br>
should grant independence to South<br>
West Africa (now Namibia).<br>
<b>The idea encouraged Pan-Africanists to</b><br>
intensify the efforts to demand Africans’<br>
liberation from European colonialism.<br>
<b>For instance, the Accra Conference of</b><br>
1958 demanded African independence.<br>
<b>Moreover, the superpowers, the USA</b><br>
and the USSR, used the principle<br>
to pressurise the European colonial<br>
powers to grant independence to their<br>
colonies. However, the superpowers<br>
had their own motives behind their<br>
supporting<br>
the<br>
decolonisation<br>
of<br>
<b>Africa. They wanted raw materials,</b><br>
markets and investment areas.<br>
<b>One would hasten to argue, therefore,</b><br>
that the Versailles Peace Treaty<br>
promoted solidarity and unity among<br>
the colonised peoples. Solidary and<br>
unite  promoted the struggle for the<br>

in<br>
question.<br>

<b>NCBW</b><br>
organised conferences in different<br>

campaigning for the improvement<br>
of African welfare in the British<br>
colonies. The Peoples Union of Lagos<br>
was established in Lagos in 1908 to<br>

protest against land alienation and<br>
taxation. The union campaigned for the<br>
freedom of the press as well. Thus, the<br>
principle of self-determination was the<br>
foundation for African nationalism and<br>
the nationalist struggle.<br>
<b>African nationalists and the principle</b><br>
of self-determination<br>
<b>As already mentioned, the principle of</b><br>
self-determination gave the nationalist<br>
leaders in Africa the reason to double<br>
their efforts to fight for independence.<br>
<b>Julius Kambarage Nyerere was one</b><br>
of the advocates of self-determination<br>
in Tanganyika. In order to make the<br>
struggle effective, he led the formation<br>
of Tanganyika African National Union<br>
(TANU) on 7th July 1954. TANU<br>
emphasised unity as the best strategy to<br>
eliminate colonialism in Tanganyika.<br>
<b>Another nationalist and Pan-Africanist</b><br>
who applied the principle of self-<br>
determination was Kwame Nkrumah.<br>
<b>He encouraged the people of the Gold</b><br>
<b>Coast to demand independence from</b><br>
Britain.<br>
<b>Another nationalist leader who applied</b><br>
the principle was Jomo Kenyatta<br>
of Kenya. He encouraged Kenyans<br>
to<br>
oppose<br>
settlers’<br>
domination.<br>
<b>His</b><br>
influence<br>
increased<br>
political<br>
consciousness among Kenyans, who<br>
rose against colonial domination. As<br>
a result, Kenya got her independence<br>
from Britain on 12th December 1963.<br>
<b>Jomo Kenyatta became prime minister</b><br>
of independent Kenya under Kenya<br>
African National Union (KANU).<br>
<b>In 1964, Kenya became a republic</b><br>
and Jomo Kenyatta became its first<br>
president.<br>
<b>Moreover,</b><br>
<b>Kamuzu</b><br>
<b>Banda</b><br>
of<br>
<b>Nyasaland (now Malawi) used the</b><br>
principle of self-determination to<br>
demand independence from Britain.<br>
<b>Banda used the United Nations</b><br>
platform to achieve the objective. As a<br>
result, Nyasaland got her independence<br>
in 1964. It was soon renamed Malawi.<br>
<b>Nnamdi Azikiwe of Nigeria applied</b><br>
the principle of self-determination to<br>
intensify the effort to liberate Africans.<br>
<b>Attending</b><br>
the<br>
<b>Accra</b><br>
<b>Conference</b><br>
of 1958, Azikiwe encouraged Pan-<br>
<b>Africanists to assist each other in the</b><br>
struggle for African independence.<br>
<b>He led Nigeria to independence on</b><br>
1st October 1960. Figure 6.1 shows a<br>
picture of Nnamdi Azikiwe.<br>

<b>Activity 6.2</b><br>
<b>Read different works on nationalism</b><br>
and then examine how the principle<br>
of self-determination influenced<br>
nationalist feelings among Africans.<br>
<b>Present your work to other members</b><br>
of the class for discussion.<br>
<b>Pan-Africanism</b><br>
<b>Pan Africanism refers to the political</b><br>
and ideological movement of the people<br>
of African origin intended to build unity,<br>
solidarity and dignity. It also refers to<br>
a strong attachment which all blacks<br>
have towards the African continent,<br>
<b>African culture, African unity and</b><br>
independence. It is an expression of<br>
<b>Africans’ wish to revive their culture,</b><br>
thereby regaining their dignity and<br>
identity. This movement was organised<br>
in order to fight against capitalist evils<br>
like racial discrimination, exclusion,<br>
humiliation, oppression, colonialism,<br>
slavery and the slave trade.<br>
<b>The movement was started by blacks</b><br>
in North America and the West<br>
<b>Indies, who suffered forced labour,</b><br>
discrimination and torture. Such black<br>
intellectuals as William Du Bois,<br>
<b>Marcus Aurelius Garvey, William</b><br>
<b>Henry Sylvester and George Padmore</b><br>
were the pioneers of the Pan-African<br>
Movement in the early 20th century.<br>
<b>The Pan-Africanists held a number of</b><br>
conferences in various towns such as in<br>
<b>London (1900), Paris (1919), Brussels</b><br>
(1921), Lisbon (1923), New York<br>
(1927), Manchester (1945) and Accra<br>
(1958). The conferences influenced the<br>
speed of African nationalism and the<br>
struggle for independence in various<br>
ways.<br>
<b>Contribution of the Pan-African</b><br>
<b>Movement to the struggle for</b><br>
independence<br>
<b>The Pan-African Movement succeeded</b><br>
in fostering unity between Africans in<br>
<b>Africa and the people of African origin</b><br>
in the diaspora. The nationalists who<br>
attended the Pan-African conferences<br>
from Africa included Kwame Nkrumah<br>
of Ghana, Jomo Kenyatta of Kenya,<br>
<b>T. R. Makonnen of Ethiopia, Peter</b><br>
<b>Abrahams of South Africa, Wallace</b><br>
<b>Johnson of Sierra Leone, Obafemi</b><br>
<b>Awolowo of Nigeria, Hastings Kamuzu</b><br>
<b>Banda of Nyasaland and Nnamdi</b><br>
<b>Azikiwe of Nigeria. Dr Peter Milliard,</b><br>
<b>George Padmore and William Du Bois</b><br>
were from the diaspora.<br>
<b>Pan-Africanism accelerated African</b><br>
consciousness of their rights. This<br>
consciousness helped them to present<br>
their human rights abuses before UNO.<br>
<b>A number of young Pan-Africanists</b><br>
such as Nyerere, Nkrumah, Banda<br>
and Kenyatta started educating their<br>
fellow Africans about their rights,<br>
including the right to self-government.<br>
<b>The movement condemned racial</b><br>

<b>Pan-Africanism</b><br>
influenced<br>
the<br>
formation of political parties in Africa.<br>
<b>The parties built unity and solidarity</b><br>
necessary for the liberation struggles.<br>
<b>Almost all African nationalist leaders</b><br>
established political parties after the<br>
<b>Second World War. Examples of the</b><br>
political parties they established are<br>
the Convention People’s Party (CPP)<br>
in the Gold Coast, which was formed<br>
in 1949 by Kwame Nkrumah, and<br>
<b>Tanganyika African National Union</b><br>
(TANU), which was formed in 1954 in<br>
Tanganyika by Julius K. Nyerere.<br>
<b>The movement mobilised financial</b><br>
support for African political leaders<br>
so that they could attend liberation<br>
meetings in various parts of the world<br>
like those of London and Accra. Pan-<br>
<b>Africanism facilitated the formation</b><br>
of the Organisation of African Unity<br>
(OAU) in 1963. The organisation<br>
played major role in the African<br>
liberation struggles in the 1960s and<br>
1970s. The OAU called for, among<br>
other things, the decolonisation of<br>
African.<br>

<b>The duty of the leaders who</b><br>

<b>Role of the Accra Conference of 1958</b><br>
<b>After the independence of Ghana</b><br>
in 1957, Kwame Nkrumah invited<br>
independent African<br>
countries<br>
to<br>
participate in a congress held in<br>
<b>Accra in April 1958. The congress</b><br>
was attended by the leaders of all<br>
<b>African countries which had already</b><br>
got independence. The countries were<br>
<b>Liberia, Ethiopia, Ghana, Tunisia,</b><br>
Morocco, the Sudan, Libya and Egypt.<br>
<b>The conference was followed by All-</b><br>
<b>African People’s Congress in December</b><br>
1958. This congress was attended by<br>
various African nationalist leaders<br>
such as Julius Kambarage Nyerere of<br>
<b>Tanganyika, Jomo Kenyatta of Kenya,</b><br>
<b>Kenneth Kaunda of Northern Rhodesia</b><br>
and Peter Abrahams of South Africa.<br>
<b>Impact of the Accra Pan-African</b><br>
<b>Conference of 1958 on the struggle</b><br>
for independence<br>
<b>The Accra conference of 1958 was</b><br>
the first Pan-African conference to<br>
be held on the African continent. The<br>

<b>African nationalists had, therefore,</b><br>

which revolutionary ideas spread to<br>
the rest of Africa. The conference<br>
under Kwame Nkrumah encouraged<br>
<b>African leaders to co-operate and</b><br>
visit each other in devising strategies<br>
for the liberation struggle. Those who<br>
attended the conference demanded an<br>
immediate end to colonialism all over<br>
the continent. The delegates discussed<br>
how best to facilitate the decolonisation<br>
of Africa, especially the Portuguese<br>
colonies of Angola, Mozambique<br>
and Guinea Bissau. The conference<br>

<b>During the April 1958 conference,</b><br>
the eight independent African states<br>
also agreed to share and exchange<br>
cultural,<br>
scientific,<br>
technological<br>
and<br>

<b>For</b><br>
example, they agreed on exchange<br>
programmes for professors, teachers<br>
and students so that they could raise<br>
<b>Africans’ awareness of the struggle</b><br>
for independence. They also agreed to<br>
improve communication lines within<br>
<b>Africa to simplify the movement of</b><br>
nationalists and the spread of Pan-<br>
Africanist ideas.<br>
<b>The conference awakened nationalistic</b><br>
feelings<br>
throughout<br>
West Africa.<br>
<b>The French colonies, which had</b><br>
been believing in the kindness of the<br>
colonial masters, started seeing light<br>
through the speeches made during<br>
the Accra conference. They came to<br>
learn that colonialism was an evil.<br>
<b>The French assimilation policy had</b><br>
deceived Africans that colonialism was<br>
a blessing to them. The participants<br>
also addressed the question of the<br>
future relationship between Africa and<br>
the rest of the world.<br>
<b>Moreover, the participants unanimously</b><br>
agreed to adopt the Non-Aligned policy<br>
in relation to the politics of the Cold<br>
<b>War. The idea of non-alignment was</b><br>
discussed and adopted by a number of<br>
participants so as to safeguard African<br>
interests. The idea was to protect<br>
<b>Africa from external influences. The</b><br>
participants feared that the superpowers<br>
would influence decolonisation in their<br>
own interest. Thus, the Non-Aligned<br>
members worked together to ensure<br>
that there was unity in the struggle<br>
against imperialism, colonialism and<br>
racism. It also condemned all forms<br>
of foreign aggression, occupation,<br>
domination and interference of the<br>
great powers and bloc politics. On<br>
returning to their home territories, the<br>
participants started to implement the<br>
ideas related to Pan-Africanism and the<br>
Non-Aligned Movement (NAM).<br>
<b>During</b><br>
the<br>
conference,<br>
<b>President</b><br>
<b>Kwame Nkrumah made it clear that</b><br>
the independence of Ghana alone was<br>
meaningless when the rest of Africa was<br>
still under colonial rule. He, therefore,<br>

<b>Effects of the Second World War</b><br>
<b>The Second World War (1939-1945)</b><br>
was a worldwide war which involved<br>
the Axis Powers, namely Germany,<br>
<b>Italy and Japan, and the Allied Powers,</b><br>
namely Britain, France, the USA and<br>
the USSR. Its immediate cause was<br>
Nazi Germany’s invasion of Poland.<br>
<b>Apart from these Western nations,</b><br>
<b>African colonies also participated</b><br>
in this war. During the war Africans<br>
served as soldiers, porters, drivers and<br>
cooks. Among other effects, the Second<br>
<b>World War led to the rise of mass</b><br>
nationalism in Africa. Before the war,<br>
<b>Africans fought colonial oppression</b><br>
and exploitation through co-operatives,<br>
welfare associations and independent<br>

the Second World War, the struggle<br>
took the form of mass political parties.<br>
<b>For example, after the war, welfare</b><br>
associations were transformed into<br>
vibrant political parties, which hastened<br>
the process of decolonisation.<br>
<b>During and after the Second World War,</b><br>
the demand for both food and cash crops<br>
increased, since the Far East, which<br>
had acted as the main source of tropical<br>
raw materials such as groundnuts, had<br>
been cut off by Japan. Apart from that,<br>
<b>India, which was the source of cotton</b><br>
for British textile industries, attained<br>
independence and, therefore, ceased<br>
to be a producer of raw materials.<br>
<b>European industrialists had to turn</b><br>
to Africa. As the demand for tropical<br>
raw materials increased, prices rose<br>
and Africans obtained better returns.<br>
<b>For example, the export of groundnuts</b><br>
from Senegal increased. As the material<br>
conditions of African peasants slightly<br>
improved, the peasants could support<br>
<b>African political parties and send their</b><br>
children to school. The return of African<br>
students from abroad contributed to the<br>
mounting of pressure for liberation in<br>
most African countries.<br>
<b>After the Second World War, the Labour</b><br>
<b>Party under Clement Atlee campaigned</b><br>
against the Conservative Party under<br>
<b>Prime Minister Winston Churchill. The</b><br>
<b>Labour Party won the 1945 general</b><br>
elections. Members of the Labour Party<br>
were liberals and socialist workers,<br>
who criticised the Conservative Party<br>
for allowing Britain to participate in<br>
the Second World War, which affected<br>
the British economy. It also condemned<br>
<b>British colonialism in Africa and Asia,</b><br>
saying it was out-dated, oppressive and<br>
exploitative. Thus, on gaining power<br>
in 1945, the Labour Party sped up the<br>
process of decolonising Africa.<br>
<b>The end of the war led to the formation</b><br>
of the United Nations Organisation<br>
(UNO) as a new body charged with the<br>
task of maintaining world peace and<br>
order. UNO was established to replace<br>
the League of Nations, which was too<br>
weak to maintain peace in the world.<br>
<b>From the start, UNO condemned</b><br>
colonialism and advocated the political<br>
rights of all the colonised peoples of<br>
<b>Africa and Asia. The organisation was</b><br>
very instrumental in raising African<br>
nationalistic feelings. It also imposed<br>
sanctions on the racist South African<br>
regime in the 1960s.<br>
<b>The Second World War exposed</b><br>
<b>African soldiers to the European</b><br>
environment and war, and hence<br>
they realised the weaknesses of the<br>
<b>Europeans. The ex-soldiers had been</b><br>
discriminated against in the army. They<br>
were also given unfulfilled promises<br>
by the colonial governments. By the<br>
beginning of the Second World War,<br>
<b>Africans had believed that Europeans</b><br>
were a superior race. On the contrary,<br>
during the war, the African Askaris<br>
on the battlefields realised the fallacy<br>

<b>Africa to liberate themselves. Thus, the</b><br>

<b>About 176 000 soldiers in British West</b><br>

<b>Encouragement to Africans also came</b><br>
from various examples of the wars<br>
that European forces had lost. For<br>
example, African nationalism was<br>
very much encouraged by the defeat<br>
of the Italian forces in Ethiopia in<br>

of British forces by Japanese forces in<br>
<b>Malaysia, Singapore, Hong Kong and</b><br>

<b>Africans decided to confront colonial</b><br>

<b>Askaris the confidence that they could</b><br>
defeat the Europeans. Therefore, when<br>
the ex-soldiers retuned from the war,<br>
they fought wars of liberation, which<br>
played a major role in weakening the<br>
colonial system. Good examples were<br>
the ex-soldiers who had fought in<br>
<b>Algeria, Kenya, South Africa, Angola</b><br>
and Namibia.<br>
<b>The decline of European capitalism</b><br>
and the decolonisation of Africa<br>
<b>The collapse of Western European</b><br>
economies was the result of the Second<br>
<b>World War. Therefore, there is a</b><br>
relationship between the Second World<br>
<b>War and the collapse of the European</b><br>
colonial powers in Africa. The European<br>
countries faced an economic crisis.<br>
<b>Most European countries like France</b><br>
and Britain had most of their physical<br>
infrastructure destroyed. Houses, roads,<br>
railways, ports, factories and mines<br>
were damaged. Evidence shows that<br>
France lost almost half of her wealth.<br>
<b>After the Second World War, European</b><br>
industries lacked enough raw materials.<br>
<b>For instance, the supply of rubber</b><br>
from Malaysia to Britain declined<br>
following the Japanese conquest of<br>
<b>Malaysia. The result was that there</b><br>
was a serious shortage of rubber in<br>
<b>Britain, something that retarded tyre</b><br>
industries. This shortage intensified the<br>
exploitation of the colonies. Several<br>
agricultural projects were established<br>
in an attempt to increase the production<br>
of raw materials like cotton and<br>
groundnuts for European industries.<br>
<b>The intensification of exploitation</b><br>
in the colonies met with a wave of<br>
nationalist struggles.<br>
<b>Contribution of the economic decline</b><br>
in Europe to the decolonisation of<br>
<b>Africa</b><br>
<b>European nations became dependent</b><br>
on the USA’s financial aid through the<br>
<b>Marshall Plan of 1947. The USA was</b><br>
financially stable because the world<br>
wars were never fought on its soil. In<br>
addition,it joined the war very late.<br>

<b>During the Second World War, the</b><br>
<b>USA increased concentration on the</b><br>
production of civilian and military<br>
products, something that boosted her<br>
economy. Through the Marshal Plan, the<br>
<b>USA gave loans to the Western powers</b><br>
to recover their economies on condition<br>
that they granted independence to their<br>
colonies. The European countries had<br>
no choice but to grant independence<br>
to their colonies. However, the end of<br>
colonial rule paved the way for the start<br>
of neo-colonialism, engineered by the<br>
<b>USA.</b><br>
<b>Therefore, the collapse of the European</b><br>
capitalist economies was mainly due<br>
to Europe’s full involvement in the<br>
<b>Second World War. The war affected</b><br>
the capitalists’ economies in such a way<br>
that the colonial governments failed<br>
to finance their colonies. Attempts<br>
by the colonial powers to recover<br>
their economies met with nationalist<br>
movements in Africa. Following the<br>
economic crisis, coupled with pressure<br>
from the USA, the colonial powers had<br>
no choice but to grant independence<br>
to their colonies in Africa. During<br>
the 1960s, most of Southern African<br>
countries fought wars of liberation.<br>
<b>Role of the USSR and the process of</b><br>
decolonisation in Africa<br>
<b>After the Second World War, the Union</b><br>
of the Soviet Socialist Republics<br>
<b>(USSR)</b><br>
emerged<br>
as<br>
a<br>
socialist<br>
superpower in the world. The USSR<br>
and other socialist states started<br>
dismantling capitalism in the world. It<br>
believed that colonialism was an arm<br>
of imperialism and an important part<br>
of the capitalist system. Driven by this<br>
idea, the socialist countries decided<br>
to support decolonisation efforts in<br>
<b>Africa. The Cold War politics inspired</b><br>
the USSR to provide much support to<br>
the nationalist movements in Angola,<br>
<b>South Africa, Southern Rhodesia,</b><br>
<b>Algeria, Mozambique and South West</b><br>
Africa (Namibia).<br>
<b>The USSR wanted to establish socialism</b><br>
in Africa to spread the socialist ideology<br>
and to strengthen her economic<br>
position. As an industrial power, the<br>
<b>USSR hoped to benefit from African</b><br>
markets and raw materials. Finally, the<br>
<b>USSR wanted to prevent the growing</b><br>
influence of US capitalism in Africa.<br>
<b>Thus, the Warsaw Pact was signed in</b><br>
1955 to defend socialism. It was a<br>
military alliance whose members were<br>
<b>Albania, Bulgaria, Czechoslovakia,</b><br>
<b>East Germany, Romania and the Soviet</b><br>
<b>Union (USSR). This occurred after</b><br>
the Western capitalists had established<br>
their military organisation known as<br>
the North Atlantic Treaty Organisation<br>
(NATO) to protect the capitalist<br>
ideology.<br>
<b>The USSR played a significant role</b><br>
in the establishment of UNO in 1945.<br>
<b>UNO condemned colonialism in the</b><br>
world. After the Second World War,<br>
the major world powers met in San<br>
Francisco and signed the UN Charter.<br>

<b>Article 13 of the charter made it clear</b><br>
that colonialism was an evil that had to<br>
be stamped out of the world. It declared<br>
all peoples’ right to choose governments<br>
that are best suited to them. Through<br>
the United Nations, the USSR used its<br>
veto power to pressurise the colonising<br>
powers to grant independence to their<br>
colonies. More importantly, the USSR<br>
was instrumental in inspiring UNO<br>
to adopt the Universal Declaration of<br>
Human Rights Charter in 1948.<br>
<b>The USSR linked economic prosperity</b><br>
to political independence. The USSR<br>
explained that she had been a very<br>
poor country just like African and<br>
<b>Asian countries, but she had attained</b><br>
industrialisation<br>
and<br>
economic<br>
prosperity through socialist principles<br>
and policies. The USSR’s explanation<br>
made African nationalists struggle<br>
for independence, since capitalism,<br>
through colonialism, hindered Africa’s<br>
development.<br>
<b>This</b><br>
caused<br>
panic<br>
amongst the colonial masters who<br>
sped up the decolonisation of Africa to<br>
prevent socialism from spreading to the<br>
entire continent.<br>
<b>In the effort to spread socialism, the</b><br>
<b>USSR identified herself with the anti-</b><br>
colonial struggles. She gave financial<br>
and military support to African<br>
nationalists. She gave support to<br>
<b>President Gamal Nasser of Egypt for</b><br>
building the Aswan High Dam. The<br>
<b>USSR also trained Egyptian soldiers</b><br>
and supplied weapons to the armed<br>
forces. She also gave financial support<br>
to Ahmed Sekou Toure of Guinea. The<br>
financial aid rescued Guinea Conakry<br>
from collapsing after Ahmed Sekou<br>
<b>Toure had voted no to the French</b><br>
<b>Referendum of 1958, which required</b><br>
every French colony to remain under<br>
<b>French rule. With this referendum,</b><br>
<b>France had to maintain her defence</b><br>
forces and influence foreign, economic<br>
as well as financial policies. Guinea<br>
<b>Conakry rejected this arrangement</b><br>
as she was not fully independent.<br>
<b>Immediately,</b><br>
<b>France</b><br>
granted<br>
independence to Guinea Conakry.<br>
<b>However, France uprooted all railway</b><br>
lines, destroyed roads and bridges<br>
and evacuated all civil servants from<br>
<b>Guinea Conakry. This paralysed the</b><br>
newly independent nation of Guinea<br>
<b>Conakry. The USSR and Ghana stepped</b><br>
in to assist Guinea Conakry financially<br>
and technically.<br>
<b>Moreover, the USSR gave military</b><br>
support to the Front de Libération<br>
<b>Nationale</b><br>
<b>(FLN)</b><br>
of<br>
<b>Algeria,</b><br>
<b>Movimento Polular de Libertação de</b><br>
<b>Angola (MPLA) of Angola,  Frente de</b><br>
<b>Libertao De Mocambique (FRELIMO)</b><br>
of Mozambique and the Southern West<br>
<b>Africa People’s Organisation (SWAPO)</b><br>
of Namibia. The support spreadheaded<br>
the liberation process and led to the<br>
independence of Mozambique, Angola,<br>
<b>Algeria, Guinea Bissau and Southern</b><br>
<b>Rhodesia. At the same time, the USSR</b><br>
condemned openly the Unilateral<br>
<b>Declaration of Independence (UDI)</b><br>

<b>The USSR used African independent</b><br>

like<br>
<b>Ghana,</b><br>

<b>The USSR used its political, economic</b><br>

and economic strength of the USSR<br>
quickly surpassed that of Britain and<br>
<b>France. After the Second World War, the</b><br>
<b>USSR emerged as the new superpower</b><br>
of the world.<br>
<b>The United State of America (USA)</b><br>
and the decolonisation of Africa<br>
<b>The USA exposed African students to</b><br>
the philosophy of liberal democracy.<br>
<b>It did not only provide scholarships</b><br>
to African students like Nkrumah and<br>
<b>Azikiwe to study abroad, but also</b><br>
allowed them to form democratic<br>
students’ organisations. For example,<br>
<b>West African students formed the</b><br>
<b>African Students’ Association of the</b><br>
United States and Canada in 1941.<br>
<b>The association published a monthly</b><br>
magazine, the African Interpreter, to<br>
allow students to exercise democracy<br>
while in the USA. It prepared them<br>
for nationalistic struggles in Africa.<br>
<b>When they returned to their home</b><br>
countries, they demanded democratic<br>
and constitutional rule from European<br>
colonial powers. By giving such<br>
freedom to the elites, the USA wanted<br>
to spread liberal democracy in Africa.<br>
The USA had anti-colonial traditions.<br>
<b>The USA had once been a colony and</b><br>
had been oppressed by Britain in the<br>
17th and 18th centuries. She gained her<br>
independence in 1776, after the bloody<br>
<b>American war of independence. It</b><br>
was only after gaining independence<br>
that she developed as a republican<br>
nation. She understood clearly the<br>
status of Africans and worked hard to<br>
help colonial Africa to free itself from<br>
European colonial rule.<br>
<b>USA</b><br>
<b>President Woodrow Wilson</b><br>
(1913-1921) encouraged the European<br>
colonial powers to adopt the principle<br>
of self-determination. At the Versailles<br>
<b>Peace Treaty, President Woodrow</b><br>
<b>Wilson gave his fourteen points, which</b><br>
laid the foundation for the principle of<br>
self-determination. Equally important,<br>
the Second World War depleted the<br>
resources of Britain and France, with<br>
the result that they turned to the US for<br>
economic aid. The USA demanded that<br>
independence be granted to African<br>
countries. This was achieved through<br>
the Marshall Plan. The US economic<br>
aid was known as the Marshall Plan.<br>
<b>The plan was named after the US</b><br>
<b>Secretary of State, George Marshall,</b><br>
who had initiated it in June 1947. The<br>
plan started to be effected in April<br>

<b>Since these countries needed economic</b><br>

<b>Contradictory roles of the USA in</b><br>
the decolonisation of Africa<br>
<b>While the USA strongly supported</b><br>
decolonisation in the British, French<br>
and Belgian colonies, she did not do<br>
the same for the Portuguese colonies<br>
of Angola, Mozambique and Guinea<br>
<b>Bissau. The reason behind this is that</b><br>
the USA had access to resources and<br>
markets in the Portuguese colonies and<br>
that meant that decolonisation of these<br>
countries would make no difference.<br>
<b>There was no protectionism in the</b><br>
<b>Portuguese colonies before and after the</b><br>
<b>Second World War. This implies that US</b><br>
capital and manufactured goods flowed<br>
easily into the Portuguese colonies.<br>
<b>Thus, the USA assisted the Portuguese</b><br>
in delaying the independence of their<br>
colonies in its own economic interest.<br>
<b>Additionally, the USA wanted to</b><br>
protect and consolidate capitalism<br>
and to prevent the spread of socialism<br>
to the Portuguese colonies. This was<br>
because the nationalist movements in<br>
the Portuguese colonies were strongly<br>
supported<br>
by<br>
socialist<br>
countries<br>
such as Cuba, China and the USSR.<br>
<b>The African leaders supported by</b><br>
the USSR were Dr Augustino Neto<br>
(Angola),<br>
<b>Dr</b><br>
<b>Eduardo</b><br>
<b>Mondlane</b><br>
(Mozambique) and Dr Amilcar Cabral<br>
(Guinea Bissau). Because the USSR<br>
supported<br>
decolonisation<br>
in<br>
the<br>
<b>Portuguese colonies, the USA allied</b><br>
with the Portuguese to fight against<br>
the socialist influence. Generally,<br>
the USA wanted to consolidate the<br>
capitalist block by supporting political<br>
parties which adopted her ideology<br>
and were against socialist parties.<br>
<b>For example, in Angola, the USA</b><br>
supported Jonas Savimbi and his party,<br>
<b>UNITA. Savimbi was fighting against</b><br>
the socialist party, MPLA, which was<br>
under Augustino Neto. In Mozambique,<br>
under the influence of the USA, South<br>
<b>Africa supported Resistencia Nacional</b><br>
<b>Mocambicana (RENAMO) (in English</b><br>
<b>Mozambican</b><br>
<b>National</b><br>
<b>Resistance)</b><br>
under<br>
<b>Afonso</b><br>
<b>Dhlakama</b><br>
against<br>
<b>FRELIMO, which was under Samora</b><br>
Machel.<br>
<b>The USA wanted to create puppet</b><br>
regimes in Africa similar to the regimes<br>
of Moise Tshombe and Mobutu<br>
<b>Seseseko in Zaire for the purpose of</b><br>
safeguarding her economic interests. In<br>
particular, it wanted copper, diamond<br>
and gold. The puppet leaders would<br>
enable the USA to build military bases<br>
necessary for protecting and promoting<br>
American interests in Africa.<br>

heavily in industries in the Portuguese<br>
colonies. The Portuguese government<br>

a)<br>
<b>Discuss the controversies</b><br>
surrounding<br>
the<br>
<b>US</b><br>

b)<br>
<b>What is the legacy of the</b><br>
<b>US decolonisation efforts in</b><br>
<b>Africa?</b><br>
<b>United Nations Organisation</b><br>
<b>In October 1945, shortly after the</b><br>
<b>Second World War, the United Nations</b><br>
<b>Organisation (UNO) was born in San</b><br>
<b>Francisco in the USA. It replaced the</b><br>
<b>League of Nations, which had failed to</b><br>
keep world peace by failing to prevent<br>
the outbreak of the Italian-Ethiopian<br>
<b>War (1935-1941) and the Second</b><br>
<b>World War (1939-1945). The formation</b><br>
of UNO had profound effects on the<br>
African decolonisation process.<br>
<b>After the Second World War, UNO</b><br>
set up a Trusteeship Council, which<br>
transformed the mandate territories<br>
into trustee territories. The council<br>
had to take care of such colonies as<br>
<b>Tanganyika, Libya, Togoland, South</b><br>
<b>West</b><br>
<b>Africa,</b><br>
<b>Rwanda-Urundi</b><br>
and<br>
<b>Cameroon, which formerly belonged</b><br>
to Germany and Italy. The trustee<br>
territories were to be prepared for self-<br>
government and the decolonisation<br>
process was monitored. For instance,<br>
the UNO Trusteeship Council sent<br>
<b>Commissioner Adrian Pelt to prepare</b><br>
the constitution of Libya, which<br>
enabled Libya to gain independence in<br>
1951. UNO’s trusteeship officials also<br>
made political reforms in Togoland,<br>
<b>Cameroon, Tanganyika, Somalia and</b><br>
<b>Rwanda-Urundi, which hastened the</b><br>
decolonisation process.<br>
<b>Furthermore, UNO upheld the ideas</b><br>
of the Atlantic Charter of 1941 in<br>
which US President Franklin Delano<br>
<b>Roosevelt and British Prime Minister</b><br>
<b>Winston Churchill declared their vision</b><br>
and intention to build a new and better<br>
world. Article 3 of the charter allowed<br>
all the peoples of the world to form<br>
governments they liked. Therefore,<br>
it is not surprising that, when UNO<br>
was established in 1945, it adopted<br>
the Atlantic Charter as a road map for<br>
achieving independence.<br>

<b>From this period onwards, UNO</b><br>
campaigned for independence. It was<br>
acknowledged that capitalism had<br>
caused the Second World War. To avoid<br>
the re-occurrence of such a devastating<br>
war, UNO worked on the demands<br>
and rights of the colonised peoples.<br>
<b>For example, in December 1956,</b><br>
<b>Nyerere addressed the 579th meeting</b><br>
of the Fourth Committee of the UN<br>
<b>Security Council. He talked about the</b><br>
decolonisation process in Tanganyika.<br>
<b>It could be argued generally that</b><br>
<b>African nationalist leaders used the</b><br>
UN forum to demand independence.<br>
<b>Thus, many African countries could</b><br>
achieve independence peacefully. In<br>
1960, UNO granted an audience to<br>
<b>President Kwame Nkrumah of Ghana,</b><br>
who presented Algerians’ grievances to<br>
the UN summit.<br>
<b>Algerians had suffered French nuclear</b><br>
bomb tests and other injustices.<br>
<b>Nkrumah’s</b><br>
speech<br>
made<br>
<b>UNO</b><br>
members condemn France and call for<br>
an immediate end to French colonialism<br>
in Algeria.<br>
<b>UNO set a decolonisation committee,</b><br>
which was charged with the task of<br>
working out strategies for decolonising<br>
all the colonised peoples of Asia and<br>
<b>Africa. Some of the committee members</b><br>
were African nationalists, who were<br>
campaigning for independence. They<br>
submitted reports and recommendations<br>
to UNO, explaining the progress of<br>
decolonisation in Africa.<br>
<b>UNO</b><br>
also<br>
issued<br>
the<br>
<b>Universal</b><br>
<b>Declaration of Human Rights in</b><br>
1948, which emphasised the dignity<br>
and equality of all human beings. It<br>
broadly defined a person’s political,<br>
economic, social and religious rights.<br>
<b>This document inspired all oppressed</b><br>
peoples of the world to fight for their<br>
rights. They hoped that UNO was<br>
concerned about the oppressed; hence,<br>
they were encouraged to fight for their<br>
freedom.<br>
<b>Moreover,</b><br>
<b>UNO</b><br>
recognised<br>
the<br>
liberation<br>
movements<br>
in<br>
Africa.<br>
<b>Whereas the colonial masters looked</b><br>
at African nationalist groups as rebel<br>
movements, UNO recognised them<br>
and allowed liberation leaders to<br>
present their grievances during its<br>
conferences. For example, the ANC of<br>
<b>South Africa, SWAPO of South West</b><br>
<b>Africa and FRELIMO of Mozambique</b><br>
used this opportunity to condemn the<br>
<b>Apartheid policy and further their</b><br>
need for independence. They were<br>
sympathetically listened to by UNO.<br>
<b>It is interesting to learn that UNO</b><br>
allied with the nationalist parties in<br>
the struggle for independence. With<br>
respect to South West Africa, the UN<br>
<b>General Assembly recognised SWAPO</b><br>
as a liberation force of that country.<br>
<b>The UN imposed economic sanctions</b><br>
on the racist regimes of South Africa<br>
and Southern Rhodesia. The UN<br>
prohibited countries from trading with<br>
those regimes. For example, economic<br>

<b>Exercise 6.5</b><br>
<b>Assess the argument that the</b><br>
