<b>The Bandung Conference of 1955</b><br>
<b>This conference was held in Bandung in</b><br>
<b>Indonesia from 18</b><br>
th to 24<br>
th April 1955.<br>
<b>Participants mainly came from African</b><br>
and Asian countries. One of the aims<br>
of the conference was to consider the<br>
problems affecting Asian and African<br>
people. The problems included the<br>
<b>Cold War, national sovereignty and</b><br>
colonialism. The conference was not the<br>
first to discuss issues that were affecting<br>
<b>Africa and Asia. A similar conference</b><br>
was held in Brussels (Belgium) in<br>
1927; during the conference African<br>
and Asian nationalists formed a league<br>
against imperialism and colonialism.<br>
<b>The conference was organised and</b><br>
sponsored by Indonesia, Burma, India,<br>
<b>Pakistan</b><br>
and<br>
<b>Sri-Lanka. Twenty-</b><br>
nine countries participated in the<br>
conference: 22 Asian countries, for<br>
example Indonesia, Burma, India,<br>
<b>Pakistan, Sri-Lanka and China, and</b><br>
7 African countries, namely Egypt,<br>
<b>Libya,</b><br>
<b>Ethiopia,</b><br>
<b>Nigeria,</b><br>
<b>Ghana,</b><br>
<b>Liberia and Sudan. The conference</b><br>
was convened by the president of<br>
<b>Indonesia, Ahmed Sukarno. Sukarno</b><br>
was a nationalist leader, who led<br>
<b>Indonesia to independence from the</b><br>
<b>Dutch colonialists. Figure 6.2 shows a</b><br>
picture of Ahmed Sukarno.<br>

<b>At the conference, it was declared that</b><br>
“colonialism and its manifestations are<br>
evil and must be brought to an end.”<br>
<b>The conference also addressed issues</b><br>
such as the tension between the USA<br>
and China over China’s invasion of<br>
<b>Taiwan and established a forum to</b><br>
discuss movements aimed at ending<br>
colonialism, racialism and imperialism<br>
in Africa and Asia.<br>
<b>The contribution of the Bandung</b><br>
<b>Conference to the struggle for</b><br>
independence in Africa<br>
<b>The conference influenced the struggle</b><br>
for independence in Africa in a<br>
number of ways. It identified three<br>
major problems that Africans were<br>
facing and vowed to end them. These<br>
were<br>
colonialism,<br>
institutionalised<br>
racism and the Apartheid policy. The<br>
conference declared that all these<br>
problems<br>
dehumanised<br>
<b>Africans</b><br>
and, therefore, had to be eradicated.<br>
<b>The conference established the Non-</b><br>
<b>Aligned Movement (NAM). Through</b><br>
this movement, Asian countries agreed<br>
to help Africa in promoting good will<br>
and in ending colonialism. The NAM<br>
agreed to remain neutral as far as the<br>
Cold War politics were concerned.<br>
<b>The conference brought together the</b><br>
first generation of African nationalist<br>
leaders. This, in turn, helped the leaders<br>
to explore and advance their mutual<br>
and common interests. That is, they<br>
established good relations and vowed<br>
to support each other. The conference<br>
aimed to end colonialism in Africa and<br>
safeguard the interests of independent<br>
<b>African and Asian countries. The</b><br>
participants<br>
agreed<br>
to<br>
promote<br>
cooperation<br>
among<br>
developing<br>
countries in order that they might solve<br>
their economic and political challenges<br>
together.<br>
<b>In 1957, African and Asian countries</b><br>
formed<br>
the<br>
<b>Afro-Asian</b><br>
<b>Peoples</b><br>
<b>Solidarity</b><br>
Organisation.<br>
<b>This</b><br>
organisation<br>
helped<br>
to<br>
reinforce<br>
solidarity between African and Asian<br>
nationalist leaders. This promoted<br>
unity and solidarity among African and<br>
<b>Asian states. Generally, the conference</b><br>
fostered the unity of developing<br>
nations. African nationalist leaders<br>
established relations with Yugoslavia,<br>
<b>Hungary and China, which were also</b><br>
<b>Third World countries. The conference</b><br>
identified strategies and techniques<br>
for achieving African independence.<br>
<b>African nationalists were encouraged</b><br>

to use constitutional means, after<br>
armed struggles had failed.<br>
<b>The participants called upon the</b><br>
colonial powers to grant independence<br>
to the colonies and agreed to support<br>
self-determination. This is one of<br>
the factors that enabled the Gold<br>
<b>Coast (Ghana) to be the first colony</b><br>
in sub-Saharan Africa to regain its<br>
independence in 1957 under Kwame<br>
Nkrumah.<br>
<b>All African countries are</b><br>
independent now, but how<br>
are they free politically and<br>
economically?<br>
<b>The independence of India and Burma</b><br>
<b>India was colonised by Britain from</b><br>
1858 to 1947. The struggle for Indian<br>
independence intensified after the<br>
<b>Second World War. It should be</b><br>
noted that many Indian soldiers had<br>
fought on the side of the British forces<br>
during the Second World War. The<br>
<b>Indian ex-soldiers came back from</b><br>
the war determined to end British<br>
colonial rule. The emergence of<br>
prominent nationalist figures in those<br>
countries during the 1940s enabled<br>
them to gain independence much<br>
earlier. For example, India got her<br>
independence in 1947 and one year<br>
later Burma, present-day Myanmar,<br>
also got her own independence.<br>
<b>Among the prominent nationalist</b><br>
leaders who led India to independence<br>
were Mahatma Gandhi, Jawaharlal<br>
<b>Nehru and Dr Rajendra Prasad. The</b><br>
achievement<br>
of<br>
independence<br>
by<br>
these countries encouraged African<br>
countries to intensify their struggle<br>
for independence; they had realised<br>
that they could also achieve the same.<br>
<b>The following section explains the</b><br>
contribution of that achievement to the<br>
nationalist struggles in Africa. India<br>
and Burma were not the only Asian<br>
countries that influenced nationalist<br>
struggles in Africa. Others were China<br>
and Vietnam.<br>
<b>Influences of India and Burma on the</b><br>
decolonisation of African countries<br>
<b>The independence of India and Burma</b><br>
influenced the struggle for independence<br>
in Africa in different ways. For example,<br>
having seen several Asian countries become<br>
independent, African nationalist leaders<br>
got motivated to struggle for their own<br>
independence. African countries also<br>
used the methods of struggle which<br>
<b>Asian countries had used such as the use</b><br>
of peaceful means. Africans boycotted<br>
<b>British shops and schools and used</b><br>
demonstrations and the press. For the<br>
same purpose they also campaigned for<br>
constitutional reforms.<br>
<b>Influence also came from experiences in</b><br>
the wars of liberation, which had been<br>
fought in the Asian world. The defeat<br>
of Russian forces by Japan in 1905, for<br>
example, taught Africans that they could<br>
also fight successful wars of liberation.<br>

<b>Again, during the Second World War</b><br>
<b>Japan defeated British and French forces</b><br>
in Hong Kong and Burma. It also defeated<br>
<b>American and Dutch forces in Indonesia</b><br>
and the Philippines. The Asian countries’<br>
military achievements weakened the myth<br>
regarding the superiority of Europeans.<br>
<b>This and the military experiences of the</b><br>
<b>African ex-soldiers promoted the wars</b><br>
of liberation in most African countries.<br>
<b>Independent Asian countries like India</b><br>
and Burma supported African liberation<br>
struggles in a number of ways. For example,<br>
they extended military support to the<br>
<b>African countries which were fighting</b><br>
wars of liberation. India and Burma<br>
gave weapons and medical support to<br>
soldiers in Africa. Records show that<br>
they also provided military training to<br>
the freedom fighters in South Africa,<br>
<b>South West Africa, Southern Rhodesia</b><br>
and Mozambique. Evidence also shows<br>
that Indian leaders campaigned against<br>
the racist South African regime through<br>
the UN organs. Other forms of assistance<br>
came from other Asian countries. Mao<br>
<b>Tse-tung of China gave financial, moral</b><br>
and military aid to the “free officer’s<br>
movement” in Egypt.<br>
<b>Some African nationalist leaders were</b><br>
groomed or at least were trained in Asian<br>
countries. They were advised on the best<br>
forms of struggle to adopt. For example,<br>
the Indian Prime Minister, Jawaharlal<br>
<b>Nehru, invited Milton Obote of Uganda</b><br>
to India and advised him on the good<br>
strategies to use in the struggle for the<br>
independence of Uganda. As a matter of<br>
fact, the Indian foreign policy focused on<br>
supporting the decolonisation of African<br>
countries.<br>
<b>Mahatma Gandhi’s teachings about the evils</b><br>
of colonialism also influenced the liberation<br>
struggles in Africa. Gandhi preached<br>
against the evils of colonialism using<br>
the Bible. His teachings condemned racial<br>
inequality in colonies, thereby motivating<br>
<b>African church leaders to preach against</b><br>
colonialism as well. Then, several African<br>
church leaders and important political<br>
figures protested against colonialism. For<br>
example, Bishop Desmond Tutu of South<br>
<b>Africa and Kenneth Kaunda of Zambia</b><br>
said that colonialism was incompatible<br>
with Christianity. Figure 6.3 presents a<br>
picture of Mahatma Gandhi.<br>

<b>Introduction</b><br>
Independence made Africans (including Tanzanians) hope that they would<br>
develop. The attainment of political independence was the beginning of the<br>
struggle for economic independence. The first president of Tanzania, Julius<br>
Kambarage Nyerere, talked about rapid economic development. In this chapter,<br>
you will learn about the economic and political development of Tanzania, since<br>
independence, including the Arusha Declaration. Similarly, you will learn<br>
about the educational development of Tanzania as well as the challenges facing<br>
the development effort in the country. The competencies developed will enable<br>
you to participate effectively in the efforts to bring about social, economic and<br>
political development in the country.<br>
Is there any relationship between freedom, politics and economic<br>
development?<br>
<b>Chapter</b><br>
<b>Seven</b><br>
<b>Political and economic development</b><br>
in Tanzania after independence<br>
<b>Political development in</b><br>
<b>Tanzania after independence</b><br>
<b>The European powers divided Africa</b><br>
at the Berlin Conference of 1884/85.<br>
<b>The powers used the Berlin Act</b><br>
in administering their colonies in<br>
<b>Africa. Colonial administrators made</b><br>
constitutions which served the interests<br>
of the colonial powers.<br>
<b>Tanganyika gained her independence</b><br>
on 9th December 1961. Since her<br>
independence, Tanzania has been<br>
experiencing several changes. The<br>
changes are the result of the  effort to<br>
bring socio-economic and political<br>
development to its citizens. One of the<br>
main issues in the post-colonial period<br>
in Tanzania and Africa at large had to<br>
do with the need to address the colonial<br>
political<br>
legacies.<br>
<b>Tanganyikan</b><br>
leaders made new constitutions that<br>

would address the needs of the newly<br>
independent nations. The focus was<br>
on the distribution of political powers,<br>
citizens’ responsibilities and the leaders<br>
drawing legitimacy from the people<br>
rather than from the colonial powers.<br>
<b>Constitutional changes in Tanzania</b><br>
after independence<br>
<b>A constitution is the basic principle and</b><br>
law of a nation, state or social group<br>
which determines the distribution<br>
of the powers and duties of the<br>
government and citizens, and which<br>
guarantees certain rights to both<br>
leaders and citizens. Therefore, the<br>
constitution is the fundamental law in<br>
a country. There are two constitutions<br>
in the United Republic of Tanzania.<br>
<b>One is the Constitution of the United</b><br>
<b>Republic of Tanzania of 1977, which</b><br>
includes the Union Matters. The other<br>
is the Constitution of Zanzibar of 1984.<br>
<b>A constitution can either be written</b><br>
or unwritten. Tanzania has a written<br>
constitution. From 1961 to the present,<br>
<b>Tanzania has undergone five major</b><br>
constitutional changes.<br>
<b>The first constitution was referred to as the</b><br>
<b>Independence Constitution of Tanganyika</b><br>
of 1961. The Independence Constitution<br>
replaced the Colonial Constitution. This<br>
constitution was promulgated in London<br>
by the United Kingdom Parliament and<br>
was directly imposed on Tanganyika.<br>
<b>It was characterised by a Westminster</b><br>
model with a sovereign parliamentary,<br>
multiparty democracy and a prime<br>
minister and a governor-general who<br>
represented Her Majesty the Queen of<br>
the United Kingdom, Elizabeth II, as the<br>
formal head of state. The constitution<br>
did not include a Bill of Rights. The<br>
executive was led by a prime minister,<br>
chosen from a party with the largest<br>
number of parliamentary seats. Under this<br>
constitution Mwalimu Julius Kambarage<br>
Nyerere became the first prime minister.<br>
<b>The constitution also established the</b><br>
independence of the judiciary. However,<br>
the Independence Constitution lasted for<br>
only one year.<br>
<b>The second constitution was the</b><br>
<b>Republican Constitution of 1962. The</b><br>
ruling party, TANU, had expressed<br>
its wish to have a republican form of<br>
government with an executive president.<br>
<b>The Government White Paper entitled</b><br>
“Proposals of Tanganyika Government<br>
for a Republic” was published, discussed<br>
and adopted by the National Assembly<br>
so that it could have legal force. As a<br>
result, Julius Nyerere was elected the<br>
first executive president of the Republic<br>
of Tanganyika in 1962.<br>
<b>The most important feature of the</b><br>
<b>Republican Constitution was that it</b><br>
concentrated powers on the executive<br>
president who was the head of state, the<br>
head of government, the commander-<br>
in-chief of the armed forces and part<br>
of parliament but not a member of the<br>
<b>National Assembly. The president was</b><br>
made part of parliament so that he could<br>
give assent to bills. Furthermore, the<br>

197<br>
president was given powers to appoint<br>
a vice-president and ministers, and<br>
to dissolve parliament under certain<br>
circumstances.<br>
<b>It should be noted that Zanzibar gained</b><br>
nominal independence on 10th December<br>
1963. There were two major political<br>
parties: the Zanzibar Nationalist<br>
<b>Party (ZNP), which represented the</b><br>
<b>Arab population, and the Afro-Shiraz</b><br>
<b>Party (ASP), which represented the</b><br>
<b>African population. Like Tanganyika’s,</b><br>
<b>Zanzibar’s Independence Constitution</b><br>
established a constitutional monarchy,<br>
which proclaimed the sultan of Zanzibar<br>
as a ceremonial head of state.<br>
<b>The ASP was not satisfied with this</b><br>
constitution. As a result, on 12th January<br>
1964 the ASP organised a revolution,<br>
which ousted the Zanzibar Arabs from<br>
power thus abolishing the sultanate. It<br>
should be noted that this constitution did<br>
not survive even a month. It was repealed<br>
and replaced with a series of presidential<br>
decrees. Under this system, the president<br>
exercised all executive and legislative<br>
authority on behalf of the Revolutionary<br>
<b>Council. To consolidate its position and</b><br>
prevent a counter revolution, the ASP<br>
merged Zanzibar with Tanganyika on<br>
26th April 1964.<br>
<b>The third constitution was known as the</b><br>
<b>Constitution of the United Republic of</b><br>
<b>Tanganyika and Zanzibar of 1964. In</b><br>
1964, Tanganyika and Zanzibar merged<br>
to form the United Republic of Tanzania.<br>
<b>Acting under authority conferred on</b><br>
him by Articles of Union and through<br>
<b>Government Notice No. 246 of 1st May</b><br>
1964, the president of Tanzania modified<br>
the Republic Constitution of 1962 in<br>
accordance with the agreements which<br>
<b>TANU and Zanzibar’s ruling party, the</b><br>
<b>Afro-Shirazi Party (ASP), had signed by</b><br>
issuing a decree entitled “The Interim<br>
<b>Constitution Decree of 1964”, which</b><br>
established the Interim Constitution of<br>
the United Republic of Tanganyika and<br>
<b>Zanzibar. The agreements were ratified</b><br>
under the name “Articles of Union” and<br>
included in the new constitution as “Acts<br>
of Union”. The most notable feature of<br>
<b>Acts of Union was the establishment of</b><br>
the two-tier government structure. This<br>
structure includes one government for<br>
the Union and another one for Zanzibar.<br>
<b>Zanzibar’s government included its own</b><br>
president and a Revolutionary Council.<br>
<b>The president of Zanzibar had executive</b><br>
powers over all matters related to<br>
<b>Zanzibar. The president of Zanzibar also</b><br>
served as the first vice-president of the<br>
<b>Union. The prime minister of the Union</b><br>
also became the second vice-president<br>
of the Union.<br>
<b>Under this Constitution, the Union made</b><br>
<b>Tanganyika and Zanzibar one sovereign</b><br>
state. The government of Tanganyika<br>
was replaced by the Union government,<br>
which dealt with the Union Matters and<br>
non-Union Matters related to Tanganyika.<br>
<b>There were 11 Union Matters:</b><br>
(a)  the Constitution and the government<br>
of the United Republic;<br>

<b>However, Zanzibar maintained autonomy</b><br>
over matters related to it. The two<br>
countries had to share a national flag<br>
and a national anthem.<br>
<b>Exercise 7.1</b><br>
<b>Compare the Union Matters</b><br>
of 1964 with those included in<br>
the current Constitution of the<br>
United Republic of Tanzania.<br>
<b>In 1965, the government passed</b><br>
another<br>
constitution,<br>
namely<br>
the<br>
Interim Constitution of Tanzania.<br>
<b>This was popularly known as a one-</b><br>
party constitution. This was the fourth<br>
constitution after the country had<br>
attained her independence. Through the<br>
report of a commission appointed by<br>
<b>President Julius Kambarage Nyerere,</b><br>
the Union Parliament enacted a law<br>
which declared an Interim Constitution<br>
of Tanzania which provided for a one-<br>
party system for Tanzania and Zanzibar.<br>
<b>As such, TANU and ASP became the</b><br>
only political parties in the country.<br>
<b>The Interim Constitution was amended</b><br>
several times. A major change was<br>
made in 1965 to formalise the one-<br>
party system. Coherent to the two<br>
government structure defined in 1964,<br>
the 1965 Constitution recognised two<br>
political parties, TANU for the Union<br>
and ASP for Zanzibar. In the following<br>
years, several amendments were made<br>
to strength the one-party system. For<br>
example, the amendment made in 1975<br>
stated that all government institutions,<br>
including parliament, were subordinate<br>
to the Party's Executive Committee.<br>
<b>In 1977, TANU and ASP merged to</b><br>
form Chama Cha Mapinduzi (CCM).<br>
<b>The constitution defined the power</b><br>
relations between the two countries of<br>
<b>Tanganyika and Zanzibar. It created</b><br>
a new post-colonial identity, namely<br>
<b>Tanzania, which did not exist in the</b><br>
colonial period.<br>
<b>It</b><br>
also<br>
established<br>
two<br>
organs<br>
with legislative powers: the Union<br>
<b>Parliament, which comprised members</b><br>
from Mainland Tanzania and Tanzania<br>
<b>Zanzibar. In Zanzibar, the president</b><br>
was the chairman of the Revolutionary<br>
Council, which governed the country.<br>
<b>Zanzibar was ruled through presidential</b><br>
decrees. The Revolutionary Council<br>

199<br>
had powers to make and amend laws<br>
concerning<br>
Zanzibar.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
<b>Union</b><br>
<b>Constitution abolished the multi-party</b><br>
system, which had been inherited from<br>
the colonialists. On the other hand, the<br>
<b>Union Constitution introduced general</b><br>
elections involving one political party.<br>
<b>The elections were held every five</b><br>
years.<br>
<b>The fifth constitution is the Constitution</b><br>
of the United Republic of Tanzania<br>
of 1977. This constitution essentially<br>
confirmed the main principles of the<br>
republic and interim constitutions,<br>
which included a strong executive<br>
presidency, a dual government structure<br>
and a one-party state. This constitution<br>
was made after the merger of the two<br>
political parties which were rulling<br>
Mainland Tanzania and Zanzibar.<br>
<b>Chama Cha Mapinduzi  was formed on</b><br>
5th February 1977. On 16th March 1977,<br>
through Government Notice No. 38 of<br>
25/ 3/ 1977, the president of Tanzania<br>
appointed a Constitutional Commission<br>
of 20 people: 10 from Mainland<br>
Tanzania and another 10 from Zanzibar.<br>
<b>The commission was supposed to</b><br>
prepare a constitution proposal. At<br>
the same time, through Government<br>
<b>Notice No. 39 of 25/3/1977, the</b><br>
president appointed representatives of<br>
the Constituency Assembly so that they<br>
could discuss the proposal drafted by<br>
the commission. On 25th April 1977,<br>
the Constituency Assembly discussed<br>
and passed the proposal to make the<br>
Union Constitution of 1977.<br>
<b>The Constitution of the United Republic</b><br>
of Tanzania of 1977 provides for three<br>
brunches of government: parliament,<br>
the executive and the judiciary.<br>
<b>In its preamble, the Constitution of the</b><br>
<b>United Republic of Tanzania of 1977</b><br>
declares that Tanzania is “building<br>
a  democratic society founded on the<br>
principles of freedom, justice, fraternity<br>
and concord.” The constitution required<br>
the executive to be accountable to the<br>
people. In the same way, the legislature<br>
has to be accountable to the people,<br>
since it represents them. To ensure<br>
equality before the law, the government<br>
set up the judiciary as an independent<br>
organ. The constitution contains 22<br>
<b>Union Matters:</b><br>

<b>Tanzania</b><br>

by<br>

<b>The constitution has been amended a</b><br>
number of times, as follows.<br>
<b>In 1983, a constitutional debate</b><br>
started in Tanzania, with the ruling<br>
party, CCM, setting the agenda for<br>
change. The debate revolved around<br>
three issues, namely executive power,<br>
the supremacy of parliament and<br>
participatory democracy.<br>
<b>In 1984, the constitution was amended</b><br>
so that it could include a Bill of Rights.<br>
<b>The bill provided for the freedom of</b><br>
movement, freedom of association,<br>
the right to privacy and personal<br>
security, the right to life, the right to<br>
personal freedom, the right to freedom<br>
of religion, freedom to participate in<br>
public affairs, the right to work, the<br>
right to just remuneration and the right<br>
to own property. The 1984 amendments<br>
also limited presidential mandates to<br>
two terms.<br>
<b>In February 1991,  the president of</b><br>
the United Republic of Tanzania, Ali<br>
<b>Hassan Mwinyi, appointed a Presidential</b><br>
<b>Commission, popularly known as the</b><br>
<b>Nyalali Commission. The commission</b><br>
consisted of 22 commissioners, with equal<br>
representation for Mainland Tanzania<br>
and Zanzibar. It was responsible for<br>
collecting Tanzanians’ views on whether<br>
the country should adopt a multi-party<br>
system or continue with the one-party<br>
system. The commission found that,<br>
although 72.2 per cent of the people it<br>
interviewed wanted Tanzania to continue<br>
with the one-party system, the changes<br>
requested could only be accommodated<br>
under a multi-party system. Consequently,<br>
on 29th May 1992 Tanzania passed the<br>
<b>Political Parties Act, which led to the</b><br>
re-introduction of multi-party politics<br>
into the country.<br>

<b>The Constitutional Amendment of</b><br>
1992, which laid the ground for<br>
the re-introduction of the multi-<br>
party system, came into force. The<br>
amendment changed the structure<br>
of the National Assembly so that it<br>
could comprise members elected from<br>
different political parties. Women<br>
representatives formed 15 per cent of<br>
the parliamentarians and were to be<br>
nominated by their political parties.<br>
<b>Furthermore, the National Assembly</b><br>
had to include five members elected<br>
from the House of Representatives in<br>
<b>Tanzania Zanzibar. The commissioners</b><br>
of the National Electoral Commission<br>
were to be appointed by the president.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
constitutional<br>
amendment<br>
of December 1992 provided for<br>
presidential elections and introduced<br>
the<br>
possibility<br>
of<br>
parliament<br>
to<br>
impeach the president through a vote<br>
of no confidence. It also separated the<br>
functions of the president and those of<br>
the prime minister.<br>
<b>In December 1994, a constitutional</b><br>
amendment was made following the<br>
report of the Bomani Presidential<br>
Commission.<br>
<b>Mark</b><br>
<b>Bomani</b><br>
had<br>
chaired<br>
the<br>
commission.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
commission recommended that the<br>
president of Zanzibar should cease to<br>
be the vice-president of the Union, but<br>
should continue to be a member of the<br>
<b>Union Cabinet. CCM implemented this</b><br>
recommendation.<br>
<b>Another</b><br>
constitutional<br>
amendment<br>
was made in April 2000. It introduced<br>
the following changes: First, before<br>
a candidate needed 50 per cent of the<br>
votes cast in a presidential election so<br>
as to be declared to have been elected<br>
as president of the United Republic of<br>
<b>Tanzania; a candidate now needs only</b><br>
a simple majority to become president.<br>
<b>Secondly, the president could not</b><br>
nominate anybody as Member of<br>
<b>Parliament. All Members of Parliament,</b><br>
except for the Attorney General, were<br>
elected in their constituencies. The<br>
constitutional<br>
amendment<br>
allows<br>
the President to nominate up to 10<br>
<b>Members of Parliament. Thirdly, it</b><br>
increased the number of special seats<br>
for women from 15 per cent to 20 per<br>
cent, depending on a declaration made<br>
by the National Electoral Commission<br>
from time to time, and the consent of<br>
the president. The legislature or the<br>
parliament of the United Republic of<br>
<b>Tanzania has two parts: the president,</b><br>
who is also the head of the executive<br>
and the legislature, which consists of<br>
<b>Members of Parliament elected in the</b><br>
constituencies, from special seats under<br>
affirmative action, members nominated<br>
by the president and the attorney<br>
general. The president does not sit in<br>
the National Assembly, but he or she<br>
exercises the authority vested in him or<br>
her by the constitution by giving assent<br>
to bills to complete the law enacting<br>
process.<br>

<b>Exercise 7.2</b><br>
<b>Assess the importance of the</b><br>
<b>Nyalali Commission to the</b><br>
development of democracy in<br>
Tanzania.<br>
<b>Zanzibar’s constitution</b><br>
<b>In Zanzibar, the history of constitution-</b><br>
making goes back to the Independence<br>
<b>Constitution of 1963. This constitution</b><br>
provided for monarchy leadership,<br>
with the sultan as the head of state and<br>
few executive powers exercised on<br>
the advice of the Cabinet. However,<br>
the constitution was repealed by the<br>
<b>Zanzibar Revolution of 1964. Zanzibar</b><br>
was ruled using a presidential decree<br>
passed by the Revolutionary Council.<br>
<b>The council held both the executive</b><br>
and legislative powers from 1964 to<br>
the early 1980. As a result, Zanzibar<br>
did not have a constitution from 1964<br>
to 1979 when the Revolutionary<br>
<b>Council passed the first democratic</b><br>
constitution of 1979 under the president<br>
of Zanzibar and the chairperson of the<br>
<b>Revolutionary Council, Aboud Jumbe</b><br>
<b>Mwinyi. The 1979 constitution created</b><br>
a legislative body called the House of<br>
<b>Representatives. This was followed</b><br>
by the first national election after the<br>
<b>Zanzibar Revolution. The house is</b><br>
mandated to oversight the government<br>
of Zanzibar. It was founded on 14th<br>
January 1980.<br>
<b>Zanzibar adopted a new constitution</b><br>
in 1979. Many scholars call this<br>
constitution the first constitution of<br>
<b>Zanzibar. They say so on the grounds</b><br>
that there was no constitutional<br>
continuity<br>
between<br>
the<br>
pre-1964<br>
and the post-1964 political state.<br>
<b>This new constitution provided for</b><br>
the same branches of government<br>
as those provided for the Union<br>
<b>Constitution. It made the nomination</b><br>
of a presidential candidate slightly<br>
more democratic; a special committee<br>
submits two proposed candidates to<br>
the National Executive Committee<br>
of the party (Chama cha Mapinduzi),<br>
which nominates one candidate. The<br>
name of that presidential candidate is<br>
presented to the electorate rather than<br>
the Revolutionary Council.<br>
<b>In 1984, the Zanzibar Constitution</b><br>
of 1979 was repealed and replaced<br>
by another constitution. Section 63<br>
of the Zanzibar Constitution of 1984<br>
re-establish the Zanzibar House of<br>
<b>Representatives as the legislative organ</b><br>
in Zanzibar. The constitution came<br>
into force on 12th January 1985. One<br>
of the most important issues included<br>
in this constitution is a Bill of Rights.<br>
<b>The rights include the right to equality,</b><br>
equality before the law, the right to life,<br>
personal freedom, the right to privacy<br>
and security, freedom of movement,<br>
the right to own property, freedom of<br>
conscience, freedom of association and<br>
assembly, the right to work, the right<br>

1. HIST-F5 (DOUBLE COLUMN) backUP.indd   202<br>

07/03/2022   19:00:20<br>
<b>Student's Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Advanced History for Secondary Schools</b><br>
203<br>
to a fair remuneration and the right to<br>
participate in national affairs.<br>
<b>The constitution separated the powers</b><br>
of three branches of government:<br>
the legislature, which is the House<br>
of Representative, the executive and<br>
the judiciary. The constitution also<br>
includes government accountability.<br>
<b>The constitution made it mandatory</b><br>
for the government be accountable to<br>
the people either directly or through<br>
their elected representatives. Finally,<br>
it established the Office of the Director<br>
of Public Prosecutions (DPP) as an<br>
independent organ.<br>
<b>The constitution gave every person</b><br>
the right to institute proceedings at<br>
the High Court if the constitution is<br>
violated, is being violated or is likely<br>
to be violated. The aim is to ensure that<br>
the rights stipulated in the constitution<br>
or any law are fully protected. In short,<br>
it ensures that there is the rule of law in<br>
<b>Zanzibar and that the basic rights of an</b><br>
individual are protected.<br>
<b>The Zanzibar Constitution of 1984 was</b><br>
amended in 2010. It was amended after<br>
a referendum held in July 2010 to give<br>
effect to arrangements which would<br>
govern the distribution of executive<br>
powers following the 2010 general<br>
elections. The post of chief minister,<br>
who was responsible for the day-to-day<br>
functioning of the government, was<br>
abolished. The president remained the<br>
head of government and chair of the<br>
<b>Revolutionary Council. The president</b><br>
serves only two consecutive terms of<br>
five years each. The president is the<br>
head of the executive. There are two<br>
vice-presidents, the first vice-presidents<br>
has to come from the main opposition<br>
party in the House of Representatives.<br>
<b>The second vice-president comes</b><br>
from the party that has won the most<br>
of the votes cast in a general election.<br>
<b>Ministries are appointed in keeping</b><br>
with the number of seats won in<br>
the House of Representatives. The<br>
president, the vice-presidents and the<br>
ministers, the attorney general and<br>
<b>MPs nominated by the president form</b><br>
the Revolutionary Council. From 1984<br>
to the present, the Constitution of<br>
<b>Zanzibar, has undergone several minor</b><br>
and major amendments.<br>
<b>Significance of constitutional changes</b><br>
in Tanzania after independence<br>
<b>The constitutional changes made in</b><br>
<b>Tanzania spearheaded the development</b><br>
of Tanzania after independence, as<br>
follows.<br>
<b>The changes abolished all forms of</b><br>
segregation in the provision of social<br>
services such as health, education,<br>
water,<br>
transport<br>
and<br>
housing.<br>
<b>Furthermore, the changes forbade</b><br>
exploitation.<br>
<b>The changes broadened participatory</b><br>
democracy in Tanzania. For example,<br>
general elections are held every five<br>
years. Parliament also got the power to<br>
impeach the president through a vote of<br>
no confidence.<br>

<b>The changes increased the percentage</b><br>
of special seats women MPs from 15<br>
per cent to 20 per cent, depending on<br>
declarations that the National Electoral<br>
Commission makes from time to time.<br>
<b>Moreover, it could be argued that the</b><br>
constitutional changes strengthened<br>
unity between the people of Zanzibar<br>
and Tanganyika (Mainland Tanzania).<br>
<b>Finally, the constitutional changes</b><br>
made the observance of human rights<br>
mandatory. This was evident in the<br>
amendment<br>
made<br>
in<br>
1983/1984<br>
when a Bill of Human Rights was<br>
incorporated into the constitution.<br>
<b>Thus, people are guaranteed freedom</b><br>
of movement, freedom of association,<br>
freedom of religion, the right to<br>
privacy and personal security, the right<br>
to life, personal freedom, freedom to<br>
participate in public affairs, the right<br>
to work, the right to just remuneration<br>
and the right to own property.<br>
<b>Generally, the independence constitutions</b><br>
were made to address the colonial<br>
political and social legacies and vested<br>
the sovereignty in the people; thus,<br>
the state and its organs drive their<br>
powers from the people. Moreover, the<br>
amendments are intended to address the<br>
political, economic and social changes<br>
taking place in the world and affecting<br>
<b>Tanzania. The amendments ensure</b><br>
that the authority to run the affairs of<br>
the country belong to the citizens. The<br>
power and authority of the government<br>
is derived from the citizens through the<br>
constitution.<br>
<b>Activity 7.1</b><br>
<b>In consultation with your history</b><br>
teacher,<br>
(a)  Visit an advocate or any other<br>
lawyer; interview them on<br>
the history of constitutional<br>
changes in Tanzania before and<br>
after independence.<br>
(b)  Use the information gathered to<br>
prepare notes for revision.<br>
<b>Economic development in Tanzania</b><br>
since independence<br>
<b>The term development is commonly</b><br>
associated with economic progress.<br>
<b>This is because, with economic</b><br>
development, there is a likelihood<br>
of accelerating the growth of other<br>
domains of life in a given society.<br>
<b>Economic development entails poverty</b><br>
reduction and increased productivity<br>
because<br>
of<br>
improved<br>
production<br>
techniques, increased opportunities of<br>
creating employment for young people<br>
and reduction of inequalities in the<br>
provision of social services.<br>
<b>Tanganyika was dominated by two</b><br>
colonialists at different times. It was<br>
under the German colonialists from 1885<br>
to 1918. It was also under the British<br>
colonialists from 1919 to 1961 when<br>
Tanganyika gained her independence.<br>
<b>The colonialists introduced a money</b><br>
economy in Tanganyika. Tanganyika<br>
1. HIST-F5 (DOUBLE COLUMN) backUP.indd   204<br>
1. HIST-F5 (DOUBLE COLUMN) backUP.indd   204<br>
07/03/2022   19:00:20<br>
07/03/2022   19:00:20<br>
<b>Student's Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Advanced History for Secondary Schools</b><br>
205<br>
became a producer of raw materials.<br>
<b>The raw materials were agricultural and</b><br>
natural products such as minerals and<br>
forest products. Moreover, Tanganyika<br>
became a market for manufactured<br>
goods from Europe. That means<br>
<b>European colonialism was responsible</b><br>
for the economic underdevelopment of<br>
<b>Tanganyika and other parts of Africa</b><br>
because the relations between the two<br>
benefited Europe in general and Britain<br>
in particular.<br>
<b>When Tanganyika gained independence</b><br>
on 9th December 1961, its economy<br>
remained tied to that of the capitalist<br>
world. Generally, the nation did not<br>
have sufficient trained manpower. The<br>
country inherited an economy, which<br>
was largely dependent on the developed<br>
countries. Moreover, its economy<br>
depended on the export of cash crops<br>
and restricted foreign investment. The<br>
economy also continued to be in the<br>
hands of foreigners, especially the<br>
<b>British, Arabs and Indians, who were</b><br>
the leading merchants. Industries,<br>
farms, plantations, banks, mines and<br>
large commercial activities continued<br>
to be under British domination.<br>
<b>At independence, Tanganyika inherited</b><br>
a poor transport network. The transport<br>
infrastructure connected the centres<br>
of production with the coast. This<br>
was done to serve the interests of<br>
the colonial power. In some areas of<br>
production like Moshi, Arusha, Tanga<br>
and Morogoro the people had more<br>
access to transport infrastructure, while<br>
the labour reserves and other areas<br>
had limited or no transport networks<br>
at all. This situation caused regional<br>
imbalances.<br>
<b>Economic development between</b><br>
1961 and 1981<br>
<b>After the attainment of independence,</b><br>
industries, farms, plantations, banks,<br>
mines and large commercial activities<br>
continued to be under foreign investors.<br>
<b>Therefore, the people of Tanzania did not</b><br>
enjoy the fruits of their independence.<br>
<b>The major task of the independent</b><br>
government was, therefore, to promote<br>
economic development. This was done<br>
in phases and with development plans,<br>
which lasted from 1961 to 1981. Four<br>
plans were implemented during this<br>
period. They were the Three-Year<br>
<b>Development Plan (1961-1964), the</b><br>
<b>First Five-Year Development Plan</b><br>
(1964-1969), The Second Five-Year<br>
<b>Development Plan (1969-1974) and</b><br>
the Third Five-Year Development Plan<br>
(1974-1981).<br>
<b>The Three-Year Development Plan</b><br>
was the first plan to be adopted. It<br>
was based on an extensive survey of<br>
<b>Tanganyika economy. It was prepared</b><br>
by a World Bank Mission. It was not<br>
a comprehensive plan which could<br>
guarantee public capital expenditure,<br>
but it reflected the main priorities<br>
of the Tanganyika government. The<br>
main economic objective of the plan<br>
was to ensure a rapid and progressive<br>
1. HIST-F5 (DOUBLE COLUMN) backUP.indd   205<br>
1. HIST-F5 (DOUBLE COLUMN) backUP.indd   205<br>
07/03/2022   19:00:21<br>
07/03/2022   19:00:21<br>
<b>Student's Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Advanced History for Secondary Schools</b><br>
206<br>
increase in the cash income per head<br>
of the population as well as a steady<br>
improvement in the people’s standard<br>
of living.<br>
This plan had three specific objectives.<br>
<b>First, it was aimed at developing</b><br>
agriculture and the livestock industry.<br>
<b>Under this objective, the plan was</b><br>
aimed at boosting crop cultivation<br>
and animal keeping by supporting<br>
farmers through the provision of<br>
credits, agricultural extension services,<br>
the establishment of co-operative<br>
societies, the search for markets, the<br>
provision of farm implements and the<br>
establishment of river-based irrigation<br>
schemes and agricultural resettlement<br>
schemes. Secondly, the plan was<br>
aimed at improving and developing<br>
communication systems, specifically<br>
the construction of feeder roads in<br>
rural areas. Thirdly, the plan was aimed<br>
at developing primary, secondary and<br>
technical education, increasing student<br>
enrollment improving and the quantity<br>
of facilities in schools and colleges. In<br>
the industrial sector, the emphasis was<br>
placed on the improvement of import-<br>
substitution industries to increase<br>
the availability of basic needs and to<br>
reduce the quantity of imported goods.<br>
<b>However, owing to limited funds,</b><br>
the implementation of the Three-<br>
<b>Year Development Plan was not as</b><br>
successful as expected.<br>
<b>The First Five-Year Development Plan,</b><br>
which lasted from 1964 to 1969, was<br>
the first comprehensive and ambitious<br>
plan. The government’s main goal<br>
was to achieve rapid economic growth<br>
and self-sufficiency. In that regard, the<br>
plan had three main objectives, which<br>
would have been realised by 1980.<br>
<b>The first objective was to raise the per</b><br>
capita income from £19 in 1964 to<br>
£45 by the end of 1980. The second<br>
objective was to ensure self-sufficiency<br>
in trained manpower. The third<br>
objective was to raise life expectancy<br>
from 35 to 40 years in 1964 to 50 years<br>
by the end of the plan. In order to<br>
increase life expectancy and improved<br>
the standard of living, emphasis was<br>
placed on ensuring that safe water was<br>
available to all and health services<br>
were improved upon through the<br>
construction of dispensaries, health<br>
centres and hospitals. However, at the<br>
end of the projected period, not much<br>
had been achieved. It was found that<br>
only five out of the 80 health centres<br>
planned had been completed. Similarly,<br>
in order to raise the per capita income,<br>
the government placed more emphasis<br>
on the development of agriculture,<br>
which was considered the backbone<br>
of the country’s economy. In order to<br>
achieve this goal, the plan allocated 15<br>
per cent of development funds to the<br>
agricultural sector. The government<br>
also aimed to train its own manpower<br>
such as teachers, doctors, administrators<br>
and factory managers. This means that<br>
the plan was aimed at speeding up the<br>
process of africanisation.<br>

<b>The Second Five-Year Development</b><br>
<b>Plan (1969-1974) was aimed at</b><br>
raising Tanzanians’ standard of living.<br>
<b>Furthermore, the plan was aimed at</b><br>
bringing about economic changes. The<br>
main objective was to identify domestic<br>
resources that would contribute to the<br>
growth of the national economy and<br>
strengthen rural development. This<br>
could be achieved by expanding and<br>
increasing<br>
agricultural<br>
production<br>
as well as identifying resources that<br>
would help to build and strengthen<br>
industries in the country. It should also<br>
be noted that the plan was aimed at<br>
implementing the Arusha Declaration.<br>
<b>Therefore, it was built on five main</b><br>
pillars:<br>
(a)	 Equality of all citizens. The plan<br>
was aimed at ensuring that all<br>
citizens benefited from national<br>
development initiatives;<br>
(b)	 Socialism (Ujamaa). The plan<br>
emphasised doing collective and<br>
co-operative economic activities<br>
and<br>
reducing<br>
the<br>
income<br>
and<br>
economic<br>
gap<br>
between<br>
individuals;<br>
(c)	 Self-reliance (Kujitegemea). The<br>
plan insisted on developing the<br>
country using our own resources,<br>
specifically the energies and<br>
resources in the villages;<br>
(d)	 Making economic changes and<br>
changing the lives of the people.<br>
<b>The plan emphasised increasing</b><br>
the national income and the per<br>
capita income, and growing the<br>
national economy; and<br>
(e)	 Cooperating with other African<br>
countries. The plan insisted on<br>
expanding<br>
and<br>
strengthening<br>
cooperation with other African<br>
countries in economic matters.<br>
<b>In the industrial sector, the plan focused</b><br>
on industrial development in Tanzania.<br>
<b>It was expected that there would be five</b><br>
categories of industries in the country.<br>
<b>These were industries which could</b><br>
manufacture simple consumer goods,<br>
industries which processed agricultural<br>
crops ready for export, industries which<br>
manufactured<br>
building<br>
materials,<br>
industries which refined oil and<br>
petrol and industries which produced<br>
agricultural implements.<br>
<b>The Third Five-Year Development</b><br>
<b>Plan (1976-1981) was the last medium</b><br>
term plan in the implementation<br>
of the long-term plan in the period<br>
from 1964 to 1981. This was the first<br>
plan made by all public authorities<br>
at the village, district, regional and<br>
national levels. This plan was made<br>
by the people through the planning<br>
commission, which comprised all<br>
<b>Members of Parliament. The Third</b><br>
<b>Five-Year Development Plan was</b><br>
aimed at furthering the objectives of the<br>
previous plans. In addition, this plan<br>
emphasised self-sufficiency in food by<br>
1981, proper and efficient utilisation<br>
of natural resources, processing of<br>
primary commodities into final and<br>

and restructuring the industrial set-<br>

education, satisfying the needs for<br>
water and electricity for industrial<br>
use and strengthening communication<br>
services.<br>
<b>Achievements of development plans</b><br>
(1961-1981)<br>
<b>Several achievements were made. One</b><br>
of the achievements was development<br>
of the agricultural sector. For example,<br>
at independence, the annual agricultural<br>
production stood at 140 000 tonnes, but<br>
after the implementation of the First<br>
<b>Five-Year Plan, production rose to</b><br>
500 000 tonnes. This increase was the<br>
result of improvements in agricultural<br>
production methods and the use of<br>
tractors and fertilizers.<br>
<b>There was also an improvement in</b><br>
the education sector following the<br>
implementation of the plans. For<br>
example, illiteracy rates were drastically<br>
reduced. This was due to various steps<br>
that the government took to improve<br>
the<br>
education<br>
sector,<br>
including<br>
establishing schools, increasing pupils’<br>
enrolment and improving the quality of<br>
adult education. These steps increased<br>
the number of Tanzanian experts in<br>
the civil service as well as in private<br>
companies.<br>
<b>Moreover, the development plans</b><br>
helped to improve the quality of health<br>
services.  For example, there was an<br>
increase in the number of dispensaries,<br>
health centres and hospitals. Therefore,<br>
rural and urban communities had more<br>
access to medical facilities. People’s<br>
living standards and life expectancy<br>
also improved. For example, life<br>
expectancy rose from 35-40 years in<br>
1964 to 40-41 years in 1967. The Gross<br>
<b>Domestic Product (GDP) grew by 5 per</b><br>
cent during the First Five-Year Plan,<br>
although the growth was lower than<br>
the projected growth of 6.7 per cent.<br>
<b>During the Second Five-Year Plan, the</b><br>
<b>GDP grew at 4.8 per cent, which was</b><br>
lower than the projected growth. The<br>
growth rate of the GDP in the Second<br>
<b>Five-Year Plan was very low owing</b><br>
to the decline in crop production as a<br>
result of prolonged drought and little<br>
investment in the sector. Other factors<br>
for the low growth of the GDP were<br>
inadequate preparation of projects,<br>
poor performance of the industrial<br>
sector and the world-wide rise of the<br>
price of oil since 1971.<br>
<b>Furthermore, the number of industries</b><br>
in the country increased. This, in turn,<br>
reduced the importation of goods and<br>
increased employment opportunities<br>
for Tanzanians. For example, in<br>
1975, a Basic Industrial Strategy was<br>
introduced. This strategy was well<br>
implemented during the period of the<br>
third plan. As a result, the government<br>
built industries such as the Tanzania<br>
<b>Petroleum Refinery Industry, Musoma</b><br>

<b>Challenges</b><br>
<b>There was an inadequate number</b><br>
of experts and skilled manpower<br>
as most of the projects were run by<br>
<b>Tanzanians who, at the time, were</b><br>
not experienced enough to implement<br>
them. For example, there was a<br>
scarcity of engineers to supervise road<br>
construction projects or skilled people<br>
to run industrial machines. This means<br>
that Tanzania was still depending on<br>
foreign experts. Such experts did not<br>
maximise the use of their energies and<br>
skills for Tanzania’s development.<br>
<b>Thus, low production was exacerbated</b><br>
by the low level of technology as<br>
most of the farmers were still using<br>
hand hoes. This led to low production<br>
and low foreign exchange. Similarly,<br>
from the mining sector very little<br>
was obtained because of using poor<br>
technology.<br>
<b>Moreover, there were inadequate</b><br>
funds<br>
for<br>
implementing<br>
the<br>
development plans. The shortage of<br>
funds resulted from the decline in<br>
the amount of foreign aid. This was<br>
partly because Tanzania had broken<br>
diplomatic relations with Britain and<br>
<b>West Germany. For example, Tanzania</b><br>
decided to break diplomatic relations<br>
with Britain because of the Unilateral<br>
<b>Declaration of Independence (UDI)</b><br>
made in Southern Rhodesia in 1965.<br>

Britain withheld all its aid to Tanzania.<br>
<b>Hence, some of the development</b><br>
projects such as the construction of<br>
industries and infrastructure were not<br>
implemented.<br>
<b>The rapid population growth was</b><br>
another challenge in the course of<br>
implementing the First Five-Year<br>
<b>Development Plan. Population growth</b><br>
increased the demand for services and<br>
commodities that could not be easily<br>
obtained. Environmental hazards such<br>
as prolonged drought also affected<br>
the plan. Finally, a drastic fall in the<br>
prices of crops at the world market<br>
lowered the value of key Tanzanian<br>
exports such as coffee, tobacco and<br>
sisal. Tanzania highly depended on<br>
the export of agricultural cash crops,<br>
especially sisal, for foreign exchange.<br>
<b>The fall of the price of sisal at the world</b><br>
market owing to the development of<br>
synthetic fibres such as nylon deprived<br>
<b>Tanzania of foreign exchange. The</b><br>
prices of other crops also declined. As<br>
a result, Tanzania did not have enough<br>
funds to implement the plan.<br>
<b>Exercise 7.3</b><br>
<b>Why did Tanzania come up</b><br>
with the national development<br>
plans in her early years of<br>
independence?<br>
<b>The Arusha Declaration</b><br>
<b>On 5th February 1967, the government</b><br>
of the United Republic of Tanzania<br>
adopted the Arusha Declaration. The<br>
<b>Declaration was adopted during the</b><br>
<b>First Five-Year Development Plan, but</b><br>
was implemented during the Second<br>
<b>Five-Year Development Plan. The</b><br>
<b>Declaration was a special proclamation</b><br>
propagated by the president of the<br>
<b>United Republic of Tanzania, Julius</b><br>
<b>Kambarage</b><br>
<b>Nyerere,</b><br>
in<br>
<b>Arusha</b><br>
through<br>
the<br>
<b>National</b><br>
<b>Executive</b><br>
<b>Committee of TANU. The Declaration</b><br>
stated that Tanzania was building a<br>
socialist society. It was a response to<br>
the growing neo-colonial influence that<br>
hindered Tanzanians from enjoying the<br>
fruits of independence and achieving<br>
socio-economic development.<br>
<b>The Declaration was aimed at building</b><br>
a socialist and self-reliant nation<br>
based on the principles of equality,<br>
justice, democracy and human dignity.<br>
<b>Education for self-reliance and rural</b><br>
development were at the heart of the<br>
<b>Arusha Declaration. The Declaration</b><br>
also focused on the eradication of all<br>
forms of exploitation, nationalisation<br>
of all major means of production,<br>
democracy and the provision of<br>
education to all. The Arusha Declaration<br>
instituted a code of leadership ethics. It<br>
provided leadership principles and rules<br>
in Tanzania. Some of the principles and<br>
rules required a leader not to have more<br>
than one salary, not to own houses for<br>

rent and not to be a board member or<br>
a director of any private company. The<br>
principles and rules laid the foundation<br>
for<br>
<b>Tanzanian</b><br>
socialism,<br>
namely<br>
<b>Ujamaa. Ujamaa implied Tanzania’s</b><br>
commitment to a more collective way<br>
of rural production.<br>
<b>Furthermore, the Arusha Declaration</b><br>
was intended to promote public<br>
ownership of the major means of<br>
production by putting them in the<br>
hands of workers and peasants through<br>
their representative government and<br>
cooperatives. The major means of<br>
production were land, forests, mineral<br>
resources,<br>
water,<br>
oil,<br>
electricity,<br>
communications,<br>
transport,<br>
banks,<br>
insurance, import-and-export trade,<br>
wholesale businesses, steel machine<br>
tools, motor cars, cement and fertilizer<br>
factories as well as textile industries.<br>
<b>It condemned the exploitation of man</b><br>
by man and emphasised collective<br>
production<br>
and<br>
distribution<br>
of<br>
resources. The Declaration openly<br>
declared that Tanzania was a nation of<br>
peasants and workers. It encouraged<br>
popular participation in all important<br>
decision-making processes.<br>
<b>Moreover, the Declaration discouraged</b><br>
foreign aid as the main source of<br>
capital for economic development.<br>
<b>Instead, it emphasised the use of</b><br>
domestic resources. However, this did<br>
not mean that the government did not<br>
receive foreign aid at all. It continued<br>
to receive foreign aid, but it was rather<br>
minimal. Foreign aid and assistance<br>
were accepted only if they did not<br>
jeopardise the country’s independence<br>
and freedom. As noted earlier, the<br>
adoption of the Arusha Declaration<br>
shaped the way the Second and the<br>
<b>Third Five-Year Plans were designed</b><br>
and implemented. Figure 7.2 shows<br>
<b>President Julius K. Nyerere and other</b><br>
leaders marching in support the of<br>
Arusha Declaration in 1967.<br>

leaders marching during the<br>
<b>Arusha Declaration</b><br>

<b>Economic changes after the Arusha</b><br>
<b>Declaration</b><br>
<b>After the Arusha Declaration, the</b><br>
government nationalised all the major<br>
means of production which were<br>
privately owned by compensating the<br>
former owners. Some of the nationalised<br>
foreign assets were Standard Bank,<br>
<b>Barclays</b><br>
<b>Bank,</b><br>
<b>British-American</b><br>
<b>Tobacco Company and Bata Shoes</b><br>
<b>Industry. The government also entered</b><br>
into partnerships with companies as the<br>
country had no capital and technology<br>
to run them. In so doing, the government<br>
ensured that all the major means of<br>
production were collectively owned by<br>
the people of Tanzania. The natural and<br>
human resources were utilised for the<br>
benefit of all, regardless of their social-<br>
economic status.<br>
<b>In</b><br>
implementing<br>
the<br>
<b>Arusha</b><br>
<b>Declaration, the government set up</b><br>
various public corporations. The public<br>
corporations included the National<br>
<b>Bank of Commerce (1967), the</b><br>
<b>National Textile Corporation (1968),</b><br>
<b>Tanzania Tobacco Authority (1970),</b><br>
the State Mining Corporation (1972)<br>
and Tanzania Cotton Authority (1973).<br>
<b>These public enterprises employed</b><br>
<b>Tanzanians and thus increased their</b><br>
purchasing power. The government<br>
owned the co-operations and authorities<br>
by 100 per cent.<br>
<b>Between 1967 and 1973 Tanzania</b><br>
experienced a remarkable rate of<br>
industrial growth. It was during<br>
this<br>
time<br>
that<br>
import-substitution<br>
manufacturing industries expanded.<br>
<b>Some</b><br>
of<br>
the<br>
import-substitution<br>
industries that did well in the mid-1960s<br>
were textile, beer, cement, cigarette<br>
and soap industries. Furthermore,<br>
the government established other<br>
industries, which could produce basic<br>
tools for farmers. For example, in 1970,<br>
the Ubungo Farmers’ Implements<br>
(UFI) was established for this purpose.<br>
<b>This industry could produce 2 500 000</b><br>
hand hoes a year. During the 1970s,<br>
plans were made to explore the Liganga<br>
iron ore and the Kiwira coal fields. The<br>
government also established bicycle,<br>
radio and battery assembling factories<br>
in Dar es Salaam and Arusha.<br>
<b>However, the industrial boom of 1967-</b><br>
1973 dropped in the subsequent years.<br>
<b>Tanzania experienced a sharp decline</b><br>
in the industrial sector between 1973<br>
and 1983. Some of the reasons for<br>
the decline were the deterioration in<br>
commercial agriculture, particularly<br>
cash crop production. Other factors<br>
were poor planning, maladministration<br>
and overstaffing. By 1975, Tanzania<br>
had eight textile industries that were<br>
performing poorly. The most famous<br>
industries<br>
were<br>
<b>Urafiki</b><br>
<b>Textile,</b><br>
<b>Sungura Textile, Musoma Textile and</b><br>
Mwanza Textile Industries.<br>
<b>After 1967,  the government embarked</b><br>
on improving agricultural output. For<br>
example, oxen training centres (OTC)<br>

peasants in how to use ox-ploughing to<br>
improve agricultural production and<br>
productivity. With the introduction<br>
of Ujamaa and the villagisation<br>
programme, the mechanisation of<br>
farms was implemented. For example,<br>
tractors were distributed to sampled<br>
<b>Ujamaa villages, depending on the</b><br>
amount of output and organisation.<br>
<b>The other efforts made to improve</b><br>
agriculture included the establishment<br>
of a tractor assembling plant (Valmet)<br>
at Kibaha in the 1980s. The plant had<br>
the capacity of assembling 500 tractors<br>
a year. The other factory for agricultural<br>
improvement was called Zana za<br>
<b>Kilimo Mbeya (ZZK). This factory</b><br>
and the Ubungo Farmers’ Implements<br>
(UFI) in Dar es Salaam produced<br>
various agricultural implements such<br>
as iron hoes, threshers, ploughs and<br>
harvesting<br>
machines. Apart<br>
from<br>
increasing<br>
agricultural<br>
production,<br>
they also provided direct employment<br>
opportunities to many Tanzanians.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
after<br>
the<br>
<b>Arusha</b><br>
<b>Declaration the agricultural sector was</b><br>
given more impetus through the banks<br>
which had been established to support<br>
farmers. For example, the government<br>
established the Tanzania Investments<br>
<b>Bank (TIB) in 1970 and the Cooperative</b><br>
and Rural Development Bank (CRDB)<br>
in 1971. These banks provided loans to<br>
African farmers.<br>
<b>The implementation of the Arusha</b><br>
<b>Declaration gained support from socialist</b><br>
countries. Various projects were funded<br>
by socialist countries. For example, in<br>
the 1970s, a railway line from Dar es<br>
<b>Salaam to Kapiri Mposhi in Zambia was</b><br>
constructed with  a loan from China. The<br>
railway came to be known as Tanzania-<br>
Zambia Railway Authority (TAZARA).<br>
<b>TAZARA and the Petroleum Pipeline</b><br>
known as Tanzania Zambia Mafuta<br>
(TAZAMA) employed many Tanzanians.<br>
<b>The Chinese government also opened the</b><br>
<b>Mbarali Rice Farm in Mbeya Region. It</b><br>
was one the most successful agricultural<br>
establishments in Africa in the 1970s<br>
and 1980s.<br>
<b>Despite the commendable efforts by</b><br>
the government of Tanzania to build a<br>
socialist state and to promote economic<br>
development<br>
after<br>
the<br>
<b>Arusha</b><br>
<b>Declaration, the government faced</b><br>
several challenges. In the first place,<br>
there were problems related to the<br>
nature of nationalisation itself. Critics<br>
argue that the process was undertaken<br>
without enough preparation, especially<br>
trained manpower to run the nationalised<br>
economic enterprises. Other critics<br>
point out that the compensation given<br>
to the former owners of enterprises<br>
was wasteful spending of limited<br>
resources. As expected, the expensively<br>
compensated enterprises failed to<br>
generate economic benefits.<br>
<b>Moreover, the oil crisis which stemmed</b><br>
from the Yom-Kippur war between<br>

<b>Israel and some Arab states in 1973</b><br>

1973, Tanzania was spending almost<br>
half of her foreign earnings buying<br>
oil for the industrial and transport<br>
sectors. While the price of oil had<br>
increased, the prices of machines like<br>
tractors and spare parts, imported from<br>
Europe, also increased tremendously.<br>
<b>This negatively affected industrial</b><br>
performance, thus leading to a decline<br>
in industrial production.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
the<br>
<b>East</b><br>
<b>African</b><br>
<b>Community collapsed in 1977, thereby</b><br>
affecting the economy of Tanzania.<br>
<b>This was due to a drop in the volume of</b><br>
<b>Tanzania’s trade with her partner states</b><br>
in East Africa. The decrease volume of<br>
trade worsened the economic situation<br>
in the country. Moreover, the collapse<br>
of the community forced Tanzania to<br>
buy ships and airplanes using foreign<br>
exchange so as to continue providing<br>
its citizens with the services which<br>
the East African Community had<br>
been providing. The aforementioned<br>
expenditure diminished the foreign<br>
currency reserve. Hence, an economic<br>
crisis manifested itself in the failure to<br>
import goods and spare parts.<br>
<b>Another economic challenge was</b><br>
caused<br>
by<br>
the<br>
<b>Kagera War</b><br>
of<br>
1978/1979, which forced the country<br>
to spend millions of shillings in the<br>
war. This means that the financial<br>
and human resources spent in the war<br>
could have been invested in economic<br>
production to stimulate development in<br>
the country.<br>
<b>Exercise 7.4</b><br>
<b>Suggest</b><br>
measures<br>
which<br>
should have been taken by<br>
the government to rescue the<br>
industrial<br>
and<br>
agricultural<br>
sectors during the 1970s and<br>
1980s.<br>
<b>Educational development in</b><br>
<b>Tanzania after independence</b><br>
<b>After the independence of Tanganyika</b><br>
in 1961 and the Zanzibar Revolution in<br>
1964, the education sector was reformed<br>
to meet the needs of the country.<br>
<b>However, in order to understand the</b><br>
reforms well, it is important to review<br>
the nature of the colonial educational<br>
system, which preceded the post-<br>
colonial educational system.<br>
<b>Colonial education policies and their</b><br>
effects in Tanganyika up to 1961<br>
<b>In any society education is an</b><br>
ideological tool aimed at shaping<br>
the society based on the interests of<br>
the people of that particular society.<br>
<b>Colonial education was an ideological</b><br>
tool used to safeguard colonial interests.<br>
<b>Therefore, the colonial education</b><br>
policy was structured in such a way<br>
that it safeguarded colonial interests in<br>
the colonies. The introduction colonial<br>
education undermined pre-colonial<br>

education.<br>
<b>To</b><br>
<b>Africans,</b><br>
colonial<br>
education was aimed at producing<br>
messengers, clerks and watchmen to<br>
assist in colonial administration and in<br>
the running of the colonial economy.<br>
<b>Therefore, colonial education was not</b><br>
given to all Africans. Rather, it was<br>
aimed at creating a class of Africans<br>
who would be used to realise  the<br>
<b>Europeans’ interests. It also prepared</b><br>
<b>Africans to be good producers of cash</b><br>
crops.<br>
<b>During the early years of colonial rule,</b><br>
most schools were run by missionaries<br>
for evangelisation purposes. In 1892,<br>
the German colonial government in<br>
<b>German East Africa (Tanganyika)</b><br>
established its first government school<br>
in Tanga. European educational system<br>
operated in the colony. By 1902, the<br>
government had three main schools in<br>
<b>Tanga, Bagamoyo and Dar es Salaam,</b><br>
and three subsidiary schools in Kilwa,<br>
<b>Pangani and Lindi. The European</b><br>
educational system in the colony<br>
developed more during the British<br>
period, especially after the Phelps<br>
<b>Stokes Commission of 1923. The</b><br>
commission said that there was a great<br>
need for education in Africa. Some of<br>
the famous schools and colleges which<br>
were established after  the Phelps<br>
<b>Stokes</b><br>
<b>Commission</b><br>
were Tabora<br>
<b>Boys (1924), Malangali, Buigiri Girls</b><br>
<b>School (1927) and Ukiliguru College</b><br>
of Agriculture (1939).<br>
<b>Objectives of colonial education</b><br>
<b>Colonial education was aimed at</b><br>
preparing a few Africans who could<br>
occupy lower positions like being<br>
messengers and clerks. This was to<br>
be achieved through the provision of<br>
elementary education in bush schools,<br>
missionary schools and government<br>
schools. The colonialists wanted to<br>
get people who could do clerical work<br>
like arranging files and posting letters.<br>
<b>The Africans who held such positions</b><br>
were paid low salaries and  helped the<br>
colonialists to reduce administration<br>
costs.<br>
<b>The colonial education provided by</b><br>
missionaries was aimed at converting<br>
<b>Africans to Christianity. In the schools</b><br>
run by missionaries, spiritual teachings<br>
were incorporated into the syllabi. This<br>
type of education was aimed at making<br>
Africans easily accept colonialism.<br>
<b>Colonial education was aimed at</b><br>
propagating<br>
racial<br>
segregation<br>
between Africans, Indians, Arabs and<br>
<b>Europeans. Thus, members of every</b><br>
racial group had their own schools. This<br>
was done purposely to make Africans<br>
feel inferior. Africans mainly received<br>
primary education and, indeed, very<br>
few got tertiary education, which was<br>
reserved for Europeans and Indians.<br>
<b>Moreover, colonial education was</b><br>
aimed at dividing and weakening<br>
colonial subjects by making them<br>
loyal and submissive to their colonial<br>

216<br>
masters. Education was a means of<br>
facilitating the ultimate goal of political<br>
domination and economic exploitation.<br>
<b>The education provided became a</b><br>
mechanism for preparing Africans to<br>
be loyal and docile. Therefore, colonial<br>
education left Africans ill-equipped<br>
in relation to their efforts to improve<br>
their living conditions because it was<br>
not meant to develop their critical<br>
consciousness and intellectual ability.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
colonial<br>
education<br>
was<br>
aimed<br>
at<br>
promoting<br>
and<br>
propagating European culture, history<br>
and geography. The nature of the<br>
knowledge taught reflected European<br>
social,<br>
political<br>
and<br>
economic<br>
achievements. For example, at school,<br>
<b>Africans learnt about the physical</b><br>
landscape of Europe like the Alps and<br>
the River Rhine, and neglected African<br>
physical geography. The colonial<br>
syllabi glorified European history.<br>
<b>History topics for Africans were full of</b><br>
<b>Europeans’ efforts to civilise Africans</b><br>
through the abolition of the slave trade,<br>
the introduction of cash crops and the<br>
building of schools, hospitals, roads<br>
and railways. Generally, the syllabi<br>
potrayed an image that showed that the<br>
colonial powers were not oppressors or<br>
exploiters.<br>
<b>Features of colonial education</b><br>
<b>Colonial education had features that</b><br>
were different from those of pre-<br>
colonial African education.<br>
<b>Colonial education was based on</b><br>
racial discrimination. For example, it<br>
favoured Europeans and Asians, while<br>
at the same time discriminating against<br>
<b>Africans. In primary and secondary</b><br>
schools, there were more Europeans<br>
and Asians than Africans. The number<br>
of Africans who attended colonial<br>
schools was very limited. Even those<br>
who attended such schools received<br>
elementary education so as to facilitate<br>
colonial production. The content of<br>
this education was designed in such a<br>
way that it served the interests of the<br>
minority Europeans rather than those<br>
of the majority Africans.<br>
<b>Colonial education was</b><br>
provided<br>
to few Africans, mainly the sons of<br>
chiefs, catechists, pastors and rich<br>
people. This was deliberately done to<br>
win their loyalty as this was important<br>
for colonial administration and church<br>
evangelisation.<br>
<b>Colonial education was based on gender</b><br>
imbalances as it was mainly provided<br>
to males. Girls were ignored on the<br>
grounds that they were economically<br>
less productive. In 1955, for example,<br>
there were a total of 150 students from<br>
<b>Tanganyika studying at Makerere</b><br>
<b>University College, but only four of</b><br>
them were women. This imbalance was<br>
also due to African culture, which made<br>
parents give more opportunities to<br>
boys than girls. Similarly, the colonial<br>
government preferred training boys to<br>
training girls.<br>

<b>Colonial</b><br>
education<br>
was<br>
very<br>
theoretical but  pre-colonial education<br>
was practical. Colonial education also<br>
concentrated on teaching reading,<br>
writing and arithmetic. This was done<br>
to orient Africans to white-collar jobs.<br>
<b>The theoretical aspect of colonial</b><br>
education was meant to make Africans<br>
dependent on the colonialists.<br>
<b>Colonial education was pyramidal in</b><br>
shape because many students were at<br>
the lower levels of education and the<br>
number kept decreasing as one moved<br>
up the education ladder.<br>
<b>Colonial</b><br>
schools<br>
were<br>
unevenly<br>
distributed,<br>
which<br>
reflected<br>
the<br>
colonial economic regional divisions.<br>
<b>Most schools were established in the</b><br>
regions where the colonialists had<br>
established economic activities such<br>
as Kilimanjaro, Tanga, Dar es Salaam,<br>
<b>Mbeya and Kagera. The labour reserve</b><br>
regions such as Kigoma, Mtwara,<br>
<b>Lindi, Singida and Rukwa were</b><br>
marginalised. In 1939, for example, the<br>
boys who attended primary school in<br>
<b>Tanga were around 27 per cent, while</b><br>
in the Southern Highlands only three<br>
per cent attended primary school.<br>
<b>Therefore, colonial education facilitated</b><br>
the process of underdeveloping and<br>
exploiting Africans because it granted<br>
elementary education to Africans. In<br>
other instances, colonial education<br>
facilitated the social stratification of<br>
<b>Africans in the same society as educated</b><br>
<b>Africans regarded themselves superior</b><br>
to their fellows who were not educated.<br>
<b>Generally, colonial education was alien</b><br>
and, as such, it imparted Western values<br>
to Africans.<br>
<b>However, it is important to acknowledge</b><br>
that the colonial education policy<br>
shed light on nationalistic sentiments,<br>
that is, it produced some prominent<br>
nationalist leaders. Like elsewhere<br>
in Africa, in Tanganyika colonial<br>
education produced radical elites like<br>
<b>Julius Kambarage Nyerere and Rashid</b><br>
<b>Mfaume Kawawa, who championed</b><br>
the struggle for independence.<br>
<b>Colonial education policies in</b><br>
<b>Tanganyika up to 1961</b><br>
<b>After the establishment of colonial</b><br>
rule in Tanganyika, there was a need to<br>
ensure that the people accepted colonial<br>
policies. This was considered very<br>
important as the colonial government<br>
was in need of raw materials, cheap<br>
labour and markets. To achieve this<br>
objective, the Germans and the British,<br>
who colonised Tanganyika at different<br>
times, introduced a European education<br>
system to maximise the exploitation of<br>
<b>African natural resources and labour in</b><br>
their own interest.<br>
<b>Between 1890 and 1918, the Germans</b><br>
introduced an education policy that<br>
reflected their mode of administration.<br>
<b>The mode of administration adopted</b><br>
was direct rule. Apart from reflecting<br>
this mode of administration, education<br>
played an important role in building<br>

<b>The main agencies that provided</b><br>
education during the German colonial<br>
administration were Protestant and<br>
<b>Catholic</b><br>
missionary<br>
organisations<br>
and the colonial government itself.<br>
<b>Their curricula put much emphasis</b><br>
on vocational education, masonry,<br>
carpentry, tailoring, road construction<br>
and<br>
agriculture.<br>
<b>However,</b><br>
the<br>
educational activities introduced by<br>
the Germans were short-lived. They<br>
ended with the First World War. Later<br>
on, educational activities were re-<br>
structured by the British when they<br>
took Tanganyika as a mandate territory<br>
under the League of Nations.<br>
<b>In 1925, the British government</b><br>
introduced a system of ruling Africans<br>
in accordance with their traditional<br>
political organisations. The system<br>
was popularly called indirect rule. It<br>
was introduced by the second British<br>
<b>Governor, Sir Donald Cameron. The</b><br>
system involved integrating African<br>
political structures into the central<br>
<b>British administrative structure. The</b><br>
indirect rule policy influenced the<br>
nature and development of education<br>
in Tanganyika. Special schools were<br>
established to train the sons of chiefs<br>
to ensure that the local leadership was<br>
indoctrinated with British values and<br>
the British political ideology.<br>
<b>The policy that guided education in</b><br>
colonial Tanganyika up to the Second<br>
World War was that of adaptation.<br>
<b>This kind of thinking was established</b><br>
in 1925 under Governor Sir Donald<br>
<b>Cameron following the report by the</b><br>
Phelps Stokes Commission of 1923.<br>
<b>The main purpose of the commission</b><br>
was to create an education system that<br>
reflected the needs of the society. The<br>
curriculum consisted of agriculture,<br>
development<br>
of<br>
local<br>
industries,<br>
improvement of health, improvement<br>
of true ideals of citizenship and the<br>
training of people in managing their<br>
own affairs. Generally, education for<br>
adaptation meant development of the<br>
rural sector and improvement of the<br>
socio-economic conditions of the local<br>
population.<br>
<b>After the Second World War, the British</b><br>
government introduced the Ten-Year<br>
<b>Educational Development Plan. It was</b><br>
implemented  from 1947 to 1956. The<br>
plan dealt with, among other things, the<br>
expansion of primary schools, middle<br>
schools and teacher training colleges,<br>
the establishment of technical education<br>
and the enrolment of girls on schools.<br>
<b>Therefore, the plan was intended to</b><br>
expand indigenous education. It placed<br>
high emphasis on primary education,<br>
teacher education and technical<br>
education. This education continued to<br>
serve the colonial needs.<br>
<b>Following the achievements obtained</b><br>
through the implementation of the Ten-Year<br>
<b>Educational Development Plan, the British</b><br>
colonial government formulated another<br>

<b>Five-Year Educational Development Plan</b><br>

insisted on improving the quality of<br>

and promoting agricultural, technical<br>

<b>However, at independence in 1961,</b><br>
there were high illiteracy rates among<br>
adults and children. There was also<br>
very small pupils enrolment on primary<br>
school. The uneven distribution of<br>
schools was marked, since the colonial<br>
education system favoured the areas<br>
which engaged in cash crop production.<br>
<b>Colonial education also created social</b><br>
stratification. For example, the educated<br>
regarded themselves as superior and<br>
regarded the non-educated as inferior.<br>
<b>Therefore, colonial education alienated</b><br>
<b>Africans, especially the educated, from</b><br>
their communities.<br>
<b>Changes in the education policy in</b><br>
<b>Tanzania after independence (1961)</b><br>
<b>Soon after independence, the president</b><br>
of Tanganyika, Julius K. Nyerere,<br>
noted some weaknesses in the system<br>
of education inherited from the British.<br>
<b>In his view, the system had been</b><br>
designed in such a way that it could<br>
create a class of a few privileged<br>
elites. In response, President Nyerere<br>
advocated a system of education<br>
that would not favour the elites and<br>
undermine others in the country. The<br>
president criticised colonial education<br>
for encouraging individualistic values,<br>
instead of promoting a cooperative<br>
spirit among the people. He also<br>
viewed colonial education as an<br>
instrument for destroying African<br>
values and replacing them with<br>
colonial and Western values. Thus,<br>
<b>President Nyerere wanted to reform</b><br>
the primary school curriculum, which<br>
was meant to promote and maintain<br>
colonial rule and its ideology. The need<br>
to improve and develop the education<br>
system was reflected in the objectives<br>
of the development plans discussed<br>
in the previous section of this chapter.<br>
<b>New curricula which emphasised self-</b><br>
reliance and agricultural production in<br>
primary and secondary schools were<br>
designed and implemented.<br>
<b>The government also sought to reduce</b><br>
illiteracy.<br>
<b>Before</b><br>
independence,<br>
<b>Tanganyikans who knew how to read</b><br>
and write were very few numerically.<br>
<b>At independence, only 10 per cent of</b><br>
the population knew how to read and<br>
write. To address this problem, the<br>
government reforms the education<br>
policy and intensified the efforts to<br>
eradicate illiteracy in the country. The<br>
<b>First Three-Year Development Plan of</b><br>
1961-1964 was aimed at expanding<br>
the enrolment of pupils on schools to<br>
reduce illiteracy.<br>
<b>The provision of education based on</b><br>

<b>Furthermore, the education provided</b><br>
emphasised solving the shortage of<br>
skilled manpower which emerged soon<br>
after independence. Much emphasis<br>
was placed on expanding secondary<br>
and higher education to train middle<br>
and high-level manpower. This was<br>
attained through the diversification<br>
of the curricula to include various<br>
disciplines. As a result, Tanzania<br>
witnessed an expansion in student<br>

example, the number of students in<br>
secondary schools from Forms I to<br>
<b>VI increased from 11832 in 1961</b><br>
and 19897 in 1964 to 31600 in 1971.<br>
<b>More emphasis were placed on science</b><br>
subjects. In 1969, out of the 2563 pupils<br>
who were in Forms V and VI, 1509<br>
