3251 pupils who studying in Forms<br>
<b>V, and VI 2158 (67 per cent) were</b><br>
studying science subjects.<br>
<b>Moreover, in the 1960s, African history</b><br>
was strengthened in order to create a<br>
spirit of patriotism and nation-building<br>
based on the African philosophy of<br>
self-reliance and socialism. Thus,<br>
the expansion of primary, secondary<br>
as well as technical education was<br>
intended to prepare skilled people who<br>
could occupy various positions. In the<br>
civil service, for example,  Tanzanians’<br>
graduates gradually replaced European<br>
civil servants in the 1960s and early<br>
1970s.<br>
<b>The education policy adopted after</b><br>
independence also emphasised the<br>
incorporation of self-reliance into the<br>
school curriculum. Teaching was directed<br>
towards imparting various skills to the<br>
learners so that they could be self-reliant<br>
after completing their studies. For example,<br>
agricultural activities were introduced into<br>
schools to familiarise students with their<br>
local settings and to make schools less<br>
dependent on external financial support.<br>
<b>They were also aimed at changing the</b><br>
mind-set of the people who associated<br>
the attainment of school certificates with<br>
doing white-collar jobs.<br>
<b>Another reform was the abolition of</b><br>
school fees. One of the main obstacles<br>
towards achieving educational goals<br>
after independence was parents’ failure<br>
to pay school fees for their children.<br>
<b>This was because many parents were</b><br>

<b>Another change was the introduction</b><br>
of adult education. The government<br>
realised that adult education was<br>
important in national development.<br>
<b>Having educated adult people would</b><br>
produce immediate results because<br>
the country was trying to speed up<br>
development<br>

<b>In</b><br>
1964,<br>
the Institute of Adult Education<br>
was established and was given the<br>
task of formulating and executing<br>

<b>Another major change made soon after</b><br>
independence was the adoption of the<br>
<b>Kiswahili language as a medium of</b><br>
instruction in primary schools. This<br>
was done to promote the local culture<br>
and values. In early 1962, the Kiswahili<br>
language replaced the English language<br>
in primary schools.<br>
<b>Other</b><br>
modifications<br>
which<br>
were<br>
incorporated into the education system<br>
after independence involved changing<br>
the curriculum. The curriculum was<br>
modified to accommodate the teaching<br>
of local ideals, values and culture.<br>
<b>Unlike the colonial curriculum which</b><br>
insisted on teaching Western values, the<br>
new curriculum emphasised teaching<br>
local values, which reflected the local<br>
environment.<br>
<b>Thus, in the early years of independence,</b><br>
education became a basic human right<br>
for everyone in the country, regardless<br>
of one’s colour, gender, religion,<br>
ethnic and economic status. Access to<br>
education was no longer determined by<br>
affiliation with the ruling class nor was<br>
education intended to prepare a class of a<br>
few privileged people. The government<br>
expanded<br>
student<br>
enrolment<br>
on<br>
primary and secondary schools and<br>
established schools in various regions<br>
to accommodate students from various<br>
parts of the country in order to foster<br>
national unity and development based<br>
on the philosophy of self-reliance.<br>
<b>Education for Self-Reliance 1967-1985</b><br>
<b>Following the Arusha Declaration,</b><br>
which instituted socialism as the<br>
country’s philosophy, the government<br>
sought to review the national education<br>
curriculum in keeping with this new<br>
philosophy. The education provided<br>
was to enable each recipient to have<br>
socialist values. It was from this drive<br>
that the philosophy of Education for<br>
<b>Self-Reliance (ESR) was published</b><br>
in March 1967. The purpose was to<br>
prepare young people to learn to serve<br>
their society. The ESR was aimed at<br>
imparting and transmitting knowledge,<br>

youth for higher learning institutions.<br>
<b>Education was, in that respect, intended</b><br>

to provide the knowledge, skills and<br>

socialist state.<br>
<b>Furthermore, the ESR philosophy</b><br>
emphasised collective making of efforts<br>

such a way that they would become<br>
economic, social and educational<br>
communities capable of meeting their<br>
own maintenance needs. In that respect,<br>
by 1968, all primary and secondary<br>
schools, as well as teacher-training<br>
colleges had embarked on farming<br>
and other self-reliance activities like<br>
poultry projects, shops and livestock<br>
keeping.<br>
<b>The ESR was a typical example</b><br>
of equality in society as the policy<br>
document was aimed at making<br>
education beneficial to the whole<br>
society. The policy objective would be<br>
attained through making educational<br>
institutions producers of goods which<br>
would, in turn, benefit the same<br>
institutions. In addition, all learners<br>
were required to participate in various<br>
communal productive activities. Under<br>
the ESR philosophy, examinations<br>
were to be structured in such a way that<br>
they measured students theoretically<br>
and practically.<br>
<b>Moreover, primary education was not</b><br>
used as a ladder to the secondary level<br>
of education. Rather, it was supposed<br>
to prepare students for future life.<br>
<b>Likewise, secondary education was not</b><br>
a ladder to universities, teacher training<br>
colleges<br>
and<br>
technical<br>
colleges.<br>
<b>Instead, it was to prepare people for life</b><br>
and service in the society.<br>
<b>The middle-level primary schools, most</b><br>
of which were boarding schools run by<br>
missionaries or private organisations,<br>
were abolished. The payment of school<br>
fees was abolished at all levels of<br>
education. This transformation had far-<br>
reaching consequences. Children from<br>
poor families began to enjoy upward<br>
social mobility.<br>
<b>Generally, the type of education</b><br>
adopted during the Second Five-Year<br>
<b>Plan, 1969-74, reflected the ESR</b><br>
philosophy. It advocated the move<br>
towards self-sufficiency in terms of<br>
skills at all levels of the economy. This<br>
type of education was aimed at giving<br>
primary education to every child.<br>
<b>Primary education was, therefore,</b><br>
declared free and compulsory.<br>
<b>The success of Education for Self-</b><br>
<b>Reliance</b><br>
<b>Despite</b><br>
the<br>
challenges,<br>
the<br>
<b>ESR</b><br>
programme<br>
realised<br>
some<br>
achievements. Among of them were<br>

the increase in student enrolment at<br>
all levels of  education. For instance,<br>
in 1962, pupil enrolment on primary<br>
schools stood at 518 663, but the number<br>
rose to 1990 650 in 1976 and 3530 622<br>
in 1981. This tremendous increase was<br>
due to the ESR programme. Some of<br>
the reasons for the increase included<br>
the abolition of school fees and the<br>
nationalisation of private schools.<br>
<b>Furthermore, the ESR helped to</b><br>
eradicate the problem of ethnicity and<br>
gender imbalance, for the students who<br>
were selected to join secondary schools<br>
were distributed nationwide, regardless<br>
of their ethnic groups and regional<br>
backgrounds. The quota system served<br>
this purpose. Emphasis was also put on<br>
gender equality. As a consequence, the<br>
<b>ESR helped to minimise the problem of</b><br>
gender discrimination in education. For<br>
instance, in 1961, female enrolment in<br>
<b>Form One was 29 per cent and Form</b><br>
<b>Six 9 per cent. By 1981, the total</b><br>
female enrolment in Forms I-VI had<br>
risen to 34 per cent.<br>
<b>Another success of the ESR was the</b><br>
introduction of political education<br>
to create political awareness and<br>
consciousness among pupils on politics,<br>
economics and the social condition<br>
of the nation. Political education was<br>
introduced as a compulsory subject for<br>
all learners.<br>
<b>Moreover, the ESR was the foundation</b><br>
for the Universal Primary Education<br>
<b>(UPE)</b><br>
whereby<br>
education<br>
was<br>
provided free of charge and was<br>
compulsory to all children without any<br>
discrimination. This measure solved<br>
the problem of school fees and rural-<br>
urban migration among school leavers.<br>
<b>To make it effective, the government</b><br>
allowed secondary school graduates to<br>
teach in primary schools.<br>
<b>Finally, the ESR imparted practical</b><br>
skills to pupils. Learners studied skills<br>
relevant to their societies such as crop<br>
farming and animal keeping.<br>
<b>Failure of Education for</b><br>
<b>Self-Reliance</b><br>
<b>Implementation of the ESR was not</b><br>
successful  for various reasons. One of<br>
the reasons was the lack of guidance as<br>
education stakeholders implemented<br>
it blindly. Neither the ESR nor later<br>
policies gave directives on how to<br>
pursue the objectives. Although in the<br>
examinations there were questions<br>
closely related to the ESR philosophy,<br>
the  questions could not be used to<br>
evaluate the implementation of the<br>
policy broadly.<br>
<b>The implementation of the ESR also</b><br>
lacked proper organisation as it was<br>
not coordinated from above. Thus, it<br>
was difficult to evaluate the strategies<br>
which were being implemented.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
the<br>
teachers<br>
who<br>
implemented this programme were<br>
not trained in how to implement it.<br>
<b>The programme integrated academic</b><br>
training and manual work. As a<br>

<b>Another problem was the students and</b><br>

towards manual work and national<br>

success.<br>

who misbehaved were punished by<br>

<b>There was also a shortage of funds</b><br>
for implementing the ESR. The<br>
government lacked enough funds to<br>
run ESR projects and buy teaching-<br>
learning materials. Thus, there was an<br>
acute shortage of relevant teaching and<br>
learning materials like books and pieces<br>
of chalk. There was also a shortage of<br>
desks. Parents did not consider the<br>
<b>ESR useful because even classrooms</b><br>
were built by them. Finally, the ESR<br>
lacked a proper assessment mechanism<br>
as it was implemented differently in<br>
different places.<br>

<b>Education policies after the failure of</b><br>
<b>Education for Self-Reliance</b><br>
<b>Since independence, the government</b><br>
has carried out reforms in the<br>
education sector to achieve the set<br>
objectives. One of the largest reforms<br>
was the 1974 Musoma Resolution,<br>
which was passed after the conference<br>
held in Musoma to assess the ESR.<br>
<b>The Musoma Resolution continued</b><br>
strategies to correct the major problems<br>
of the ESR. The strategies included the<br>
universalisation of primary education<br>
and the integration of vocational<br>
training with academic training.<br>
<b>Another reform was the 1990 education</b><br>
review. In 1990, a National Task Force<br>
on Education was appointed to evaluate<br>
and review the education system and<br>
recommend a suitable educational<br>
system for the 21st century. The task<br>
force recommended improving the<br>
quality of education and strengthening<br>
the link between the education provided<br>
at all levels and socio-economic<br>
development in Tanzania.<br>
<b>Moreover, in 1995, the government</b><br>
introduced the Education and Training<br>
<b>Policy (ETP). The policy was aimed at</b><br>

of the ETP was on decentralisation of<br>
education, establishment of schools and<br>
expansion of training opportunities,<br>
access, equity curricula, examination<br>
and certification, teacher education<br>
and  tertiary and higher education.<br>
<b>Under this policy the role and visibility</b><br>

increased. There was also a reduction<br>
of subsidies and the introduction of<br>
cost-sharing in education.<br>
<b>Additionally, in early 1997, the</b><br>

developed<br>

<b>Basic</b><br>

<b>BEMP was intended to ensure that the</b><br>
quality of education was good.<br>
<b>There was the Local Government</b><br>
<b>Reforms Agenda in 1998. Under such</b><br>
reforms, less developed regions were<br>
given preference in the establishment<br>
of new schools. Regions like Mtwara<br>
and Lindi were given priority to bring<br>
about equality in the education sector.<br>
<b>Furthermore,</b><br>
between<br>
1999<br>
and<br>
2001 the government implemented<br>
the Primary Education Development<br>
<b>Programme (PEDP). PEDP was part</b><br>
of the 2025 development vision. PEDP<br>
resulted in the abolition of school fees<br>
and mandatory contributions. It also<br>
resulted in the recruitment of 50 per cent<br>
of teachers in five years, the building<br>
of over 40 000 new classrooms and the<br>
introduction of an annual capitation<br>
grant of 10 USD per pupil.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
<b>Secondary</b><br>
<b>Education</b><br>
<b>Development</b><br>
<b>Programme</b><br>
<b>(SEDP)</b><br>
was also introduced. Under SEDP,<br>
emphasis<br>
was<br>
put<br>
on<br>
specific<br>
subjects, especially Information and<br>
Communication Technology (ICT).<br>
<b>Generally, education policies have</b><br>
been changing to reflect the needs of the<br>
country in different economic phases.<br>
<b>For example, as explained earlier, the</b><br>
education policies implemented  from<br>
the 1960s to early 1980s suited the<br>
objective of building a socialist and<br>
self-reliant society.<br>

<b>Challenges that faced development</b><br>
efforts in Tanzania<br>
<b>Like</b><br>
any<br>
other African<br>
country,<br>
<b>Tanzania faced various challenges</b><br>
before and after independence. Tanzania<br>
faced certain economic, social and<br>
political challenges that she inherited<br>
from colonialism. It also faced certain<br>
challenges from the mid-1970s to early<br>
1980s. The challenges are commonly<br>
called economic crises which are<br>
discussed in the following section.<br>
<b>The economic crises of the 1970s</b><br>
and 1980s<br>
<b>The economic crises of the 1970s and</b><br>
1980s were caused by a shortage of<br>
consumer goods and basic production<br>
inputs such as agricultural implements<br>
and industrial inputs such as spare parts<br>
and raw materials. Basic needs such as<br>
home-based commodities like cooking<br>
oil, salt, soap, sugar, batteries and<br>
kerosene were also insufficient.<br>
<b>Moreover, there was high inflation. The</b><br>
rate of inflation, for example, increased<br>
from 10 to 12 per cent in the early<br>
1970s to about 25 per cent in the late<br>
1970s. In the early 1980s it increased<br>
to 30 per cent.<br>
<b>Furthermore, there was a decline in</b><br>
the performance of the manufacturing<br>
sector. The decline in commercial<br>
agriculture, particularly export crops,<br>
was a major factor for the decline of<br>
the industrial sector.<br>
<b>There was also the deterioration of the</b><br>
quality and provision of social services<br>
which was observed in several sectors<br>
like the health, education and water<br>
sectors. The government’s ability<br>
to allocate funds to these services<br>
declined remarkably. For example,<br>
the funds allocated to the education<br>
sector declined in relation to the total<br>
government<br>
expenditure<br>
between<br>
the 1970s and 1980s. Thus, from the<br>
beginning of the 1980s, the education<br>
sector faced several problems such<br>
as lack of textbooks, lack of desks,<br>
lack of staff houses and poor school<br>
maintenance.<br>
<b>There was also deterioration in the</b><br>
transport sector. The problem was<br>
seen in the lack of spare parts and poor<br>
maintenance of roads and railways. This<br>
affected the collection of agricultural<br>
products and the delivery of inputs<br>
and basic consumer goods to the rural<br>
areas. As a result, there was a decline in<br>
agricultural production. Agriculture is<br>
the backbone of Tanzania’s economy.<br>
<b>This decline resulted in the fall in</b><br>
the volume of exports, especially the<br>
volume of cashew nuts, coffee, sisal,<br>
tea and tobacco, which were the main<br>
export crops. A serious drop in these<br>
crops was witnessed between the early<br>
1970s and mid-1980s. As a result,<br>
there was further depletion of foreign<br>
exchange.<br>

07/03/2022   19:00:25<br>
<b>Student's Book Form Five</b><br>
<b>Advanced History for Secondary Schools</b><br>
227<br>
<b>Factors for the economic crises of the</b><br>
1970s and 1980s<br>
<b>There were several factors for the</b><br>
economic crises of the 1970s and<br>
1980s. The major factors included the<br>
following:<br>
<b>One of the factor for the economic</b><br>
crises was an increase in the price of<br>
petroleum oil. The war between Israel<br>
and the Arab states of Egypt and Syria<br>
in 1973 increased the price of oil more<br>
than four times at the world market.<br>
<b>This increase came about because the</b><br>
<b>Arab nations did not export oil to the</b><br>
<b>USA and Western Europe because these</b><br>
powers supported Israel during the war.<br>
The price of oil increased again in 1974.<br>
<b>Like other countries, Tanzania was</b><br>
affected by this increase. In this regard,<br>
the government earned less foreign<br>
exchange from its exports and used a<br>
huge amount of foreign exchange to<br>
import oil at very high prices. At the<br>
same time, there was a drastic decline<br>
in the price of agricultural exports.<br>
<b>The occurrence of persistent drought</b><br>
in the early 1970s also contributed<br>
to the economic crises. The country<br>
was hit hard by a severe drought in<br>
1973 and 1974. This caused  severe<br>
famine throughout Tanzania. The<br>
country exhausted all its food reserves<br>
to enable the victims to survive the<br>
famine. Thus, the government used<br>
much money to import food, especially<br>
maize, wheat and beans for the victims<br>
of famine. Thus, funds which should<br>
have been used to implement national<br>
development plans were used to import<br>
relief food from the USA an North<br>
America.<br>
<b>Moreover, the collapse of the East</b><br>
<b>African Community (EAC) in 1977</b><br>
adversely affected the economy of<br>
<b>Tanzania. Tanzania was forced to</b><br>
establish new structures for civil<br>
aviation,<br>
harbour<br>
administration,<br>
railways,<br>
postal<br>
services<br>
and<br>
telecommunications. Investment in<br>
infrastructure made the government<br>
spent a large amount of funds highly<br>
needed for other domestic purposes.<br>
<b>Hence, the economic crises were</b><br>
inevitable.<br>
<b>The war between Tanzania and Uganda,</b><br>
which was fought from 9th October<br>
1978 to 3rd June 1979, also caused<br>
the economic crises of the 1970s and<br>
1980s. The government used much<br>
money during the war by buying<br>
weapons and financing the logistics<br>
of the war such as the movement of<br>
soldiers, foodstuffs and other war<br>
needs from Tanzania to Uganda.<br>
<b>As a consequence, the government</b><br>
experienced a serious shortage of funds<br>
for use in the  economic and social<br>
sectors. The estimated cost of the war<br>
was US $500 million. This cost was so<br>
big that it had a direct negative impact<br>
on the country’s budget.<br>
<b>The oil price shock of 1979 was yet</b><br>
another reason for the economic crises.<br>

<b>The price of oil almost doubled in the</b><br>
period between 1978 and 1979, thus<br>
increasing the import bill which was<br>
three times more than that of 1974 and<br>
1975. The price of oil destabilised the<br>
economy as in the 1980s it accounted<br>
for over 55 per cent of export earnings<br>
and 23 per cent of its imports.<br>
<b>Measures the government took to</b><br>
overcome the challenges<br>
<b>Initially, the government sought to</b><br>
improve domestic policies before<br>
finding foreign-based measures to<br>
solve the crises. Some of the measures<br>
taken are discussed below.<br>
<b>Internally-designed reforms</b><br>
<b>The first major attempt that the</b><br>
government made was to formulate<br>
a<br>
<b>National</b><br>
<b>Economic</b><br>
<b>Survival</b><br>
Programme (NESP) in 1981-1982.<br>
<b>The objectives of the NESP were to</b><br>
increase substantially foreign exchange<br>
earnings, save on imports, eliminate<br>
food shortages through village-centred<br>
irrigation schemes, controlling public<br>
expenditure in both government and<br>
parastatals, formulating development<br>
plans that emphasised the consolidation<br>
of activities, instead of introducing new<br>
activities, and raising the productivity<br>
of workers and farmers through<br>
appropriate incentive schemes.<br>
<b>The objectives of the NESP were</b><br>
not realised because they were ill-<br>
defined. It was argued that the NESP<br>
was formulated in a hurry and that<br>
it<br>
contained<br>
unrealistic<br>
targets.<br>
<b>Therefore, the programme did not lead</b><br>
to economic recovery.<br>
<b>The government also implemented</b><br>
the<br>
domestically-based<br>
<b>Structural</b><br>
<b>Adjustment</b><br>
<b>Programmes</b><br>
(SAPs)<br>
between 1982 and 1985. The major<br>
objective was to finance the fiscal<br>
deficit<br>
largely<br>
through<br>
domestic<br>
borrowing in the absence of external<br>
assistance to reduce inflation, restore<br>
production, maintain social services<br>
and revive the economic infrastructure.<br>
<b>The SAPs were also intended to</b><br>
promote agricultural development and<br>
improve efficiency.<br>
<b>Furthermore, several provisions were</b><br>
put in place to fight economic sabotage<br>
which was common at the time of<br>
the crises. The government passed<br>
<b>Economic Sabotage Act No. 9 of 1983</b><br>
to fight overpricing and the hoarding<br>
of goods done because of the scarcity<br>
of commodities in several parts of the<br>
country. The government established<br>
special tribunals to deal with economic<br>
sabotage offences.<br>
<b>To contain the decline in the price of</b><br>
crops, the government reintroduced<br>
cooperatives through the Cooperatives<br>
<b>Act of 1982. It should be noted that</b><br>
cooperatives  had been banned in<br>
1976. Moreover, the 1982 Act allowed<br>
private ownership of land, advocated<br>
the liberalisation of several aspects<br>
of agricultural marketing and paved<br>

229<br>

<b>Despite the government’s efforts to</b><br>
address  challenges, most domestic<br>
measures did not bear fruit. Most of<br>
them failed either because of financial<br>
constraints or because of inefficiency and<br>
maladministration. Foreign assistance<br>
was, therefore, sought to supplement<br>
the domestic initiatives, which had<br>
failed to recover the economy. Thus, the<br>
persistence of the challenges  left the<br>
country with no option but to introduce<br>
donor-funded economic recovery<br>
programmes in 1986.<br>
<b>Activity 7.3</b><br>
<b>Imagine</b><br>
you<br>
were<br>
<b>Mwalimu</b><br>
<b>Nyerere, the first president of</b><br>
Tanzania.<br>
<b>Address</b><br>
the<br>
nation<br>
on the economic situation after<br>

<b>Suggest</b><br>

Present your speech in class.<br>
<b>Structural Adjustment Programmes</b><br>
(SAPs)<br>
<b>In the mid-1980s, it was realised that,</b><br>
in order to overcome Tanzania’s socio-<br>
economic challenges, there was a need<br>
to re-adjust the economy. Thus, the<br>
<b>World Bank (WB) and the International</b><br>
<b>Monetary Fund (IMF) were consulted</b><br>
to secure either loans or grants for that<br>
purpose. The assistance received from<br>
the international community saved the<br>
economy from declining and, thus, the<br>
economy started growing in the late<br>
1990s.<br>
<b>Tanzania adopted a donor-funded</b><br>
<b>Economic Recovery Programme (ERP)</b><br>
in 1986 after its domestic policies had<br>
failed. The ERP came up with a set of<br>
<b>Structural Adjustment Programmes</b><br>
(SAPs) and a package of reforms that<br>
were essentially aimed at liberalising<br>
the<br>
economy.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
<b>SAP-related</b><br>
measures included the devaluation of<br>
the shilling, the liberalisation of trade<br>
and the encouragement of local and<br>
private capital producer incentives.<br>
<b>Others were the liberalisation of internal</b><br>
trade by removing price controls,<br>
cutting government expenditure on<br>
social and productive services through<br>
cost-sharing measures and eliminating<br>
subsidies<br>
and<br>
allowances.<br>
<b>The</b><br>
government was also advised to reform<br>
the political system by re-introducing<br>
the multiparty system, which was<br>
expected to bring about checks and<br>
balances and, indeed, accountability.<br>
<b>Moreover, the SAPs were meant to</b><br>
increase production for internal and<br>
external markets and to improve the<br>
country’s physical infrastructure and<br>
the industrial sector. However, as<br>
explained earlier, in the long run the<br>
<b>SAPs did not enable the government to</b><br>
solve the economic crises.<br>

<b>Exercise 7.6</b><br>
The SAPs have been interpreted differently by different scholars. Some<br>
scholars view the SAPs as a perfect solution to Tanzania’s socio-economic<br>
challenges. Other scholars view them as a source of other economic problems.<br>
<b>What are your views on this and why?</b><br>

Provide a brief history of the constitutional changes made in Tanzania<br>
from 1961 to the 2000s.<br>
2.<br>

3.<br>
Evaluate the effects of the colonial education policy up to 1961.<br>

231<br>
<b>Act</b><br>
a law that has been officially accepted by the governing body<br>
of a country<br>
<b>Amendment</b><br>
a minor change or addition made to improve a piece of<br>
legislation<br>
<b>Ancient</b><br>
belonging to the distance past or something dating to the past<br>
<b>Articulation</b><br>
includes transformation, some changes, adoption and<br>
preservation of some pre-colonial elements<br>
<b>Barbaric</b><br>
extremely cruel and unpleasant<br>
<b>Bill</b><br>
a draft of a proposed law presented to parliament for discussion<br>
and approval<br>
<b>Cathedral</b><br>
a church that contains a seat of a bishop, thus serving as a<br>
central church in a diocese<br>
<b>Coercive</b><br>
using force to make people do things that they are unwilling<br>
to do<br>
<b>Compelled</b><br>
forcefully driven to a particular course of action<br>
<b>Crown land</b><br>
the territorial area belonging to the monarch, governor or<br>
president who holds it on behalf of his or her subject<br>
<b>Declaration</b><br>
the act of making an official statement about something<br>
<b>Diaspora</b><br>
dispersion of people from their homeland or a community<br>
formed by people who have exited or been removed from their<br>
homeland<br>
<b>Evict</b><br>
officially forcing someone to leave a house or piece of land<br>
<b>Gandhism</b><br>
a body of ideas and principles that describes the inspiration,<br>
vision, life and work of Mahatma Gandhi<br>
<b>Hinterland</b><br>
the remote areas of a country away from the coast or the banks<br>
of the major rivers<br>

<b>Sabotage</b><br>

<b>Sanction</b><br>

<b>Trustee</b><br>

<b>Union Jack</b><br>
